# 道德真经衍义手钞

**The *True Classic of the Dao and Its Virtue: Expanded Commentary in Manuscript Form**
*(Title: Expanded Commentary on the True Classic of the Dao and Its Virtue)*
By Yuan-dynasty scholar **Wang Shouzheng** (Five-Peak Qing’an Hermit)
Twenty scrolls (originally missing Scroll One and Scroll Two)
Source: *Zhengtong Daozang* (Orthodox Daoist Canon of the Zhengtong Era), *Dòngshén* (Cavernous Gods) Section, *Yùjuè* (Jade Precepts) Category

---
**Scroll Three of the Expanded Commentary on the *True Classic of the Dao and Its Virtue***

**Chapter Nine: "Holding Fullness Without Excess"**
*"Gold and jade fill the hall, yet none can guard them well.
Wealth and honor breed arrogance—self-inflicted ruin follows."*

**Expanded Commentary:**
The *Spring and Autumn Annals* records that **Bai Gong Sheng** of Chu, though he held the kingdom, could not distribute its treasures to the people.

**Manuscript Annotation:**
From the *Huainanzi*’s *"Honglie Jiedao Training"*:
Bai Gong’s given name was **Sheng**; he was enfeoffed at Bai, hence "Bai Gong." He was the grandson of Chu Ping Wang and son of Crown Prince Jian.

*(Note: The full account is already preserved in the original text.)*

**Expanded Commentary:**
In the *Spring and Autumn Annals*, **Ruan Xuanzhi** (Range the Elder), son of Fan Wenzi, governed as chief minister during the reign of Duke Zhao of Lu. The tribute from other lords grew heavy.

**Manuscript Annotation:**
The people of Zheng resented this, so **Zi Chan** (Child of Production, i.e., **Zi Chan of Zheng**, named **Qiu**, the senior minister) sent a letter to Xuanzhi.
*(Zi Chan was the chief minister of Zheng.)*
*"Leaving a message"* (寓书) means sending a letter.
This is to inform Xuanzhi.

*(The full account is already complete and need not be repeated.)*

**Expanded Commentary:**
In the *Spring and Autumn Annals*, **Wang Zi Chao** and **Bin Meng** were favored by King Jing of Zhou, who wished to establish Meng as heir.

**Manuscript Annotation:**
Wang Zi Chao was the eldest son of King Jing.
Bin Meng’s given name was **Qi**, and he was Chao’s tutor.
Meng said:
*"Why should a rooster fear being used by man?*
*Men differ from this.*
Though the sacrificial rooster may be adorned with honor, it will still be slaughtered.
If a man is adorned with honor, he will rise to nobility—his fate differs from the rooster’s."*

*(This implies Meng advised Chao to let the king bestow early favor upon him, lest he later be discarded.)*
*"When achievement is complete and name secured, withdrawal is the Dao of Heaven."*

**Expanded Commentary:**
The *Classic of Changes* states:
*"To know advancement yet not retreat, to know preservation yet not destruction, to know gain yet not loss—only the Sage avoids the regret of excess."*

**Manuscript Annotation:**
From the *Zhouyi* (Commentary on the *Yijing*), the *File of Qian* (乾卦) states:
*"‘Excess’ (亢) means—*
*to know advancement yet not retreat,*
*to know preservation yet not destruction,*
*to know gain yet not loss—*
*who but the Sage can do this?*
*Who but the Sage knows advancement, retreat, preservation, destruction, gain, and loss without losing the proper way?"*

This clarifies the meaning of the **ninth line of Qian**.
*"If one does not know advancement, retreat, preservation, destruction, gain, or loss,*
*then this uppermost line (上九) will bring regret through excess.*
*This is because of these three failings.*
*If one fully knows these three, even in the highest position, one will not exceed."*

**Expanded Commentary:**
*"When the sun reaches its zenith, it declines.
When the moon is full, it wanes.
The seasons cycle—achievement brings withdrawal."*

**Manuscript Annotation (Du Qujing’s Interpretation):**
*"When the sun is at its peak, the moon at its full,*
*summer gives way to winter,*
*spring’s flourish yields to autumn’s fall—*
*such is the Dao of Heaven.*
If man aligns with Heaven’s fullness and emptiness,
forgets merit and name,
withdraws from office and declines honor,
he will not stray from Heaven’s natural cycles—
then there will be no cause for blame."*

**Expanded Commentary:**
Take **Zhang Liang of Han**, the **Leave-Home Master** (i.e., **Zhang Zifang**).
Though ennobled with a ten-thousand-house fief,
he abandoned worldly affairs to follow **Chisongzi** (赤松子).

During the **Han Xuan Emperor’s** reign, **Su Guang** (字仲翁), whose nephew **Su Shou** was his adopted son, both hailed from **Lanling, Donghai**.
Guang served as **Grand Tutor to the Heir Apparent**, while Shou was **Minor Tutor**.
Guang said:
*"I have heard: ‘Knowing when to stop brings no peril; knowing contentment brings no disgrace.’*
*I have also heard: ‘When achievement is complete and name secured, withdrawal is the Dao of Heaven.’"*

Father and son thus resigned on grounds of illness.
The Emperor granted them **thirty *jin* of gold**, while the Heir Apparent gave **fifty *jin***.
They submitted a memorial requesting retirement and returned home.
Is this not the Dao of Heavenly withdrawal?

**Chapter Ten: Nurturing the Vital Essence and Embracing the One**

When nurturing the vital essence and embracing the One,
how could it ever depart?

**Explanatory Elucidation:**
*"To bear"* (載) means to carry by vehicle.
*"To bear"* (載) also means to ascend by vehicle.

Quoted from *Broad Exalted Meaning*:
*"Nurturing the vital essence"*—
The human body is where spirit-qi resides and where soul-spirit (魂魄) takes form.
To move and carry the body is like a vehicle bearing goods.
The *Western Ascension Scripture* states:
*"The body is the chariot of the spirit."*
Since it is likened to a vehicle, it must be carried.

The **empty shell** (虚魄) is the manifestation of yin-qi—
the form of a person.
Yang-qi is formless—
the spirit of a person,
the vessel of form.
Yet when yang-qi has not yet descended,
it lies inert, devoid of awareness,
like a dull stone or withered wood.
When yang-qi descends,
it gains movement—
thus, form is called *arcane shell* (魄), belonging to yin.
Spirit is called *soul* (魂), belonging to yang.
As long as there remains even a thread of yang-qi,
death cannot be complete.
As long as there remains even a thread of yin-qi,
transcendence cannot be achieved.
Thus, refining yin-qi to exhaustion grants ascent to the Nine Heavens as an immortal,
consorting with yang.
Refining yang-qi to exhaustion plunges one to the Nine Abysses as a ghost,
consorting with yin.
Therefore, one must preserve the yang-soul,
nurture the yin-shell,
to safeguard life,
embrace the One and uphold the Dao,
lest it ever depart for an instant.

**Explanatory Elucidation:**
Qu Yuan’s *Li Sao* states:
*"Bearing the vital essence and ascending the crimson mist,
parting the floating clouds to mount upward."*

Quoted:
Qu Yuan was a Grand Minister of the Three Gorges in Chu.
*Li Sao* is the title of his literary work.
*"Bearing the vital essence"* means to carry soul-spirit and ascend the crimson mist—
the crimson mist being the rising vapor of earthly breath.
*"Clouds"* refers to the vapors of mountains and rivers.
*"Parting"* means to depart.
To depart from floating clouds,
to carry soul-spirit,
to stride across the crimson mist and soar,
to part from floating clouds and ascend—
this is the mastery of qi to achieve supreme softness.
Could it be like the infant brother?

**Explanatory Elucidation:**
Thus it is said:
*"Hold firm to your resolve, yet do not exhaust your qi."*
*"Shuffling"* and *"hurrying"*—
these are movements of qi,
yet they agitate the mind.

Quoted from *Mencius’ Discussion with Gongsun Chou, Upper Section*:
Gongsun Chou was a disciple of Mencius from Qi.
He saw Mencius speak of resolve first, then qi.
Thus he asked:
*"If so, then focusing solely on resolve suffices."*
Again he asked:
*"Why must one not exhaust qi?"*
Mencius replied:
*"When resolve is singular, it moves qi.
**Annotation:** *Singular* means unified.
*Shuffling* means stumbling.
*Hurrying* means rushing.
Mencius said that when resolve is directed with singularity,
qi follows inevitably.
Yet when qi is concentrated in one direction,
resolve itself is moved in turn—
as when a person stumbles or rushes,
qi is fixed upon that action,
and the mind is disturbed in return.
Thus one must hold firm to resolve
while ensuring qi is not exhausted."*

**Explanatory Elucidation:**
*"To wail all day yet the throat unsore—
this is the pinnacle of harmony."*

Quoted from the original text of *Chapter Fifty-Five: The Depth of Virtue*.
Sū’s annotation:
*"When the heart stirs, qi is injured.
When qi is injured, the throat grows sore.
To wail all day yet the throat unsore—
this proves the heart undisturbed and qi in harmony."*

**Could governance of the people and the realm be without action?**

**Explanatory Elucidation:**
*"Cultivate within yourself, and virtue becomes genuine.
Cultivate for the world, and virtue becomes universal."*

Quoted from *Bìxū Zhēnrén’s* *Subtle Gatherings*:
*"Nearly applied to the self, yet extending far to the world."*
To cultivate the Dao within the body—
few thoughts, few desires—
extends life and strengthens vitality,
while all falsehoods are exhausted.

Sū’s elucidation:
*"Once the self is cultivated,
its virtue naturally extends outward,
even to governing the world."*

**Could the opening and closing of the Celestial Gate be without duality?**

**Explanatory Elucidation:**
The *Celestial Gate* is the gate of Nature.
Nature gives birth to the Supreme Ultimate.

Quoted from *Zhuangzi’s* *"Gēngsāng Chú"*:
Guo Xiang’s annotation:
*"The Celestial Gate is the name of the origin of all things.
It is called the Celestial Gate.
It is also called the Gate of All Marvels."*
Chéng Fashi said:
*"Heaven is the name of Nature.
The principles of creation and transformation,
though named, lack substance—
only observing all things emerging from this gate,
thus the Scripture calls it the Gate of All Marvels."*

**Explanatory Elucidation:**
Zhuangzi said:
*"There is birth, there is death,
there is exit, there is entry."*

Quoted from *Zhuangzi’s* *"Gēngsāng Chú"*—
the text has already been detailed.

**Translation:**

Again, the *Yì Jì* says:
*"Opening the gate is called **Qián (乾)**,
closing the gate is called **Kūn (坤)**,
alternating closure and opening is called **change (變)**.
Endless coming and going is called **harmony (通)**.
Bright and clear in all directions—
how could one remain unaware?"*

**Explanatory Elucidation** continues:
*"Like the reflection of objects in a mirror."*

Quoted from **Bìxū (碧虚)**:
*"This is used to illustrate the mirror—
the mirror’s inherent clarity and purity,
yet sometimes obscured by dimness,
for dust and grime adhere to it.
If one wipes away the dust and grime,
all things are reflected with perfect lucidity.
This metaphorizes the practitioner of Dao,
who nurtures their **hún (魂)** and **pò (魄)**,
embracing unity (抱一),
preventing separation—
then their true nature (一性) shines forth."*

**Explanatory Elucidation** continues:
*"Thus, the ruler of a state,
though their **virtue/power (德)** be bright and clear,
like the sun and moon illuminating the land,
should practice **withdrawing the senses (收視反聽)**,
sealing their ears (塞聪) and dimming their sight (蔽明),
so that the common folk live their daily lives
unaware of its presence."*

Quoted from *Guǎng Shèng Yì (廣聖義)*:
*"Bright and clear—
the wisdom of illumination (明白慧照).
The wisdom of illumination,
without near or far—
when the mind is stilled,
the heart forgotten,
it is as though one has no knowledge.
If one boasts of their knowledge,
they cling to attachments
and lose the Dao."*

*"The sovereign bears the singular vision (負獨見之明),
governing their realm—
yet their governance (政察察) is too scrutinizing,
and the people grow weary and impoverished.
The **Laozi (老君)** warns against this,
commanding them to forget their deeds (忘功),
cease their illumination (息照),
just as one might stuff their ears with cloth (黈纩塞耳)
to close their hearing,
or let their crown veil (冕旒垂目)
obscure their sight—
this too is the meaning of **withdrawing the senses (收視反聽)**,
sealing their ears (塞聪) and dimming their sight (蔽明),
so that the common folk live their daily lives
unaware of its presence."*

The text above has already been detailed—
no need to repeat it.
**Thirty Spokes Chapter (三十辐章)**, the eleventh.

**Thirty Spokes Chapter (三十辐章)**

The thirty spokes converge at a single hub—
when the hub is empty, its use is fulfilled.

**Elaborated Meaning:**
The *Zhou Li* (《周礼》) *Kaogong Ji* (考工记) states:
*"The carriage roof is round to symbolize Heaven,
the carriage body is square to symbolize Earth."*

The meaning is clear—no further elaboration is needed.

When one hollows out doors and windows to make a dwelling,
its emptiness is what makes it useful.

**Elaborated Meaning:**
In antiquity, people lived in caves.
The *Yi* (《易》) *Xici* (系辞) says:
*"In the upper antiquity, people dwelled in caves and lived in the wilds;
later, the sages transformed this with palaces and dwellings."*

The hexagram *Dazhuang* (大壮) is taken.
In the upper antiquity, before palaces were built,
people merely nested in summer and dwelled in caves in winter.
Thus, dwellings were constructed to replace this.

**Elaborated Meaning:**
The *Shijing* (《诗》) says:
*"Taofu, Taofu—digging and covering the cave."*
Meaning: they dug into the earth and covered it above.

The *Mao Shisan* (《毛诗》) on *Wenwang’s* (文王) *Mian* (绵) ode says:
*"Ancient Gong Danfu,
dug and covered the cave—
no home yet existed."*

Ancient Gong was the Duke of Nan (邠公).
*Ancient* means *long-standing*.
*Dan* is his given name.
He was the ancestor of King Wen.

Dwelling in Nan, he governed with virtue,
and many followed him.
Though he had no residence,
he shaped the earth and covered it,
dug the ground and hollowed it—
father, son, husband, wife dwelling together became a *family*.

**Annotations:**
The ministers of feudal lords addressed their lord as *Jun* (君).
*Fu* (复) means to cover upon the earth.
*Zaotü* (凿穴) means to dig—all like the shaping of clay.
This is like the kilns of today.

**Elaborated Meaning:**
Zhuangzi says:
*"If a dwelling has no empty space,
mother-in-law and daughter-in-law will clash in the paths."*
The *Zhuangzi* (《庄子》) *Waiwu* (外物) chapter says:
*"If a dwelling has no empty space,
the paths become narrow and congested,
and mother-in-law and daughter-in-law will quarrel over passage."*

The daughter-in-law should yield to her mother-in-law as a mark of filial piety,
yet now they contend over paths,
with nowhere to go but to harbor private grudges—
this leads to rebellion and strife.

Thus, *having* is for *utility*,
*emptiness* is for *use*.

**Elaborated Meaning:**
The *Yi* (《易》) says:
*"That which is above form is called the Dao;
that which is below form is called instruments."*
From the *Yi* (《易》) *Xici* (系辞).

The *Guangsheng Yi* (《广圣义》) says:
*"That which is above form is the root of the Dao—
pure, tranquil, and non-acting in its loftiness.
That which is below form is the function of the Dao—
receiving essence and flowing into form in its lowliness."*

---
**Five Colors Chapter (五色章) [Twelfth]**

The five colors blind the eyes,
the five tones deafen the ears,
the five flavors dull the palate.

**Elaborated Meaning:**
The five colors were established
to manifest the symbols of the Five Phases.
Embroidered patterns of *fǔfú* (黼黻) distinguish the adornments of rank.

**Annotations:**
The five colors are: *qīng* (青, blue-green), *huáng* (黄, yellow), *bái* (白, white), *chì* (赤, red), *hēi* (黑, black).
The Five Phases: *jīn* (金, metal), *mù* (木, wood), *shuǐ* (水, water), *huǒ* (火, fire), *tǔ* (土, earth).

*Fǔfú* (黼黻) embroidery—
the *Shangshu* (《尚书》) *Yì Jì* (益稷) says:
*"The Emperor said to Shun:
'You are my arms, legs, ears, and eyes.
I wish to govern left and right with the people—
you shall assist me.
I wish to exert strength in the four directions—
you shall lead.
I wish to observe the symbols of ancient times—
the sun, moon, stars, mountains, dragons, and *huá-chóng* (华虫),
so make them manifest.
The ancestral vessels, *zǎo* (藻), fire, powdered rice, *fǔfú* (黼黻), *xì* (絺), and embroidery—
with five colors vividly applied,
make the garments—
you shall clarify them.'*

**Explanation:**
Whenever *rǔ* (汝) is spoken,
it refers to the ministers of the time—
those below Yu.

The making of garments must be distinguished:
*Clothing* (衣) is for the upper body,
*skirt* (裳) is for the lower.
Together, they have twelve chapters
to differentiate the adornments of rank.

All clothing is dark (*xián* 玄),
all skirts are embroidered (*shù* 肃, *qǐ* 绮).
The sun, moon, stars, mountains, dragons, and *huá-chóng* (华虫)—
these six patterns appear on the clothing,
and are also seen on the ancestral vessels in the ancestral temple.

*Zǎo* (藻), fire, powdered rice, *fǔ* (黼), *fú* (黻)—
these six are embroidered on *xì* (絺) for the skirts alone.

The sun, moon, stars symbolize *clarity*,
the mountains symbolize *benevolence*.

The dragon symbolizes the manifestation of transformation.

The *huá-chóng* (华虫) are creatures of luminous clarity.

The Sage governs the world with clarity,
and enacts virtue through action—
though their methods may be unfathomable,
yet their governance, teachings, laws, and rites are fully realized.
With these, they rule the people,
and the people find peace.
With these, they serve the spirits,
and the spirits are honored.
Thus, these six motifs appear on the ancestral vessels,
and again on the garments.

Softness, compliance, purity, and cleanliness—
these are the qualities of *zǎo* (藻),
fit for presenting offerings.

Radiance, brightness, and swiftness—
these are the qualities of fire,
fit for cooking and preparation.

Rice nourishes humanity,
powdered rice (粉) adorns the world,
while *fǔ* (黼) serves as a boundary,
and *fú* (黻) distinguishes rank.

The Sage adorns truth with *zǎo*,
and polishes the Dao of kingship with powdered rice,
to nourish the world—
lying in their ability to set boundaries while maintaining distinction.

Only then can governance be perfected.
These six motifs appear on the skirts.

Yet later generations, with their embroidered finery,
pursued dazzling hues—
green and yellow, pleasing to the eye,
but straying into excess,
draining vitality and dimming wisdom,
unable to perceive the color beyond color.
What else could this be but blindness?
This is the corruption of the five colors.

**Extended Meaning:**
The establishment of the five tones
was meant to manifest the sounds of the Five Phases.

Metal, stone, silk, bamboo—
these convey the *qi* of Heaven and Earth.

**Annotation:**
The five tones, as per Han Sima Xiangru’s account:
*Gōng* (宫) is centrality—
the virtue of the ruler.
*Shāng* (商) is clarity—
the completion of things.
*Jiǎo* (角) is the beginning—
the emergence of things.
*Zhēng* (征) is prosperity—
the flourishing and abundance of things.
*Yǔ* (羽) is concealment—
the gathering and storage of things.

The *Shàngshū* [益稷篇] states:
"The Emperor said:
'I wish to hear of the six *lǜ* (律), the five tones, and the eight sounds,
to govern with precision,
so that my words may be properly expressed.
You must listen.
If I err, correct me.
Do not merely assent on the surface—
afterward, speak freely.'

**Explanation:**
The six *lǜ* are:
Eleventh month—Huángzhōng (黄钟),
First month—Tàizhū (太簇),
Third month—Gūxǐ (姑洗),
Fifth month—Ruìbīn (蕤宾),
Seventh month—Yízé (夷则),
Ninth month—Wúshè (无射).

The six *lǘ* (吕) are:
Second month—Jiázhōng (夹钟),
Fourth month—Zhōnglǘ (中吕),
Sixth month—Línzhōng (林钟),
Eighth month—Nánlǘ (南吕),
Tenth month—Yìngzhōng (应钟),
Twelfth month—Tàilǘ (太吕).

These are the six *lǘ*.

The five tones are *gōng, shāng, jiǎo, zhēng, yǔ*.
The eight sounds are metal, stone, silk, bamboo, clay, wood, gourd, and leather.

You, Yu, must heed and follow these,
and thus create the *lǐ* (礼) and *fú* (服),
making them clear and manifest.

This is the corruption of the five tones above.

**Extended Meaning:**
The establishment of the five flavors
was meant to manifest the harmony of the Five Phases,
yet people indulge in coarse and refined tastes,
their appetites insatiable,
clouding their spirit and defiling their essence.

**Annotation:**
The five flavors are sweet, bitter, sour, salty, and spicy.
*Shuǎng* (爽) means "to err"—
when taste is overindulged, harm follows,
leading to deafness, blindness, and loss of discernment.

**Extended Meaning:**
The delight in *chúhuàn* (刍豢).

**Annotation:**
From *Mèngzǐ* [告子章句上]:
"Mencius said:
'The joy of ritual and righteousness to my heart
is like the joy of coarse and refined foods to my mouth.'
*Sū* (刍) refers to grass—
grass-fed animals, such as cattle and sheep.
*Huàn* (豢) refers to grain-fed livestock—
pigs and dogs of the sort.
Thus, it is said that *chúhuàn* delights the palate.

**Extended Meaning:**
The insatiable gluttony of *tāotiè* (饕餮).

**Annotation:**
Greed for wealth is called *tāo* (饕),
greed for food is called *tiè* (餮).
It speaks of those who are insatiable in their greed for wealth and food.

All these—*tāotiè*—
obscure the spirit and defile the essence,
losing their proper balance.

Hunting and falconry drive men to madness.

**Extended Meaning:**
As recorded in the *Lǐjì* [王制].

「Annotations from *Zhōulǐ* [周礼] state:
Spring *sōu* (搜), selecting those barren.
Summer *miáo* (苗), taking those that do not bear grain.
Autumn *xiàn* (狝), slaughtering in abundance.
Winter *shòu* (狩), hunting without restraint.

*Shòu* (狩) means to take without discrimination.

**Extended Meaning:**
Excessive hunting without measure—
as in Summer *Tàikāng*’s hunt beyond *Luòbiǎo* (洛表),
where the Five Sons all lamented.

**Annotation:**
Excessive hunting is recorded in *Shàngshū* [五子之歌]:
"Tàikāng occupied the throne,
indulging in ease and pleasure, destroying his virtue.
The people all turned against him.
He wandered recklessly beyond *Luòbiǎo*,
returning only after ten cycles of the moon.
The Five Sons all lamented."
The Five Sons were Tàikāng’s five younger brothers.
They composed this song to admonish *Dà Yǔ*’s warnings.

Its second verse states:
*"Teachings say:
Within, moral corruption spreads;
without, hunting grows wanton.
Sweet wine and music,
tall halls and carved walls—
if one indulges in these,
ruin is inevitable.*
Now, this serves as proof:
If laws are disregarded,
chasing wild pursuits without restraint,
exhausting the land and harming farmers,
climbing cliffs and crossing dangers,
returning only after months—
what else but madness could follow?
This is the folly of hunting."

**Extended Meaning:**
The *Aī Gōng* of *Qí* loved hunting,
a subject of poetic censure.

**Annotation from *Máo Shī* [毛诗卷第五]:
*"Returning, he censures wastefulness."*
Aī Gōng delighted in hunting,
following beasts without restraint,
and the people imitated him,
making it a national custom.
Skill in hunting was called *xián* (accomplished),
leisure in chasing was called *hào* (virtuous).
Rare treasures became obstacles to conduct.

**Extended Meaning:**
The *Míngyuè* (明月) Jewel.

**Annotation:**
From *Tōngjiàn* [通鉴], *Xiǎnwáng*’s fourteenth year:
When *Qí* and *Wèi* met for a hunt in the wilds,
*Wèi Huì Wáng* asked *Qí Wēi Wáng*:
"Does *Qí* possess any treasures?"
Wēi Wáng replied, "None."
Huì Wáng said, "Though my kingdom is small,
I still have ten *jǐncùn* pearls—
each illuminating twelve chariots before and behind."
Wēi Wáng said, "What I treasure differs from yours.
I have *Tán Zǐ*, who guards the southern city—
then the *Chǔ* dare not raid.
*Yǎn Zǐ*, who guards *Gāotáng*—
then the *Zhào* dare not fish east of the *Hé*.
*Pínfū*, who guards *Xúzhōu*—
then *Yān* and *Zhào* migrate there, seventy families strong.
*Zhǒngshǒu*, who guards against thieves—
then the roads are safe, no lost goods.
These four ministers illuminate a thousand *lǐ*—
how much more than twelve chariots!"
Huì Wáng was ashamed.

**Extended Meaning:**
The *Yèguāng* (夜光) Pearl.

**Annotation:**
The *Chū* king possessed a *Yèguāng* pearl.
Asked where it came from, he replied:
*"A Chū minister, *Suī Hòu*, once traveled
and saw a cowherd boy strike a snake,
wounding it fatally.
Suī Hòu pitied it, saved it, washed it in water,
and sealed its wound with medicine—reviving it.
The snake vanished into the water,
returning as the *Hǎilóng Wáng*’s son.
Later, it brought a seven-*cùn* pearl in gratitude.
Suī Hòu presented it to the *Chū* king,
who placed it on his hall at night—
its light illuminated the palace,
earning it the name *Yèguāng* pearl."*

**Extended Meaning:**
Mencius said:
*"Treasure jade and pearls,
and calamity will befall."*

**Annotation from *Mèngzǐ* [尽心章句下]:
Mencius said:
"The treasures of a ruler are three:
land, people, and governance.
Treasure jade and pearls,
and calamity will befall."
*Yín Shì* commented:
*"Those who hold true treasure are secure;
those who lose true treasure are in peril."*

**Extended Meaning:**
Thus, when *Xī Lǚ* presented the *Yáng* (獒) hound,
*Chào Gōng* composed a warning to *Zhōu Wǔ Wáng*.

**Annotation from *Shàngshū* [旅獒篇]:
*"Xī Lǚ presented the *Yáng* hound."*
The western *Yáng* people offered a great dog—
four *chǐ* tall, called *Yáng*,
remarkable for its size.
*Tàibǎo* composed *"Lǚ Yáng."*
Chào Gōng, named *Shì*, served as *Tàibǎo*,
admonishing *Wǔ Wáng* with these words:
*"Ah!
A sage king governs with virtue,
and all distant peoples come as guests.
None are too far or near—
all bring their offerings.
If one does not create useless things,
great achievements are made.
If one does not value rare objects
but instead values useful ones,
the people will prosper.
Dogs and horses not native to the land
should not be kept.
Exotic birds and beasts
do not benefit the state—
they only bring harm.
If one does not treasure distant objects,
distant peoples will respect.
If one treasures only the virtuous,
nearby peoples will be at ease.
Thus, it is said:
Rare treasures obstruct conduct."*

**Extended Meaning:**
*Lín Yì* presented a five-colored parrot,
and *Wèi Zhèng* advised *Tàizōng* against it.

**Annotation:**
During *Zhēnguān*’s reign,
*Lín Yì* offered a white parrot,
renowned for its eloquence,
often speaking of cold and hunger.
The emperor, moved by pity,
ordered its envoy to return it to the wilds.
*Zǎixiàng Wèi Zhèng*, of *Jùlǔ*,
then submitted a memorial urging restraint.

**Extended Meaning:**
Once, a man of *Qí* desired gold.

**Annotation from *Lièzǐ* [说符第八篇]:
As recorded in its text,
already fully cited.»

Thus, the Sage acts for the belly, not for the eyes,
and so abandons that to take this.

**Extended Meaning:**
He who does not act for the eyes
abandons the Six Passions and his eyes do not gaze in vain.

**Annotation:**
*"The Six Passions"*—
honor, wealth, high rank, grandeur, reputation,
and profit—are what excites the will.

**Commentary:**
Honor, wealth, high rank, grandeur,
reputation, and profit—these six things
are the instruments that disrupt the mind and will.

**Extended Meaning:**
Speaking of the harm of the Five Dusts,
one should forget external things to preserve the True.

**Annotation from *The Dao of the Jade Emperor* [玉皇本行经]:**
The Six Dusts stain one-sidedly,
the Three Actions are bound by glory.
What are the Six Dusts?
The dusts of the six sense-bases—
eyes, ears, nose, tongue, body, and mind.
These ranks of offenders have long fallen into the sufferings of *kalpa* [劫难],
indulging their dust-clouded natures,
seeing nothing clearly,
and thus are stained by the Six Dusts.
End of *Daoist Virtue and Power: Extended Commentary on the True Classic of the Dao* [道德真经衍义手钞] Volume Three.
Begin of *Daoist Virtue and Power: Extended Commentary on the True Classic of the Dao* [道德真经衍义手钞] Volume Four.

*Wáng Shǒu Zhèng* of *Wǔfēng Qīng’ān Yìshì* [五峰清安逸士]:
Chapter Thirteen: *"Favor and Humiliation"* [宠辱章]

**Favor and Humiliation**

What is meant by favor and humiliation?
Favor is lowly—
to receive it is as if in shock,
to lose it is as if in shock:
this is called the shock of favor and humiliation.

*The Extended Commentary* states:
The child of *Lǐzi* [列子] bore a hungry look.
Its meaning is already detailed in the *Extended Commentary*.

**Zǐ Wén** [子文], the *Língyīn* [令尹] of Chu,
held office three times as *Língyīn*,
yet showed no joyous expression.
When dismissed three times,
he bore no angry look.

**Annotation**:
*Língyīn* [令尹]—a title of Chu’s highest minister.
Zǐ Wén [子文] (surname: Dòu [斗], given name: Gǔyúzhū [谷於菟]),
as a man, neither his joy nor anger was evident;
he was undivided in his relation to things.
Yet his three promotions and three dismissals
all came from *heavenly principle* [天理],
and he harbored no private human desires.

*The Extended Commentary* states:
Liù Xià Huì [柳下惠] served as a *shìshī* [士师],
was dismissed three times,
yet did not leave.
People said: "Master, can you not depart?"
He replied: "I follow the *Dao/Way* in serving others—
where would I go and not be dismissed?
If I bend the *Dao/Way* to serve others,
why must I abandon my parents’ land?"

**Annotation**:
*Shìshī* [士师]—a judicial officer.
Though dismissed three times,
he did not leave,
and his demeanor was dignified and composed—
thus, he may be called wise.
Yet his resolve to never compromise the *Dao/Way*
was firm and unyielding.
This is what is meant by
*"he must follow his own Dao/Way and never lose himself."*

*The Extended Commentary* states:
**Zhèng Kǎo Fǔ** [正考父], the great-grandson of *Mǐnggōng* [愍公] of Sòng,
was promoted three times—
once, he walked hunched,
twice, he walked stooped,
thrice, he walked bowed,
following walls as he went—
who would dare to act disorderly?

**Annotation**:
*Zhèng* [考]—accomplished.
*Kǎo* [考]—great.
With accomplished great virtue and upright *Dao/Way*,
he was named **Zhèng Kǎo Fǔ** [正考父],
thus a noble ancestor of Confucius,
a Sòng minister.
At the first promotion, he walked hunched;
at the second, he walked stooped;
at the third, he walked bowed,
following walls in utmost reverence and humility—
who would dare to commit wrongdoing and defy him?

What is meant by *"treating great calamities as one’s own body"*?
My great calamity arises
from my having a body—
and when I have no body,
what calamity do I face?

*The Extended Commentary* states:
To destroy one’s *support* [隳支体],
to dismiss one’s *cleverness* [黜聪明],
to sever one’s form and abandon one’s *intelligence* [离形去智],
to merge with the *Great Harmony* [大同],
then there is nothing that enters
without one finding it in accord with oneself.

**Annotation**:
*Dà Tóng* [大同]—the *Great Harmony*.
**Yán Huì** [颜回] replied to *Zhōngnǐ* [仲尼]’s words.
On another day, he said:
"Huì has made progress."
Zhōngnǐ asked: "How so?"
Huì replied: "I have attained *zuòwàng* [坐忘]."

**Annotation**:
*Zuòwàng* [坐忘]—*forgetting through sitting*.
With a mind emptied of attachment,
one can sit upright and forget.
Zhōngnǐ frowned—*in surprise*—
and asked: "What is *zuòwàng*?"
Yán Huì [颜回] said: "To destroy one’s *support* [隳支体]."

**Annotation**:
*Hidden* [隳]—to ruin or abandon.
*Dismissed* [黜]—to withdraw or cast aside.
*Severed* [离]—to part from.

Though the ears hear and the eyes perceive,
yet the *virtue/power* of clarity [德明] originates in the heart.
Once the spirit awakens to the One,
the body is no longer bound by myriad phenomena—all is emptiness.
Thus, one can abandon the four senses and a hundred limbs,
dismissing the cleverness of the mind and the intelligence of the heart.

**Great Harmony** [大通]—this is the **Great Dao/Way** [大道].
The Dao can give birth to ten thousand things,
hence it is called the **Great Harmony**.

**Extended Meaning**:
As when Confucius said, *"No-self"* [毋我].
From *The Analects*, Book 5, *Zǐ Hān* [子罕], Chapter 9:
The Master rejected four things:
No scheming [毋意],
No certainty [毋必],
No rigidity [毋固],
No-self [毋我].

**Huì Àn’s Commentary**:
To utterly sever all attachments.
*"Po"* [毋] in *Records of the Grand Historian* [《史记》] is written as *"wú"* [无], meaning *"not"* or *"without"*.
*Yì* [意]—private inclination.
*Nèi* [必]—fixed expectation.
*Gù* [固]—obstinate adherence.
*Wó* [我]—selfish ego.
These four are cyclically linked:
Inclination arises first,
certainty follows,
rigidity enforces it,
and ego solidifies it.
For inclination often precedes action,
while ego solidifies after—
when ego begets new inclination,
worldly desires drag one in an endless cycle.

**Extended Meaning**:
As when **Nánguō Zǐ Qí** [南郭子綦] lost his self.
From *Zhuangzi* [《庄子》], *"Qí Wù Lùn"* [齐物论]:
Nánguō Zǐ Qí reclined against his staff and sat,
gazing upward to the sky and sighing—
his expression seemed to have lost his mate.
Since he harmonized with Heaven and humanity,
discarding distinctions of self and other,
he stood alone in joyless dissolution,
as though bereft of his pair.

Yán Chéng Zǐ Yóu stood before him and asked:
"Why this state?
Can one truly make the body like withered wood,
and the heart like dead ashes?
The reclining figure before me is not the same as before!"

Zǐ Qí replied:
"Yǎn, is this not excellent?
Yet you ask of it.
Now, I have lost my self—do you understand?"
This is also akin to saying *"I have no body"* [吾无身].
When one attains such a state,
former honors and humiliations fade away—
what need remains for them?

Thus, to cherish the body as the world’s treasure,
as though it could be entrusted with the world,
to love the body as the world’s foundation,
as though it could bear the world’s weight.

**Extended Meaning**:
As when **Shùn’s** [舜] offering the throne to **Zǐzhōu Zhīfù** [子州支父].
From *Zhuangzi* [《庄子》], *"Ràng Wáng Piān"* [让王篇]:
Shùn offered the throne to Zǐzhōu Zhīfù—also called Zhīfù.
He said: *"I suffer from a deep affliction,
and must first attend to it—
I have no leisure to govern the world."*

**Commentary**:
Surname Zǐ, given name Zhōu, styled Zhīfù—
a recluse who harbored the Dao within.
Shùn, recognizing his virtue, offered him the throne.
Zhīfù explained:
*"Yōu"* [幽]—profound and secluded.
*"Yōu"* [忧]—laboring in care.
He said his mind was trapped in deep seclusion,
his heart burdened with toil—
he must first cultivate himself,
so as to align with the Dao,
before he could govern the myriad affairs of state.

**Extended Meaning**:
As with **Yuè’s** [越] **Wángzǐ Sōu** [王子搜] and his followers.
From *Zhuangzi* [《庄子》], *"Ràng Wáng Piān"* [让王篇]:
The people of Yuè killed their rulers for three generations.
Wángzǐ Sōu, fearing this, fled to **Dān Xué** [丹穴]—
a cave in the southern mountains.
Yuè, having no king, searched for Wángzǐ Sōu but could not find him,
so they pursued him into the cave.
They burned him with mugwort and carried him in a jade carriage—
the royal litter.
Wángzǐ Sōu grasped the reins and ascended,
gazing upward to the sky, he cried:
*"O ruler, ruler!
Why must you abandon me?"*

Wángzǐ Sōu did not dislike being king—
he disliked the burdens of kingship.
One such as Wángzǐ Sōu
can be said to refuse letting the state harm his life.

---
**Vision Unseen** [视之不见] is called *Yí* [夷],
**Hearing Unheard** [听之不闻] is called *Xī* [希],
**Grasping Unfelt** [搏之不得] is called *Wēi* [微].

**Extended Meaning**:
**Play of the Mysterious and the Manifest** [能玄能黄].
From *The Classic of Changes* [《易》]:
*"Heaven above is round—its hue is mysterious [玄],
Earth below is square—its hue is yellow."*
From *Liezi* [《列子》], *"Tiān Ruì Piān"* [天瑞篇]:
To master *gōng* [宫] and *shāng* [商],
to embody *yīn* [阴] and *yáng* [阳],
to wield *róu* [柔] and *gāng* [刚],
to embody *yuán* [圆] and *fāng* [方],
to stretch and contract at will—
if one claims to know and to be able,
it is but a fragment of the Dao,
not its full realization.

Yet to have no knowledge yet know all,
to have no ability yet be capable of all—
then nothing is beyond reach.
No limit remains—
what cannot be done?

**Daoist Essentials**:

*Mysterious and Yellow* [玄黄], are **colors** [色].
*Gōng* [宫] and *Shāng* [商], are **sounds** [声].
*Yīn* [阴] and *Yáng* [阳], are **qi** [气].
*Róu* [柔] and *Gāng* [刚], are **materials/natures** [材].
*Yuán* [圆] and *Fāng* [方], are **vessels/forms** [器].
*Duǎn* [短] and *Cháng* [长], are **shapes** [形].
All transformations and changes,
are contained within these.

**Extended Meaning**:
*To dwell beneath as still as rubble and tiles* [在下与瓦砾同寂].
From *Zhuangzi* [《庄子》]—*"Journey to the North in Search of the Dao"* [智北游]:
**Eastern Guozi** [东郭子] asked **Zhuangzi** [庄子]:
*"Where does this Dao/Way you speak of reside?"* [所谓道，恶乎在].
**Explanation**: [疏] He dwelled in the Eastern suburb of Guo [郭], hence his name **Eastern Guozi** [居在郭东，故号东郭子].
**Guo Shunzi** [东郭顺子], was the **Master of the Unselective** [无择之师].

**Eastern Guozi** [东郭子] asked **Zhuangzi** [庄子]:
*"Where does this **Vacuous and Permeating Ultimate Dao/Way** [所谓虚通至道] reside?"*
**Zhuangzi** [庄子] replied:
*"It is nowhere not present."* [於何处在乎。无所不在。]
**Eastern Guozi** [东郭子] said:
*"Then I may seek it?"* [期而后可。]
**Zhuangzi** [庄子] replied:
*"It resides in the ant."* [指名所在。在蝼蚁。]
**Eastern Guozi** [东郭子] said:
*"How lowly!"* [曰：何其下耶。]
**Zhuangzi** [庄子] replied:
*"It resides in the weeds."* [在稊稗。]
**Eastern Guozi** [东郭子] said:
*"How even more lowly!"* [曰：何其愈下耶。]
**Zhuangzi** [庄子] replied:
*"It resides in the tiles and bricks."* [在瓦甓。]
**Eastern Guozi** [东郭子] said:
*"How much lower still!"* [曰：何其愈甚耶。]
**Zhuangzi** [庄子] replied:
*"It resides in excrement and urine."* [在屎溺。]
**Eastern Guozi** [东郭子] did not respond.
**Zhuangzi** [庄子] said:
*"Your inquiry, Master, does not reach the essential substance."* [夫子之问，固不及质。]

**The Ancient Virtuous Scholar** [古之善为士章第十五]:
The ancient virtuous scholar,
was subtle and mysterious, permeating the Dao/Way,
deep and unfathomable.
Because it was unfathomable,
one must forcefully create its semblance.

**Extended Meaning**:
**Master Liezi** [子列子] dwelled in the **Pu Garden of Zheng** [郑圃] for forty years,
yet none recognized him.
From *Liezi* [《列子》]—*"Heaven’s Auspicious Signs"* [天瑞第一]:
**Master Liezi** [子列子], is the one whose surname is **Zi** [载子於姓上者],
as noted in the first chapter:
*"This is what the disciple recorded, hence the title."* [是弟子之所记故也。]
The meaning has been detailed earlier,
and need not be repeated.

*"Like crossing a river in winter, one must fear the cold and the neighbors"* [豫若冬涉川，犹若畏四邻].
**Extended Meaning**:
**Guard against distractions and preserve sincerity** [闲邪存诚].
From *The Classic of Changes* [《周易》]—*"The Auspicious Signs of Qian"* [乾卦九二爻辞正义]:
*"One who guards against distractions and preserves sincerity"* [闲邪存其诚者],
must say:
*"Guard against distractions and preserve one’s sincerity"* [言防闲邪恶，当自存其诚实也].

**Extended Meaning**:
*"Like Eastern Guo Shunzi’s upright bearing in understanding things,
it dissipates the minds of others"* [若东郭顺子正容悟物，使人之意消].
From *Zhuangzi* [《庄子》]—*"Outer Chapters: Master Tianzi’s Garden"* [外篇田子方]:
*"Upright bearing in understanding things,
dissipates the minds of others"* [正容悟物，使人之意消].
**Master Xihua** [西华法师] **Cheng Xuan** [成玄英] **commented**:
*"When hearing things without Dao/Way,
or encountering those of perverse ways,
Eastern Guozi’s upright bearing
makes them enlightened,
dissipating their confused thoughts naturally."* [出闻无道之物，邪僻之人，东郭子正容仪，令其晓悟，使惑乱之意自然消除也。]
The full text has already been detailed in the eighth chapter *"The Supreme Good is Like Water"* [上善若水],
and need not be repeated.

**Extended Meaning**:
*"Even in a dark room, **Li Han Guang** [李含光] conducts himself as if facing his sovereign and father"* [李含光虽处暗室，如对君父].
**Master Sima** [司马子微] **praised him highly** [激赏之].
**Explanation**:
**Li Han Guang** [李含光], from *"High Daoist Transmission"* [《高道传》], ninth chapter:
*"The Daoist Li Han Guang of Jinling"* [道士李含光者，晋陵人也],
at age twelve, **devoted himself earnestly to learning the Dao/Way** [笃好学道],
even in a dark room,
his demeanor was as if facing his sovereign and father.
Others seeing him,
**his facial expressions and gestures were outwardly composed** [人见之情色外敛].

「He first mastered the art of seal script and clerical script [篆隶], some even saying he surpassed his father; yet, upon hearing the Dao/Way once, he never again took up the brush. Later, he studied under Master Zheng Yi [贞一先生].

**Master Sima** [司马子微] **said of seal script and precious books [篆宝书]**: "He emptied his purse and entrusted them to me, saying: 'This is the true **Li Han Guang**, a Daoist guest of pure **virtue/power** [真玉清仙客也].' Today, I take these two instances as proof—his conduct was steadfast and sincere, his reverence strict, truly like a guest of honor [可谓俨若客也].

**When in darkness**, the words of **Ling Sheng Zhen Jun** [翎圣真君] state:
*"Above, he is observed by the Heavenly Mirror [天鉴], below, he is scrutinized by the Earthly Spirits [地祇].*
*In the light, there are punishments that follow; in the dark, there are spirits and ghosts that attend.*
*In his actions and words, good and evil are known to him alone—*
*only what is upright can be preserved; the heart cannot be deceived.*
*Who can make turbidity cease?*
*Let stillness settle, and clarity will emerge.*
*Who can remain at peace for long?*
*Let motion arise, and disturbance will follow.*
**Extended Meaning**: *The **Yijing** [《易》] says:
*"Quietly unmoved, yet responsive—*
*thus the **Dao/Way** of all things under heaven unfolds.*
*None but the most divine of all under heaven*
*can attain this—*
*who else could be so aligned?"*
**Quoted from *Yi Xi Shang Ci*** [《易系上辞》]:
*"The **Yijing** is without thought, without action.*
*Quietly unmoved, yet responsive—*
*thus the **Dao/Way** of all things under heaven unfolds.*
*None but the most divine of all under heaven*
*can attain this—*
*who else could be so aligned?"*
**Extended Meaning**: *The Dao gathers because of its emptiness [虚].*
**Quoted from *Zhuangzi* [《庄子》]—[Chapter on **Geng Sang Chu** [庚桑楚]]:
*"The heart within is upright—*
*upright, it is still; still, it is clear; clear, it is empty; empty, it does nothing yet nothing is left undone.*
**Chapter 16: *Extreme Emptiness and Sincere Stillness*** [致虚极章第十六]:
*"Extreme emptiness, sincere stillness—*
*all things arise together,*
*and I observe their return.*
**Extended Meaning**:
*致 (zhi) means "to attain."*
*This aligns with *The Great Learning* [《大学》], where "attaining knowledge" [致知] is spoken of.*
**Quoted from *The Great Learning*** [《大学》]:
*"To sincerely settle one’s intentions [诚其意],*
*first, one must attain knowledge.*
*Attaining knowledge lies in investigating things [致知在格物].*
**Huai’an Commentary** [晦庵注]:
*"Sincere (cheng) means real [实].*
*Intentions (yi) are what the heart emits [心之所发].*
*To make real what the heart emits—*
*to align it with the good*
*and avoid self-deception.*
*致 (zhi) means to push to the utmost [推极].*
*Knowledge (zhi) is akin to discernment [识].*
*To push to the utmost one’s knowledge—*
*to exhaust all that can be known,*
*leaving nothing unattained.*
**Extended Meaning**:
*It also draws upon the meaning of "attaining" in the *Spring and Autumn Annals* [《春秋》], where the state of Chu sought to provoke the Jin army.*
**Quoted from *Spring and Autumn Annals* [《春秋》]—Year 12 of Duke Xuan [宣公十二年]**:
*"The state of Chu, under King Zhuang [庄], sought to establish terms with Jin [求成於晋].*
*Jin agreed, and a day for the covenant was set.*
*Chu’s **Gongshi** [许伯] and **Yuele Bo** [乐伯] took their places,*
*with **She Zhu** [摄叔] as the right-hand assistant,*
*and sought to challenge Jin’s commander in single combat,*
*pretending reluctance to make peace,*
*thus provoking Jin’s forces.*
*By this means of provocation,*
*the army was made into an enemy—*
*just as stillness provokes the Dao,*
*and the Dao can thus be attained."*

**Extended Meaning**: *The **Master Liezi** [列子] said:
*"Nothing surpasses stillness, nothing surpasses emptiness.*
*Stillness, emptiness—*
*these are the dwelling places one attains.*
*Taking and giving—*
*these are where one loses one’s footing.*
*If emptiness is not pushed to its utmost,*
*and stillness does not reach its sincerity,*
*then none can attain the Dao.*
*Thus, the utmost value is placed on emptiness and stillness."*

**Quoted from *Liezi* [《列子》]—[Chapter on *Heavenly Blessings* [天瑞篇]]**:
*"Someone asked **Master Liezi** [子列子]:
*'Why do you esteem emptiness?*
**Master Liezi** replied:
*'Emptiness is without esteem.*
*It is not its name that is esteemed,*
*but stillness and emptiness—*
*these are the dwelling places one attains.*
*Taking and giving—*
*these are where one loses one’s footing.*
*If emptiness is not pushed to its utmost,*
*and stillness does not reach its sincerity,*
*then none can attain the Dao.*
*Thus, the utmost value is placed on emptiness and stillness."*

**Extended Meaning**: *"All things are manifold yet my heart remains clear;*
*all transformations differ yet my heart remains still.*
*This is called **Heavenly Music** [天乐]."*

**Quoted from *Zhuangzi* [《庄子》]—[Chapter on *The Way of Heaven* [天道篇]]**:
*"To extend **stillness and emptiness** to heaven and earth,*
*and communicate with all things—*
*this is called **Heavenly Music** [天乐]."*

**Master Xi Hua** [西华法师] **commented**:
*"Thus, with a single mind settled,*
*all things submit—*
*it is only that through the wisdom of **stillness and emptiness***
*one investigates the principles of the Two **Dao/Way**s [二仪之理],*
*flows with the sentiments of all things,*
*and adapts to their transformations without fail,*
*hence it is called **Heavenly Music**."*

**Extended Meaning**: *"Let us return to the heart of heaven and earth [复其见天地之心乎]."*

**Quoted from the *Yijing* [《易》]—**Fu** [复] Hexagram’s **Simian** [彖辞]**:
*"**Fu** means to return to the origin [反本之谓也].*
*It speaks of the **Dao/Way** of **Yin-Yang** [乾元一阳之炁]*
*—from here it departs,*
*and from here it returns,*
*thus it is called **Fu** [来复].*
*It speaks of returning to the **Dao/Way**—*
*this is the movement of heaven.*
*There is advantage in forward action,*
*for the **virtue/power** of the strong grows.*
*Returning to the heart of heaven and earth—*
*this is the **Dao/Way** of **Yin-Yang** [乾元一阳之炁],*
*the still and utterly void principle.*
*The reason the initial line of the **Fu** [复] hexagram says*
*'**Hidden Dragon, No Action** [潜龙勿用]'*
*—is that the **Yang qi** [阳气] is concealed and below,*
*and this is how one sees the heart of heaven and earth.*
*Things are diverse and manifold [物芸芸],*
*yet each returns to its root.*
*Returning to the root is **stillness** [静],*
*stillness is **recovering the vital breath** [复命],*
*and recovering the vital breath is **constancy** [常].*
**Extended Meaning**: *The **Yijing** says:
*"Exhaust the principles, fathom the nature,*
*and reach the vital breath [穷理尽性以至於命].*
*The vital breath is the marvel of nature [命者，性之妙也].*
**Quoted from *Yi Xi Gua Zhengyi* [《易系卦正义》]**:
*"The vital breath is the utmost limit of life [生之极].*
*Exhausting the principles means reaching their utmost [穷理，则尽其极也].*
*Knowing constancy is clarity [知常曰明],*
*not knowing constancy leads to reckless action and disaster [不知常，妄作凶]."*

**Extended Meaning**: *Confucius said:
*"A person without constancy*
*cannot be a shaman or physician."*
**Quoted from *The Analects* [《论语》]—Book 13**:
*"The Master said:*
*'There is a saying in the south:*
*A person without constancy*
*cannot be a shaman or physician.*
*Good words these!'*
**Huai’an Commentary** [晦庵注]:
*'The southerners (nanren) are people from the southern lands.*
*Constancy (heng) means enduring steadfastness.*
*Shamans deal with spirits and ghosts,*
*physicians with life and death,*
*so even in these lowly roles,*
*constancy is especially indispensable.*
*Confucius praised their words as good."*

**Extended Meaning**: *Mencius said:
*"If a person lacks constancy,*
*they will abandon propriety and indulge in licentiousness and extravagance,*
*doing nothing they will not do for themselves.*
*Only when they fall into crime*
*does punishment follow—*
*this is to mislead the people.*
*How can a benevolent person in power*
*mislead the people and still be acceptable?"*
**Quoted from *Mencius* [《孟子》]—[Chapter on *Teng Wen Gong* [滕文公章句上]]**:
*"When people speak of the **Dao/Way** [道],*
*those who have a constant livelihood [恒产] have constant hearts [恒心],*
*and those without a constant livelihood lack constancy.*
*If a person lacks constancy,*
*they will abandon propriety and indulge in licentiousness and extravagance,*
*doing nothing they will not do for themselves.*
*Only when they fall into crime*
*does punishment follow—*
*this is to mislead the people [罔民].*
*How can a benevolent person in power*
*mislead the people and still be acceptable?"*

「**Huì'ān’s Annotation [晦庵注]**: Yáng Hǔ [阳虎], whose given name was Hǔ [虎], was a retainer of the Jì family in Lǔ [鲁季氏家臣也].
The **Dao/Way** [天理] and human desires [人欲] cannot coexist.
Hǔ’s words here fear that **virtue/power** [仁] may harm wealth [富].
Mencius cites them, fearing that wealth may harm **virtue/power** [仁].
The **noble** [君子] and the **petty** [小人] are always in opposition [每相反而已矣].
**Knowing constancy [恒] permits tolerance [容]**, tolerance becomes **impartial** [公], **impartial** becomes **the king’s** [王], **the king’s** becomes **the heavens’** [天], **the heavens’** becomes **the Dao/Way** [道], **the Dao/Way** is eternal [乃久], and one’s life will not be in peril [没身不殆].

**Extended Meaning [衍义云]**: The *Rites of Zhou* [《礼记》] states:
*"When the **Great Dao/Way** prevails,*
*the world becomes **impartial** [为公],*
*and people do not exclusively love their own parents,*
*nor exclusively cherish their own children."*
**Quoted from *Li Yùn* [《礼记□礼运篇》]**:
*"Confucius said:*
*The **Great Dao/Way** in practice,*
*the world becomes **impartial** [为公],*
*the worthy and capable are selected,*
*faithfulness is cultivated,*
*and harmony is nurtured.*
*Thus, people do not exclusively love their own parents,*
*nor exclusively cherish their own children."*

The **Dao/Way** of filial piety and compassion [孝慈] is vast [广也].
**Ensuring the aged have a place to die [终],**
**the strong have useful labor [用],**
**the young have proper growth [长],**
**and the widowed, orphaned, disabled, and infirm [矜寡孤独废疾者]**
**all receive sustenance [皆有所养].**

**End of Daoist Virtue/Power True Classic Extended Meaning Handwritten Copy, Volume Four.**
**Beginning of Daoist Virtue/Power True Classic Extended Meaning Handwritten Copy, Volume Five.**

**Five-Peak Pure and Leisurely Scholar Wang Shouzheng’s Collection**
**Chapter Seventeen: The Most Exalted Dao/Way**

The Most Exalted Dao/Way exists naturally among the people.
The next level is to honor them and praise them.

**Extended Meaning Annotation:**
*The Zuo Commentary* states:
*"The Most Exalted Dao/Way establishes virtue; the next level establishes deeds."*

Quoting from *The Spring and Autumn Annals, Duke Xiang’s Twenty-Fourth Year*:
Mu Shu went to Jin, and Fan Xuanzi received him, asking, *"The ancients said, ‘To die and yet not perish—what does this mean?’"* Mu Shu did not respond.
Fan Xuanzi said:
*"The ancestors of my house, from the time of Yu onward, were the Yao-Tang Clan. In the Xia, they were the Yu-Long Clan—this refers to Liu Lei. See the account in Duke Zhao’s Twenty-Ninth Year. In the Shang, they were the Shi-Wei Clan—Shi-Wei was the name of a state. Southeast of Bai-Ma County in Dong Commandery lies Wei City. In the Zhou, they were the Tang-Du Clan—Tang and Du were two states. At the end of the Yin, the Shi-Wei State was moved to Tang, and Duke Cheng of Zhou destroyed Tang, relocating them to Du, where they became Duke Bu of Du. Duke Bu’s son, Yi Shu, fled to Jin, and four generations later, Shi Hui received a fief in Fan, restoring the Fan Clan. Du is now Du County in Jingzhao Commandery. The Jin ruler made Fan the host of the Summer Alliance—this is what is meant, is it not?"*

Mu Shu replied:
*"From what I’ve heard, this is called ‘hereditary rank,’ not ‘not perishing.’ Ru had a former minister named Zang Wen Zhong. After his death, his words endured—this is what is meant, is it not?"*

I have heard that the Most Exalted Dao/Way establishes virtue; the next level establishes deeds; the next establishes words. Historians like Shi Yi, Zhou Ren, and Zang Wen Zhong—though time passes, they are not forgotten. This is what is meant by ‘not perishing.’ As for preserving one’s lineage and surname, guarding ancestral altars, and ensuring one’s descendants never perish—no state lacks this. The greatest of ranks cannot be called ‘not perishing.’"

**Extended Meaning Annotation:**
*"Above, like a tree branch; below, like wild deer."*

Quoting from *The Zhuangzi, Heaven and Earth Chapter*:
*"In the age of supreme virtue, no one is praised for talent, no one is employed for skill. Above, like a tree branch; below, like wild deer."*

**Explanation:**
*"Above, since there is no governance, below, all are pure and natural—like wild deer, free from royal rites."*

**Extended Meaning Annotation:**
*Zhuangzi said:*
*"When it comes to the Sage, to force oneself for benevolence, to strain for righteousness—then the world begins to doubt."*

Quoting from *The Zhuangzi, Horsefoot Chapter*:
*"When it comes to the Sage, to force oneself for benevolence, to strain for righteousness—these are the appearances of exerting effort for benevolence and righteousness. To force oneself for benevolence, to strain for righteousness—both are the outward forms of contriving benevolence and righteousness."*

**Extended Meaning Annotation:**
*"Yao’s benevolence was like the heavens; those who approached him were like the sun. Shun had a fragrant virtue, and the people rejoiced in him. Thus, he moved his capital three times to Chengdu, and when he reached Deng’s ruins, ten thousand families followed him."*

Quoting from *The Zhuangzi, Xu Wu Gui Chapter*:
*"Shun, in humble attire, wandered through Kang Qu. He heard children singing:*
*'Establish the people, do not deviate from your utmost!*
*Unknowing and unthinking,*
*Follow the Way of the Sovereign.’*
*‘Cheng’ means ‘the many.’*
*He who makes all things reach their utmost*
*does not violate their natural dispositions.*
*If one governs by knowledge and understanding,*
*how could this be in accord with the Dao of Heaven?’"*

**Extended Meaning Annotation:**
*"The people’s songs praised Shun."*

Quoting from *The Mencius, Chapter on Wan Zhang (Upper)*:
Mencius answered Wan Zhang:
*"Shun served Yao for twenty-eight years—this was not something a man could do alone; it was Heaven’s will. When Yao died, after three years of mourning, Shun moved Yao’s son to the south of the Nan River. The feudal lords who came to pay homage did not go to Yao’s son but to Shun. Those who had disputes did not bring them to Yao’s son but to Shun. Those who sang praises did not sing of Yao’s son but of Shun. Hence, it was Heaven’s will."*

*The Nan River is in the southern part of Ji Province, also in Yu Province. ‘Disputes’ refers to unresolved legal cases.*

The next level is to be feared and scorned.
When trust is insufficient, distrust arises.

**Extended Meaning Annotation:**
*"When the Shang made oaths, the people began to rebel. When the Zhou held alliances, the people began to doubt."*

Quoting from *The Spring and Autumn Annals, Duke Zhao’s Fourth Year*:
*"The Duke of Chu, Ling Wang, assembled the feudal lords at Shen. Jie Ju spoke to the Duke of Chu:*
*'I have heard that when feudal lords do not return in proper attire, they are not to be followed. This is the ritual norm for returning.*
*Jie Ju said:*
*'The deeds of the Six Kings and Two Dukes—*
*—the Six Kings’ deeds: Qi Yu’s fishing pavilion feast, Tang’s command at Jing Bo, Wu Wang’s assembly at Meng Jin, Cheng Wang’s search at Qi Yang, Kang Wang’s court at Tang Guan, and Mu Wang’s gathering at Tu Shan.*
*—the Two Dukes’ deeds: Qi Huan’s army at Ling and Jin Wen’s alliance at Qian Tu.*
*All these were meant to demonstrate ritual to the feudal lords, the means by which they were commanded.*
*But Xia Jie held the Ren Alliance—Ren was a state—and the ruler of Man rebelled. Shang Zhou held the search at Li—Zhou hunted in the eastern Yi state of Li—and the Yi people rebelled. Zhou You held the alliance at Da Shi—the central peak—and the barbarians rebelled against Zhou. Because of this, they rebelled against Zhou. All these were meant to show extravagance and excess to the feudal lords. The feudal lords thus abandoned their commands.*
*Now, Your Majesty, with your excess, will this not fail?’*
*The King did not listen.*

*The Shang Classics contain the text of Tang’s oath. The Zhou had the assembly at Mu Ye and Meng Jin."*

The *Yan Yi* states: **"Sincerity (chéng) is what is called *having naivety (fú) in the heart.**"*
From the *Book of Changes*, the hexagram *Zhong Fú* (Inner Truth), line five:
*"With sincerity, it is as though there were no response (wú dá)."*
*"As though there were no response"*—this is the language binding one’s trust.
When one is in the central position, relying on sincerity to interact with others, and occupies a high station as the master of oaths, how could one abandon trust? Thus, *having naivety (fú)*—because one *has naivety*, one avoids blame.

The *Yan Yi* states: **"Trust is what can be repeated in speech."**
From the *Analects* (Book of Learning, First):
Master You said:
*"Trust is close to righteousness—speech can be repeated.*
*Reverence is close to ritual—it keeps one distant from shame and disgrace.*
*Thus, one does not lose one’s kin, and they can be revered as well."*

The *Yan Yi* states: **"Qi Huan did not betray Cao Gui—also called Cao Mò—at the Alliance."**
From the *Records of the Grand Historian*:
Cao Mò was a man of Lu.
He served Duke Zhuang of Lu through his strength and courage.
Duke Zhuang favored martial prowess, and Cao Mò became Lu’s general.
He fought against Qi three times and was defeated each time.
Duke Zhuang, fearful, offered the territory of Suì to make peace.
Yet he still kept Cao Mò as general.
Qi Huan Gong agreed to meet with Duke Zhuang at Kē and take the oath.
After the two dukes had sworn on the altar,
Cao Mò seized a dagger and held Qi Huan Gong hostage.
None of Qi Huan Gong’s attendants dared move.
He asked: *"What do you intend?"*
Cao Mò replied:
*"Qi is strong, Lu is weak, and the great state has already aggressed against Lu severely. Now Lu’s walls are ruined, pressing against Qi’s borders—you must consider this!"*
Qi Huan Gong then agreed to return all the lands he had seized from Lu.
After speaking, Cao Mò cast his dagger aside, descended from the altar, and knelt facing north among the assembled ministers, his expression unchanged, his words as before.
Qi Huan Gong, enraged, wished to break his oath.
Guan Zhong said: *"That cannot be done."*

The **Way** is abandoned when greed for petty gains leads one to abandon trust among the **lords of the states** and lose the support of the world—better to yield than to persist.

Thus, Qi Huan Gong finally ceded the lands he had seized from Lu.
All the territories lost by Cao Mò in three battles were restored to Lu.

*Extended Meaning*: The people of Jin did not abandon the promise to attack Yuan.
*Recorded*: The Jin army attacked Yuan.
Duke Wen of Wei led his soldiers to attack Yuan.
The Jin lord besieged Yuan, ordering three days’ worth of provisions. When Yuan refused to surrender, he ordered his troops to withdraw.
A spy reported: *"Yuan is about to surrender."*
The military officers said: *"Let us await it."*
The duke replied: *"Trust is the treasure of a state, the shelter of its people. To gain Yuan but lose trust—how could we shelter them?*
The more we lose, the greater the harm. Withdraw one step, and Yuan will surrender itself."
From then on, the **lords of the states** submitted in allegiance, just as they had to the promise to attack Yuan.

*Extended Meaning*: Wu Qi did not renege on the reward for moving the chariot axle.
*Recorded* from *The Historical Records*: Wu Qi, a general of Wei, sought to attack Qin but feared the soldiers and troops would not trust him. He buried a chariot axle at the eastern market gate.
He posted: *"If anyone can move this axle to the western gate, I will grant them land, fields, and a residence of one hundred *mu* and one hundred *jin* of gold."*
After three days, no one dared to move it.
He revised the posting: *"If anyone can move it, I will grant them land, fields, and a residence of five hundred *mu* and one hundred *jin* of gold."*
At that time, a man came forward and moved it, so he was rewarded accordingly.
Thus, he summoned recruits to attack Qin and succeeded.
This was a case where he did not renege on the reward for moving the axle.

*Extended Meaning*: The Marquis of Wei did not violate the hunter’s appointment.
*Recorded* from *The Historical Records*: The Marquis of Wei was Wei Wen Hou.
The hunter was an official overseeing mountains and marshes.
Wen Hou had made an appointment with the hunter for a hunt, but the next day, as he was about to depart, he happened to be enjoying wine and music with his ministers, when a heavy rain fell. He ordered his carriage to proceed to the wilds.
His attendants said: *"Today we are drinking and enjoying ourselves, and now it rains—where shall we find shelter?"*
Wen Hou said: *"I do not rest upon beasts and birds. I have made an appointment with the hunter—how could I fail to meet him? I fear losing trust, so I will go despite the rain."*

**The Great Dao Abandoned, Virtue/Power Emerges** (Chapter XVIII)
When the **Great Dao** is abandoned, there arise **virtue/power** and righteousness.

*Extended Meaning*: Zhuangzi said: *"Fish forget each other in the water; men forget each other in the arts of the **Way**."*
*Recorded* from *The Zhuangzi* (□ *Great Master [Da Zong Shi]*) states: *"When the spring dries up, fish share the land together. They breathe upon one another to keep moist, and splash water upon one another to keep wet—this is not as good as forgetting each other in the rivers and lakes."*

When wisdom arises, great deceit follows.
*Extended Meaning*: Zhuangzi said: *"Discard petty wisdom, and great wisdom will shine forth."*
*Recorded* from *The Zhuangzi* (□ *External Things [Wai Wu]*) states: *"Discard petty wisdom, and great wisdom will shine forth."*
Pettiness is self-serving; great wisdom follows the natural course.

*Extended Meaning*: How difficult it is to indulge in petty cleverness!
*Recorded* from *The Analects* (□ *The Duke of Wei [Wei Ling Gong]*) (Chapter XV): The Master said: *"To dwell together all day without discussing **virtue/power**, and to delight in petty cleverness—how difficult it is!"*
Pettiness is the indulgence of private desires.
When words do not touch upon **virtue/power**, the heart grows reckless and extravagant.
When one delights in petty cleverness, the inclination toward reckless risk and fortune-seeking matures.
"*How difficult it is!"* means that such a person cannot attain **virtue/power**, and disaster will surely follow.

*Extended Meaning*: Zhuangzi said: *"The true nature of the horse lies in its grazing on grass, drinking water, and lifting its hoof to stand."*
The text from *The Zhuangzi* (□ *The Horse’s Hoof [Ma Ti]*) has already been detailed in the **Meaning**, so it is not repeated here.

「Extended Meaning」:

In the age of **Dao**’s pristine origin, the people did not know what to do, nor where to go. They carried their food in their mouths while strolling in contentment, drumming their bellies as they wandered freely.

*Quoted*: In the time of **Hēxiū** (the Radiant Virtue), the people dwelled without knowing what to do, walked without knowing where to go, carried their food in their mouths while strolling in contentment, drumming their bellies as they wandered freely. The people could attain this state.

*Commentary*: *Zhī* (adapt). **Hēxiū**, an ancient sovereign. Also, one whose radiant **virtue/power** caused the people to rally to him—thus called **Hēxiū**. This refers to the **Flame Emperor** (Yán Dì).

It is said: To carry food in the mouth while frolicking, indistinguishable from an infant; to drum the belly while roaming, no different from a child.

**Extended Meaning**:
To bend and twist **ritual and music** to rectify the forms of the world,
To stretch and strain **benevolence and righteousness** to soothe the hearts of the world.

*Quoted from* *The Zhuangzi* (□ *The Horse’s Hoof [Mǎ Tí]*).

*Commentary*: To fold, bend, and rotate in performing **ritual and music** to correct the body’s posture; to yearn and aspire to soothe the soul. Thus, they stand on tiptoe, proud of their knowledge, and engage in deceitful scheming, all vying for profit and gain—yet they know not when to stop. Alas, the excesses of the sages!

Among kin, discord arises, yet there is filial piety and kindness.
In a chaotic state, loyal ministers emerge.

**Extended Meaning**:
*The Classic of Rites* states: *"When the **Dao** prevails, one does not love only one’s own parents, nor cherish only one’s own children."*

*Quoted from* *The Classic of Rites* (□ *The Great Plan of Sacrifice [Lǐ Yùn]*):

*"When the **Dao** prevails, the world is held in common. The worthy and capable are selected, trust is cultivated, and harmony is fostered. Thus, one does not love only one’s own parents, nor cherish only one’s own children. The aged have a place to end their days, the strong have useful labor, the young have nurturing, and the widowed, orphaned, disabled, and infirm all receive sustenance."*

Again, citing filial piety and kindness.

*The Analects*: Duke Kāng of Zhōu asked: *"How can one govern the people so that they are respectful, loyal, and encouraged?"* The Master replied: *"Rule them with solemnity, and they will be respectful. Be filial and kind, and they will be loyal."* Filial to one’s parents, kind to the multitude.

*The Erya* states: *"To serve one’s parents well is called filial piety."*

**Extended Meaning**:
Ministers who gouge out eyes and split open hearts.

*Quoted from* *Records of the Grand Historian* (□ *The Annals of the Shang Dynasty [Yīn Běn Jì]*):

*"Zhòu Xīn (King Zhòu) was unrighteous, and the world called him **Jiè** (the Tyrant)."* **Jiè** refers to one who violates laws, destroys righteousness, and harms the good.

King **Jiè**’s debauchery knew no end. **Mǐzǐ** repeatedly admonished him but was ignored, so he fled with the Grand Music Master and the Minor Music Master. **Bǐ Gān**, the king’s son, said: *"As a subject, one cannot fail to contend to the death."* He then forcibly admonished **Jiè**.

**Jiè** raged: *"I have heard that sages have seven orifices in their hearts. Let us dissect **Bǐ Gān** to see!"*

*The Zhuangzi* (□ *The Thief and the Ruined Wall [Tóu Huàn]*) states:
*"**Bǐ Gān**’s heart was split open, **Wú Zǔ**’s eyes were gouged out—these were the calamities of loyalty."*

These are ministers who gouge eyes and split hearts, whose loyalty and righteous death only manifest when the ruler is corrupt. Hence, *"In a chaotic state, loyal ministers emerge."*

**Extended Meaning**:
**Gǔsōu** was obstinate, yet **Shùn** was called the Great Filial Son.

*Quoted from* *The Book of Documents* (□ *The Record of Yáo [Yáo Diǎn]*):

*"The blind father, the obstinate mother, the arrogant brother—yet he harmonized them all. With filial piety unceasing, he did not tolerate evil. This was the Great Filial Son."*

*The Mencius* (□ *The First Chapter of Wàn Zhāng [Wàn Zhāng Zhāng Jù Shàng]*):

*"The truly filial serve their parents with reverence all their lives. At fifty, I saw such filial piety in the Great **Shùn**."*

**Extended Meaning**:
**Zēng Zhì** was stern, yet **Zēng Shēn** was praised for nurturing.

*Quoted from* *The Mencius* (□ *The First Chapter of Lí Lóu [Lí Lóu Zhāng Jù Shàng]*):

*"When **Zēng Shēn** cared for **Zēng Zhì**, he always ensured there was wine and meat. When serving, he would ask who to give the leftovers to. If there were any remaining, he would say, ‘There is.’ Thus, **Zēng Shēn** could be said to nurture his **virtue/power**."*

To serve one’s parents as **Zēng Shēn** did—this is acceptable.

**Chapter XIX: Discarding the Sage, Abandoning Wisdom**

**Extended Meaning**:
Zhuangzi said: *"To strike down sagehood and wisdom, to release and unite thieves and robbers—then the world begins to flourish."*

*Quoted from* *The Zhuangzi* (□ *The Winnowing Basket [Zhuàn Kuāng]*):

*"To strike down sagehood and wisdom."*

*Commentary*: *"Strike down"* means to criticize and reject. Now, to speak of striking down is also to denounce **ren and yì**, to abandon the sage and discard wisdom. To not value rare and difficult goods, thus releasing and pardoning thieves and robbers, without resorting to harsh punishments—then the world attains great peace.

**Extended Meaning**:
To abandon petty wisdom, then great wisdom becomes clear.

**Chapter XIX: Discarding the Sage, Abandoning Wisdom**

*Extended Meaning*:
Zhuangzi said: *"To strike down sagehood and wisdom, to release and unite thieves and robbers—then the world begins to flourish."*

*Quoted from* *The Zhuangzi* (□ *The Winnowing Basket [Zhuàn Kuāng]*):

*"To strike down sagehood and wisdom."*

*Commentary*: *"Strike down"* means to criticize and reject. Now, to speak of striking down is also to denounce **ren** and **yì**, to abandon the sage and discard wisdom. To not value rare and difficult goods, thus releasing and pardoning thieves and robbers, without resorting to harsh punishments—then the world attains great peace.

*Extended Meaning*:
To abandon petty wisdom, then great wisdom becomes clear.

**Quoted from** *The Zhuangzi* (□ *The Outer Things [Wài Wù]*):

*"Small wisdom is self-serving; great wisdom follows things."*

*Extended Meaning*:
Zhi’s disciples asked him: *"Does theft too have the Dao/Way?"*
He said: *"Where could there be a place without Dao/Way?"*

*Quoted from* *The Zhuangzi* (□ *The Winnowing Basket [Zhuàn Kuāng]*)—*its meaning already fully covered above.*

*Extended Meaning*:
*The Zhuangzi* recounts: *"The Lord of Song dreamed at midnight of a man with unbound hair peering at his private chamber door and said..."*
*Quoted from* *The Zhuangzi* (□ *The Outer Things [Wài Wù]*):

*"The Lord of Song dreamed at midnight of a man with unbound hair peering at his private chamber door."*

*Commentary by* **Chéng Fǎshī** (成法師): *"The ruler of the state of Song, whose posthumous title was 'Lord of Song,' was surnamed Jié (镒) and named Yuán (元), i.e., the Lord of Song, Yuán."* *"Á"* (阿) means "crooked," referring to the crooked room of the private chamber—its meaning already explained in the commentary on the *Wài Wù* section.* Absolute **ren** and discard **yì**, and the people return to filial piety and natural kindness.

*Extended Meaning*:
Mencius said: *"There has never been one who practiced **ren** and neglected his parents."*
*"There has never been one who practiced **yì** and put his lord after his own interests."*

*Quoted from* *Mencius* (□ *The Sententious Chapter of the Duke of Liang [Liáng Huì Wáng Zhāngjù Shàng]*). Mencius’ reply to the king.

---

**Annotations by [Author]**:
*"遗"* (yí) means "abandon," akin to *"橐"* (tuó, "to neglect").
*"后"* (hòu) means "to delay," or "not to prioritize."

The **Dao/Way** states that one who practices **ren** must love their parents, and one who upholds **yì** must serve their lord with urgency. When **ren** and **yì** are discarded, and clever artifices and material gains abandoned, thieves and bandits vanish.

**Extended Meaning**:
*"巧"* (qiǎo) means "artifice," referring to intricate carvings, mechanical devices, and locks like *"扃鐍"* (jiōng shū, "double bolt and lock").
Quoted from *Zhuangzi* (□ *The Chapter on Opening Coffers and Chests [Kōu Qìè]*):

*"Yet when great thieves arrive, they carry off the chest and the coffer, sling the bags over their shoulders and run away, fearing only that the cords and bolts may not be secure enough. They carve and shape every form yet do not consider it clever—like the craftsmen of the State of Wei who carve date monkeys, or those of the State of Song who polish mulberry leaves."*

*"琢"* (zhuó) means "to polish," as in refining jade or stone. *"既琢之而复磨之"* (jì zhuó zhī ér fù mó zhī) means "after polishing, they grind it again."
From *The Chapter on Heaven and Earth* (□ *Tiāndì Piān*):

*"The gardener angrily flushed and laughed, saying: 'I have heard from my teacher that one who uses artifices will always have artificial matters to attend to. One who attends to artificial matters will always have artificial thoughts. When artificial thoughts dwell in the heart, purity and wholeness are lost.'"*

**Extended Meaning**:
*"珠玉斗斛权衡符玺"* (zhū yù dǒu hú quán héng fú xǐ) refers to pearls, jade, measures of grain and liquid, scales, and royal seals.
Quoted from *Zhuangzi* (□ *The Chapter on Opening Coffers and Chests [Kōu Qìè]*):

*"They make measures of grain and liquid to gauge them—ten sheng make a dou, four dou and four sheng make an hu. Then they steal both the measures and the grain. They make scales and balance beams to weigh them—*权* (quán) being the weight, *衡* (héng) being the beam. Then they steal both the scales and the goods. They make royal seals and tally tokens to authenticate them—*符* (fú) being the copper fish and wooden contracts, *玺* (xǐ) being the jade seal of the sovereign. Then they steal both the seals and the goods."*

**The Chapter on Discarding Learning and Having No Worries [Jué Xué Wú Yōu]**, the Twenty-First:
Discarding learning and having no worries—how far apart is this from *"唯"* (wéi, "simply") and *"阿"* (ā, "crooked")?
How great is the difference between good and evil?

**Extended Meaning**:
*Zhuangzi*'s **"Dao/Way"** speaks of *"俗学"* (sú xué, "common learning")," seeking to restore one's original nature.
Quoted from *Zhuangzi* (□ *The Chapter on Cultivating the Nature [Shàn Xìng Wài Piān]*):

*"Annotation: One has already conformed to the ways of the world, yet wishes to use common learning to restore the original nature—thus, the more one seeks, the further one strays from the **Dao/Way**.**
What people fear, one must indeed fear.
Vast, its end is boundless!"*

**Extended Meaning**:
*The Classic of Poetry* (□ *Shī Jīng*) says: *"The night is endless,"* meaning the water clocks still have much time to run.
Quoted from *Mao Poetry* (□ *Mao Shī* □ *The Lesser Odes [Xiǎo Yā]* □ *Court Torches [Tíng Liáo]*):

*"A praise of King Xuan of Zhou. The night, how it lingers! The light of the court torches flickers—when a nobleman arrives, the sound of the *luán* (鸾, a musical instrument) resounds."*

**The Chapter on the Appearance of the Sage [Kōng Dé Zhī Róng]**, the Twenty-First:
The **Dao/Way** as a thing is but a dim and hazy perception—
Hazy and indistinct, yet within it lies an image.
Indistinct and dim, yet within it lies a substance.

**Extended Meaning**:
*Zhuangzi*'s *"真君"* (zhēn jūn, "True Sovereign") is what we now call *"性"* (xìng, "inherent nature").
Quoted from *Zhuangzi* (□ *The Chapter on Equalizing Things [Qí Wù Piān]*):

*"If this is so, then do all things have ministers and servants?
Are their ministers and servants inadequate to govern them?
Do they alternate as ministers and servants?
Or is there a True Sovereign within them?
Whether one finds its essence or not, it does no harm or benefit to its True Sovereign.
This means that those who attain the True Nature act according to its own **Dao/Way**—even as a lowly laborer, they remain unshaken by praise or blame, content in their own work. Thus, the knowing and the unknowing are all the same.*

*Deep and mysterious, yet within it lies essence.
Its essence is most true, yet within it lies faith."*

**Extended Meaning**:
*"犹乌足之根为跻增"* (yóu wū zú zhī gēn wéi jī zēng) is like the roots of the *wūzú* (乌足, "grass with hoof-like leaves") becoming *jīzēng* (跻增, "a kind of plant").
*"人血之为野火"* (rén xuè zhī wéi yě huǒ) is human blood becoming *"wildfire"* (野火, "a spontaneous combustion phenomenon").
*"朽瓜之为鱼"* (xiǔ guā zhī wéi yú) is a rotten gourd becoming *"fish"* (鱼, "a parasitic beetle in gourds").
*"贤女之为石"* (xián nǚ zhī wéi shí) is a virtuous woman becoming *"stone"* (石, "a fossilized human shape").

Quoted from *Liezi* (□ *The Chapter on Heaven’s Bounties and Earth’s Treasures [Tiān Ruì Piān]*):

*"Annotation: The transformations of life and death—how can one measure them?
What is born here may die there; what dies there may be born here.
The changes of **qi** (炁) adapt to myriad forms—all these are natural responses to what they sense."*

From *The Book of Transformation* (□ *Huà Shū*):

*"乌足"* (wūzú) is the name of a grass.
*Zhèng Shì* (郑氏) annotated *Er Yǎ* (尔雅), saying: *"It grows in woodlands; commonly called *yì* (蜴, "lizard"), yet its appearance varies by region."*

**Extended Meaning**:
*"During the reign of Emperor Hui of Jin, Zhang Hua served as the Minister of Works—his deeds are well-documented and need not be repeated."*

**The Fifth Scroll of the Marvelous Volume on the Extended Meanings of the *Dao De Jing*** ends.

**The Sixth Scroll of the Marvelous Volume on the Extended Meanings of the *Dao De Jing*
Collected by the Recluse Qing’an of Wǔfēng, Master Wáng Shǒuzhèng**

**Chapter Twenty-Two: "The Fullness of the Bent" (曲则全章)**

*"The bent remains whole; the twisted becomes straight.
The hollow becomes full; the worn becomes new.
The small attains; the excessive confuses.
Extended Meaning: This is as seen in Lín Xiàngrú’s yielding to Lián Pó."*

**Annotation:**
Lián Pó and Lín Xiàngrú both served in the state of Zhào. Pó held a lower rank than Xiàngrú, and in anger he declared: *"I have rendered great service to the state, yet I am placed beneath Xiàngrú—this is a shame I cannot bear. When I encounter him on the road, I shall humiliate him."* Xiàngrú, hearing this, always avoided him by turning his carriage aside. His attendants were puzzled and asked: *"Why does the Prime Minister not fear the mighty Qin, yet fear Lián Pó?"* Xiàngrú replied: *"Qin does not dare to lightly attack Zhào because it fears me and General Pó. If I were to meet General Pó, two tigers would fight, and neither could survive—this would endanger Zhào and benefit Qin. I avoid him only to preserve the state. How could I act from private resentment?"* When Pó heard this, he stripped bare, bore a bundle of thorns, and apologized. This shows how one who fears must refine himself, and how straight virtue can correct the bent.

**Extended Meaning:** *"There are those who, though talented, fragment their virtue."*

**Annotation from *Zhuāngzǐ* (人间世):**
*"Zhīlí Cū"* was a man whose jawbone was hidden beneath his navel, whose shoulders rose above his neck, and who could point to the sky by grasping his hair. He could mend needles and weave mats to sustain himself. When beating a drum and playing the *shí* (a divination instrument), he could feed ten men. When summoned for military service, he would roll up his sleeves and stand among the soldiers. When the state demanded labor, he would feign illness to avoid work. When the state gave grain to the sick, he would accept three *zhòng* (a measure) of six *shǐ* (a smaller measure) and four *dòu* (a still smaller measure), along with ten bundles of firewood. Though his body was deformed, he could still nourish himself and live out his natural span. But one who fragments his virtue—his talents go unexalted, his name unsung. If a man understands this and cultivates himself, how could he not preserve his enduring reputation?

**Extended Meaning:** *"The *Yì* says: 'Simple and unadorned, yet all under heaven’s principles are attained.'"*
**Annotation from *Yìjīng* (系辞上):**
*"Simple and unadorned, yet all under heaven’s principles are attained."* **Commentary:** *"No principle under heaven does not arise from simplicity, nor do all things in the world achieve their proper order without it."*

**Extended Meaning:** *"Zhuāngzǐ said: 'When knowledge fails to settle the world, then adorn it with words, augment it with learning. But when words overshadow substance, and learning drowns the mind...'"* **Annotation from *Zhuāngzǐ* (缮性篇):**
*"Knowledge cannot settle the world, so one adds words and learning to it.
