When words overshadow substance, and learning drowns the mind..."* **Exegesis:** *"Though one studies widely to benefit the world, if they do not recognize that substance is the root of words, then ornamentation will obscure the plain. If they do not see that learning is but the fringe of the mind, then excessive study will drown its essence. Only by abandoning learning and discarding words can one attain the beauty of non-action."*

**Extended Meaning:** *"Lièzǐ said: 'The scholar perishes through multiplicity.'"*
**Annotation from *Lièzǐ* (说符篇):**
*"Xīn Dūzǐ said: 'The Great Way loses its way through branching paths, and the scholar perishes through multiplicity.'"*
**Lu’s Commentary:** *"The sheep is a metaphor for the spirit. If one guards the spirit without loss, this is the Way. But once the sheep is lost in wandering paths, it cannot be recovered. Only by guarding the spirit—neither losing nor gaining—does one attain non-dependence. The same applies to excessive adherence to benevolence and righteousness."*

**Extended Meaning:** *"Gēngsāngzǐ said: 'If ten thousand men each draw their bows to shoot at a single hawk, the hawk cannot escape being struck.'"*
**Annotation from *Lièzǐ* (清神第一):**
*"How can one sorrow or rejoice in turn without disturbing the true nature? Much less can the myriad things, in their multitude, uplift and agitate the heart and spirit! Thus, if ten thousand men bend their bows toward a single hawk, how could the hawk escape being struck? This speaks of the hawk as a cunning bird, and all men turn their arrows upon it. It is a metaphor for the heart’s myriad distractions—when emotions surge and the mind is tossed, it is like that hawk pierced by stillness. When the myriad things dazzle and confuse, how could life remain unharmed?"*

**Extended Meaning:** *"Only water approaches the Way: flowing east or west, yet nothing can harm it."*

**Annotation from *Mèngzǐ* (告子章句上):**
*"Gào Zǐ said: 'Human nature is like a rushing stream—if released toward the east, it flows east; if released toward the west, it flows west.'"*
*"This speaks of water’s nature: it follows downward, benefits all things without contention, and thus nothing harms it. Therefore, the sage embraces unity as the model for all under heaven. Not seeing himself, he becomes bright. Not asserting himself, he becomes manifest. Not boasting of himself, he achieves deeds. Not vaunting himself, he endures."*

**Extended Meaning:** *"Yú Shùn had four eyes."*

**Annotation from *Shàngshū* (舜典):**
*"On the first day of the first month, Shùn paid homage at the ancestral temple, consulted the Four Yù (regional officials), opened the four gates, and illuminated the four senses. [Commentary:] 'Consulted' means 'planned.' 'Opened' means 'unlocked.' After Shùn ascended the throne and reported to the ancestral temple, he sought governance from the Four Yù, opened the gates of the four directions to attract the worthy of all under heaven, and expanded the sight and hearing of the four quarters to resolve the obstructions of the world."*

**Extended Meaning:** *"The *Huáinánzǐ* records: When Zhào Jiǎnzǐ died and had not yet been buried, Zhòngmò refused to assist in the funeral rites. Zhào Shuāngzǐ raised an army to attack them."*
**Annotation:** *"This is already detailed in the *Huáinánzǐ*; no need to repeat it."*

---
**Chapter Twenty-Three: "Speak Seldom and Return to Nature" (希言自然章)**

*"Speak seldom and return to nature—
the gusting wind does not last a morning,
the sudden rain does not last a day."*

**Extended Meaning:** *"'Hī' means sparse, few. The *Yì* says: 'The words of the virtuous are few; the words of the hasty are many.'"*
**Annotation from *Yìjīng* (系辞).*

The one who would rebel, his words are ashamed;
the one whose heart is doubtful, his words are disjointed.
The virtuous speak sparingly, the hasty speak much.
Those who slander the good, their words are wandering;
those who lose their steadfastness, their words are twisted.
These six kinds of speech—only the virtuous speak sparingly,
thus proving the meaning of *"Speak Seldom and Return to Nature."*

Who does this for? The heavens and the earth.
The heavens and the earth cannot endure long,
how much less can mortals?

**Extended Meaning:** *"Man dwells between heaven and earth—
is he not like a speck of dust upon a horse’s body?"*
(Quoted from *Zhuāngzǐ*, *"Autumn Waters"* chapter)

This comparison to all things—
is he not like a speck of dust upon a horse’s body?
Great and small each have their ranks,
unable to surpass one another—
how could they be worthy of concern?

Those who align with the Dao, the Dao responds to them.
Those who align with virtue, virtue responds to them.
Those who align with loss, loss responds to them.
If trust is insufficient, there will be distrust.

**Extended Meaning:** *"Men gather by their kind—
thus, when the tiger roars, the wind rises."*
(Quoted from *Yì*, *"Qián"* Nine-Five: *"Clouds follow the dragon, winds follow the tiger."*)
The dragon is a water creature, clouds are water vapor—
when the dragon hums, mist arises: this is clouds following the dragon.
The tiger is a fierce beast, wind is a stirring force—
when the tiger roars, valley winds arise: this is wind following the tiger.
Emotion from the sentient affecting the insentient—
this clearly shows that like qi responds to like qi.

**Extended Meaning:** *"The crane’s cry, its chick answers."*
(Quoted from *Yì*, *"Upper Sequence"*:
*"The crane cries in the shade, its chick answers it.
I have a fine goblet—let me share it with you."*)
**Commentary:** *"The crane cries in a secluded place,
yet its chick, though distant, answers it—
because they share the same kind and respond to one another.
'I have a fine goblet'—this means I possess a noble virtue,
not keeping it to myself.
'Let me share it with you'—
if I scatter it outward, things will also sense my benevolence
and come to follow me."*

**Extended Meaning:** *"If natures differ, then the liver and gall differ—
the distinction of inherent traits.
If the Dao aligns, then the Yi and Xia become one people—
by kind gathering, nothing resists.
Like the *fāngzhū* drawing moonlight to flow water."*

**Extended Meaning:** *"The *fāngzhū* shell—
between the Eastern Sea and Fángzhū, there is a giant clam.
Its length is two *cùn* more than a *chǐ*—
thus it is named *fāngzhū*.
Taking its shell, soft and white, and polishing it for a long time,
then holding it beneath the moon and catching the dew,
water is obtained."*

**Extended Meaning:** *"The *yángyù* draws sunlight to ignite fire."*
*The yángyù*—a bronze vessel shaped like a cup or mirror.
Polishing it with black silk, holding it to the sun,
and placing millet beneath it, fire is obtained.
These two things—*fāngzhū* and *yángyù*—
use the light of sun and moon,
and through like qi responding to like qi,
water and fire are produced.

---
**Chapter Twenty-Four: "The Unsteady Cannot Stand" (跂者不立章)**
The unsteady cannot stand;
the overreaching cannot walk.

**Extended Meaning:**
*"The **unsteady** lifts the heel and gazes forward.
The **overreaching** grasps things with outstretched limbs.
[Annotation: *Tang* Dù Tiānshī’s commentary states:] ‘To walk on the toes with greasy feet is called **unsteady**.
To stretch out and hope for lasting progress is difficult indeed.
Straining neck and lifting heel—
these are the words of Chén Hòu’s *Longmén Fu* [Lament of the Long Gate], expressing longing for favor.
To grasp at things while seeking to walk far is also unattainable.
This illustrates how one who cannot sincerely commit,
doubts the Dao/Way, and with fleeting resolve,
hopes for swift success—how difficult that is!’

**Extended Meaning:**
‘Have you not heard of the youth from Shòuling who went to study walking in Hándān,
but failed to master the art of that land,
and even lost his original way,
so that he crawled back home on all fours?’
[Annotation: From *Zhuāngzi*’s *Qiūshuǐ* [Autumn Water].]
Xīhuá Fǎshī’s commentary:
‘Shòuling was a city of Yān [Yan].
Hándān was a commandery of Zhào [Zhao].
A youth in his weak years, before full strength—
this is the **Shòuling youth**.
The people of Zhào’s commandery were skilled in walking.
Thus, a young man from Yan traveled far to learn the gait,
but his efforts only distorted his nature,
and he failed to acquire Zhào’s skill.
Abandoning his own way to imitate others,
he lost his original manner of Shòuling,
so he used his hands to push himself along,
crawling back in defeat.’

‘Those who see only themselves remain obscure;
those who insist on their own way are not illustrious;
those who praise themselves achieve no merit;
those who boast of themselves grow not long.’

**Extended Meaning:**
‘Yángzǐ, styled **Zǐjū**, dwelt in the south at Péi,
and encountered Lǎozǐ in Liáng.
Lǎozǐ, halting mid-Dao, gazed heavenward and sighed:
*“Once you might have been taught,
but now you cannot be.”*
For his arrogance had only increased since before.
Yángzǐ humbly asked what his fault was.
Lǎozǐ replied:
*“Your strides are **sleep-sleep**,
your posture is **wān-wān**,
and with whom do you dwell?”*
[Annotation: From *Lièzǐ*’s *Huángdì Piān* [Huangdi Chapter].]
Yángzǐ’s gait was **sleep-sleep**—
inverted, not flowing;
**wān-wān**—
dry, not upright.
It seemed he inwardly saw his own failure,
yet outwardly feigned a show of effort.

**Extended Meaning:**
‘Like the frog of a shallow well,
[it] told the **turtle of the Eastern Sea**:
*“I find joy here!
When I leap, I reach the brink of the well’s rim;
when I rest, I nestle in the hollow of the broken tiles.
Dive into water, and I touch the armpits and chin;
plunge into mud, and I sink to the ankles, obliterating my tracks.
I command the water of a single ditch,
and with my **clambering** and **crawling**,
enjoy the pleasures of the shallow well—
this is the utmost delight!
Why do you not often come to visit?”*
The turtle of the Eastern Sea had not yet entered with its left foot,
when its right **claw** was already bound and constrained.
Thus, it journeyed far to the sea and declared:
*“The frog of the shallow well,
when it hears my words,
stares in shock and loses its composure—
it is utterly lost!
Is this not the case of one who insists on his own way and remains obscure?”*
[Annotation: From *Zhuāngzi*’s *Qiūshuǐ* [Autumn Water].]

The **Master Xīhuà** expounded:

*"The shallow well*—that is, the shallow pit—*the frog*, which is the toad. *Dry*—the well’s railing. *Tiles*—the stacked bricks within the well. *Ankles*—the foot’s instep. *Armpits*—the underarm. *Chin*—the lower jaw.

As for the turtle of the Eastern Sea, its form is vast and grand; it rides the waves and plays at will, occasionally dwelling upon the land. Yet the toad, a mere small creature, resides in a narrow well, its physique and dwelling both meager. It finds contentment within its pit, and though it sees the great turtle, it feels no fear. It declares:

*'When I emerge, I leap upon the well’s railing;
when I retreat, I rest upon the broken tiles’ edge.
Swimming, I touch my armpits and chin;
plunging into mud, I vanish my insteps and sink to my ankles.
Gazing upon the shrimp and crab,
and peering down at the insects and beetles,
I wander freely and revel in joy—
none surpasses my delight!*

*Further elaboration states:* *One who boasts of himself*—like the man from Zhèng who lamented the Kòu family’s land, yet in three years became a Confucian scholar, and whose influence spread nine *lǐ* along the river, benefiting three generations—*his parents, his wife...*

The text from *Zhuangzi*—**《列御寇》**—has been transcribed in full and need not be repeated again.

**Commentary:**
*"Verse"* (呻吟) refers to recitation. *Jiū Shì* (裘氏) is a place name. *Zhǐ* (祇) means "just" or "exactly." The man from Zhèng, named *Huǎn*, studied in Jiū land, and in exactly three years became a Confucian scholar. Engaging in debates with Daoists, Mohists, and Confucians, his father aided Zhì, yet within ten years, Huǎn took his own life. Truly, is this not proof that those who boast of themselves reap no fruit?

**Extended Meaning:**
Those who pride themselves—like the Jin historian *Qí Wáng Tóng*, who raised an army to attack Zhao Wang Lún—are detailed elsewhere and need not be repeated here.

To indulge in excess beyond one’s nature is to overflow.

From *Zhuangzi*—**《骈拇》**—it is said:
*"Twisted toes and extra fingers—are these not born of nature? Yet to indulge in deformity, to cling like a parasitic growth or a pendulous wart—these too are born of form, yet to indulge in excess beyond nature."*

**Chapter Twenty-Five: *The Undifferentiated Whole***
There exists an undifferentiated whole that arose before Heaven and Earth.

**Extended Meaning:**
Zhuangzi said:
*"The Dao precedes the Supreme Ultimate yet is not exalted;
it lies beneath the Six Extremes yet is not profound;
it arises before Heaven and Earth yet is not enduring;
it endures beyond the ancient past yet is not aged."*

From *Zhuangzi*—**《大宗师》**—it is said:
*"The Dao precedes the Supreme Ultimate yet is not exalted;
it lies beneath the Six Extremes yet is not profound;
it arises before Heaven and Earth yet is not enduring;
it endures beyond the ancient past yet is not aged."*

**Commentary:**
The *Supreme Ultimate* (太极) refers to the Five Qi (五气).
The *Six Extremes* (六极) are the Six Directions (六合).
Yet the Dao, above the Five Qi, is not distant;
beneath the Six Directions, it is not abyssal;
before Heaven and Earth, it is not eternal;
enduring beyond the ancient past, it is not aged.
It is said that the Dao is neither high nor deep, neither long nor old—
thus, the Dao is everywhere, yet in all places, it is nowhere.

**Extended Meaning:**
This is what the *Yijing*—**《系辞》**—refers to as the *Supreme Ultimate*.
It is said:
*"Thus, the *Yijing* has the Supreme Ultimate,
which generates the Two Complements,
the Two Complements generate the Four Symbols,
the Four Symbols generate the Eight Trigrams."*

**Annotation:**
*"What exists must begin with the nonexistent,
thus the Supreme Ultimate generates the Two Complements.
The Supreme Ultimate is the nameless name—
yet it can be named, taking its extreme from what has,
thus it is called the Supreme Ultimate."*

The Four Symbols are Metal, Wood, Water, and Fire.
Silent and boundless, it stands alone yet never changes,
circulates endlessly yet never falters—
it can be the Mother of all under Heaven.

**Extended Meaning:**
Even form and shadow depend on each other,
yet this is called *self-so* (独化).

From *Zhuangzi*—**《齐物论》**—it is said:
*"Wángliǎng asked his shadow:
'Just now, you walked; now you stand.
Just now, you sat; now you rise.
Why do you lack consistency?'

The shadow replied:
'Do I depend on something to be thus?
What I depend on also depends on something to be thus.
Do I depend on the serpent’s scales or the cicada’s wings?
How would I know why it is so?
How would I know why it is not?'

Guó Xiāng’s annotation says:
'If it depended on the serpent’s scales or the cicada’s wings,
then the reason for its lack of consistency would not be difficult to discern.
But since it does not depend on such things and yet transforms alone,
this is why it is said.'

Thus, the extended meaning takes this to illustrate
that which stands alone yet never changes.

**Extended Meaning:**
When gathered, it is the essence of pure substance,
hidden within millet grains.

**Annotation:**
*"Millet grains"* are the words from *The Scripture on Nourishing Life* (*Dù Rén Jīng*).
Xué Yōuxī’s annotation says:
*"Millet grains are the first of the five grains.
Rice is the foundation of human life—
thus, the saying that one who attains the Dao
has the resources of the Way spread throughout the cosmos,
yet even in its smallest form, it is hidden within millet grains.
This illustrates that the Dao encompasses all without burdening existence—
how then could there be any danger or peril?
Thus it is said: it circulates endlessly yet never falters.

**Extended Meaning:**
It flows through the Six Void Directions,
its applications inexhaustible.

From the *Yijing*—**《下系辞》**—it is said:
*"The *Yijing* as a book cannot be distant;
the Way it describes is ever-shifting.
It does not remain fixed, flowing through the Six Void Directions,
above and below without constancy,
the strong and weak transforming—
it cannot be codified as a fixed standard,
only adapting to change.
This is how the great Dao, as the One Qi,
circulates through the six lines,
extending throughout Heaven and Earth,
in all four directions, above and below—
its applications are inexhaustible,
thus it is said: it circulates endlessly yet never falters."*

I do not know its name—
I call it *Dao*.
If I must name it, I call it *Great*.
Great is *Passing*;
Passing is *Distant*;
Distant is *Returning*.

**Extended Meaning:**
*"Name"* (名) refers to its *virtue/power*;
*"Character"* (字) refers to its *essence*.
From *Du Tian Shi’s* *Broad Explication of Sacred Meaning* (*Guang Shèng Yì*) it is said:
*"To name a thing is to define its essence;
to characterize a thing is to define its virtue/power.
When a thing is born, its name is established—
this is the norm.
There has never been a thing without a name."*

In the *Spring and Autumn Annals*, Duke Huan of Lu, sixth year, ninth month, *Dingmǎo*, Prince Zǐtóng was born.
The Duke asked Shēn Rú for a name.
Shēn Rú replied:
*"Names have five categories:
*Trustworthy* (信), *Virtuous* (义), *Symbolic* (象), *Borrowed* (假), and *Resemblance* (类).
To name based on birth is trustworthy—
like Táng Chú Yú or Lǔ Gōngzǐ Yǒu.
To name based on virtue is virtuous—
like Wén Wáng Chāng or Wǔ Wáng Fā.
To name based on resemblance is symbolic—
like Kǒngzǐ’s *Xiàng* (象) and *Níqiú Shān* (尼丘山).
To borrow from things is borrowed—
like Bó Yú’s birth, when someone presented fish,
so he was named *Lǐ* (鲤, ‘carp’).
To take from the father is resemblance—
like Zǐtóng’s birth, born on the same day as his father."*

**Extended Meaning:**
Before Heaven and Earth arose, it is difficult to speak of its lineage.

**Annotation:**
*"Lineage"* (氏族) is from the *Spring and Autumn Annals*, Duke Huan’s eighth year, winter—
Prince Wúhài died, and Prince Yǔfù’s father requested a posthumous title from the clan.
The Duke asked Zhòng Zhōng about the clan.
Zhòng Zhōng replied:
*"The Son of Heaven establishes virtue,
grants a surname based on birth,
and bestows a clan name with land,
thus naming the lineage."*

The feudal lords took their ancestral fathers’ posthumous titles as surnames, and from these surnames came the clans. If an official had meritorious service passed down through generations, then there were official clans—meaning they took the old offices and old domains as their clans.

Domains were also so named. The Duke bestowed the posthumous title *Zhǎn* (展) as a surname. Prince Wúhài (无骇) was the grandson of the Duke of Zhǎn (展), hence he was of the Zhǎn clan.

Thus, the Dao is vast, Heaven is vast, Earth is vast, and the King is also vast. Within the realm, there are four vast things, and the King occupies one of them.

**Extended Meaning:**
Under all Heaven, there is nothing that is not the King’s land.
Within the borders of the land, there is nothing that is not the King’s subjects.

*(Quoted from the *Mèngzǐ’s* "Wànchāng [上]" chapter.)*

Xiánqiū Méng (咸丘蒙) said: “Mèngzǐ was a disciple of the Master.” When Shùn (舜) did not serve Yáo (尧), he had already received the Mandate.

The *Shījīng* (Book of Songs) states: *“Běiyǎn”* (北山) from the *Xiǎoyǎ* (小雅), saying:
Under all Heaven, there is nothing that is not the King’s land.
Within the borders of the land, there is nothing that not the King’s subjects.

*(普, pǔ, means "all-pervasive"; 率, shuài, means "to follow in order.")*

**Extended Meaning:**
From Earth upward, all things belong to Heaven.

*(Quoted from: "Things on Earth belonging to Heaven." From the *Lièzǐ’s* "Tiānruì [篇]" chapter.)*

There was a man from Qǐguó (杞国) who worried that Heaven and Earth would collapse and fall, leaving him nowhere to rest his body, and so he neglected his sleep and food.

There was also someone who worried about his worry, and so went to enlighten him, saying:
“Heaven is but accumulated *qi* (炁), where there is no place where *qi* does not exist.
Like bending and stretching, inhaling and exhaling, one moves within Heaven all day.
Why should you worry about its collapse?”

If we examine this passage, its words are broad and its meaning profound, and it cannot be exhaustively cited.

But according to the theory of Heaven as accumulated *qi*, since it says one moves within Heaven all day,
then it is known that from Earth upward, all things belong to Heaven.

Thus it is said: *“Not necessarily lofty and distant, but vast and boundless.”*
Again, the *Zhuāngzǐ’s* “Xiāoyáo Yóu” (逍遥游) chapter states:
“Heaven’s vastness, vastness—
is it its true color?
Its boundlessness, without end?
Its gaze upon the world below—
is it also like this.”

People follow Earth, Earth follows Heaven, Heaven follows the Dao, and the Dao follows Nature.

**Extended Meaning:**
People refers to the King.
The King follows Earth’s tranquility, hence without action, the world’s affairs are accomplished.

*(Quoted from the *Zhuāngzǐ’s* "Tiāndào [篇]" chapter.)*
It says:
“Heaven does not give birth, yet all things transform;
Earth does not grow, yet all things nurture;
the Sovereign does nothing, yet the world’s affairs are accomplished.”

*(Commentary states: The King, like Heaven and Earth, contains and nurtures all things,
follows the four seasons to cultivate life,
allows all things to act naturally,
thus the world’s affairs are accomplished.)*

End of the *Sixth Scroll* of the *Mìngjìng* (Mingjing) *Hand Mìngjìng* (Hand Quotations).
End of the *Seventh Scroll* of the *Mìngjìng* (Mingjing) *Hand Quotations*.

**Five-Peak Daoist Master Rǔ Guózhèng’s *Qīng’ān Yìshì* Collection**
**Scroll Twenty-Six: "Heavy as the Root, Light as the Sovereign"**

Heavy as the root, light as the sovereign.
**Extended Meaning:**
When dragons and serpents coil in deep repose and stillness,
then their transformations rise and ascend.
When tigers and leopards rage with wild agitation,
they are met by arrows and hunted, their lives cut short.

*(Quoted from the *Xìazì’s* "Lower Appendices" [下系辞] chapter.)*
It says:
"The inchworm’s bending is to seek sincerity.
The dragon-serpent’s repose is to preserve its life."

*(Commentary states:)*
The inchworm’s bending at the start is stillness,
for it seeks the trust of what follows behind.
Sincerity requires bending,
and bending is to seek sincerity—
this is mutual necessity.

The dragon-serpent’s repose is to preserve its life,
meaning stillness seeks movement.
At first, the dragon-serpent lies in repose—
this is stillness.
Through this, it preserves its life,
and thus its transformations soar and ascend.

*(Quoted from the *Zhuāngzǐ’s* "Gēngsāng Chǔ [篇].")*
Gēngsāngzǐ said:
"My surname is Gēngsāng, my given name is Chǔ.
I am a disciple of Lǎojūn."

A young scholar came and asked:
"Sir, the beast of the *hànchē* [函车]—
its body is as large as a chariot.
If it stands alone and departs from the mountains,
it cannot escape the nets and traps."

This beast is very large,
its mouth able to contain a chariot.
Standing alone and leaving the mountains,
it cannot escape the snares—
this is its peril.

Thus, the true sage moves through life daily,
never abandoning his baggage.
Though he may enjoy grandeur and splendor,
he rests in serene detachment.

**Extended Meaning:**
Grandeur, like that of Nánhóng,
is swift in its movement—
yet still, one cannot abandon his baggage.

*(Quoted from the *Zhuāngzǐ’s* "Gēngsāng Chǔ [篇].")*
Nánhóng, upright and seated, said:
"If I were to live as long as the *Yuè* people,
where would I place my legacy to reach this point?"

Gēngsāngzǐ replied:
"Now, my abilities are small,
insufficient to transform you.
Why not journey south to see Lǎojūn?"

Nánhóng, weak from hunger and travel,
reached Lǎojūn in seven days and seven nights.
This shows that seeking learning is swift,
yet even so, he endured seven days of provisions.
Though he had grandeur and splendor,
how could he abandon his baggage?

Yet how can the ruler of a *wànshèng* [万乘] realm
be light in his governance of the world?
Lightness leads to losing his ministers,
agitation leads to losing his throne.

**Extended Meaning:**
The ruler holds the honored position of *wànshèng* [万乘],
sits upon the grandest treasure of the realm.

*(Quoted from the *Mèngzǐ’s* "Discourse on King Liáng Huìwáng [梁惠王章句上].")*
It says:
"A realm of *wànshèng* [万乘]."
*(Annotation: "Shèng" refers to the number of chariots.)*
A realm of *wànshèng* [万乘]—
the Son of Heaven’s domain within the thousand-li boundary,
where chariots number ten thousand.

*(Quoted from the *Xìazì’s* "Lower Appendices" [下系辞] chapter.)*
It says:
"The Sage’s greatest treasure is called *wèi* [位]."
How does one uphold *wèi* [position]?
By *rén* [benevolence].
How does one gather the people?
By *cái* [wealth]."
*(Justification states:)*
The Sage’s greatest treasure is *wèi* [position].
*Wèi* is the land of a realm,
a treasure is a useful thing.
If one resides in a grand position,
able to widely benefit without bounds,
then it is called the greatest treasure.

**Extended Meaning:**
He who sits in such a position
will invite exile and ruin,
and cannot serve his lord.

*(Quoted from the *Shàngshū’s* "Ballad of Shùn [舜典].")*
It says:
"To banish and exile him far away."

Gōnggōng in Yōuzhōu,
a worthless son of Shàohào,
whom the world called *Qiōngqí*—
this was Gōnggōng himself.
He served as Emperor Yáo’s Water Officer.
Yōuzhōu lay in the north,
the land of Yōu Commandery—
as Zhuāngzi spoke of *Yōudū*.
*"Fàngfàng"* means to place and confine,
to be confined with nowhere else to go.

Gǔndòu at Chóngshān,
a worthless son of the ancient Jiū Emperor,
whom the world called *Hùntún*—
this was Gǔndòu himself.
He conspired with Gōnggōng in the southern marches.
*"Cuàncuàn"* means to exile and imprison.

The Sānmiáo at Sān Yuè,
a worthless son of the Zhěn Yún clan,
whom the world called *Tāotiè*—
he was a vassal of Yáo.
Their realm was sealed in the west,
its borders stretching from the left of Lake Dongting
to the right of Lake Poyang, near Yùzhōu,
close to the southern Nányuè Mountains.
Sān Yuè was a mountain name,
located in the western marches—
the land of the Xi Qiáng in Qín Commandery.
*"Zhìjí"* means to detain, confine, and torment.

Gǔn at Yǔshān.
Gǔn was the father of King Yǔ.
He failed to tame the floods.
All four men harbored wickedness,
defying Yáo’s transformation.
Thus, they were banished to the four marches.

**Extended Meaning:**
*"If a ruler is not discreet, he loses his ministers;
if a minister is not discreet, he loses his life."*
*(Quoted from the *Yì’s* "Systematic Commentary" [系辞]:)*
*"If a ruler is not discreet, he loses his ministers;
if a minister is not discreet, he loses his life.
If minor matters are not handled discreetly, ruin follows—
thus, the sage is cautious, speaking little."*

*"Minor matters"* refers to subtle affairs
requiring careful discretion to prevent disaster.
If indiscretion leads to leaks,
calamity ensues—
hence, *"ruin follows."*

**Extended Meaning:**
From the *Zuǒzhuan*, *Xuān Gōng* Year 9:
*"Duke Xuān of Chén conspired with Kǒng Níng and Yí Xíngfù
over Xià Jī."*

*(Note: Meaning already detailed; no repetition.)*

**Extended Meaning:**
*"Hán Xīn’s defeat of Qí."*
*(Note: Meaning already complete; no further recording.)*

---
**Chapter Twenty-Seven: The Virtue of the Good Way**

The good way leaves no trace.
Good speech leaves no flaw.
Good strategy requires no calculations.
Good closure needs no lock or key to remain sealed.
Good binding requires no cords or knots to remain fastened.

**Extended Meaning:**
Like Zhuāngzi’s *"coverage speech"*—
*"coverage speech"* being words spoken in moderation.
*(Quoted from the *Zhuāngzi’s* "Parables" [寓言]:)*
*"Parables are nineteen in ten,
repeated sayings seventeen in ten.
Coverage speech emerges daily,
harmonized with Heaven’s natural flow.
Thus, one elaborates endlessly,
spending a lifetime in speech.
Yet when one does not speak, it is complete—
speech and non-speech are both complete,
and complete speech is non-speech.
Thus, it is said: ‘No speech.’
No-speech, yet lifelong speech,
never ceasing to speak.

There are things that are so by themselves,
and things that are not so by themselves;
things that are good by themselves,
and things that are not good by themselves.
How can they be so? They are so by being so.
How can they not be so? They are not so by not being so.
How can they be good? They are good by being good.
How can they be not good? They are not good by not being good.
Things inherently have their ‘so’ and their ‘good’—
none are inherently ‘not so’ or ‘not good.’
If not for coverage speech emerging daily,
harmonized with Heaven’s natural flow,
who could endure it long?
If one seeks to speak,
and aligns with this,
what flaw could there be?
Thus, it is said: *‘Good speech leaves no flaw.’*

**Extended Meaning:**
Zhuāngzi said:
*"One and speech make two;
two and one make three.
From here onward,
even the most skilled cannot proceed,
let alone the ordinary."*

*(Quoted from the *Zhuāngzi’s* "Equating Things" [齐物论]:)*
*"Nothing in the world is greater than the tip of an autumn hair,
yet Tai Mountain is small.
No one is shorter-lived than a stillborn child,
yet Pángzǔ is prematurely dead.
Heaven and Earth were born with me,
and all things are one with me.
Since we are already one,
how can there be speech?
Since we are already one,
how can there not be speech?
One and speech make two;
two and one make three.
From here onward,
even the most skilled cannot proceed,
let alone the ordinary.
Thus, from non-being to being, up to three—
how much more so from being to being?
No place to go,
thus it is so.
This is the value of having nowhere to go,
returning to the origin of One,
harmonized with the Dao—
what need for calculations?
Thus, it is said: *‘Good strategy requires no calculations.’*

**Extended Meaning:**
The nine orifices are the gates of *spirit-qi*.
*(Quoted from the *Zhuāngzi’s* "Northward Expanse" [北游]:)*
Zhuāngzi said:
*"The luminous arises from the obscure,
order from the formless,
spirit-qi from the Dao,
form from spirit-qi—
and all things are born from form.
Thus, those with nine orifices are born from the womb,
those with eight from eggs.
Their coming has no trace,
their birth no boundary—
no doors, no rooms,
yet they move freely in all four directions.
This is the nature of *spirit-qi*,
moving without progress,
abandoning the body in non-progress,
lodging the spirit beyond the eight directions—
thus, no gates or thresholds,
no borders or limits,
yet they pervade the four quarters,
communing with all things.
Now, extending this meaning,
the nine orifices are the gates of *spirit-qi*—
thus, it is said: *‘Good closure needs no lock or key.’*
Therefore, the sage always rescues people well,
leaving none abandoned.
Always rescues things well,
leaving none discarded.
This is called *‘following clarity.’*

**Extended Meaning:**
Even tiles and bricks, ladders and screens,
each have their proper use—
thus, it is said: *‘Always rescues things well.’*
*(Quoted from the *Zhuāngzi’s* "Northward Way" [北道]:)*
*"Dōngguōzi dwelled in the Eastern Suburb."*

Dōngguōzi asked of Zhuāngzi: *"Where, then, is this Dao you speak of?"*
Zhuāngzi replied: *"It is nowhere and everywhere."*

Dōngguōzi said: *"Only after you specify can I understand."*
He wished Zhuāngzi to name its exact location.

Zhuāngzi said: *"It is in the ant."*
*"How low!"* Dōngguōzi exclaimed.
*"It is in the millet."* Zhuāngzi replied.
*"How even lower!"*
*"It is in the tiles and bricks."*
*"How even more so!"*
*"It is in excrement and urine."*

Dōngguōzi had no response.

Zhuāngzi said: *"Your question, Master, did not reach the essence. This speaks of the Dao—though pure and exalted, it embraces all things, high or low, fulfilling their nature without abandoning any."*

**Extended Meaning:**
The *Huáinánzǐ* records that when Gōngsūn Lóng was in Zhào,
*(Quoted from the *Huáinánzǐ Hóngliè Jiědào Yìngxùn* [淮南子鸿烈解道应训篇]):*
This meaning is already complete; no need to repeat it.
It fundamentally confirms the principle of *"always rescuing people well, leaving none abandoned."*

Thus, the virtuous person is the teacher of the unvirtuous,
and the unvirtuous person is the resource of the virtuous.

**Extended Meaning:**
In ancient times, the Chu general Zǐfā sought those skilled in arts.
*(Quoted from the *Huáinánzǐ Hóngliè Jiědào Yìngxùn*):*
The meaning is already recorded; no need to transcribe again.
It fundamentally confirms the principle of *"the unvirtuous as the resource of the virtuous."*

To neither honor one’s teacher nor cherish one’s resource—
though one may claim knowledge, it is utter confusion.
This is called *"the pinnacle of subtlety."*

**Extended Meaning:**
Seeking its ultimate truth, it arises from innate nature—
this is called *‘self-so transformation.’*
*(Quoted from *Zhuāngzi*’s "Form and Shadow" [形影篇], Guōxiàng’s annotation:)*
*"If one depends on serpent scales or cicada wings,
there is no singular virtue to recognize,
yet it is not difficult to discern.
Now, those who fail to recognize it
do so precisely because they do not depend on such things
and instead manifest self-so transformation."*

Now, the reclusive Daoists take this as proof
that self-so transformation stems from innate nature—
why then honor or cherish it?

**Extended Meaning:**
Bólè cannot transform a dull horse into a fine steed.
*(Annotation: Bólè, surname Sūn, given name Yáng, styled Bólè,
a man of Qin Mùgōng’s time,
skilled in horsemanship. *Nǔtuí* refers to a dull horse;
*qílì* refers to a dragon-horse.
It states that though Bólè was skilled in horses,
he could not make a dull horse into a dragon-horse.)*

**Extended Meaning:**
A master craftsman cannot carve *zhū* or *lì* into beams.
*(Annotation: The *zhū* tree, as in *Zhuāngzi*’s "Free and Easy Wandering" [逍遥游篇],*
where Huìzǐ, surname Huì, given name Shī—
a friend or disciple of Zhuāngzi—
said to Zhuāngzi: *"I have a great tree,
which people call *zhū*.
If you hollow it, it is damp inside;
it is a worthless wood.
Its trunk is swollen and does not conform to the plumb line,
its branches are twisted and do not fit the compass.
Place it by the road, and no craftsman will look at it.
Now, your words are great yet useless—
just as this tree, which all reject."*
Zhuāngzi replied: *"Have you not seen the weasel?
It crouches low, waiting for the arrogant.
It pounces on the haughty wild rat,
leaping east and west without fear of height or depth.
It falls into snares and dies in nets.
So too, those who cling to the traces of the sage,
feigning emotions and actions to gain immediate advantage,
avoiding no peril—
they will surely meet misfortune later.
Now, the ox *máo* is vast as a cloud hanging from the sky.
This is greatness, yet it cannot catch a mouse.
Now, you have this great tree, yet you lament its uselessness—
why not plant it in the land of *Wúhéyǒuyǒu*,
in the boundless wilderness,
where it wanders freely beside the Uncarved,
and reposes at ease beneath its shade?
No axes or blades will harm it,
no thing will injure it—
where is its constraint or suffering?"*

Again, the *lì* tree, as in *Zhuāngzi*’s "Human World" [人间世]:
When Jiàngshí arrived in Qūyuán,
he reached Qūxián’s crooked path—
a road named after its crookedness—
and saw a *lì* tree.
Its trunk covered an ox,
its girth measured a hundred spans,
its height surpassed the mountain by ten *rèn* and seven feet.
Yet it had branches,
and ten or more could be made into boats.
Onlookers gathered like a market,
but Jiàngshí passed without a glance,
walking on without pause.
His disciple, weary of observing,
ran to catch up with Jiàngshí and said:
*"Since I followed you with axe and adze,
I have never seen wood as fine as this.
Yet you refuse to look, walking on without stopping—why?"*
Jiàngshí said: *"Enough! Do not speak of it again.
This is useless wood—
if made into a boat, it sinks;
if made into a coffin, it rots quickly;
if made into doors or windows, it leaks.
Its sap and pitch cannot be cut by axe or adze.
If made into a pillar, it is riddled with worms.
This is wood of no use,
and thus it lives so long."*

Now, those who cite this speak of the transmission between teacher and student:
If one encounters those with innate talent,
they can be taught and shaped into vessels.
If one lacks innate talent,
even instruction will not succeed—
just as Bólè could not transform a dull horse into a dragon-horse,
or a master craftsman could not carve *zhū* or *lì* into beams.

**Extended Meaning:**
Lìzǐ studied under Lǎoshāngshì and befriended Bó Gāozǐ,
and thus attained the inner and outer winds of the transcendent.
*(Quoted from *Lièzǐ*’s "Huángdì" [黄帝篇]:*
Lìzǐ studied under Lǎoshāngshì and befriended Bó Gāozǐ,
advancing their teachings and returning by wind.
Yǐn Shēng heard of this and resided with Lìzǐ for months without asking.
Yet when he secretly requested the method ten times,
Lìzǐ refused him ten times.
Yǐn Shēng, angered, sought to leave,
but Lìzǐ did not inquire.
Yǐn Shēng withdrew for months,
his mind unsettled, and again went to him.
Lìzǐ said: *"Why do you come and go so frequently?"*)

「Yǐn Shēng said: *"In the past, when Zhāng Dài requested [the method] from you, you did not tell him, and he certainly held resentment toward you. Now you have suddenly abandoned him, so that is why he comes again."*

Lìzǐ said: *"In the past, I considered you as one who had attained the Dao. Now I see your vulgarity has reached this point! I shall reveal to you what I learned from my Master.*

*When I first served my Master and befriended others like you, after three years, my heart dared not ponder right or wrong, my mouth dared not speak of gain or harm, and only then did my Master cast a single glance upon me.*

*After five years, my heart still hesitated to ponder right or wrong, my mouth still dared not speak of gain or harm, and only then did my Master begin to smile.*

*After seven years, I followed my heart’s thoughts—no right or wrong—and my mouth’s words—no gain or harm. Only then did my Master invite me to sit beside him.*

*After nine years, I aligned my heart’s thoughts and my mouth’s words, neither knowing my own right or wrong, gain or harm, nor his own, nor even recognizing my Master as my Master or my friends as my friends. The inner and outer winds of the transcendent had fully awakened in me.*

*Then my eyes became like ears, ears like nose, nose like mouth—no distinction between them. My mind concentrated, my form released, my bones and flesh dissolved into oneness, and I no longer knew where my body leaned or my feet trod. I drifted east and west with the wind, like a dry leaf or empty husk, utterly unaware whether the wind rode me or I rode the wind.*

*Now you dwell at your Master’s doorstep, not even completing the minimal span of time, yet you resent and grumble repeatedly. Your slightest part will be abandoned by qi, your smallest node will be cast off by the earth. How can you hope to walk upon the void and ride the wind?*

Yǐn Shēng was deeply ashamed, held his breath for a long while, and dared not speak again.

*If Lìzǐ and Lǎoshāngshì could truly be called those who forget their teachers and their teachings, then today I take this to prove the meaning of not valuing one’s teacher and not cherishing one’s teachings.*

**Extended Meaning:**
*Confucius, upon seeing Tàigōng Rèn, then abandoned his disciples and renounced his associations.*

Quoted from *Zhuāngzǐ*’s *"Shānmù"* [山木篇]:
*Confucius was trapped between Chén and Cài, seven days without food. Tàigōng Rèn went to comfort him and said: "Master, are you near death?" He replied: "Yes." "Do you dislike death?" He replied: "Yes." Tàigōng Rèn said: "You have spoken of the way of not dying. In the Eastern Sea, there is a bird called Yìdài. Its appearance is drooping and sluggish, unable to soar high. Yet it seems powerless, yet it rises with a gentle push and rests in the shelter of others, never daring to lead when advancing, nor lag when retreating, never daring to taste first when feeding—it must wait for the remnants. Thus, its movements are never singled out, and it blends with the crowd. Yet outsiders can never harm it, so it avoids calamity. Straight trees are cut first, sweet wells are drained first. Your cleverness deceives the ignorant, your polished conduct reveals the filthy—your brilliance is like displaying the sun and moon in daylight, so you cannot escape misfortune."*

*I once heard from a Great Achiever say: "Those who boast of their own merits have no true merit. When merit is attained, it is lost. When fame is gained, it is diminished. Who can abandon merit and fame and return to the common folk? The Dao flows without claiming residence, virtue acts without seeking recognition—pure and constant, this is the way of the mad. They discard their traces and abandon their influence, not striving for merit or fame. Thus, they bear no blame from others, nor do others blame them. The Perfect Ones are unheard of. Why do you take such joy in this?"*

Confucius said: *"Excellent! To renounce one’s associations and abandon one’s disciples, to flee to the Great Marsh, wearing coarse hemp garments, eating acorns and chestnuts, entering the realm of beasts without disturbing their order, entering the realm of birds without disrupting their flight—if beasts and birds do not harm them, how much more so will humans?"*

**Extended Meaning:**
*The Seven Saints were all lost in confusion, with nowhere to inquire about the Dao.*

Quoted from *Zhuāngzǐ*’s *"Xúwúguǐ"* [徐无鬼篇]:
*Huángdì was about to meet Dàwěi at Jùcí Mountain. Fāngmíng acted as charioteer, Chāngyú as the companion riding beside the chariot, Zhāng Ruò and Zhōu Yǐn as the leading horses. Kūn Hūn and Huá Gǔ served as rear attendants. When they reached the wilderness of Xiāngchéng, the Seven Saints were all lost in confusion. Today, your state has a Xiāngchéng County, located south of Tàiyǐ Mountain—this is where Huángdì sought the Dao.*

*From Huángdì onward, including Huá Gǔ, there were a total of seven such Saints.*

Annotation: *Saints—this is a title. Once names arise, the Way is lost.*

*Even if you desire it, Dàwěi is beyond reach. Nowhere to inquire about the Dao.*

*They encountered a young boy tending horses, and asked him about the Way. The boy was a Daoist.*

*"Do you know of Jùcí Mountain?"* The boy replied: *"Yes."* *"Do you know where Dàwěi resides?"* The boy replied: *"Yes."*

Huángdì said: *"Strange, little boy! Not only do you know of Jùcí Mountain, but you also know where Dàwěi resides. May I ask you about governing the world?"*

The boy said: *"Governing the world is no different from this! What more is there to do?*

*Knowing its grandeur yet guarding its humiliation—this is the way to govern the world.*

*To govern the world by guarding its humiliation, one’s constant virtue is then sufficient, returning to simplicity.*

**Extended Meaning:**
*Grass and trees flourish as honor. Quoted from the* **Zhōuyǐ** [周易] *Būn* [坤] *Hexagram’s text:*

*"Heaven and Earth undergo transformations, and grass and trees flourish. Heaven and Earth close in, and the virtuous withdraw."*

*Kǒng Yǐngdá’s* **Zhèngyì** [正义] *states:*

*"This passage explains the six lines: Heaven and Earth undergo transformations refers to the interaction of yin and yang, nurturing all things, so grass and trees flourish. Simplicity disperses to form vessels; the Sage employs it to become an official or leader, so great institutions do not sever."*

**Extended Meaning:**
*Zǐgōng asked Confucius: "How would I fare, Master?"*

Confucius said: *"You are a vessel."* [Quoted from *Lúnyǔ* [论语] *Gōngyězhǎng* [公冶长] Fifth]

Zǐgōng asked: *"How would I fare, Master?"*
Confucius said: *"You are a vessel."*
Zǐgōng said: *"What kind of vessel?"*
Confucius said: *"A hu-lü."* [A vessel of refined use]
Vessels are made from materials fit for purpose—
In the Xia, it was called *hu*;
In the Shang, it was called *lü*;
In the Zhou, it was called *fúguǐ*;
All were ritual vessels for storing millet in ancestral temples, adorned with jade.
They are the most noble and splendid of vessels.

**Chapter 29: Jiāngyù** [将欲]
*"If one would take the world by force, I see that it cannot be done."*

**Extended Meaning:**
Shun avoided Emperor Yao’s son in the southern reaches of the River,
And the lords of the states—those who came to pay homage,
those who sought justice,
those who sang and praised—
all went to Shun, not to Yao’s son.
This was not the way of seizing the world by force.

[Quoted from *Mèngzǐ* [孟子] *Wànzhāng Zhāngjù Shàng* [万章章句上]]
Shun served as Yao’s minister for twenty-eight years—
a feat no mortal could achieve; it was the will of Heaven.
When Yao passed away and the three-year mourning ended,
Shun retreated to the southern bank of the River,
yet the lords of the states came to pay homage to him,
those who sought justice came to him,
and those who sang and praised sang and praised him.
Thus it was said: *"It was Heaven’s doing."*
Only then did he enter the Central States and ascend to the throne of the Son of Heaven.

Now, this serves as proof:
Shun’s rule was granted by Heaven and accepted by the people—
not seized by force.

The world is a sacred vessel—
it cannot be taken by force.
Those who attempt to seize it will fail;
those who grasp it will lose it.

**Extended Meaning:**
The sacred vessel refers to the supreme position,
a divine instrument of Heaven and Earth.
[Quoted from *Yì Xìshí* [易系辞]]
*"The greatest virtue of Heaven and Earth is to give birth.
The greatest treasure of the Sage is his position.
How does one preserve this position? By virtue (rén)."*
The world is vast, governed by a divine principle—
how could it be seized by force?
Even if obtained, it must be lost—
how can one not be cautious?

**Extended Meaning:**
*Zhuāngzǐ* recounts how Tiāngēn wandered in the realm of Yīn Tāng,
reaching the banks of Liǎoshuǐ,
where he encountered a nameless man and asked him:
*"May I ask about ruling the world?"*
The nameless man replied: *"Leave! You are a vulgar man—
why ask what you cannot comprehend?"*
[Quoted from *Zhuāngzǐ* [庄子] *Yìngdìwáng* [应帝王]]

Tiāngēn was a proper name wandering in Yīn Tāng’s domain,
arriving at Liǎoshuǐ’s banks,
where he met the nameless man and questioned him—
his intent was on the world.
The nameless man again said:
*"You wander in the boundless,
harmonizing your qi with the void,
following nature without artifice—
and the world will be governed."*

**Extended Meaning:**
*"When the seven orifices were pierced, HúnDùn died."*
[Quoted from *Zhuāngzǐ* [庄子] *Yìngdìwáng*]
The passage has been detailed elsewhere and need not be repeated.

The extended meaning states:
*"Whipping and urging with threats exhausts the horse’s strength."*
[Quoted from *Zhuāngzǐ* [庄子] *Mǎtí* [马蹄] passage.]

The Great Dao is abandoned—its principles have been fully expounded elsewhere and need not be repeated.

Thus, the Sage avoids excess, avoids extravagance, avoids excessiveness.

The extended meaning states:
*"Hence, Zhòngní [Confucius] did not overdo things."*
[Quoted from *Mèngzǐ* [孟子] *Líliú Zhāngjù Shàng* [离娄章句下], where Mèngzǐ [孟子] says:]
*"Zhòngní [Confucius] did not overdo things."*
[Commentary by Yáng Shì [杨氏] states:]
*"This means the Sage never exceeded his proper bounds—not even by a hair’s breadth. This was not Mèngzǐ’s true insight; Zhòngní [Confucius] could not have been praised in such terms."*

The extended meaning states:
*"Ritual—when in doubt, choose frugality over extravagance."*
[Quoted from *Lúnyǔ* [论语] *Bāyì Dìsān* [八佾第三], where Zǐ [子] says:]
*"Great indeed is your question!
Ritual—when in doubt, choose frugality over extravagance.
Mourning—when in doubt, choose grief over ease."*
These are the words of Zhòngní [Confucius] in response to Lín Fàng [林放], a man of Lǔ [鲁].

The extended meaning states:
*"Shùn [舜] received Yáo’s [尧] realm yet did not consider it excessive."*
[Quoted from *Mèngzǐ* [孟子] *Téngwén Gōng Zhāngjù Xià* [滕文公章句下], where Páng Gēng [彭更], a disciple of Mèngzǐ [孟子], asked:]
*"With tens of chariots following, hundreds of attendants, and provisions received from the lords—is this not excessive?"*
Mèngzǐ [孟子] replied:
*"If it is not the proper path, even a single bowl of rice cannot be accepted from others.
If it is the proper path, then Shùn [舜] received Yáo’s [尧] realm without considering it excessive—do you deem it excessive?"*

This speaks of three faults in the Sage’s actions that, if committed, bring ruin. Thus, they are all avoided—through humility, restraint, and guarding the void—so as to align with the Dao. Only then will there be no burden of excess.

**End of *Zhēn Dàodé Jīng Yuànyì* [道德真经衍义] Handwritten Manuscript Volume Seven.**
#1 [Note: A section appears missing before *"Knowing its Honor"*]

**Beginning of *Zhēn Dàodé Jīng Yuànyì* [道德真经衍义] Handwritten Manuscript Volume Eight**
*Wǔfēng Qīng’ányì Shì Wáng Shǒuzhèng* [五峰清安逸士王守正] Collection.

**Volume Eight of *Zhēn Dàodé Jīng Yuànyì* [The Elucidated Dao De Jing]**
*Collection of Wáng Shǒuzhèng [王守正], the Reclusive and Serene Scholar of Wǔfēng [五峰].*

---

**Chapter Thirty: Aiding the Sovereign with the Dao**

*"Aiding the sovereign with the Dao"* means not to use military force to dominate the world.
Its actions favor reversibility.

The *Yuànyì* [Elucidation] states:
*"He who kills another’s father will have his own father killed.
He who kills another’s elder brother will have his own elder brother killed."*
This passage is excerpted from *Mèngzǐ [Mencius]—Exhausting the Heart, Lower Part*.

Mèngzǐ [Mencius] said:
*"Now I understand the weight of killing one’s own kin.
He who kills another’s father will have his own father killed.
He who kills another’s elder brother will have his own elder brother killed.
Yet this is not truly self-inflicted harm—it is merely a single step removed.
**Annotation:** A single step removed (*yī jiān* 一问) means: I go, and he comes; one person’s action returns upon another. In truth, it differs not from harming one’s own kin.*

The *Yuànyì* [Elucidation] further states:
*"What you send forth will return to you."*
This passage is excerpted from *Mèngzǐ [Mencius]—The Sententious Discourse of King Xuan of Liang, Lower Part*, where Zǒu [邹] and Lǔ [鲁] quarreled, and Mùgōng [穆公] inquired.
**Annotation:** *Zhǎn*  is the sound of combat.

Mèngzǐ [Mencius] replied:
*"In years of famine and scarcity, your people’s aged and infirm perish in ditches and ravines, while the strong flee to the four quarters—nearly a thousand have already departed! Yet your granaries overflow, your treasuries are full, and no official dares report this. This is to scorn above and oppress below.
Zēngzǐ [曾子] said: ‘Beware! Beware! What you send forth will return to you.’
Now the people have the means to repay you.
Let the sovereign take no offense."*

**Elucidation** further states:

*"The King of Chu, the King of Qi, Qin Shi Huang, and Han Emperor Wu—each met their fate either through death of themselves or misfortune upon their descendants."*

**Excerpted from:** *Chūnqiū [Spring and Autumn Annals]—The Sententious Discourse of Duke Zhao,* where the Prince Bǐ of Chu, Prince Hēigōng of the Lǔ clan, Prince Zhì, and Mànchéng Rén, the Lord of Cāi and Cháo, led the armies of Chén, Cāi, Bùjiāng, Xǔ, and Yè to invade Chu. They slaughtered Prince Lù and Prince Bàidí, then sent a messenger to summon the troops of the Ouyáng clan to join them at Qiānxī, where they turned against Chu. The armies were routed at Zǐ Liáng.

Upon hearing of the deaths of the princes, the King of Chu cast himself beneath his carriage and lamented: *"How could people love their children as I love mine?"*
The king declared: *"I have slain many sons of others—how could I avoid this fate?"*
*"Is this not Heaven’s retribution upon me?"*

Right Minister Zǐgé said: *"Let me wait at the outskirts to hear the voices of the people."*
The king replied: *"The wrath of the masses cannot be provoked."*
Zǐgé said: *"If we enter the capital and beg aid from the feudal lords."*
The king replied: *"They have all turned against me."*
Zǐgé said: *"If we abandon the feudal lords and instead submit to the designs of the great powers against you."*
The king replied: *"Great blessings do not repeat—only shame shall I reap."*

Yet, Dān returned to Chu. Zǐgé was strangled by the Lord of Yǐn, Shēnxià. The King of Chu died in exile at Gòng,—this was not death of himself alone, but misfortune upon his descendants, was it not?

**Excerpted from:** *Tōngjiàn [Comprehensive Mirror to Aid in Governance]—The Era of Duke Qi,* where the General Lèyù of Yān led the armies of Qín, Wèi, Hán, and Zhào to attack Qi. Yù declared: *"The King of Qi boasts of his merits and flaunts his abilities, his schemes fail to reach the common folk, he discards the virtuous and relies upon flatterers, his decrees are harsh and cruel, the people resent him—his subjects will surely rebel, and Qi can be conquered."* The King of Qi fled to Jǔ.

The State of Chu sent Ruǎnchǐ to lead troops in Qi’s defense. Chǐ then became Qi’s prime minister. Desiring to divide Qi’s lands with Yān, he seized and executed the King of Qi.

Gǔzǐ [Gongsun Chuo] said: *"A state is the common benefit and advantage of all under Heaven. If one holds it by the Dao/Way, it brings great peace, great prosperity, and the accumulation of virtue. If one holds it without the Dao/Way, it brings great peril, great burdens, and it is better to have it not at all. When its decline reaches its extreme, even a commoner cannot escape it. The cases of Qi’s Duke Mǐn and Song’s Duke Xiàn are examples of this. This was not without the Dao/Way—yet each met their fate through death of themselves."*

The First Emperor of Qin, in his first year of Zhengshì (Year 1), from the *yǐmǎo* (2nd month of the 1st year) to the twenty-sixth year of *gēngchén* (7th month of the 26th year), pacified the feudal lords and extinguished the Six States, and took the title of First Emperor.

In the autumn of the thirty-seventh year, the seventh month, he died at the Sandbank Terrace.

Hú Hài ascended the throne, styling himself the Second Emperor. In the third year, the chancellor Zhào Gāo, fearing execution for himself, secretly ordered the magistrate of Xiányáng, Yān Lè, to lead a thousand or more scribes and soldiers to the Palace of the Watching Tides. Yān Lè went before the Second Emperor and repeatedly said: *"Your Majesty is arrogant and unchecked, slaughtering without the Dao/Way. All under Heaven have rebelled against Your Majesty—Your Majesty should consider your own fate."* Lè then advanced his troops. The Second Emperor killed himself. The child Yīng was then enthroned as the Qin King.

In the winter of the first year of the High Ancestor [Emperor Gaozu], the tenth month, Pèi Gōng arrived at Bàshàng. The Qin King Yīng came forth in a plain carriage with white horses, his neck bound with a rope, surrendering the imperial seal and the credentials of command. The *Zìshū* records the character *穿* as "chāo" (to pierce), and the *Zhìyǐn* (phonetic annotation) explains: *"The piercing of the wheel is pronounced 'chāo' with the tone of 穿."* Pèi Gōng then led his forces westward to slaughter Xiányáng, killing the surrendered Qin King Yīng and burning the Qin palaces. Yīng ruled for forty-six days until the state fell and he died—was this not a calamity upon his descendants?

In the autumn of the second year of Emperor Wǔ’s reign of *Hé* (Peace), the fourth month, the Emperor visited Gānquán. In the seventh month, the autumn, the Crown Prince Jù killed the envoy Jiāng Chōng, and, revealing this to Empress Hòu, raised troops in rebellion. The decree ordered the chancellor Qū Máo to suppress them. Jù fled to Lake Lú, and Empress Hòu Wèi, along with Jù, both killed themselves. The Hú clan [Empress Hòu’s family] said: *"The Emperor’s ambitions were vast, his desires many—he exhausted military strength and waged wars without restraint, undertook massive construction projects, and never ceased his tours. When the people’s strength was spent, bandits swarmed, and then great disaster befell his descendants—was this not fitting?"*

Where the Teacher’s forces have passed, thorny thickets spring up.
After the great armies, there will surely be years of famine.

The *Yan Yi* states: *"Fields lie fallow, stones exposed—thorny thickets spring up."* The annotation reads: *"Fields lie fallow, stones exposed"*—this is from the *Zhuangzi*’s [*Fisherman*] chapter: *"When officials attend to their duties and people worry over their affairs, then none overstep their bounds. Thus fields lie fallow, stones are exposed, clothing and food are insufficient, taxes and levies go unpaid, wives and concubines are discordant, and elders and juniors lack order—these are the woes of the common folk."* This clearly shows that after great armies pass, farmers lose their livelihoods, fields become overgrown with thorny thickets, and years of famine are sure to follow.

Thus, the virtuous act decisively but do not seek strength through it. Act decisively yet take no pride, act decisively yet boast not, act decisively yet grow not arrogant—when action is unavoidable, this is acting decisively without seeking strength.

The *Yan Yi* states: *"Since the Sage drew the trigrams, bending wood into bows and shaping wood into arrows, the sharpness of bows and arrows was used to awe the world."* This is quoted from the *Zhouyi’s* [*Lower Appendices*]. *"Bending wood into bows and shaping wood into arrows, the sharpness of bows and arrows was used to awe the world"*—this is derived from *Kui* [Divergence]. *Kui* means "contrariety." When things are contrary, disputes arise, and the use of bows and arrows is to awe and suppress such disputes. This was the Sage’s reluctant resort to force, demonstrating his awe—not that he delighted in killing.

The *Yan Yi* states: *"Gengsangzi said: The origin of war began with the first appearance of humankind."* He also said: *"If there were a throat medicine that could kill, one should not ban all physicians in the world."* As for warfare, it cannot be abolished in the world. Those who govern with the Dao as their assistant should uphold the yielding and tranquil, not rely on force to dominate the world. If attacked by an enemy and forced to respond, then it is called skillful use of arms. This is quoted from the *Kangcangzi’s* [*On Military Strategy*] chapter. Now, these are taken as evidence: though war cannot be abolished, one must not rely on it, warning against using force to dominate the world.

The *Yan Yi* states: *"The Spring and Autumn Annals says: Killing the enemy is decisive."* The annotation reads: *"In the second year of Duke Huan of Lu, Spring, the Spring and Autumn Annals records that the Marquis Gui of Zheng, having received his orders from Chu, attacked Song. Hua Yuan of Song, leading the armies of Le and Ju, confronted them. On the 12th day of the second month, they clashed at Da Ji, and the Song forces were routed. Hua Yuan was captured, and his capture was made known to demonstrate his survival. They seized Le and Ju’s commander, Hua Yuan, along with 460 chariots and 250 captives, and cut off the heads of 100 others. The Song minister Kuang Jiao welcomed the Zheng forces, but the Zheng soldiers threw him into a well. Kuang Jiao was a Song minister. They overturned their spears to rescue him, capturing Kuang Jiao instead. The sage said: ‘He violated protocol and disobeyed orders—it was fitting that he be taken prisoner.’ In warfare, the display of decisive resolve and attentive listening is called ‘protocol.’ Listening means keeping orders constantly in mind, internalizing them, and imagining their implementation. Killing the enemy is decisive; achieving decisiveness is resolute. If one reverses this, one will surely be punished. Reversing it is punishment. Before the battle, Hua Yuan slaughtered sheep to feed the soldiers, but his charioteer, Yang Zhen, refused to partake. In battle, Yang Zhen said: ‘Yesterday’s sheep was yours to command; today’s matter is mine to command.’ He fought against the Zheng forces, and thus their army was defeated. The sage said: ‘Yang Zhen was not a true man. Out of personal resentment, he caused the nation’s treasures to be exhausted.’ What greater crime is there than this? The *Shijing* speaks of ‘the unvirtuous among men’—taking from the *Small Odes*, it refers to how unvirtuous men blame one another and bring ruin. This was Yang Zhen’s case. Thus, killing the enemy is decisive, meaning to stop the enemy. When war is unavoidable, it is used to stop the enemy, so that they no longer become raiders—hence, ‘not daring to seek strength through it.’"*

The *Yan Yi* states: *"After Tang conquered Xia, he spoke of his temporary virtue."* The annotation reads: *"From the Shang Book’s* [*Commissioner Zhi’s Instructions to Tang*]. Tang exiled Jie to Nan Chao, a place named ‘Only Shameful Virtue.’ He said: ‘I fear that future generations will use this as grounds for criticism.’ Though Tang followed Heaven and responded to the people, having inherited the succession from Yao, Shun, and Yu, he still felt uneasy in his heart, ashamed that his virtue did not match the ancients, as if fearing that the world would later use this as grounds for criticism."*

The *Yan Yi* states: *"When returning to Bo, he had the fear of standing at the brink."* The annotation reads: *"Tang’s Speech."* The king, having conquered Xia, returned to Bo and proclaimed his intentions to all under Heaven. Bo was Tang’s capital, located in Gu Shu County of Song Prefecture. He said: *"Let me, as one man, unite and pacify this land—thus securing my state. But I fear I have not yet measured up to the expectations of Heaven and Earth, trembling with dread as if about to fall into a deep abyss."* Heaven entrusted me to unite and pacify this land, but the weight of this responsibility is so great that I fear I am unworthy. I do not yet know if I have offended Heaven and Earth, and I am filled with terror, as if standing at the brink of a deep abyss.

The *Yan Yi* states: *"In the Spring and Autumn period, Wu had once defeated Yue but harbored contempt for Chu."* This is detailed in the *Spring and Autumn* annotations and need not be repeated. *"When things flourish, they decline—this is called ‘not the Way.’ The un-Way perishes early."*

The *Yan Yi* states: *"The Way exists before the ancient sages but is not called ‘first.’"* This is quoted from the *Zhuangzi’s* [*Master of the Heavenly Pattern*] chapter. *"Before the Great Ultimate, it was not called ‘high’; beneath the six extremes and six unities, it was not called ‘deep’; before Heaven and Earth came into being, it was not called ‘long-lasting’; enduring beyond the ancient sages, it was not called ‘old.’"* This speaks of the Way being everywhere yet nowhere, its presence everywhere yet its essence nowhere. The *Chapter on the Mysterious Oneness* has already elaborated on this. The *Chapter on Fine Arms* follows as the thirty-first.

The *Yan Yi* states: *"The gentleman values the left when at home but the right when wielding arms. Arms are an ill-omened instrument, not the tool of the gentleman; when unavoidable, they are used with equanimity as the highest principle."*

The *Yan Yi* elaborates: *"When barbarians and Yi tribesmen [or ‘uncivilized’ peoples] act deceitfully against the Xia—when raiders or violent bandits prey upon others—this is quoted from the *Shun’s Appointments* chapter. The Emperor Shun commanded Gao Yao: ‘Barbarians and Yi tribesmen act deceitfully against the Xia; raiders are called *kòu*, bandits *zéi*, the treacherous are *jiān*, and those who sow internal discord are *niè*. You shall be the *shì* [officer], overseeing the administration of punishments. The five punishments must be meted out with due deliberation, and the five stages of exile must be justly applied. Only the enlightened can truly fulfill this.’ This speaks of Shun’s command to Gao Yao as the *shì*, where the barbarians and Yi tribesmen, the *Róng* and *Dì* [or ‘uncivilized’ peoples], sow chaos within the central Xia, harming the common folk. You, as the *shì*, must govern and resolve these matters, ensuring your wisdom and discernment are unerring so that punishments fit the crimes and all submit without doubt. The five stages of exile refer to the severity of the crime, while the ‘three applications’ are interpreted by Confucius as: the greatest crimes are punished in the open fields, lesser ones by the great ministers in the court, and the smallest by the officers in the marketplace. The five degrees of exile denote those deserving leniency, yet their dwellings are still classified into three ranks—just as the five grades of nobility are divided into three tiers. Though the use of arms and warfare may be unavoidable, the gentleman acts only when compelled, hence the saying: ‘The gentleman of the Dao uses arms only when he has no choice.’*

The *Yan Yi* elaborates further: *"To conquer and settle chaos and violence is the proper use of arms. To prevail in battle is called ‘conquest’ (*kè*), to inflict harm on others is ‘calamity’ (*huò*), and to act contrary to virtue, spreading confusion, is ‘disorder’ (*luàn*). This is quoted from: ‘To conquer and settle chaos’—this is how Jiāng Zǐyá [Maizi of Qi] of the Jiāng clan, fishing at the Pán River, split a fish and found a jade pendant within, inscribed with this text, titled *The Six Taos* (*Liù Táowéi*). The first is the *Wén Táowéi* [‘Cultural Tao’], adhering to the Dao of the state without transgressing Heaven’s natural order. The second is the *Wǔ Táowéi* [‘Martial Tao’], conquering chaos and subduing the eight directions with might. The third is the *Lóng Táowéi* [‘Dragon Tao’], harmonizing yin and yang without overstepping seasonal command. The fourth is the *Hǔ Táowéi* [‘Tiger Tao’], skillfully employing claws and fangs to crush the wicked. The fifth is the *Bào Táowéi* [‘Leopard Tao’], seizing opportunities to quell disasters with wisdom rooted in strategy and timing. The sixth is the *Quǎn Táowéi* [‘Hound Tao’], probing the subtlest intelligence or achieving unexpected feats through reconnaissance. *Táo* means ‘to conceal’—the arts of warfare and stratagem must not be lightly revealed to others, hence the term ‘concealed Tao.’*

The *Yan Yi* elaborates: *"This is not for the sake of coveting land or wealth. This is quoted from the time of Emperor Xuān of the Han Dynasty, when Wèi Xián served as Chancellor. During the *Yuán Kāng* era, the Xiongnu sent troops to attack the Han, encroaching upon their cultivated fields. The military commander could not repel them. The emperor consulted with General Zhōngguó Zhāng Zǐguó and others, intending to exploit the Xiongnu’s decline by sending troops to strike their right flank, preventing further harassment of the Western Regions. The chancellor submitted a memorial advising: ‘I have heard that armies raised to rescue chaos and punish tyranny are called *yìbīng* [‘righteous armies’], and armies of righteousness bring victory. When enemies attack us and we rise in response, this is called *yìngbīng* [‘retaliatory armies’], and armies that retaliate prevail. When one fights over petty grievances, unable to tolerate anger, this is called *fènbīng* [‘wrathful armies’], and armies born of wrath are defeated. When one seeks to seize another’s land or treasures, this is called *tān bīng* [‘greedy armies’], and greedy armies are destroyed. When one relies on the state’s power and the people’s numbers, desiring to display one’s might over enemies, this is called *jiāo bīng* [‘proud armies’], and proud armies perish. These five principles are not merely human affairs; they align with the *Xuán Tóng* [‘Mysterious Unity’], taken as evidence to prove their truth, and when unavoidable, they are the truth—yet one must not force them.’ The above aligns with the *Wénzǐ’s* [*Chapter on the Martial Dao*] section.*

The *Yan Yi* elaborates: *"When Bái Gōng of Chu rebelled, his forces were subdued by the *jiā* [a penal measure], and Yí Liáo of Shì Nán [Market South] played with his balls, resolving the feud between the two families. Sūn Shū’áo [Chancellor of Chu] lay down with a feather fan, and the people of Yǐng [Chu capital] abandoned their rebellion. This is quoted from the *Xuánwúguǐ* [‘Spiritless Xuán’] chapter of the *Zhuangzi*. Though these matters have been detailed elsewhere, I quote them here to illustrate how Yí Liáo of Shì Nán and Sūn Shū’áo both employed the un-Way of the Way and the unstated eloquence of the unstated to resolve the feuds of the two families. This may be called ‘victory without battle, equanimity as the highest principle.’ To relish in victory yet not take pride in it, while others praise it, is to delight in killing. Those who delight in killing cannot achieve their ambitions in the world. This is quoted from the *Liáng Huì Wáng* [‘Dialogues with King Liang of Huì’] chapter of the *Mencius*. Mencius saw King Xiāng of Liáng, the son of King Huì, named Hé. Upon leaving, he remarked: ‘Looking at him, he did not seem like a ruler; approaching him, I saw no one to fear. Suddenly, he asked: “How shall the world be settled?” I replied: “It is settled by unity.” Who can achieve unity? The king asked again, and I answered: “Those who do not delight in killing can achieve unity.” *Shì* means ‘to love’ or ‘to be fond of.’ Yet among those who shepherd the world, there are none who do not delight in killing. If there were someone who did not delight in killing, then the people of the world would all crane their necks, eagerly awaiting him. Truly so, the people would flock to him like water rushing downward, unstoppable.’ This is quoted from the *Lièzǐ* [‘Liezi’], where Zhào Xiāngzǐ dispatched his newly appointed officer Xīnzhì Mùzǐ to attack the Zhái [a tribe], and he emerged victorious. This is also quoted from the *Shuō Fú* [‘Discussing the Seal’] chapter of the *Zhuangzi*. Though these matters have been detailed elsewhere, I quote them here to illustrate how those who do not delight in killing can achieve unity."*

「When it comes to auspicious matters, precedence is given to the left; for inauspicious matters, to the right. The *Piān Jiāngjūn* [“Associate General”] is placed on the left, and the *Shàng Jiāngjūn* [“Commanding General”] on the right—this is explained through the *Sāng Lǐ* [“Funeral Rites”]. Quoted from: *‘Precedence on the left, precedence on the right’*—this is from the *Tán Qióng* [“The Dried Bamboo”] chapter of the *Lǐjì* [“Book of Rites”]. Its meaning has already been detailed elsewhere; no need to repeat it here.

Now, if one were to take this as an analogy: suppose a valiant warrior achieves victory and earns distinction, rising to the rank of *Shàng Jiāngjūn* [“Commanding General”] and dwelling on the right to preside over killing—then it is said that victory is treated with the rites of mourning, and he is not worthy of elevation.

**Chapter Thirty-Two: The Dao/Way is Ever Nameless**
The Dao/Way is ever nameless.
*Pǔ* [“uncarved wood”/“primordial simplicity”], though humble, yet the world dares not subjugate it.
If lords and kings could uphold it, all things would naturally submit in reverence.

**Elaborating the Meaning:**
The talents of the world include those who can command the eyes through clarity, the ears through sharpness, the strength through valor, and the speech through eloquence. These are all named qualities, which the Dao/Way of nameless simplicity does not employ. Quoted from the *Zhòng Nǐ* [“Confucius”] chapter of the *Lièzǐ* [“Liezi”]:
The marshes of *Zhèng* [“Zheng”] were abundant with sages, and the *East Rì* [“East Village”] was rich with talents.
Among the marsh’s retainers was a man called *Bó Fēngzǐ* [“Master Bo Feng”], who, while passing through the East Village, encountered *Dèng Xī* [“Deng Xi”].
Deng Xi glanced at his followers and laughed: *“You dance and prance to deceive that coming one, Master Bo Feng! What do you think of him?”*
His followers replied: *“We wish to know his reputation.”*
Deng Xi was a renowned debater and sage of Zheng.
He asked Bo Fengzǐ: *“Do you understand the principle of nurturing?”*
*“He who loves and nurtures others yet cannot nurture himself is but a dog or pig.”*
*“To nurture things while they in turn serve you—that is the power of man.”*
*“If you command your followers to eat until full, wear until at ease, and manage state affairs, then you are fulfilling the role of a governing official. Yet they gather in age groups, and you provide them with nothing more than prison cells, stables, and kitchens—what difference is there between you and a dog or pig?”*
Bo Fengzǐ did not respond.
Then Bo Fengzǐ’s followers, one by one, stepped forward and said: *“Master, you have not heard of the abundance of skilled craftsmen in Qi and Lu? There are those who excel in earthwork and carpentry, those who excel in metalwork and leathercraft, those who excel in music and song, those who excel in arithmetic and record-keeping, those who excel in military strategy, and those who excel in temple rites—all talents are present. Yet none hold official rank, nor can they be properly employed. Those who place them in office lack knowledge, and those who employ them lack ability. Yet it is knowledge and ability that are needed to employ them. The officials are merely those we appoint; why do you boast?”*
Deng Xi had no reply, and he fixed his gaze upon his followers before departing.
This illustrates how those with knowledge, talent, and ability are employed by those without—how can they be held in esteem?

Thus, those who embrace the Dao/Way of nameless simplicity do not flaunt their reputation or display their talents. They remain obscure and silent, wandering freely through the world, and none dare employ them. When ordinary men see such individuals, they do not need words to explain—merely by observing them, the Dao/Way is preserved. All things naturally submit and follow their transformation.
Hence, the Classic states:
*The Dao/Way is ever nameless.
Uncarved wood, though humble, yet the world dares not subjugate it.
If lords and kings could uphold it, all things would naturally submit in reverence.*

**Elaborating the Meaning:**
Even dust and chaff can mold the *Yáo Shùn* [“Emperors Yao and Shun”].
Quoted from the *Xiāoyáo Yóu* [“Free and Easy Wandering”] chapter of the *Zhuangzi*:
*“When the great flood submerged the heavens, they did not drown. In the great drought, metal and stone melted, mountains withered, yet they did not feel the heat.”*
It is their dust and chaff that could shape Yao and Shun—who would dare to treat such matters as their own?

**Annotation:**
Yao and Shun are merely names of worldly affairs.
Those who act for the sake of names are not true to the Dao/Way.
Thus, Yao and Shun are not merely Yao and Shun—they must embody the reality of the *Shénrén* [“Divine/Sage Person”].
Now, what we call Yao and Shun are but labels for their dust and chaff.

**End of the Eighth Scroll of *Mìo* [“Marvelous”] from *Yùjué Lì* [“Elaborations on the *Dàodé Zhēnjīng* [‘*Dao/Way and Virtue/Power Classic*’]**
**End of the Ninth Scroll of *Yùjué Lì* [“Elaborations on the *Dàodé Zhēnjīng* [‘*Dao/Way and Virtue/Power Classic*’]"]**

「The *Five-Peak* [Wǔfēng] Collection of Master Rùshòu Guózhèng

**Chapter Thirty-Three: *Knowing Others is Wisdom***

Knowing others is wisdom; knowing oneself is enlightenment.

**Elaborations on the *Dàodé Zhēnjīng* [‘*Dao/Way and Virtue/Power Classic*’] say:**
If one distinguishes black and white.

Quoted from the *Zhuāngzi* [“Master Zhuang”]—**Outer Chapters, *Tiānyùn* [“Heaven’s Destiny”] Scroll**—
Zhuāngzi said: “The wild goose [huà, also *hè*], like the crane, does not need to bathe daily to become white, nor does the crow need to be struck with pitch to become black. The simplicity of black and white is not what makes them distinct. This is because black and white are naturally so.”

**Elaborations on the *Dàodé Zhēnjīng* [‘*Dao/Way and Virtue/Power Classic*’] say:**
Wisdom lies in discerning the vice and virtue of others.

Quoted from *Mèngzǐ* [“Master Meng”]:
“What resides in people is nothing finer than their pupils. The pupils cannot conceal their evil. If the heart is upright, the pupils will be bright. If the heart is not upright, the pupils will be dull. Listen to their words and observe their pupils, and how can one deceive you? How can one hide from you? *Yù* [“hide”]. This means that a person’s vice and virtue are revealed in their pupils. When people interact with things, their spirit manifests in their eyes. Thus, if the heart is upright, the spirit will gather and the eyes will shine. If the heart is not upright, the spirit will scatter and the eyes will grow dim. By observing this, one cannot fail to see a person’s vice and virtue, good and evil. Hence it is said: *Knowing others is wisdom; knowing oneself is enlightenment.*”

**Elaborations on the *Dàodé Zhēnjīng* [‘*Dao/Way and Virtue/Power Classic*’] say:**
Yet they cannot see their own eyelashes.

Quoted from the *Zhuāngzi*—**Scroll of *Gēngsāng Chǔ* [“Elder Geng Sang”]**:
Lǎozi [Master Lao] said: “I once saw you as though you were my eyebrows and eyelashes, my hair and fur.” *Zhījiān* [“between”].

**Elaborations on the *Dàodé Zhēnjīng* [‘*Dao/Way and Virtue/Power Classic*’] say:**
Then again, one must know oneself.

Quoted from the *Zhōuyǐ Xìcí* [“Appendices of the *Book of Changes*”]:
“Then again, one must know oneself”—meaning that if one can repeatedly reflect upon oneself, one will know one’s own gains and losses.

The one who overcomes others has strength; the one who overcomes themselves is powerful.

**Elaborations on the *Dàodé Zhēnjīng* [‘*Dao/Way and Virtue/Power Classic*’] say:**
The way of constant victory.

Quoted from the *Lièzǐ* [“Master Lie”]—**Scroll of *Huángdì* [“The Yellow Emperor”]**:
“There is a way of constant victory in the world, and there is a way that is not constant. The way of constant victory is called *softness* [rǒu]; the way that is not constant is called *strength* [qiáng]. These two terms may also be read as *ease* [yì]. Yet people do not know this. Hence, the teachings of antiquity say: ‘The strong first do not act like the strong; the soft first act like the soft.’ Those who first do not act like the strong, when they reach the point of acting like the strong, are in danger.”

**Elaborations on the *Dàodé Zhēnjīng* [‘*Dao/Way and Virtue/Power Classic*’] say:**
Yì [Yi the Archer] excelled in archery, and Jīu [Jiutang] excelled in rowing boats, both relying on strength to overcome others. Yet neither attained their own death.

Quoted from the *Lúnyǔ* [“Analects”]—**Scroll of *Xiànwèn* [“Conduct and Military Affairs”], Chapter Fourteen**:
Nángōng Shì [Nan Gongshi] asked Confucius: “If it were Nan Róng [Nan Rong], who would Yi the Archer and Jiutang overcome in boat rowing, both would not attain their natural end. Yi was the ruler of the state of *Yǒuyú Shì* [‘State of Youyu’], the most skilled archer of his time. He usurped the throne of *Tàikàng* [‘Great Kang’] of the Xia dynasty and established himself, but his minister *Hándí* [Han Ni] killed him and took his place. The *Chūnqiū Zhuàn* [“Transmissions of the *Spring and Autumn Annals*”] states that Jiutang was the son of *Jiāo Tìng* [‘Jiao Ting’], whose strength allowed him to row a boat across land. Later, he was killed by *Xiàhòu Shàokāng* [‘Xiahou Shaokang’].”
