Yì and Jiutang were both powerful and violent figures of old, yet neither attained their natural end. Both relied on their strength not only to overcome others but also to fail in overcoming themselves—this is what Lǎozi [Master Lao] warned people against.

**Elaborations on the *Dàodé Zhēnjīng* [‘*Dao/Way and Virtue/Power Classic*’] say:**
“To sit on gold and leather [*rèn jīn gé*], dying without regret”—this is the strength of the North.

Quoted from *Zhōngyōng* [“The Doctrine of the Mean”], Chapter Ten:
“To sit on gold and leather, dying without regret”—this is the strength of the North. *Rèn* [“sitting on”], meaning a mat or a bedroll. *Jīn gé* [“gold and leather”] refers to weapons: *gold* [jīn] to spears and *leather* [gé] to armor. The North’s climate is harsh and resolute, so to conquer others with daring strength is considered *strength*—the deed of the strong.

**Elaborations on the *Dàodé Zhēnjīng* [‘*Dao/Way and Virtue/Power Classic*’] say:**
The Master [Confucius] spoke of “harmony without dissipation, steadfastness without leaning”—this is the power of *rectification* [jiāo].

Quoted from *Zhōngyōng* [“The Doctrine of the Mean”], Chapter Ten:
“The gentleman is harmonious without dissipation, powerful in his steadfastness!” *Jiāo* [“powerful”] is described in the *Shījīng* [“Book of Odes”] as: *“Jiāo jiāo wǔ fū”* [“Resolute, resolute, the martial man”]. *Lì* [“lean”] means partiality or bias. This is what is called the impossible feat of the *middle path* [zhōngyōng]. It is not because they cannot overcome themselves; rather, it is because their personal desires cannot choose and hold to virtue and righteousness. Where does the Master’s power lie? In this, the Master instructed Zǐlú [Zilu], suppressing his impulsive vigor and advancing him with the courage of virtue and righteousness.

The contented attain wealth; the resolute attain purpose.

**Elaborations on the *Dàodé Zhēnjīng* [‘*Dao/Way and Virtue/Power Classic*’] say:**
Zhuāngzi said: “Zēngzǐ [Zengzi] dwelt in Wèi [Wei], wearing a patched hemp cloak with no lining.”

Quoted from the *Zhuāngzi*—**Scroll

「Not losing one’s place brings lasting stability; dying yet not perishing brings enduring life.

**Elaborations on the *Dàodé Zhēnjīng* [‘*Dao/Way and Virtue/Power Classic*’] say:**
It is the abiding constancy. The *Xiàng* [“image”/symbol] states: *“The noble man stands firm without altering his Dao/Way, thus not losing his place.”*
Quoted from the *Zhōuyǐ Dáguà* [“*Yì* [‘*Changes*]—**Hexagram of Constancy**] are the words of many: *“Constancy (héng) is enduring duration (jiǔ). The strong ascends while the yielding descends. The strong is revered; the yielding is humbled—thus one attains their yielding.”*
The *Xiàng* [“image”/symbol] says: *“Thunder and wind in constancy—thus the noble man stands firm without altering his Dao/Way.”*
The noble man establishes himself, attaining the Dao/Way of enduring duration, unchanging in his course. His course is like the Dao/Way itself.

**Elaborations on the *Dàodé Zhēnjīng* [‘*Dao/Way and Virtue/Power Classic*’] say:**
Lǐzǐ [Liezi]’s refusal to transform. Quoted from the *Lìzǐ* [“*Liezi*”]—**Scroll of Heaven and Destiny** are the words of his disciples. He said: *“There is birth yet no birth, there is transformation yet no transformation. Not born by things, not transformed by things—thus he can be born among all beings, transformed by all transformations.”*
He who does not birth can birth all things; he who does not transform can transform all things. The one who births cannot avoid birth; the one who transforms cannot avoid transformation.

**Elaborations on the *Dàodé Zhēnjīng* [‘*Dao/Way and Virtue/Power Classic*’] say:**
Zhuāngzi’s [Zhuangzi’s] immortality. Quoted from the *Zhuāngzi* [“*Zhuangzi*”]—**Scroll of the Great Master** says:
*“Only when external birth is achieved can one then attain morning clarity. With morning clarity, one can then perceive the singular. Perceiving the singular, one can then transcend past and present. Transcending past and present, one can then achieve neither death nor birth.”*
He who can harmonize with the *rén* [“human”] yet not exalt the morning, who forgets the far and near of time—this is the one who perceives the singular.

**Elaborations on the *Dàodé Zhēnjīng* [‘*Dao/Way and Virtue/Power Classic*’] say:**
The *Shì* [“Buddha”/“*Religion of the Thusness*”]’s imperishability. Quoted from the *Zhuándēng Lù* [*“Record of the Transmission of the Lamp”*]:
Huìnéng [Huineng] the Great Master said to the inner attendant Xuē Jiǎn [Xue Jian]: *“When I speak of neither birth nor annihilation, it is because originally there was no birth, and now there is no annihilation. This is the clear understanding of attaining eternal life.”*
This means: when a person dwells in the world between heaven and earth, if they rest in their innate nature, entrust themselves to the harmony of the Dao/Way, and come and go with the transformations of nature—then the noble man observes the actions of a person, and knows their longevity or brevity. How much more so for those who cultivate the Great Dao/Way and nourish the primordial *hé* [“harmony”], yet cannot attain eternal life?

**Elaborations on the *Dàodé Zhēnjīng* [‘*Dao/Way and Virtue/Power Classic*’] say:**
*“The body is like cicada shell or snake skin—it is merely shed and abandoned.”*
Quoted from the *Zhuāngzi* [“*Zhuangzi*”]—**Scroll of Equalizing All Things** are the words:
Wàngliǎng [Wangliang] asked the *Yǐng* [“shadow”]. The shadow said: *“I await the snake’s shedding, its skin clinging beneath its belly, unable to move.”*
Is it like cicada wings? If one awaits the snake’s shedding like cicada wings, then there is no particular purpose to cling to—thus one seeks endless blame, ultimately reaching ‘no awaiting’ and not uniquely transforming in the *Xuānmíng* [“Mysterious Darkness”].

---
**Chapter Thirty-Four: The Great Dao/Way is Boundless**
The Great Dao/Way is boundless, yet it can be found on either side.

**Elaborations on the *Dàodé Zhēnjīng* [‘*Dao/Way and Virtue/Power Classic*’] say:**
The Dao/Way is supremely high and has no above; supremely deep and has no below. Above it, there is no above; below it, there is no below. It can be high and deep, can ascend and descend. Level as a carpenter’s rule, straight as a plumb line—yet not a rule to level, thus all is even; not a plumb line to straighten, thus all is upright.
Round as a compass, square as a set-square—yet not round to make round, thus without roundness; not square to make square, thus without squareness. It envelops heaven and earth yet has no outer or inner; its greatness has no limits, its subtlety no depths.
Piercing like an unfurling cover, yet nothing obstructs it. Great roundness without boundary, great passage without hindrance.
Thus, those who embody the Dao/Way do not grow angry nor rejoice. Their sitting is without care, their sleep without dreams—thus they are in perfect *Xuāntóng* [“Mysterious Harmony”].
As proof: the Dao/Way is vast and boundless, yet it can be found on either side.

**Elaborations on the *Dàodé Zhēnjīng* [‘*Dao/Way and Virtue/Power Classic*’] say:**
The Great Dao/Way is nowhere not present—whether left or right, all encounter its source.
Quoted from the *Mèngzǐ* [“*Mencius*”]—**Scroll of Leaving and Liao** [“*Leaving and Liao*”] says:
*“The noble man deeply cultivates with the Dao/Way, seeking to attain it himself. Attaining it himself, he rests in it securely. Resting in it securely, his resources grow profound. His resources profound, he can take from it on either side and always find its source.”*
Thus, the noble man seeks to attain it himself. This is the one who embodies the Dao/Way, turning inward to find it within.

---
**Chapter Thirty-Five: Grasping the Great Symbol**
Grasping the Great Symbol, all under heaven come forth.»

**Chapter Thirty-Five: Grasping the Great Symbol (Continued)**

*"Going forth without harm, peace and ease prevail."*

**Elaborations on the *Dàodé Zhēnjīng*:**
Shun’s conduct was pure and benevolent—his people rejoiced in him.
Three times he moved the capital to Chengdu, until at Deng’s ruins, ten thousand families came to him.
Quoted from the *Zhuāngzǐ* [“*Chuang Tzu*”]—**Section on Xu Wu Gui** [“*The Spirit of the Dead*”]—its meaning is already complete; no need to repeat it.

**Elaborations on the *Dàodé Zhēnjīng*:**
The Great King resided in Fēng, but the Di people invaded, so he settled beneath Qí Mountain, and those who followed him came as if to market.
Quoted from the *Mèngzǐ* [“*Mencius*”]—**Scroll of the Dialogues with King Liang Hui of Qi, Lower**—its meaning is already detailed; no need to repeat it.

**Elaborations on the *Dàodé Zhēnjīng*:**
When the people of Teng asked: *“The people of Qi are about to fortify Xué—how can this be prevented?”*
Mencius’ reply to the Lord of Teng is already thoroughly explained; no need to repeat it.

**Elaborations on the *Dàodé Zhēnjīng*:**
When the realm is secure and the people prosperous, all rejoice in great peace.
Quoted from the *Língbǎo Wúliàngdù Rén Jīng* [“*Scripture of the Soul’s Infinite Liberation*”], Upper Scroll:
*“Equal in compassion, differing in form yet united in kinship—when the realm is secure and the people prosperous, all rejoice in great peace.”*
This speaks of the sage-ruler, to whom all people flock without harm, all joyfully finding ease and peace.

**Elaborations on the *Dàodé Zhēnjīng*:**
When balanced, there is no peril of steepness or decline.
Quoted from the *Yì Jìshí* [“*Appended Statements of the *Yì* [‘*Changes*’]*”]:
The *Yì* says: *“Without balance, there is no decline—decline is the result of imbalance.”*
Music and bait—even travelers will stop.

**Elaborations on the *Dàodé Zhēnjīng*:**
The *Zhuāngzǐ* records: North Gate Cheng questioned the Yellow Emperor, saying:
*“Your Majesty displayed the music of Xiánchí in the wilds of Dòngtíng.”*
Quoted from the *Zhuāngzǐ*—**Section on Heaven’s Design**—
North Gate Cheng, a subject of the Yellow Emperor, sought to elucidate the Dao of supreme music, thus engaging these two in dialogue.
Xiánchí, a name for music. *Zhang*, to perform. *Xián* is harmony. Dòngtíng’s wilds refer to the space between Heaven and Earth—not the lake Dòngtíng.
*Yù*, to retreat in weariness. *Zú*, to conclude. *Fù*, to repeat. *Huò*, to be confused. *Bù wù*, not to awaken to supreme music.
At first hearing, one is startled in fear.
Upon hearing it again, one gradually grasps its meaning, so fear retreats in weariness.
At last hearing, one knows supreme music—harmonizing with the Two Forces, sight and hearing vanish, the mind undivided, like one in darkness and confusion.
Gentle and even, without bias, silent and without knowing, lost in seated forgetfulness—self and objects both forgotten—this is *zuòwáng* [“seated forgetfulness”].

**Elaborations on the *Dàodé Zhēnjīng*:**
When the people of Qi suffered famine, Qián’áo prepared food by the roadside to feed the hungry.
A starving man, sleeves tucked and sandals worn, came shuffling in exchange—Qián’áo held food in his left hand and drink in his right, saying: *“Come, eat!”*
The man raised his brows and glared, saying: *“I would rather starve to death than accept food given with such contempt.”*
Quoted from the *Mèngzǐ*—**Scroll of Dialogues with Gào Zǐ, Upper**—
Mencius said: *“A single basket of rice, a single bowl of soup—if obtained, one lives; if not, one dies. If given with a sneer, even a wayfarer would refuse it. If given with a kick, even a beggar would scorn it.”*
Zhū Xī’s annotation: *Dòu*, a wooden vessel. *Hūtuò*, the sound of spitting. *Xíngdào zhī rén*, an ordinary traveler.

「**Zhū Xī’s annotations:**
*Cù*, to trample or tread upon. *Qǐrén*, the beggar. *Bùxiè*, not deeming it pure or worthy. Though the hunger is dire, yet he still abhors rudeness—there are those who would rather die than eat from such contempt. This shows that the innate sense of shame and disgust in all people is stronger than the fear of death.

This passage shares similar meaning with the refusal of food given with a sneer, hence its inclusion as evidence. It illustrates that hunger is a grave affliction for man, yet when food is offered without respect, even travelers would refuse it, let alone beg for it. This is the rejection of food due to insufficient courtesy.

**Extended meaning:**
Zhuangzi said: *“Righteousness and justice are but the guesthouses of ancient kings—one may lodge there for a night, but cannot dwell long.”*
Quoted from the *Zhuangzi*—**Chapter on Heaven’s Destiny**—
*Schūlù*, guesthouses or inns. One cannot stay long.

To employ righteousness for universal love and justice to restrain wrongdoing—what is this but a temporary shelter, like lodging in an inn? It cannot be a lasting bond. The Daoist’s transformation of people is through the Way itself, enduring and tranquil. How could it be dispersed?

The Way’s expression is subtle, without taste. To look at it, one cannot see its form; to listen to it, one cannot hear its sound; to use it, one cannot exhaust its potential.

**Extended meaning:**
Liezi said: *“The form that shapes the form is substantial, yet the shaping of forms is not always present.”*
Quoted from the *Liezi*—**Chapter on Heaven’s Bountiful Grace**—
The meaning is already complete; no need to repeat it. This speaks of the efficacy of *wuwei* (non-action) aligning with the Way, its truth indeed boundless.

**Chapter Thirty-Six: The Principle of Inversion**
When one would gather, one must first let loose.
When one would weaken, one must first let grow strong.
When one would discard, one must first let flourish.
When one would take away, one must first give.
This is called *subtle illumination* (*wēimíng*).

**Extended meaning:**
The profound mysteries of the Sage are nowhere more exquisite than in the interplay of *power* (*quán*) and *substance* (*shí*).
Quoted from the *Tang* annotation by **Du Tian Shi** (Du the Master of the Tang):
The Sage’s teachings are divided into two paths—*power* and *substance*. The superior scholar, with keen insight, grasps the true essence of *substance*. The mediocre or lesser scholar must be guided through the path of *power*.

The methods of *power* are vast and adaptable. Following the scriptures, four key principles are outlined:
1. *“When one would gather, one must first let loose”*—this restrains the mind.
2. *“When one would weaken, one must first let grow strong”*—this restrains the nature.
3. *“When one would discard, one must first let flourish”*—this restrains desire.
4. *“When one would take away, one must first give”*—this restrains greed.

If the mind is restrained in its extravagance, the Way becomes harmonious.
If the mind is unrestrained, excess arises.
By fostering excess, one invites error, then reflecting, one returns to restraint.
This is the *power* of gathering the mind.

If the nature is weak, virtue is preserved whole.
If the nature is strong, calamity ensues.
By fostering strength, one invites calamity, then reflecting, one returns to weakness.
This is the *power* of subduing the nature’s strength.

If desire is few, conduct remains pure.
If desire is many, the spirit becomes turbid.
When desire deepens and turbidity reaches its extreme, one reflects and returns to purity.
This is the *power* of discarding desire and clarifying the spirit.

If one is not greedy, frugality prevails.
If greed is extreme, ruin befalls the body.
By fostering greed, one invites ruin, then reflecting, one returns to frugality.
This is the *power* of cultivating frugality to overcome greed.

All these methods first push to the utmost the unrestrained heart, so that it becomes entangled in avarice and desire, before guiding it back to its true nature and the Way.

At first glance, this may seem contrary to the principle of *wuwei* (self-transformation through non-action), but the nature varies in acuity, and enlightenment differs in gradual or sudden attainment. Those who attain sudden enlightenment need no guidance through *power*; those who progress gradually require skillful inducement. Thus arises the distinction between *power* and *substance*.

The Way and its teachings ultimately converge in great principle, hence it is said that *power* is precious in harmonizing meaning.

The petty man scorns *power*, and the Way is thereby accused of deceit. Hence, *power* cannot be lightly revealed to those who do not understand the Way.

The *Analects* say: *“One may be able to stand with [him], but not yet to employ *power* with [him].”* This speaks of the difficulty of using *power*.

**Extended meaning:**
This is how the Sage knows that when one treads upon frost, the coming of solid ice is near.
Quoted from the *Zhouyi*—**Hexagram *Kūn* (The Receptive)**—
**Line One:** *Treading upon frost, solid ice comes.*

This refers to the subtle *yin* energy of the first line, like the beginning of cold, yet by gradually accumulating its presence, solid ice forms. Though the Way initially appears soft and yielding, its gradual accumulation leads to firmness and strength.

**Extended meaning:**
Gōu Jiàn (勾践) sought to weaken the state of Wu, yet he urged them to attack Qi.
Quoted from the *Shiji* (Records of the Grand Historian):
**Confucius in Wei**, hearing that **Tiān Cháng** (田常) of Qi sought to create chaos, told his disciples: *“I cannot bear to see my ancestral homeland suffer at the hands of an enemy.”*

Now I desire to humble my stance before **Tiān Cháng** (田常) to save **Lǚ** (鲁), yet who shall I send as an envoy?

Zǐ Gòng (子贡) volunteered to serve as the envoy. The Master (夫子) consented.

Thus, he went to Qi (齐) and persuaded **Tiān Cháng** to delay his troops. "I will seek aid from Wu (吴) to save Lǚ, but now I ask you to attack Qi (齐) while Wu's forces welcome you." **Tiān Cháng** agreed.

Zǐ Gòng then went south to persuade the **Wu King** (吴王), saying: *"In name, we save Lǚ, but in truth, we aim to constrain the powerful state of Qi. By aiding Lǚ and attacking Qi, our prestige shall extend to the Jin state (晋国). The other lords will surely follow suit and pay homage, and the hegemonic enterprise will flourish."*

The **Wu King** then dispatched Zǐ Gòng to the **Yue** (越). Zǐ Gòng said: *"Wu has persuaded our king to aid Lǚ and attack Qi, yet their true intent is to weaken Yue. The Wu King desires this but fears Yue."*

**Gōu Jiàn** (勾践) bowed his head to the ground and said: *"I, the humble one, have always been unprepared for strength, yet I dared to oppose Wu (吴). I was constrained at **Kuài Jī** (会稽), my suffering piercing to the marrow, my lips and tongue parched day and night. I only wished to follow the Wu King to death in his footsteps—that was my earnest desire. Now, if you, my great minister, would kindly inform me of the advantages and harms, I would be most grateful."*

Zǐ Gòng said: *"The Wu King is a fierce and violent man. His ministers cannot endure him, and his state is exhausted. This is the opportune moment to retaliate against him."*

The **Yue King** (越王) **Gōu Jiàn** (勾践) then sent his minister **Wén Zhǒng** (文种) to bow before the Wu King and say: *"Yue offers all three thousand warriors within its borders to serve Wu."*

The Wu King accepted the **Yue King’s** terms, pardoned **Gōu Jiàn** (勾践), and allowed him to remain. He then mobilized his own troops to attack Qi and defeated them.

Zǐ Gòng then went north to see the **Jin Lord** (晋君). *"Now that Qi is weakened, Wu and Jin shall meet at **Huáng Pó** (黄池)."* The **Yue King** (越王) seized the opportunity to attack Wu (吴). The Wu King returned and fought against Yue, but was annihilated.

The **Master** (孔子) said: *"If one could indeed disrupt Qi to save Lǚ, that was my initial wish. If they could further strengthen Jin to weaken Wu, and if Wu were to perish while Yue became hegemon—that was Zǐ Gòng’s strategy. Yet, ‘beautiful words harm sincerity; be cautious with speech.’"*

**Extended meaning:**
The **Qi King** (齐桓公) loved delicacies, and **Yì Yá** (易牙) cooked his own son to please him.
The **Yu Lord** (虞君) loved treasures, and the **Jin King** (晋献公) used a **carriage of horses** (璧马) to lure him.
The **Hu Lord** (胡王) loved music, and the **Qin King** (秦穆公) enticed him with female musicians.

Quoted from the *Huáinánzǐ*—**Section *Zhǔshù* (主术训)**—
The *Hóngliè Jiě* (鸿烈解) states: *"The Qi King loved delicacies, and Yì Yá cooked his own son to please him."* According to the *Shiji*—**Qi Family History** (齐世家)—
Initially, the **Qi King** (齐桓公) had three wives, yet none bore him children. He indulged in concubines, numbering six like his wives, with the eldest being **Wèi Jī** (卫姬), who bore **Wú Kuī** (无亏). The younger **Wèi Jī** bore **Huì Gōng** (惠公元). **Zhèng Jī** (郑姬) bore **Xiào Gōng** (孝公昭). **Gě Yíng** (葛嬴) bore **Zhāo Gōng** (昭公潘). **Mì Jī** (密姬) bore **Yì Gōng** (懿公商人). **Sòng Huá Zǐ** (宋华子), as **Jiá Qí** (贾逵) said, was the daughter of the **Sòng Huá** (宋华) clan, of the **Zǐ** (子) lineage, and bore **Gōng Zǐ Yōng** (公子雍). **Yōng** (雍) was the chief of the **Yōng** (雍) officials, who served at the **Master’s** (桓公) table. There were inner and outer **Yōng** officials, and this man was named **Chéng** (丞) for the **Yōng** (雍) office—**Yì Yá** (易牙) was he. Through the eunuch **Shù Dāo** (竖刁), he offered lavish gifts to the **Qi King**, gaining favor. The **Qi King** granted his request.

Once **Yì Yá** (易牙) gained the **Qi King’s** favor, he petitioned for the eldest **Wèi Jī** (卫姬) to establish **Wú Kuī** (无亏) as heir. **Guān Zhòng** (管仲) fell ill, and the **Qi King** asked: *"Among my ministers, who is capable of succeeding you?"* **Guān Zhòng** replied: *"No one knows his subjects better than the ruler."* The **Qi King** asked: *"What of **Yì Yá** (易牙)?"* He answered: *"He killed his own son to please you—this is not human nature, and he cannot be trusted."* The **Qi King** asked: *"What of **Kāifāng** (开方)?"* He answered: *"He abandoned his own son, the crown prince of a thousand chariots, to serve you—this is not human nature, and he is hard to approach."* **Kāifāng** (开方) was the **Qi King’s** son, who had removed the crown prince of a thousand chariots and submitted himself to his father.

The **Qi King** asked: *"What of **Gōng Zǐ Shù Dāo** (公子竖刁)?"* He answered: *"He castrated himself to serve you—this is not human nature, and he is hard to trust."*

When **Guān Zhòng** (管仲) died, the **Qi King** did not heed his warnings and ultimately relied on these three men. The three men held absolute power—this is what is meant by **Yì Yá** (易牙) cooking his own son to ingratiate himself with the **Qi King**.

The **Yu Lord** (虞君) loved treasures, and the **Jin King** (晋献公) used a **carriage of horses** (璧马) to lure him. According to the *Spring and Autumn Annals* (春秋), **Lí Gōng** (僖公) in the second year said:
**Jìn Gǒu Xī** (晋苟息) requested to use the **carriage of horses** from **Qú** (屈产) and the **jewel of Chuí Jié** (垂棘) to borrow passage through **Yu** (虞) and attack **Huò** (虢). **Xún Xī** (荀息) was **Xún Shù** (荀叔). **Qú** (屈) produced fine horses, and **Chuí Jié** (垂棘) yielded beautiful jade—thus their names. Four horses make a **carriage** (乘). The path from Jin to Huò passed through Yu, so passage was requested.

The **Jin King** said: *"These are my treasures."* **Xún Xī** replied: *"If you gain passage through Yu, it is like possessing an outer granary."* The **Jin King** said: *"**Míng Zhī Qí** (宫之奇) remains there."* **Míng Zhī Qí** (宫之奇) was a loyal minister of Yu. **Xún Xī** replied: *"**Míng Zhī Qí** (宫之奇) is a weak man who cannot give strong advice. Moreover, he is younger than you, and you favor him. Even if he advises you, you will not listen."*

The **Jin King** did not heed **Míng Zhī Qí**’s warnings. He then borrowed passage through Yu to attack Huò, and **Xún Xī** (荀息) offered rich gifts to win favor. **Míng Zhī Qí** (宫之奇) advised against it, but was not listened to. Jin’s army then destroyed **Xià Yáng** (下阳).

In the fifth year, the **Jin King** again borrowed passage through Yu to attack Huò. **Míng Zhī Qí** (宫之奇) advised him: *"Yu and Huò are neighbors. If Huò falls, Yu will surely follow. Jin cannot be provoked, nor can an enemy be trifled with. To do this once is already too much—how can it be done twice?"* The proverb says: *"Like a carriage and its axle, or lips and teeth—if one perishes, the other cannot survive."* This refers to Yu and Huò.

The **Yu King** (虞公) did not listen. In the twelfth month of winter, on the first day of the new moon (**Gěng Zǐ** 丙子), Jin destroyed Huò. The **Huò King** (虢公), **Chǒu** (丑), fled to the capital of **Jīng** (京师). Jin’s army returned and camped in Yu, then attacked and annihilated it. They captured the **Yu King** (虞公) and his ministers, including **Bó** (伯), and used them to humiliate **Qín Mù Gōng** (秦穆公)’s daughter, who was sent as a **bride** (媵).

The **Hu Lord** (胡王) loved music, and the **Qin King** (秦穆公) enticed him with female musicians. According to the *Shiji*—**Qin Family History** (秦本纪)—
In the thirty-fourth year of **Qín Mù Gōng** (秦穆公), the **Róng King** (戎王) sent **Yóu Yú** (由余) to Qin.

Yóu Yú, whose lineage was originally from **Jìn**, fled and entered the **Róng**, yet he retained his ability to speak **Jìn’s tongue**. Having heard of **Qín Mù Gōng**’s virtue, the **Róng King** thus sent Yóu Yú to observe Qin.

At this, **Qín Mù Gōng** withdrew and consulted **Nèishǐ Liào** (内史廖), saying: *"I have heard that neighboring lands possess a sage—this is a cause for concern among rival states. Now Yóu Yú is virtuous, and he is my undoing. What shall I do with him?"*

**Nèishǐ Liào** replied: *"The **Róng King** dwells among the **Qún Nì** (群匿), and has yet to hear the sounds of the **Central States** (中国). Your Highness might try sending him female musicians, to disrupt his resolve, and then request Yóu Yú’s presence, to create distance between them. Retain him without sending him back, thus breaking his expectations. The **Róng King** will surely suspect him, and if there is discord between the king and his subject, he may be captured. Moreover, the **Róng King** loves music, and will surely neglect his governance."*

**Qín Mù Gōng** said: *"Excellent."* Then, he seated Yóu Yú on a curved couch, served him food with ceremonial vessels, and interrogated him thoroughly about the terrain and military strength of the **Róng**. Only afterward did he command **Nèishǐ Liào** to send the **Róng King** eight sets of female musicians. The **Róng King** received them and delighted in them, never returning for an entire year. Thus, Qin recalled Yóu Yú. He then advised, but the **Róng King** did not heed him. **Qín Mù Gōng** repeatedly sent envoys to entice Yóu Yú, who eventually departed and submitted.

**Qín Mù Gōng** treated him with guest courtesies and inquired about the strategy for attacking the **Róng**. In the thirty-seventh year, Qin employed Yóu Yú’s counsel to assault the **Róng King**, expanding its territory by twelve states and a thousand *lǐ*, and thus became the hegemon of the **Western Róng**.

This is how **Qín Mù Gōng** used female musicians to entice the **Hu Lord** (胡王).

Hence, **Xuán Tóng** cited this to illustrate how **Shè Zhāng** (歙张) wielded the power of attraction and repulsion in this manner.

**Yǎn Yì** (衍义) states: The **Master Zhuāng** (庄子) recounts how **Xu Nuóguǐ** (徐无鬼), surnamed Xu and given the name Nuóguǐ, a **recluse** (隐士) of **Wèi**, first attended to the **Wèi King’s** (魏武侯) dogs and horses, then spared his **spirit and form** (神形), and finally proposed matters of governance. This is the wisdom of one who understands the **art of attraction and repulsion** (歙张之权).

He excerpted the text from the *Zhuāngzi*—**Xu Nuóguǐ Chapter** (徐无鬼篇):

*"Xu Nuóguǐ, surnamed Xu and given the name Nuóguǐ, was a **recluse** (隐士) of **Wèi**. Through **Yú Shāng** (女商), he saw **Wèi Wǔhóu** (魏武侯). Yú Shāng, with the surname **Nǚ** (女) and given name **Shāng**, was a favored minister of **Wèi**. **Wèi Wǔhóu** was the son of **Wèi Wénhóu** (魏文侯), the eighth-generation descendant of **Bì Wàn** (毕万)."*

**Wèi Wǔhóu** addressed him, saying: *"Master, you must be weary. You endure the toil of the mountains and forests, and thus deign to see me, your humble subject."*

**Xu Nuóguǐ** replied: *"I toil for you, yet what toil do you endure for me? If you indulge your desires and prolong your pleasures and aversions, your very essence and life will fall ill. If you suppress your desires and constrain your pleasures and aversions, your ears and eyes will suffer. I toil for you—what toil do you endure for me?"*

**Wèi Wǔhóu** remained silent, his expression aloof.

After a moment, **Xu Nuóguǐ** said: *"Let me speak of my observations of dogs. The lowest kind seeks only to fill its belly—that is the nature of a fox. The middle kind gazes at the sun, while the highest seems to have lost a part of itself. But even my observations of dogs pale beside my observations of horses. A straight horse aligns with the plumb line, a curved one with the hook, a square one with the carpenter’s square, a round one with the compass—that is the horse of a kingdom. Yet even that does not compare to the horse of the world.

The horse of the world, when it has reached its peak, is like one who fears and trembles. If it stumbles, if it seems to lose a part of itself—such a horse transcends dust, leaving no trace, its whereabouts unknown."*

**Wèi Wǔhóu** was overjoyed and laughed aloud.

As **Xu Nuóguǐ** departed, **Yú Shāng** said: *"Master, how did you so delight our lord?"*

*"Those who counsel our lord,"* replied **Xu Nuóguǐ**, *"speak to him of *shī* and *shū*, of *lǐ* and *yuè* when addressing him from afar, or of *Jīnbǎn Liùtāo* and *Jīnbǎn Liùwéi*—these are titles from the *Zhōu Shū*. They also speak of *wénwǔ hǔbàolóngquǎn*, which is called *Lǚ Wàng’s Liùwéi*. Those who serve with great merit are countless, yet our lord has never opened his mouth to them. Today, how did you so delight our lord?"*

*"I spoke only of my observations of dogs and horses,"* said **Xu Nuóguǐ**.

*"Is that all?"* asked **Yú Shāng**.

*"Have you not heard of the exiled man of Yúe?"* he said. *"After a few days away from his homeland, he rejoices to see those he knows. After a month, he rejoices to see those he once saw at home. After a year, he rejoices even at the sight of one who resembles a man. Is it not that the longer one is distant from people, the deeper one’s longing for them becomes? The man who flees into emptiness clings to the narrow paths of *lídié*, stumbling through the void—yet when he hears the sound of footsteps approaching, he rejoices. How much more so when he hears the cough of kin or loved ones at his side! For long have none spoken the words of the True Person to our lord, their voices coughing beside him. This is how one must first strike the chord before speaking of governance—then he will surely take delight. This too is the art of attraction and repulsion."*

Thus **Xuán Tóng** recorded this to illustrate the principle.

*"The soft overcomes the hard. Fish cannot be dragged from the depths, and the sharpest tools of a kingdom must not be displayed to the people."*

**Corollary:** *"Just as fish cannot be dragged from the depths—for if they were, they would be caught. I excerpt from the *Zhuāngzi*—**Gēngsāng Chú Chapter**:*

*The beasts of the wicker carriage, if they break free and climb the mountains, will not escape the nets. The fish that swallows a boat, if it is stranded and loses the water, even ants will torment it. Thus birds and beasts do not tire of heights, and fish and turtles do not tire of depths. Is this not what is meant?"*

**Corollary:** *"This is called *dúhuà*—self-so transformation."*

I excerpt from the *Zhuāngzi*—**Zài Yòu Chapter**: *"The man of singular possession—this is called the utmost nobility."*

This speaks of the man of singular possession, whom the people gladly follow, whom the masses bear aloft, whom they would make their lord—not merely the utmost nobility, but the utmost reverence.

From the *Dà Zōngshī Chapter*: *"Only when the morning is clear can one see the singular. Only when one sees the singular can one transcend past and future. To follow the ceaseless renewal of the Transforming, to move with the ever-changing and yet never be swayed by things—this is called *dúhuà*."*

**Corollary:** *"The *Yì* says: *‘Sūn proceeds by exercising influence.’* I excerpt from the *Yì Jìshī*:*

*‘Sūn proceeds by exercising influence’—this means compliance. To act in accordance with the times and suit the occasion is to exercise influence. If one does not comply with the times and adapt to change, one cannot exercise influence."*

**Corollary:** *"To fish, one must use the right bait."*

I excerpt from the *Zhuāngzi*—**Xu Nuóguǐ Chapter**: *"Thus the True Person detests the multitude, for the multitude brings discord, and discord brings harm. Therefore, he has no particular favorites, no particular estrangements. Embracing virtue and harmony, he aligns with the Dao of the world—this is the True Person."*

*"To ants, knowledge is discarded; to fish, strategy is found; to sheep, intention is abandoned.*

**Explication:** *One who does not covet mutton discards knowledge for ants. One who does not teach others through the stench of mutton abandons intention for sheep. Having cast aside *rén* and *yì* in harmony with the Ultimate Way, unharmed and forgotten in the rivers and lakes, thus to fish, strategy is found—this is why these words censure Yú Shùn’s teaching through the stench of mutton."*

**End of handwritten notes from *Yàodé Zhēnjīng Yǎnyì*, Volume Nine. Beginning of Volume Ten.**

**Five-Peak Hermit Wang Shouzheng’s Collected Works**
**Chapter Thirty-Seventh: The Dao Ever Does Without Doing**

The Dao ever does without doing, yet nothing remains undone.
*The Yanyi* explains: The Dao’s constant is non-action.
[Quoted from *Zhuangzi*’s *Tian Di* chapter.]

Thus it is said:
The ancients who nurtured the world—
without desire, the world was sufficient;
without doing, all things transformed.

Again it is said:
To do without doing is called Heaven;
to speak without speaking is called virtue.
*The Yanyi* explains: Few are the virtuous in the world, many the unvirtuous.
[Quoted from *Zhuangzi*’s *Qū Qìe* chapter—
the words of the robber Zhí answering his followers.]

From this, it is clear:
The virtuous cannot stand without the Sage’s Dao,
nor can Zhí act without it.
Few are the virtuous in the world, many the unvirtuous—
thus the Sage’s benefit to the world is little,
while his harm to it is great.

**Chapter Thirty-Eighth: The Supreme Virtue Does Not Claim Virtue**

The supreme virtue does not claim virtue,
and thus has virtue.
The lesser virtue does not lose virtue,
and thus has no virtue.

*The Yanyi* explains: *Zhīlí*’s actions—
the people use them daily yet know them not.
[Quoted:] *Zhīlí*’s actions—
his virtue is disjointed, fractured, and scattered,
not displaying its function, not revealing its name’s meaning.

From *Zhuangzi*’s *Ren Jian Shi* chapter:
*Zhīlí* the crippled—
his chin hid in his navel, his shoulders higher than his neck,
his hair tied upward, pointing to Heaven.
Five orifices above—
the five orifices, the five viscera.
His thighs served as ribs.
He sharpened needles, mended nets,
sewed clothes, and cleaned courtyards—
enough to fill his belly.
He divined with tally sticks and milfoil stalks.

**The Scattered Virtue**

Sowing seeds, enough to feed ten people.
When the ruler summoned warriors, *Zhīlí* would bare his arms among them.
When the ruler imposed great labor, *Zhīlí* would refuse work due to his chronic illness.
When the ruler gave grain to the sick, *Zhīlí* would accept three *zhōng* of six *shí*, four *dòu*—a *zhōng*—and ten bundles of firewood.
Though *Zhīlí*’s body was disjointed,
still he was able to nourish himself,
and live out his natural years—
how much more so for one whose virtue was scattered!

If one could preserve his original Dao,
thus, the people use it daily yet know it not.

*The Yì Xìyì* states:
One yin and one yang—the Dao.
To follow it is good.
To complete it is nature.
The benevolent see it and call it benevolence,
the wise see it and call it wisdom.
The people use it daily yet know it not,
thus the Dao of the noble is rare.
This is the Way of supreme virtue,
which does not claim virtue,
and thus the people use it daily yet know it not.

When the ruler performed rites and there was no response,
he would bare his arms and press forward.
*The Yanyi* explains:
Rites are to be followed—
to be practiced and enacted.
They distinguish the high and low,
and establish noble and ignoble.

*The Yì Lǚ Guà Dàxiàng* states:
Above, Heaven; below, the marshes—
the *Lǚ* hexagram.
The noble one uses it to distinguish the high and low,
and settle the people’s intentions.

*The Yanyi* explains:
Jade and silk exchanged in procession,
and dignified bearing in response.
*The Lǘ Yǔ* (from the *Yáng Huò* chapter) states:
Confucius said:
“Rites, rites—
is it jade and silk that is spoken of?
Music, music—
is it bells and drums that is spoken of?”

*The Lǐjì* (from the *Zhōngyōng* chapter) states:
Three hundred rites of decorum,
three thousand dignities of bearing—
in one word, it may be summed:
Do not fail in reverence.

Rites are rooted in reverence,
music in harmony.
That which commands awe is dignity,
that which sets the example is decorum.
Jade and silk are the implements of rites,
to express their intent.
Bells and drums are the instruments of music,
to guide its harmony.
Thus, reverence within harmony—
this is the foundation of rites and music.
Bells and drums—
this is the superficial aspect of rites and music.

Hence it is said:
Jade and silk exchanged in procession,
dignified bearing in response,
uplifted hands, bowed arms, clenched fists—
advancing and retreating with measured glances and gestures—
this is the outward form of rites.
Thus, when the superficial rites are met with no response,
it reaches the point of bared arms and pressing forward.

*The Yanyi* explains:
Uplifted hands, bowed arms, clenched fists—
this is the ritual of a subject.
*Uplifted*—interlaced hands.
*Bowed arms*—kneeling with bent knees.
*Clenched fists*—bent waist.

*The Zhuangzi* (from the *Dà Zōngshī* chapter) states:
The noble one uses rites as his wings,
to move through the world.
Since it is said that great rites align with Heaven and Earth,
and that rites are used as wings to move through the world,
how could there be such disorder as bared arms and pressing forward?

*The Yanyi* explains:
To come without returning is not rites.
To return without coming is also not rites.

*The Lǐjì* states:
This is the way rites distinguish high and low,
and separate near and distant.

*Bared arms*—to roll up sleeves and bare arms,
then press forward and tug—
this is the ritual of response and reciprocity.
When one acts thus and receives no response,
then in anger he presses forward and tugs.

*The Yanyi* explains:
A single word unanswered,
a single bow unreturned—
two states would become desolate,
then one would bare his arms and press forward.

*The Chūnqiū* (from the *Xuān Gōng* year twelve) states:
The Duke of Chu again sent envoys to seek alliance with Jin,
and the people of Jin agreed.
The Duke of Chu said:
“This is not within your knowledge.”

King Wu conquered Shang and composed a hymn:
*"To pacify the myriad states and ensure abundant harvests."*

The Way of **virtue/power** (武) restrains violence and ceases war,
preserves the great and stabilizes achievements,
settles the people and harmonizes the masses,
and enriches wealth—
this is its **virtue/power** (德).
Thus, he commands his descendants never to forget its principles.
Now, I have caused the two states to suffer desolation and violence.
Today, I wage a single battle and leave the people of Jin and Chu as desolate bones in the wild—
this is the failure to restrain violence.

*The Yanyi* further expounds:
During the Spring and Autumn period, the Duke of Chu (宣王) courted the feudal lords,
and the records of *rites* (义) were fully documented.

It is also said:
In the age of **ultimate virtue/power** (至德), one acts sincerely without knowing it as loyalty.
*The Yanyi* cites the words from the *Zhuangzi*—[*Xuan Tian Di* §] (天地篇):
In the age of **ultimate virtue/power** (至德),
one does not exalt the worthy,
nor employ the capable.
The ruler is like a tree trunk,
the people like wild deer.
One acts upright without knowing it as **rites** (义),
loves one another without knowing it as **benevolence** (仁),
acts sincerely without knowing it as loyalty,
responds appropriately without knowing it as **trustworthiness** (信),
and moves in harmony without considering it as a gift.
Thus, the true **Daoist** (大丈夫) dwells in its substance,
not in its superficiality.
He resides in its reality,
not in its embellishments.
Therefore, he abandons that and takes this.

*The Yanyi* further expounds:
This is why the scholars of Jin and Song indulge recklessly and forget restraint—
until their states collapse and they perish without knowing it.

*The Yanyi* cites:
During the era of **Sima Zhao** (司马昭), three years passed,
and **Qi Kang** (嵇康) of **Jiao Prefecture** (樵郡)
was renowned for his grand and splendid literary style,
fond of discussing the *Zhuangzi* and *Laozi*,
yet also valuing the odd and the chivalrous.
He was closely acquainted with **Ruan Ji** (阮籍) of **Chenliu** (陈留),
his brother **Ruan Xuan** (阮咸),
**Shan Tao** (山涛) of **Hejin** (河内),
**Xiang Xiu** (向秀) of **Henan** (河南),
**Wang Rong** (王戎) of **Langye** (琅琊),
and **Liu Ling** (刘伶) of **Pei** (沛).
Together, they were called the **Seven Sages of the Bamboo Grove** (竹林七贤),
all embracing emptiness,
despising rites and laws,
indulging in wine and revelry,
and neglecting worldly affairs.
**Zhong Hui** (锺会), favored by **Sima Zhao**,
heard of **Qi Kang**’s reputation and visited him.
**Qi Kang** sat cross-legged, his sleeves rolled up,
and did not rise to greet him.
Hui was about to leave.
**Qi Kang** asked:
“What have you heard that brought you here?
What have you seen that makes you leave?”
Hui replied:
“I have heard what I heard and seen what I saw.”
**Qi Kang** deeply resented him.
**Shan Tao**, serving as a **Briefing Department Officer** (吏部郎),
recommended **Qi Kang** to replace himself.
**Qi Kang** wrote to him:
“You claim I am unfit for the common world,
yet you do not denigrate the **virtue/power** (德) of the **Three Sovereigns and Five Emperors** (汤武).”
**Sima Zhao** heard this and grew angry.
**Qi Kang** was close friends with **Lü An** (吕安) of **Dongping** (东平),
so **Zhong Hui** slandered them,
saying that **Lü An** and **Qi Kang** had great reputations
yet their discussions were licentious,
and they disturbed the times and corrupted teachings,
making it appropriate to eliminate them.
Thus, **Lü An** and **Qi Kang** were executed.
This is cited as proof—
indeed, the reckless forget restraint,
and thus their states collapse and they perish without knowing it.

*The Yanyi* cites the *Southern History*—[*Song Dynasty Records* §] (宋纪):
**Xie Lingyun** (谢灵运), of **Yangxia** (阳夏) in **Chen Prefecture** (陈郡),
was the son of **Xie Ying** (谢瑛), the **Secretary of the Imperial Library** (秘书郎).
He was appointed **Prefect of Yongjia** (永嘉太守).
The prefecture had famed mountains and waters,
which **Xie Lingyun** had always loved.
Since he could not fulfill his ambitions as prefect,
he indulged in unrestrained wanderings,
visiting every county,
often staying beyond the prescribed terms.
He no longer cared for governing or hearing cases.
Wherever he went, he composed poems to express his thoughts.
After one year in Yongjia,
he feigned illness and resigned his post,
then moved to **Kuiji** (会稽),
restoring his old estates,
adjoining mountains and rivers,
and enjoying the finest secluded residences.
He consorted with the recluse **Wang Hongzhi** (王弘之),
**Zha Chun** (扎淳之), and others,
indulging in licentious pleasures,
with the intention of ending his days there.
From **Shi Ning** (始宁) in the **Southern Mountains** (南山),
he began clearing paths and cutting trees,
until he reached **Linhai** (临海), with hundreds following him.
The **Prefect of Linhai** (临海太守), **Wang Zhu** (王珠),
was alarmed and thought them bandits.

*The Yanyi* says:
The **Xie** (谢) family, since the **Jin Dynasty** (晋),
has passed down the **Dao of elegance** (雅道).
**Xie Jing** (景恒) and **Xie Jingren** (景仁)
were renowned for their **virtue/power** (德) and substance (素).
**Xie Jingmao** (景懋) and **Xie Jingxian** (景先)
were praised for their integrity (节) and elegance (美).
**Xie Fang** (方明) upheld the standards of conduct (行已之度),
and **Xie Xuanhui** (玄晖) excelled in literary brilliance (藻续之奇).
Each dominated their time,
and they can truly be called a **house of virtue** (德门).
**Xie Lingyun**’s talent and fame were unparalleled in the **Jiangzuo** (江左) region,
yet he alone grew reckless without restraint,
leading to his own downfall.
People have their own strengths,
but this is indeed the truth—
alas, what a pity.

*The Yanyi* cites *The Chapter on the One from Ancient Times* (昔之得一章, third ninth):
Those who obtained **the One** (一) in ancient times—
**Heaven** (天) obtained **the One** and was clear.
**Earth** (地) obtained **the One** and was tranquil.
**The Spirit** (神) obtained **the One** and was responsive.
**The Valley** (谷) obtained **the One** and was full.
All things obtained **the One** and thus were born.
The feudal lords and kings obtained **the One**,
and thus became the **rightful rulers of the world** (天下正).

*The Yanyi* further expounds:
It cites the original scripture:
*The Dao* generates **the One** (一). For things, **the One** is their **vital essence** (精).
**Primordial Qi** (元气) is the child of *The Dao*.
**Primordial Qi** is the **subtle and harmonious essence** (冲和之妙气).
It becomes the foundation of **Yin and Yang** (阴阳),
the mother of all things,
unchanging from ancient to modern,
endless from beginning to end.
How vast is **the One** (一)!

**The One** is **Primordial Qi** (元气).
It can manifest as **Yin** (阴) and **Yang** (阳),
yet does not belong to **Yin and Yang**,
ascending to the **Jade Capital and Golden Gates** (玉京金阙)
without becoming pure.
Dwelling in **clay tiles, thatch, and coarse mats** (瓦甓茅薜),
it does not become impure.
**The Heavenly Primordial** (乾元) relies on it for its beginning,
**The Earthly Primordial** (坤元) relies on it for its growth.
When **the Sovereign** (制御) acts with purpose,
then **the True Ruler** (真宰) emerges.

*The Yanyi* further expounds:
It refers to **the True Sovereign** (真君) as the one who governs the form,
and **the True Ruler** (真宰) as the one who governs the body.
*The Yanyi* cites the *Zhuangzi*—[*Qiwu Lun* §] (齐物论):
Without that, there is no self.
Without self, there is nothing to take.
Yet it is close at hand,
and one does not use it to act.
If there is a **True Ruler** (真宰),
yet one cannot discern its traces (朕),
then all would have ministers and servants.
Their ministers and servants would be insufficient to govern them.
They would alternately become rulers and ministers.
Yet there is a **True Sovereign** (真君) who truly exists.
Whether one finds its essence or not,
it neither benefits nor harms its true nature.

*The Yanyi* further expounds:
The five ranks of feudal lords—
**Kings** (侯王) are the **highest rank** (五等之爵).
The **King** (王) is the **Sovereign of the Four Seas** (四海之尊).
The **Marquis** (侯) is the second rank among the five.
Indeed, the five ranks were established,
with **Duke** (公), **Marquis** (侯), **Earl** (伯), **Viscount** (子), and **Baron** (男),
to assist the **King** (王者).

The **King** (王) is the **Sovereign of the Ten Thousand Chariots** (万乘之君).
**Sovereign** (君) refers to the **King** (王),
the **Son of Heaven** (天子).
The **Son of Heaven** holds the **sealed lands of a hundred thousand households** (提封百万赋),
and **sixty-four thousand wells** (六十四万井).
He issues **a hundred thousand warhorses** (戎马百万匹),
and **ten thousand chariots of war** (兵车万乘),
thus he is called the **Lord of the Ten Thousand Chariots** (万乘之主).

「Thus, the **feudal lords** (侯王) can **permanently hold the Mandate of Heaven** (永有天下), with none failing to submit (无思不服).
　　**Explication:** The phrase *"none failing to submit"* (无思不服) appears in the *"Mao Poems"* (《毛诗_), specifically in the *"Great Odes"* (□大雅)—the *"King Wen’s Resounding Virtue"* (文王有声篇), where it states:
*"At Hao, King Wu established his capital and cleared the festering sores [of the land],
offering rites from the west, from the east, from the south, from the north—
none failing to submit (无思不服)."*
　　*"None failing to submit (无思不服), the august **Sovereign** (皇王) shines forth!"*
　　It speaks of **King Wu** establishing his domain at **Hao** (镐京).
　　**Commentary:** *"From"* (自) means *"by"* or *"through"* (由), indicating that **King Wu** at **Hao** (镐京) performed the **clearing of festering sores** (辟痈) ritual, and all came from the four quarters to witness.
All were moved by **King Wu’s virtue and transformation** (德化), their hearts without fail submitting to him.

　　It further states: *"The august **Sovereign** shines forth!"* (皇王烝哉).
*"August"* (皇) means *"great"* (大), *"shines forth"* (烝) means *"to rule"* (君).
This refers to the **greatness of King Wu’s virtue and transformation** (德化大), allowing him to rule (君) throughout the ages.

　　Now, this passage is cited to prove that when the **feudal lords** attain **Oneness** (一) and align with it (正), all **ten thousand states** (万国) rejoice and submit.
Thus, it is said: *"The **feudal lords** who attain **Oneness** (侯王得一) set it as the standard for all under Heaven (以为天下正)."*

　　The consequences of failing to achieve this are dire:
If the **Way** (道) is not upheld, **Heaven** (天) will fear to remain clear (清), and may split asunder (裂).
If the **Way** is not aligned, the **Earth** (地) will fear to remain at peace (宁), and may erupt (发).
If the **Way** is not perfected, the **Spirit** (神) will fear to remain responsive (灵), and may grow dormant (歇).
If the **Way** is not sustained, the **valleys** (谷) will fear to remain full (盈), and may run dry (竭).
If the **Way** is not upheld, **all things** (万物) will fear to thrive (生), and may face extinction (灭).
If the **feudal lords** fail to attain **Oneness**, they may fear losing their exalted status (贵高), and their rule may falter (蹶).

　　**Explication:** *"The **Lingzhi** (《列子》) records that a man from **Qi State** (杞国) worried that Heaven and Earth would collapse and perish, leaving no place for his body to rest."*

　　**Annotation from *Lingzhi’s* "Heaven’s Bounties" (□天瑞篇):**
*"In **Qi State** (杞国), there was a man. **King Tang of Yin** (殷汤) granted the **descendant of Xia** (夏后) the land of **Qi**, and the **Zhou** (周) later reaffirmed this grant.
Now, it lies within **Chenliu County, Yongqiu** (陈留雍丘县), which is the territory of **Qi Prefecture** (杞州) today."*

　　*(The **Explication** already contains this record, so it is not repeated here.)*

　　Therefore, **exalted status** (贵) must be grounded in **humility** (贱), and **height** (高) must be rooted in **lowliness** (基).
Hence, the **feudal lords** (侯王) refer to themselves as *"the lone one"* (孤), *"the few"* (寡), or *"not the grain"* (不谷)—
this is their way of grounding themselves in humility (以贱为本), is it not?
Indeed, it is not so.

　　**Explication:** The phrase *"the lone one"* (孤) appears in the *"Left Transmission"* (《左传》):
*"The lone one (孤) and his few ministers (二三臣) grieve in their hearts, their plans in disarray (失图).
The **Altar of the State** (社稷) is not secure (遑), how much less could they cherish and ponder the **virtue of their Sovereign** (君德)."*

　　**Annotation:** In the *"Spring and Autumn Annals"* (《春秋》), during the **seventh year of Duke Zhao** (昭公七年), the **King of Chu** (楚子) built the **Chengfang Terrace** (成章华之台) and wished to share it with the **feudal lords** (诸侯).
To uphold this tradition, he dared not let it fall into ruin (弗敢失陨坠), and so summoned the **ancestral spirits** (诸宗桃), saying:
*"My **ancestral Sovereign**, **King Gong** (恭王), stretched his neck and gazed northward,
longing for the **Sun and Moon** (日月) to fulfill his hopes (以冀).
He passed down his **Mandate** (序) and entrusted it (相授).
To this day, there have been four **Sovereigns** (王) of Chu (四王矣)."*
These are **King Gong** (恭), **King Kang** (康), **King Jiao Ao** (郏敖), and **King Ling** (灵王).

*"Yet their blessings and grace (嘉惠) did not reach far,
only **Duke Xiang** (襄公) came to honor my funeral rites (临我丧)."*
*"The lone one (孤) and his few ministers (二三臣) grieve in their hearts, their plans in disarray (失图).
The **Altar of the State** (社稷) is not secure (遑), how much less could they cherish and ponder the **virtue of their Sovereign** (君德)."*
This refers to the **great mourning rites for King Kang** (康王大丧) being neglected, and **King Ling of Chu** (楚灵) lacking **trustworthiness** (信), thus his rule did not endure (不终).

　　**Explication:** The phrase *"the few"* (寡人) follows the precedent set by earlier **Sovereigns** (先君) who deemed themselves *"the few"* (寡人) as **virtuous** (贤).

　　**Annotation:** In the *"Spring and Autumn Annals"* (《春秋》), during the **third year of Duke Yin** (隐公三年), **Duke Xuan of Song** (宋宣公) abandoned his son **Yi** (与夷) and established his younger brother **Duke Mu** (穆公) as ruler.
When **Duke Mu** (穆公) fell ill, he summoned **Great Marshal Kong Fu** (大司马孔父) and entrusted him, saying:
*"My **ancestral Sovereign** (先君) abandoned his son **Yi** (与夷) and established **the few** (寡人) as ruler,
deeming **the few** (寡人) virtuous (为贤),
and commanding him to oversee the **Altar of the State** (社稷).
If **the few** (寡人) were to abandon **virtue** (德) and refuse to yield (不让),
and if **Yi** (与夷) were to succeed me (段后),
then when my **ancestral Sovereign** (先君) inquires of **Yi** (与夷),
what words would he use to respond?
I beg you, **Great Marshal Kong Fu** (子), to uphold this (奉之),
and oversee the **Altar of the State** (社稷).
Even if **the few** (寡人) were to die,
he would have no regrets (无悔焉)."*

　　**Explication:** The phrase *"not the grain"* (不谷) follows the precedent of **Duke Ling of Chu** (楚灵) condemning those who lack **perfected virtue** (成德).

　　**Annotation:** In the *"Spring and Autumn Annals"* (《春秋》), during the **eleventh year of Duke Cheng** (成公十一年), there was the **Orderfu Meeting** (令狐之会) between **Qin and Jin** (秦晋).
**Duke Li of Jin** (晋侯) arrived first, but **Qin’s Marquis** (秦伯) refused to cross the **River Fen** (河).
These were **Duke Li of Jin** (晋厉公) and **Qin’s Marquis Huan** (秦桓公).

*"Qin betrayed the Jin alliance (背晋盟),
and **Duke Li of Jin** sent **Lu’s Minister** (吕相) to sever ties with Qin (绝秦)."*
**Lu’s Minister** (吕相) questioned **Qin’s Marquis** (秦伯), saying:
*"The **people of Chu** (楚人) detest those who divide their **virtue** (德) into two or three (二三其德),
and they have come to inform us:
'Qin betrayed the **Orderfu Alliance** (令狐之盟),
yet now comes to seek a new alliance from us (来求盟于我).
We proclaim to **Heaven’s Emperor** (皇天上帝), **Qin’s Three Dukes** (秦三公), and **Chu’s Three Kings** (楚三王):
Though we have relations with Jin (余虽与晋出入),
we only seek our own advantage (余惟利是视).
**Not the grain** (不谷) detests those who lack **perfected virtue** (无成德),
and thus we declare this to warn against **disunity** (不一).'"*

---
**The Tenth Scroll of *Daoist Classics: Explication of the *Daodejing* by Lingzhi* (《道德真经衍义手妙卷》) ends here.
**The Eleventh Scroll of *Daoist Classics: Explication of the *Daodejing* by Lingzhi* (《道德真经衍义手钞卷》) concludes.**

The **Eleventh Scroll** of *Daoist Classics: Explication of the *Daodejing* by Lingzhi* (《道德真经衍义手钞卷》) continues:

**Chapter Forty: "The Dao/Way's Movement Through Reversal" (反者道之动)**

The **Dao/Way's Movement Through Reversal**:
The *Lingzhi Explication of the *Yijing* (易复卦)* states: *"The strong reverses; moving yet proceeding with gentleness, thus entering and exiting without strain, companions arriving without blame."*

The *Lingzhi Abridgment of the *Yijing* (易经复卦众)* records: *"The *Fuxi* hexagram: Reversal, success. The strong reverses; moving yet proceeding with gentleness, thus entering and exiting without strain, companions arriving without blame."*

Wang Bi annotates: *"Entering is reversal, exiting is the growth of the strong; hence there is no strain."* Strain is akin to illness, companions refer to yang; hence it is said, *"Thus entering and exiting without strain, companions arriving without blame."*

The *Lingzhi Explication* states: *"Motionless and still, yet responding and thus communicating throughout the world."* This is from the *Zhouyi Xici* (周易系辞).

The *Yijing* (易) is without thought, without action. Motionless and still, yet responding and thus communicating throughout the world—what can be more sincere than this? Who else could attain this?

The *Lingzhi Explication* states: *"Gazing upward at Heaven, looking downward at Earth."* This is from the *Yijing Xici* (易系辞).

Wang Bi annotates: *"Thus it can fully expound the Dao/Way of Heaven and Earth: gazing upward to observe celestial omens, looking downward to examine terrestrial geography. Hence one knows the principles of darkness and light. It begins with the original and ends with the terminal, thus understanding the sayings of death and life."*

The *Lingzhi Explication* states: *"Among the Five Phases, water reverses its flow, fully unified, and moves beneficially with the times."* This is from the *Liezi* (列子□黄帝篇).

The full meaning is already contained in the *Daodejing* (道经□内道冲章第四篇), so I shall not repeat the major points here. *"Reversing its flow, fully unified"* means that when water reverses, it aligns with the Dao/Way; when it is deep and still, it attains full unity. Laozi says: *"When the heart is good, it is deep and vast, empty and still, not mingling with things. Though the waves and currents may vary, the nature of the depths remains unchanged, and when it moves, it does so in perfect timing."* Hence it is said: *"Reversing its flow, fully unified, and moving beneficially with the times."*

**The Dao/Way's Use Through Weakness (弱者道之用)**

The *Lingzhi Explication* states: *"Pointing at me, you overcome me; stepping on me, you overcome me. Yet, as the wind travels, the boundless void can be said to be weak."* This is from the *Zhuangzi* (庄子□秋水篇).

The wind said: *"When I move your spine and ribs, there is a likeness. Yet now I rise from the Northern Sea and enter the Southern Sea without any likeness—why is this?*
*The wind replied: *"Indeed. I rise from the Northern Sea and enter the Southern Sea, yet when you point at me, I cannot bend your finger; when you step on me, I cannot bend your foot. This is the weakness of the small overcoming the small. I alone can uproot great trees and raise great houses, strike the vast expanse of the sea."*

The *Lingzhi Explication* states: *"Release it to the east, and it flows east; release it to the west, and it flows west. Thus, water entrusted to the boundless void can be said to be weak."* This is from the *Mengzi* (孟子□告子章句上).

Gaozi said: *"Human nature is like rushing water: released to the east, it flows east; released to the west, it flows west. Human nature’s lack of distinction between good and evil is like water’s lack of distinction between east and west."* Rush refers to the swirling and winding appearance of waves.

The *Lingzhi Explication* states: *"Liezi said: 'Form arises from the formless; thus how did Heaven and Earth come to be?'"* This is from the *Liezi* (列子□天瑞篇).

Liezi said: *"In ancient times, the Sage followed yin and yang to govern Heaven and Earth. The formless gives rise to form, thus how did Heaven and Earth come to be?*
*This refers to the greatest forms of Heaven and Earth. Yin and yang are not divine consciousness, yet form arises from the formless—does Heaven and Earth possess divine consciousness and innate nature? If they do not, then from where does it arise?"*
*This hypothetical question serves to illustrate that all things have their origin in the formless.*

**Chapter Forty-One: "The Superior Person Hears the Dao/Way" (上士闻道章)**

The superior person hears the Dao/Way and diligently practices it.
The middle person hears the Dao/Way and holds it uncertainly, as if it exists or does not exist.
The inferior person hears the Dao/Way and laughs at it.
If one does not laugh, it is not the Dao/Way.

The *Lingzhi Explication* states: *"Speaking to him and he does not grow weary—his response is like this."* This is from the *Lunyu* (论语□子罕第九).

The Master said: *"Speaking to him and he does not grow weary—his response is like this."* Fan Shi annotated: *"Yanzi heard the Master’s words and understood them with his mind, practicing them with his strength. In the midst of chaos or adversity, he never failed to follow them. Like all things receiving timely rain and nourishment, flourishing and growing, how could he grow weary? This is what the other disciples could not match."*

The *Lingzhi Explication* states: *"As in the *Lunyu* where one can speak to the superior and the inferior."* This is from the *Lunyu* (论语□雍也第六).

The Master said: *"Above the middle person, one can speak to him of the superior Dao/Way. Below the middle person, one cannot speak to him of the superior Dao/Way. This is because the quality of those below the middle person is such that if spoken to abruptly of things too lofty, not only can they not comprehend, but they will also presumptuously aspire to equal them, incurring the flaw of detachment from their own condition and ultimately remaining inferior."*

The *Lingzhi Explication* states: *"Like Zi Xia, who upon seeing the bustling splendor of the world rejoiced in returning, yet hearing the Master’s Dao/Way found joy in it."* This is from the *Shiji* (史记□礼书), where Zi Xia’s eminent disciple is recorded.

It is said: *"Upon seeing the bustling splendor of the world, he rejoiced in returning; yet hearing the Master’s Dao/Way, he found joy."* Both cases, his heart was conflicted and unable to decide itself, much less those below the middle person who are gradually steeped in losing the teachings and conforming to customs.

The *Lingzhi Explication* states: *"The eyes wish to see colors, the ears wish to hear sounds, the mouth wishes to discern flavors, the will and qi wish to be fulfilled."* This is from the *Zhuangzi*, in the dialogue between Daozi and Confucius.

Daozi said: *"Now I tell you: according to human desires, the eyes wish to see colors, the ears wish to hear sounds, the mouth wishes to discern flavors, the will and qi wish to be fulfilled."*

「A man’s lifespan may reach a hundred years at its best, eighty in the middle, and sixty at its worst. Yet even when excluding the days lost to sickness, emaciation, death, mourning, sorrow, and calamity, one may open their mouth in laughter no more than four or five times within a month.

Heaven and Earth are boundless, yet the eight deaths are limited in time. Wielding finite tools within the infinite expanse, one’s life passes swiftly—like a galloping *qí* (骐) and *jì* (骥) racing through a narrow gap. This is akin to the *zhāngfú* (章甫) hat being deemed worthless by the people of *Yuè* (越).

From the *Zhuangzi*’s *"You’re So Uplifted"* (逍遥游) chapter: *"A man of Sòng (宋) sold his *zhāngfú* hat as merchandise."* The *zhāngfú* was the ceremonial hat of *Zōu* (邹) and *Lǔ* (鲁). The people of *Yuè* (越), however, had the custom of cutting their hair and tattooing their bodies, prizing neither hats nor headgear. Thus, it was said that the *zhāngfú* was deemed worthless by the people of *Yuè*.

This is also like *Hé Pú* (和璞) being abandoned by the state of *Chǔ* (楚).

From the text: *Hé Pú* (和璞), whose given name was *Hé* (和) and surname *Biàn* (卞), was a man of *Chǔ* (楚). He dwelled beneath the *Jīng* (荆) Mountains, which lie in *Chǔ*’s territory and yield fine jade. *Biàn Hé* (卞和) found a jade *pú* (璞) and presented it to the *Chǔ* king. The king flew into a rage and had his left foot amputated. When the *Chǔ* king died and *Wǔ* (武) the king ascended the throne, *Hé* (和) presented the jade again. *Wǔ* the king said: *"You deceived my predecessor, and now I have had your right foot amputated."* *Hé* (和) embraced the jade and wept at the *Jīng* (荆) Mountains. When *Wǔ* the king died and *Chéng* (成) the king ascended the throne, *Hé* (和) once more sought to present the jade. *Chéng* the king ordered his ministers to carve it, and indeed it proved to be fine jade. Its value was beyond estimation.

This is also like saying: *"A rigid scholar cannot be spoken to about the Dao/Way."*

From the *Zhuangzi*: *"A rigid scholar cannot be spoken to about the Dao/Way."* This passage appears in *"Qīu Shuǐ"* (秋水), where it states: *"The fish of the Northern Sea cannot be spoken to about the ocean, for it is confined by the shallows. The summer insects cannot be spoken to about ice, for they are bound to their season. A rigid scholar cannot be spoken to about the Dao/Way, for he is enslaved by doctrine."* This implies that lesser scholars do not grasp the subtleties of the Dao/Way, and that to make them laugh is insufficient to reveal its true nature.

There are sayings to this effect: *"The Way of the Sage is like a valley, the great clarity like shame, the boundless virtue like insufficiency, the established virtue like theft, the pure truth like deception."* This is also like *Confucius* saying: *"I transmit but do not innovate, I trust and love the ancient ways. In this, I modestly compare myself to my old teacher, *Péng* (彭)."* From the *"Lunyu"* (论语) chapter *"Shùér"* (述而), it states: *"The Master said: I transmit but do not innovate, I trust and love the ancient ways. In this, I modestly compare myself to my old teacher, *Péng* (彭)."* The note explains: *"Transmitting (述) means passing on what is old; innovating (作) means creating new beginnings."* Thus, only the Sage can innovate, while the virtuous may transmit. *"Modestly compare"* (窃比) is a respectful phrase, *"I"* (我) is a term of affection. *Péng* (彭) was a great minister of the *Shāng* (商) dynasty, as recorded in *"Dàzǎi Lǐ"* (大载礼). He was one who trusted in antiquity and transmitted its wisdom.

This is also like saying: *"A good merchant conceals his wares as if they were nonexistent, and a man of great virtue appears humble in his bearing."*

From the *"Shǐjì"* (史记) *"Lìe Zhuàn"* (列传): During the reign of *Yōu* (幽) King of *Zhōu* (周), *Confucius* visited the *Zhōu* capital. He served the elderly *Lǎozǐ* (老子) with great reverence and asked him about rites. *Lǎozǐ* (老子) replied: *"The person you speak of, his flesh and bones, have all decayed. Only his words remain in your ears. Moreover, a gentleman rides when the times are favorable, and walks with a staff when they are not. I have heard that a good merchant conceals his wares as if they were nonexistent, and a man of great virtue appears humble in his bearing. Abandon your arrogance and excessive desires, your ostentatious demeanor and licentious thoughts—these are all of no benefit to your person. What I tell you is this: rid yourself of such traits."*

This is also like *Zhuangzi* saying: *"Action only when necessity compels."*

From the *"Zhuangzi"*’s *"Kèyì"* (刻意) chapter: *"Thus, the Sage’s birth is the unfolding of Heaven’s will, and his death is Heaven’s course. At rest, he aligns with the stillness of *yin* (阴) and harmonizes with its virtue; in motion, he surfs the waves of *yáng* (阳) and rides its flow. He does not precede things, nor does he initiate calamity. He responds only when moved, and acts only when necessity compels."* This is also like the *Hànyīn* (汉阴) old man, who *"stood alone, bearing the True as his sole responsibility, and kept apart from the world’s conventions."*

From the *"Zhuangzi"*’s *"Tiāndì"* (天地) chapter: *Zǐgōng* (子贡) traveled south to *Chǔ* (楚), then returned to *Jìn* (晋). Passing through *Hànyīn* (汉阴), he saw an old man diligently preparing a garden plot, dividing fifty *mǔ* (亩) into *qí* (畦). The old man dug a well, entered it with a bucket, and emerged to pour water, his movements clumsy yet laborious. *Zǐgōng* (子贡) said: *"There is a device here—a *gāo* (槔) lever—that can irrigate a hundred plots in a day, requiring far less effort while achieving far more."* The gardener raised his head to look at him and said: *"How so?"* *Zǐgōng* (子贡) explained: *"Carve a wooden lever, with the rear weighted and the front light. Draw water like pulling a string, and release it in measured surges—this is called a *gāo* (槔)."* The gardener flushed with anger and laughed: *"I have heard from my teacher: whoever uses a device will inevitably engage in contrivance, and whoever engages in contrivance will inevitably harbor a contriving heart. A contriving heart, once lodged in the chest, leaves no room for pristine whiteness. When pristine whiteness is incomplete, the spirit’s emergence is unstable. An unstable spirit cannot be borne by the Dao/Way. I am not unaware, but I choose it out of shame."*

*Zǐgōng* (子贡) was abashed and bowed his head, offering no further reply.

This is also like *"Harmonizing with things, blending one’s light."* From the *"Zhuangzi"*’s *"Gēngsāng Chǔ"* (庚桑楚) chapter: *"He moves without knowing where he goes, dwells without knowing what he does—this is how he follows things and rides their flow, the very essence of nurturing life."* This means that things flow as they are, and the Sage blends into their currents, his light harmonized and his traces obscured. In this, the Perfect Man has no heart, merging with the *Xuándào* (玄道, Mysterious Way).

The greatest square has no corners, the greatest vessel takes time to complete, the greatest sound is faint, the greatest form is shapeless. This is also like the *Jiǔdǐng* (九鼎) and *húliè* (瑚琏).

From the text: The *Jiǔdǐng* (九鼎) are great tripods. The *"Huángdì Nèi Zhuàn"* (黄帝内传) states: *"The Yellow Emperor explored the copper of *Shǒu* (首) Mountain and cast tripods at *Jīng* (荆) Mountain, which is today’s *Láochéng* (湖城) to the south. These were called the *Jiǔdǐng* (九鼎) of *Jīng* (荆) Mountain."*

「The *Húliè* (瑚琏) is referenced in *Lǔyǔ* (《鲁语》□公冶长篇). Zǐgòng (子贡) asked: *"How might I compare, Master?"* The Master replied: *"You are a vessel."* He asked: *"What vessel?"* The Master said: *"The *Húliè* (瑚琏)."* The text records: *"The *Jiǔdǐng* (九鼎) of the *Rǔ* (鲁) were made of millet and wheat; the *Xià* (夏) called them *Hú* (瑚), the *Shāng* (商) called them *Liè* (琏), the *Zhōu* (周) called them *Fǔguì* (簠簋). All were sacred vessels for ancestral temples, valued for their nobility."* Thus it is said: *"The greatest vessel takes time to complete."*

Expounding further: *Zhuāngzǐ* (庄子) said: *"True beauty lies in enduring duration."* Referencing *Zhuāngzǐ* (《庄子》□人间世篇), *Yègōng Zǐgāo* (叶公子高), about to be dispatched to *Qí* (齐), consulted *Zhòngní* (仲尼). Zhòngní instructed him accordingly. Hence the saying: *"Do not hasten the completion, nor urge it to fruition—excess only harms."* To hasten completion is perilous, yet true beauty lies in enduring duration; what is forced to completion cannot be altered for the worse—should one not fear this?

**Chapter 42: *Dàoshēngyī* (道生一)**
The Dao/Way gives rise to One (一), One gives rise to Two (二), Two gives rise to Three (三), Three gives rise to myriad things (万物).
Expounding further: *"In undifferentiated unity, the One *Qì* (氤氲) does not yet scatter."* This undifferentiated unity is the One (一). The One (一) is the *Tàijí* (太极). Referencing *Lièzǐ* (《列子》□天瑞篇), Zǐ Lièzǐ (子列子) said: *"In ancient times, the Sage followed the interplay of yin and yang to govern the heavens and earth. For what has form arises from what is formless—how then did the heavens and earth come into being?"* Hence it is said: *"There is the *Tàiyì* (太易), the *Tàichū* (太初), the *Tàishǐ* (太始), and the *Tàisù* (太素)."* The *Tàiyì* (太易) is the state before *Qì* (氣) manifests; the *Tàichū* (太初) is the origin of *Qì*; the *Tàishǐ* (太始) is the origin of form; the *Tàisù* (太素) is the origin of substance. When *Qì*, form, and substance are present yet not yet differentiated, it is called *Húnlún* (浑沦). *Húnlún* means that myriad things are undifferentiated yet not yet separated. It is unseen when viewed, unheard when listened to, untraceable when followed—thus it is called *Yì* (易). *Yì* has no fixed form; it transforms into One (一), One into Seven (七), Seven into Nine (九). Nine transformations (九变) signify completion (究). Then it transforms again into One (一). One (一) is the beginning of form’s transformation (形变之始). The light and pure *Qì* ascends to become the heavens, the heavy and turbid descends to become the earth, and the harmonious *Qì* coalesces as humanity. Thus it is said: *"The heavens and earth contain the essence, and myriad things are born through transformation."* Expounding further: *"The heavens and earth in their *Yīnyūn* (氤氲) give rise to myriad things through transformation."*

Referencing *Zhōuyǐ Xìcí* (《周易系辞》): *"The heavens and earth in their *Yīnyūn* (絪缊) embrace, and myriad things are refined through transformation (化醇)."* When male and female intermingle their essences, myriad things are born through transformation (化生). *Yīnyūn* (絪缊) signifies mutual attachment; it speaks of the heavens and earth without intention, naturally attaining unity. Only when the two *Qì* (二气) embrace in *Yīnyūn* (絪缊), harmoniously converging, do myriad things respond and transform into refined essences (精醇). All things bear yin and embrace yang, their harmonious *Qì* (冲气) as their mediator.

Expounding further: *Zhuāngzǐ* (《庄子》□田子方) states: *"The utmost yang blazes with radiance, the utmost yin is solemn and grave. This solemnity emanates from the heavens, this radiance arises from the earth."* The utmost yin is solemn and grave, the utmost yang blazes with radiance; this solemnity emanates from the heavens, this radiance from the earth. This solemnity is yin-*Qì* (阴气), this radiance is yang-*Qì* (阳气)—it speaks of their harmonious balance (交泰). When the two *Qì* (二) intermingle and achieve harmony, then things are born.

Expounding further: *"The Perfect Man empties his spiritual chamber, and pure white *Qì* converges."* Referencing: *"The heart is the abode of the spirit and soul."* Hence it is said: *"The spiritual chamber is an empty space, where pure white *Qì* arises."* Expounding further: *"The virtuous man does not perturb his heart, and thus his *Hàorán* (浩然) *Qì* may be nurtured."* Referencing *Mèngzǐ* (《孟子》□公孙丑章句上), *Mèngzǐ* (孟子) said: *"At forty, I do not perturb my heart."* Forty is the age when a strong and capable man’s Dao/Way is clear, his virtue/power established. *Kǒngzǐ* (孔子) also said: *"At forty, one is free from confusion"*—meaning the same as *Mèngzǐ*’s *"not perturbing the heart."*"* *Gǎnwèn* (敢问): *"Master, how do you excel?"* He replied: *"I know speech, and I excel at nurturing my *Hàorán* (浩然) *Qì*."* This was *Gōngsūn Qiú* (公孙丑) asking *Mèngzǐ* (孟子) why his *"not perturbing the heart"* differed from *Tàozǐ* (告子). *Hàorán* (浩然) *Qì* is what one gains by not perturbing the heart. *Gǎnwèn* (敢问): *"What is this *Hàorán* (浩然) *Qì*?"* He replied: *"It is difficult to speak of."* As *Qì*, it is vast and resolute; if one nourishes it uprightly without harm, it fills the space between heaven and earth. What men abhor are solitude, poverty, and lack of grain—yet kings and nobles take these as their titles. Thus, things may sometimes be diminished to gain greater benefit, or increased to greater detriment.

Expounding further: *"Solitude, poverty, and lack of grain"* are omens of misfortune. Referencing *Zuǒzhuàn* (《左传》) and *Chūnqiū* (《春秋》)—already recorded in the earlier chapter on *"Obtaining the One"* (得一), and thus not repeated here.

Expounding further: *"The Dao/Way of heaven diminishes excess and benefits humility."* Referencing *Zhōuyǐ Tiǎn Coǎ Tuǎncí* (《周易谦卦彖辞》): *"The Dao/Way of heaven diminishes excess and benefits humility; the Dao/Way of earth transforms excess into flowing humility; spirits and gods harm excess and favor humility; human nature abhors excess and loves humility. Humility is exalted and luminous, lowly yet uncrossable—this is the end of the virtuous man."*

Expounding further: *"Lowly to preserve oneself."* Referencing the *Yáo cí* (爻辞) of *Zhōuyǐ Tiǎn Coǎ* (《周易谦卦》), *Chūliù* (初六): *"Humility, humility—the virtuous man. Crossing great rivers, auspicious."* The *Xiàng cí* (象辞) says: *"Humility, humility—the virtuous man, lowly to preserve himself."* This means that the humbly virtuous man always nurtures his fortune through humility.

Expounding further: *"The gluttonous and greedy seek wealth and nobility."* Referencing: *"The *Tāotiè* (饕餮) were the unworthy sons of *Zǐyún Shì* (缙云氏)."* The gluttonous (餮) and the greedy (饕) are those who covet wealth and profit to attain riches and nobility—yet do they not know the Sage’s words: *"To be unrighteous yet rich and noble is to me like floating clouds"*?

Expounding further: *"The *Shū* (《书》) says: *"Fullness invites loss."*"* Referencing *Shàngshū* (《尚书》□大禹谟), *Yǔ* (禹) was praised: *"Only through virtue does one move heaven, reaching even the distant—excess invites loss, humility invites benefit. This is the Dao/Way of time."*

「Expounding further: *"The mighty Qin perished through its despotism."* Referencing *Guǎng Sàng Yì* (《广圣义》), it says: *"In ancient times, Qin devoured the seven kingdoms, unified the nine regions, its strength was mighty and its armies invincible. Yet in the end, it crumbled like earth and shattered like pottery, not even lasting a generation. Fú Sū (扶苏) died at the Great Wall, and Yīng (子婴) surrendered at Zhì Dào (轵道), reigning for but forty-six days before Xiàng Yǔ (项羽) arrived to behead him—its lineage extinguished, its state annihilated. From this, is it not clear that despotism brings ruin?"*

Expounding further: *"The Great Han flourished through harmony and benevolence."* Referencing: *"The Han High Ancestor, Liú Bāng (刘邦) of Pēngchéng (彭城) and Fēngpèi (丰沛), promoted the worthy and employed the capable, waged a fierce battle with Xiàng Yǔ (项羽) to seize the realm. His virtue subdued mighty Chu (楚), his benevolence extended to all living beings. Wise strategists plotted for him, and virtuous advisors turned their talents toward him, allowing him to achieve imperial success through the Three Heroes (三杰)—his conquests were sealed by their counsel. With the Three Chapters (三章), he eased the weariness of the people: In the eleventh month of the second year of his reign, the Stripe Prince (沛公) summoned all the elders and leaders of the counties, saying: 'Elders, you have suffered under Qin’s harsh laws for so long. I have made a pact with the feudal lords: whoever enters the Central Plains first shall be king. I shall be king of the Central Plains, and with you, I shall enact only Three Chapters of law. Those who kill shall die, those who wound or steal shall be punished accordingly—all other Qin laws shall be abolished. Let all officials and citizens rest in peace as before.' Thus, the people found security under his rule."*

Expounding further: *"Zǐ Lù (子路) loved valor, Yì (羿) excelled in archery, and Jié (奡) was skilled in rowing—yet none met a good end."* Referencing: *"Zǐ Lù (子路) loved valor, yet did not meet a good end."* This is from *Lúnyǔ* (《论语》□《公冶长第五》). The Master said: *"When the Dao/Way cannot be practiced, one may board a raft and float upon the sea."* Those who follow me, it is Zǐ Lù (子路) and me. Zǐ Lù heard this and rejoiced. The Master said: *"Zǐ Lù (子路), his love of valor surpasses mine, yet he lacks the means to wield it properly."* Zǐ Lù heard this and rejoiced, assuming the Master’s words were a compliment, unaware of their true intent. The Master praised his valor yet criticized his inability to measure actions with righteousness.
