Yì (羿), the skilled archer, was the ruler of the State of Yǒuqióng (有穷氏), whose strength lay in his archery. Jié (奡), the boat rower, relied on his physical prowess—able to navigate vessels on land, as previously recorded in the thirty-third chapter, and thus not repeated here. These three men all depended on their valor and strength to triumph over others, yet none met a good end—all failed to grasp the virtue of gentleness and yielding. Therefore, the ancient Master warns through this example."*

The *Zhēn Jīng* (《道德真经》) annotated handwritten notes conclude here, Volume the Eleventh.

**Volume the Twelfth of *Zhēn Jīng* Annotated Handwritten Notes**
*Collected by Master Wáng Shǒuzhèng, the Recluse of Wǔfēng Qīng’ān*

**Chapter Forty-Three: The Supreme Gentleness of the World**

The supreme gentleness in the world
Overcomes the supreme firmness in the world.

**Annotated Commentary:**
*"The flowing and surging of water."*

**Handwritten Note:**
The flowing and surging of water—from *Lìezǐ*’s *"Huángdì Pān"* (黄帝篇), where Pán Jū notes the distinctions between backwash and forward flow, already recorded in *Dàodé Jīng*, Chapter Four: *"The Way’s Pervasiveness."* No repetition here.

**Without form, it enters the formless.**
**Annotated Commentary:**
*"Piercing metal, penetrating stone."*

**Handwritten Note:**
Quoted from *Lìezǐ*’s *"Zhōu Mùwáng Pān"* (周穆王篇):
In the time of King Mù of Zhōu, a transformed being arrived from the Western Extremity. He entered fire and water, pierced metal and stone, reversed mountains and rivers, shifted cities, rode the void without falling, struck rocks without obstruction—his transformations endless, beyond measure.

**Annotated Commentary:**
*"Circulating through the boundless void, ascending to meet the heavens, descending to coil upon the earth."*

**Handwritten Note:**
Quoted from *Zhuāngzǐ*’s *"Kèyì Pān"* (刻意篇):
*"Spirit pervades the four quarters, flowing without obstruction, reaching the heavens above and coiling upon the earth below."*

Flow—this means *to traverse*.
He who nurtures his spirit thus
Can traverse the four directions,
Flow without hindrance,
Descend to coil upon the earth, thin yet vast,
Ascend to meet the lofty heavens—
In all four directions, above and below, without limit.

**Annotated Commentary:**
*"As in *Lìezǐ*, where Zhào Xiāngzǐ leads ten thousand troops."*

**Handwritten Note:**
From *Lìezǐ*’s *"Huángdì Pān"*—its meaning already detailed above; no repetition.

**Therefore I know the benefits of non-action.**
The teaching of the unsaid,
The benefit of non-action—
Few in the world can attain it.

**Annotated Commentary:**
*"The Sage observes the natural course of the Heavenly Dao."*

**Handwritten Note:**
Quoted from *Huángdì Yīnfǔ Jīng* (黄帝阴符经):
*"The gods and immortals embrace the One, unfolding the Dao"*—
Chapter One, *"Embodying the Dao"*:
*"Observe the Dao of Heaven, follow Heaven’s actions—exhausted."*

**Annotated Commentary:**
*"With composed bearing, one awakens things—thus teaching without words."*

**Handwritten Note:**
From *Zhuāngzǐ*’s *"Tiánzǐ Fāng Pān"* (田子方篇):
Tiánzǐ Fāng attended upon Wéi Wén Hóu, often praising the craftsman of Xī.
The prince asked: *"Is the craftsman of Xī your teacher?"*
Tiánzǐ Fāng replied: *"No, he is a villager of Wúzé."*
*"Yet you praise him so often?"*
*"His words on the Dao are precise, so Wúzé praises him."*
*"Then you have no teacher?"*
*"I do."*
*"Who is your teacher?"*
*"Dōngguō Shùnzǐ."*
*"Then why have you never praised him?"*
*"His nature is true—his face human, his spirit boundless.
He clings to his authenticity, pure yet receptive to things.
When things lack the Dao, he composes his bearing to awaken them."*

「From *Zhuāngzǐ*’s *"Tiánzǐ Fāng Pān"* (田子方篇):

If one’s **Dao/Way** is fully realized—his **qi** harmonized—then Wúzé’s praise is insufficient. Extending the meaning: **Gāoyáo** was mute yet served as the Grand Judge, and under his rule, no cruel punishments existed throughout the land. Thus, there must be value in **speech**—the ability to convey justice.

**Shī Guāng**, blind yet appointed as the Grand Steward, ensured **Jìn**’s governance remained uncorrupted. Thus, there must be value in **vision**—the ability to discern order.

Quoting *Wénzǐ*’s *"Spiritual Essence"* (精神篇), it is said: **"Lǎo Jūn** spoke: ‘The sun’s warmth in winter, the shade in summer—all things return to them without being summoned. They come when not invited, depart when not dismissed, vast and mysterious, their work accomplished without conscious effort. Yet men wait for the sun to illuminate their sight and for words to issue commands. In governance, such reliance is difficult indeed.

**Gāoyáo**, though mute, administered the Grand Judiciary, and no cruel punishments existed under his rule. Thus, there must be value in **speech**—the ability to convey justice.’ From *Huáinán Hóngliè*’s *"Explanations of the Master’s Arts"* (解主术训篇): ‘Though his judgment may seem obscure, the laws and disputes were handled with fairness, so there is value in **speech**—the ability to convey justice.’

**Shī Guāng**, though blind, governed with great order, ensuring **Jìn**’s realm had no chaotic rule. Thus, there must be value in **vision**—the ability to discern what is seen.’ From *Huáinán Hóngliè*’s *"Explanations of the Master’s Arts"* (解主术训篇): ‘Though he was blind, he brought great order, and **Jìn**’s realm was free from corruption—thus, there is value in **vision**—the ability to discern what is seen.’

The **Dao/Way’s** silent command, its unseen insight—this is how the **sage** serves as a teacher. The people are transformed by what they observe, not by his words alone.

Extending the meaning, this passage is cited to prove the **teaching without words**, the **benefits of non-action**—though few can attain such harmony, it is no empty claim.

Extending the meaning: Among the **Nine Schools** and **Hundred Masters**, few can truly grasp the **non-action** of the **Dao/Way**.

Quoting: The **Nine Schools**—as recorded in the *Han Records*—are nine of the ten **schools of thought** worthy of observation.

The nine families are:
1. **Confucianism** (儒家),
2. **Daoism** (道家),
3. **Yin-Yang** (阴阳家),
4. **Legalism** (法家),
5. **Logicians** (名家),
6. **Mohism** (墨家),
7. **Diplomatic Strategists** (纵横家),
8. **Miscellaneous** (杂家),
9. **Agrarianism** (农家).

The **Hundred Masters** of **military schools**—outside the **Six Classics**’s orthodox teachings—each composed their own works. From the **Zhōu** dynasty onward, they established doctrines and recorded texts, numbering over a hundred, all titled **"Zǐ"** (子).

**"Zǐ"** (子) is the general term for **scholar** or **master**. Not daring to equal the **Six Classics**, they were all called **"Zǐ"**, writing **treatises**, **records**, and **books**, sometimes named after their **clans**, sometimes after their **era-specific titles**. Thus we have **Chángzǐ**, **Zēngzǐ**, **Yànzǐ**, **Mèngzǐ**, **Guānzǐ**, **Sūn Qíngzǐ**, **Lǔ Liánzǐ**, **Lièzǐ**, **Zhuāngzǐ**, **Gēngsāngzǐ**, **Wángsūnzǐ**, **Yín Wénzǐ**, **Gōngsūn Nízǐ**, *Lǚshì Chūnqiū* (吕氏春秋), **Dèng Xīzǐ**, **Guǐgǔzǐ**, **Lù Jiā**, **Cháo Cuò**, **Jiǎ Yì**, **Huán Tán**, **Cuī Zǐ**, **Zhōushēng Liè**, **Wèi Lǎng**, **Rén Huì**, **Kāi Xuán**, **Sū Yàn**, **Fú Xuán**, **Táng Bàng**, **Qín Jīng**, **Ruǎn Wǔ**, **Shāng Jūn**, **Lù Yún**, **Biànzǐ**, **Fú Lǎng**, **Sūn Wǔ**, **Shì Shèngzhī**—these are the ones.

Some adopted **alternate titles**, such as:
**Zǐsīzǐ** (子思子), *Tàigōng Jīnkuì* (太公金匮), **Huáinánzǐ** (淮南子), *Yán Tiě Lùn* (盐铁论), *Shuōyuàn* (说苑), *Xīn Xù* (新序), *Lùnhéng* (论衡), *Qiánfú Lùn* (潜夫论), *Fēngsú Tōng* (风俗通), **Wénzǐ** (文子), **Shēn Jiàn** (申鉴), *Chángyán* (昌言), *Diǎnlùn* (典论), *Dǔlùn* (笃论), *Zhōnglùn* (中论), *Wànjī Lùn* (万机论), *Zhōngjīng* (忠经), *Yìlín* (意林), *Dàoyán Lù* (道言录), *Shíjì Jì* (岁时记), *Qíjiù Jì* (耆旧记), *Fǎxùn Wǔjiào* (法训五教), *Shūruò Diǎn yǔ* (刍荛典语), *Mòjì* (默记), *Zhèngshū* (正书), *Zhènglùn* (正论), *Wùlùn* (物论), **Hánzǐ** (韩子), *Rénwù Zhì* (人物志), *Chéngbài Zhì* (成败志), *Tōnglùn* (通论), *Zhèngbù Zhì* (正部志), *Wéitōng Yǔ* (纬通语), *Guóyǔ* (国语), *Yuè Juéshū* (越绝书), *Bàopǔzǐ* (抱朴子), *Shìyào Xīnlùn* (世要新论), *Xīyán Yōuqiú* (析言幽求), *Chánglèzǐ* (长乐子), *Jiāyǔ* (家语), *Tàixuān Jīng* (太玄经), *Fāngyán* (方言), *Fǎyán* (法言), *Zhìlín* (志林), *Sōushén Jì* (搜神记), *Bówù Zhì* (博物志), *Yìxùn* (义训), *Shānhǎi Jīng* (山海经), *Shuǐjīng* (水经), *Dàyuāng Jì* (大荒记), *Shízōu Jì* (十洲记), *Shíyí Lù* (拾遗录), *Běncǎo* (本草), *Xiàngniú Jīng* (相牛经), *Xiàngmǎ Jīng* (相马经), *Xiànghè Jīng* (相鹤经), *Zhōubì* (周髀), *Zhúpǔ* (竹谱), *Sūnzǐ Bīngfǎ* (孙子兵法), *Sīmǎ Bīngfǎ* (司马兵法), *Sūnzǐ Suànjīng* (孙子算经), *Huángshí Gōngjì* (黄石公记), *Xiàngbèi Jīng* (相贝经), and *Wànbì Shù* (万毕术)—these are the ones.

Some delve into **Daoist** profundities, others into **Confucian** rites and scriptures, some adhere to **music, calendrics, and astronomy**, others follow **Yin-Yang** symbols and **divinatory classics**, some constrain themselves by **logical methods**, others by **strategic expedience**. Each exhausts their own strengths, presenting their views in turn. Yet their doctrines vary—now strict, now lenient, now active, now passive—each confined to one corner, few able to **unify and penetrate** all things. To measure them against the **non-action** of the **Dao/Way**, few can attain its heights.

Only the **ruler who embodies the Dao/Way**, the **sovereign of supreme virtue/power**, whose mind harbors no concerns, whose spirit no thoughts—whose movements are formless, whose stillness boundless—who aligns with the **Dao/Way** and is vast in harmony, can follow in the footsteps of **primordial purity** (玄元).

**Chapter Forty-Four: "Names and Reality"**
What is closer: **names** and **reality**?
What is more abundant: **reality** and **wealth**?
What is more harmful: **gain** and **loss**?

Extending the meaning: **Reality** is the host, while **names** are the guest. **Names** are the guest of **reality**. To abandon the host for the guest is called **inversion**.

Quoting *Lièzǐ*’s *"Yáng Zhū"* (杨朱篇): **Yáng Zhū** taught in **Lǔ**, lodging at **Mèng**’s house.
**Mèng**’s family asked: *"Enough with the self—why seek after **names**?"*
He replied: *"**Names** bring **wealth**."*
*"Once wealthy, why persist?"*
*"To attain **nobility**."*
*"Once noble, why continue?"*
*"To secure **death**."*
*"Once dead, why bother?"*
*"For one’s **descendants**."*
*"What benefit do **names** bring to descendants?"*
*"**Names** are the means to burden one’s own **body**, to exhaust one’s **heart**. Those who ride upon **names** shower blessings upon their **clans**, favoring their **kinfolk** and benefiting the entire **neighborhood**. What more for descendants?"*

Extending the meaning: Like **Bào Jiāo** and **Zǐ Tuī**’s kin, they are those who **cling to names** at the expense of **reality**.」

「Excerpt: **Bào Jiāo**—as recorded in *Zhuāngzǐ*’s **"Thieves Zhí" chapter**—was a man whose conduct defied the world. He died by embracing a tree. His given name was Jiāo, of the Bào clan, a recluse of the Zhou era. His conduct was pure and unyielding: he carried burdens, gathered firewood, and gathered acorns for sustenance, neither serving the Son of Heaven nor associating with feudal lords. When **Zǐ Gòng** passed by, he said, *"I have heard that one does not tread upon lands whose governance is unjust, nor accept benefits from a disgraced lord. Yet you walk this land and consume its bounty—how can this be just?"* Bào Jiāo replied, *"I have heard that a virtuous man values advancement but despises retreat, that the worthy are easily shamed but lightly die."* Thus, he stood and embraced the tree until he withered away.

**Jiè Zǐ Tuī** was one of unparalleled loyalty. He cut his own thigh to feed **Wén Gōng**, but when Wén Gōng later betrayed him, Jiè Zǐ Tuī, enraged, fled and burned to death while embracing a tree. In his youth, **Jìn Wén Gōng** (Zhòng ěr) faced persecution from his stepmother **Lì Jī**, whose son, **Yí Jiù**, sought to supplant him. Lì Jī slandered Zhòng ěr to **Xiàn Gōng**, who believed her, forcing Zhòng ěr into exile. Starving along the road, Jiè Zǐ Tuī cut his own thigh to feed him. Later, when Zhòng ěr returned to ascend the throne as Wén Gōng, he rewarded his followers—all but Jiè Zǐ Tuī. The latter composed *The Dragon-Snake Song*, inscribed it on the camp gate, and departed in fury. Wén Gōng, repentant, pursued him to **Jiè Shān** (also called **Mián Shān**), but Jiè Zǐ Tuī refused to emerge. The king set the mountain ablaze, hoping to drive him out, but Jiè Zǐ Tuī made no attempt to escape. Instead, he embraced a tree and burned to death.

Thus, these two exemplify those who cling to **names** while burdening **reality**.

**Extended Meaning:** The wealthy toil endlessly to enrich themselves, the noble exhaust themselves in ceaseless scheming. As recorded in *Zhuāngzǐ*’s **"The Ultimate in Joy" chapter**:
*"What the world reveres are wealth, nobility, longevity, and virtue. What it delights in are bodily ease, rich flavors, fine silks, beauty, and music. What it despises are poverty, lowliness, early death, and vice. What it suffers are unease of the body, lack of delicacies, absence of fine garments, deprivation of beauty, and silence of music. If these are denied, great anxiety and fear arise. How foolish the body is! The wealthy exhaust themselves in labor, hoarding wealth they cannot fully enjoy—their bodies are thus enslaved by externals. The noble toil night and day, fretting over right and wrong—their bodies are thus estranged. Man is born with care, and the long-lived merely delay death through marriage and wisdom. What suffering is this! Their bodies are thus alienated.

By this reasoning, the wealthy and noble—greedy for **names**, enslaved by **gain**—bear lifelong burdens and know no moment of true joy. This serves as proof for the argument: *Which is greater, the body’s toll or the cost of wealth?*

**Extended Meaning:** The scholar’s sacrifice is for **names**; the greedy man’s for **gain**. As recorded in *Zhuāngzǐ*’s **"Overlapping Thumbs" chapter**:
*"Let us examine this: since the Three Dynasties, none under heaven have failed to trade their nature for material things. The petty man sacrifices his body for profit, the scholar for **names**, the noble for his clan, and the sage for the world. Thus, though their pursuits differ and their **names** and **reality** diverge, all harm their nature by sacrificing their bodies—one end, the same result."*

**Extended Meaning:** **Bó Yí** died beneath the **Shǒuyáng** slope. (The full account is detailed in *Zhuāngzǐ*’s **"Overlapping Thumbs" chapter**, and thus is omitted here.)

**Extended Meaning:** **Zhí Zhí** died for **gain** upon **Dōng Líng**’s heights. (As recorded in *Zhuāngzǐ*’s **"Overlapping Thumbs" chapter**): Zhí Zhí, a younger kinsman of **Liǔ Xià Huì**, bore the name Zhí, commanding nine thousand soldiers as a notorious bandit. **Dōng Líng** was the name of a mountain—also called **Tài Shān**—located within Jì Zhōu, fifteen *lǐ* from **Dōng Píng**. There, Zhí Zhí met his end.

**Extended Meaning:** **Méng Zhòng** refused the楚相’s offer, preferring instead to be a **yèwěi** (dragging-tail) tortoise. (As recorded in *Zhuāngzǐ*’s **"Autumn Water" chapter**): **Zhuāngzǐ** fished in the **Pú** River, while **Chǔ Wēi Wáng** sent two officials to invite him, saying, *"We would honor you with our entire realm."* Zhuāngzǐ, holding his fishing rod, paid them no mind, replying, *"I have heard that楚 has a sacred tortoise, dead these three thousand years. The king wraps it in silk and enshrines it in his ancestral temple. Would it not prefer life, dragging its tail in the mud, over death, honored as a relic?"* The two officials answered, *"It would prefer life, dragging its tail in the mud."* Zhuāngzǐ said, *"Then go—I too shall drag my tail in the mud."*

**Extended Meaning:** **Tiān Shī** declined the post of **Dàfū**, instead wishing to study the **Way of Xuān Yuán**. (As recorded in *Zhāng Shì Jiā Zhuán*): **Tiān Shī**, surnamed Zhāng with the given name Dào Líng and courtesy name **Fú Hàn**, was the sixth-generation descendant of **Xiù Hóu**. Naturally reticent and fond of antiquity, he mastered the classics and histories, as well as the studies of stars, constellations, and *tú wéi* (divinatory texts). He relieved the urgent needs of others without regard to kinship, his reputation spreading through court and commoner alike. When **Hàn Cháng Dì** issued an edict summoning him as **Jiànyì Dàfū**, he declined.*

**Translation:**

「Sighing, he said to his disciples: *"Human lifespan lasts but a hundred years—an instant in time! Even parents, wives, and children, though most beloved, cannot be kept forever. And if my own body is not yet perfected, how could I ever possess the noble wealth beyond it? I have heard that in the ancient age, Xuān Yuán and Qiáo Sōng attained the Dao, ascending to the heavens and wandering in the boundless. They are my teachers. I shall abandon the world, sever all ties, and study their Way. I now bid farewell to you all."*

Thereupon, his disciples wept and departed, but only **Wáng Cháng** begged to follow. The **Tiān Shī**, pitying his resolve, and after attending to his own affairs, entered **Qián Héng** and reached **Sōng Yáng**, where he taught the *Huángdì Jiǔ Dǐng Dān Jīng* to the **Shén Rén**. Later, he sought **Xīchéng Shān**, built an altar to worship the **Zhēn**, and refined the **Jiǔ Dān**.

In the ninth month on the ninth day of the **Yǒngshòu** era’s second year, the **Tàishàng** sent a **Yún Yú** (cloud carriage) to welcome him at the **Yúntái Fēng** Peak. In broad daylight, he ascended to the heavens, at the age of **one hundred and twenty-three**.

During the **Tiānbǎo** era’s seventh year, he was posthumously honored as **Tiān Shī**. In the **Mén Hé** era’s fourth year of the **Lí Zōng** reign, he was again honored as the **Sān Qīng Fújiào Dàfǎshī** (Grand Master of the Dao who upholds the Teachings). The **Sòng** court conferred upon him the titles **Sān Tiān Fǔyuán Dàfǎshī** (Grand Master of the Three Heavens who aids the Origin) and **Jìng Yìng Zhēnjūn** (Resplendent and Responsive True Lord).

**Extended Meaning:** As recorded in *Zhuāngzǐ*: **Yuán Xiàn** dwelled in a **huán dǔ** (circular, thatched) hut in **Lǔ**. It states in the *Zhuāngzǐ*’s **"□ Ràng Wáng" (Possibly "□ Let to King" or "□ Ràng Wáng Píàn" if referring to the *Ràng Wáng* chapter): **Yuán Xiàn**, a disciple of **Kǒngzǐ**, was surnamed **Yuán**, named **Sī**, and had the courtesy name **Xiàn**. **Zǐ Gòng**, another disciple of **Kǒngzǐ**, was named **Zì**, skilled in speech, and fond of luxury and splendor. The text fully preserves his account, without repetition.*

「Extended Meaning states: As recorded in *The Northern History (Běi Shǐ)*: The **Empress Dowager of the Northern Wei (Wèi Míng Tàihòu)** visited the **Glass Cave (Jiǎn Zàng)**.

Transcription from *The Northern History*: The Empress Dowager of the Northern Wei made a state visit to the Glass Cave.

---
**Handwritten Notes on the Extended Meaning of the *Dao De Jing* (Moral Teachings of the Way and Its Virtue) – Volume XII concludes. Volume XIII begins.**

**Five-Peak (Wǔfēng) Recluse Róng Shòuzhèng’s Collection: Chapter 45 – "The Perfect and the Imperfect" (Dàchéng Ruò Quē)**

*"The perfect yet appears incomplete—its use is inexhaustible.
The fullest yet seems empty—its use is unfailing."*

**Extended Meaning states:** The Sage-King **Yáo** achieved great success yet saw himself as lacking, thus becoming the most illustrious of the Five Sovereigns.

Transcription from *The Analects* (Lúnyǔ) – [Possibly "Chapter on Tàibó" (Tàibó Píàn), Eighth]:
The Master said: *"Great indeed was Yáo as a ruler! Lofty and majestic, only Heaven is greater, and Yáo followed it. Broad and boundless, the people could not name its virtue. Lofty and majestic were his achievements. Resplendent were his institutions. Yet he saw himself as lacking, as it is said in the *Zhuāngzǐ*’s **[Possibly "Free and Unfettered Roaming" (Xiāoyáo Yóu Píàn)]**:

Yáo said to **Xǔ Yóu**, offering him the Mandate of Heaven: *"The sun and moon have risen, yet the candle still burns—how difficult it is to match their brilliance! The timely rains have fallen, yet I still must irrigate—how laborious it is to rival their bounty! The Master stands, and the world is governed, yet I remain as a corpse. I see myself as lacking—let me govern the world."*

Xǔ Yóu replied: *"You govern the world, and the world is already governed. What more is there to say?"*

**Extended Meaning states:** Confucius gathered the teachings into perfection yet did not claim sainthood, thus becoming the teacher for all ages.

Transcription from *Mencius* (Mèngzǐ) – **[Possibly "Chapter on Wàn Zhāng" (Wàn Zhāng Zhāngjù Shàng)]**:
Mencius said: *"Confucius is what we call the ‘Gatherer of Perfection.’*
*‘Gathering perfection’ means harmonizing the golden tones with the jade resonances. The golden tones are the beginning of order, and the jade resonances are its completion. The beginning of order refers to the threads of wisdom, like the strands of music. This is the work of intelligence. The completion of order is the work of sainthood. That Confucius did not claim sainthood is as stated in **[Possibly "Chapter on Gōngsūn Chǒu" (Gōngsūn Chǒu Zhāngjù Shàng)]**:

Gōngsūn Chǒu asked: *"Then the Master was indeed a sage?"*
The Master replied: *"How can you say such a thing?*
*Once, Zǐ Gòng asked me: ‘Are you a sage?’*
*I said: ‘Sainthood is beyond me. I only learn without satiety and teach without weariness.’*
*Zǐ Gòng said: ‘To learn without satiety is wisdom. To teach without weariness is benevolence. With both wisdom and benevolence, are you not a sage?’*
*Yet the Master did not claim sainthood—how can you say so?"*

*"The most straight appears bent,
The most skillful seems clumsy,
The most eloquent seems tongue-tied."*

**Extended Meaning states:** **Liǔ Xià Huì** served others with uprightness, yet was dismissed three times without leaving.

Transcription from *The Analects* – **[Possibly "Chapter on Wēizǐ" (Wēizǐ Píàn), Eighteenth]:**
Liǔ Xià Huì was a virtuous high official of **Lǔ**, named **Zhǎn Qín**. As **Shìshī** (a judicial officer), he was exiled—*Shìshī* being a position overseeing legal matters. *"Out"* here means *"banished."*

「Once banished twice, someone said to him: *"You cannot yet leave."* He replied: *"If one serves others with uprightness, where would one not be banished three times? If one serves others with crookedness, why must one leave one’s parents’ domain?"*

**Extended Meaning states:** *Carving and shaping myriad forms, yet not claiming skill.*
Transcription: *The greatest skill, as Zhuangzi said, is to "cover and load the heavens and earth, carve and shape myriad forms, yet not claim skill." Would one who makes mulberry-leaf paper claim skill?*
**Extended Meaning states:** *As in Ziqing’s carving of wood into a vessel.*
*Ziqing was the Grand Carpenter of Lu. Ziqing was his name, and "Ziqing" was his official title.*
Transcription: *The text from the "Dàshēng" (On Nurturing Life) chapter of Zhuangzi is fully detailed above.*

**Extended Meaning states:** *Not adorning with frivolous tales.*
Transcription: *The "xiaoshuo" (novelistic) tradition emerged from the "baiguan" (popular records), the gossip of streets and alleys, and hearsay from the roads. These texts comprised fifteen families and one hundred thirteen chapters.*

**Extended Meaning states:** *Thus, the Master said, "I wish to speak not at all."*
Transcription: *What speech does Heaven make? The four seasons proceed, and all things flourish. What speech does Heaven make?*
From the *Yánghuò* (Yang Huo) chapter of *The Analects*:
The Master said: *"I wish to speak not at all."*
Zigong said: *"If you do not speak, how shall we, your disciples, have anything to transmit?"*
The Master said: *"What speech does Heaven make? The four seasons proceed, and all things flourish. What speech does Heaven make?"*
Agitation overcomes cold, stillness overcomes heat—*tranquility and purity are the natural order of the world.*

**Extended Meaning states:** *Wearing coarse linen in summer.*
Transcription: *In the "Xiāngdàng" (Conduct) chapter of The Analects:*
When the heat was intense, he wore a single layer of coarse linen, *zì* (fine hemp cloth) and *xì* (coarse hemp cloth). The finest hemp cloth was called *zì*, the coarser *xì*. He would first put on his inner garment, then the outer layer of *zì* and *xì*.

From *Chapter on "Tianxia You Dao"* (The World in Harmony), Forty-Sixth:
*No guilt is greater than covetousness,
No calamity is greater than insatiability,
No blame is greater than the desire to seize.*

**Extended Meaning states:** *The calamity of greed and recklessness.*

From the *Lower Appendices* of the *Yì* (Book of Changes):
*"Goodness not accumulated is insufficient to establish a name,
Evil not accumulated is insufficient to destroy one’s life.
The petty man considers minor goodness as useless and does not practice it,
And considers minor evil as harmless and does not abandon it.
Thus, evil accumulates and becomes irrepressible,
Guilt grows great and becomes unforgivable.*
The *Yì* says: *Greed and recklessness bring ruin.*

**Extended Meaning states:** *Like the case of the Qí man who snatched gold—
He who sees gold but not the man is truly afflicted.*
From the *Lìezǐ* (Liezi)—*Chapter on "Sayings of the Spirit"*:
Once, a man from Qí coveted gold.
At dawn, he dressed formally and went to the market,
Where he encountered a seller of gold,
And seized the gold before fleeing.
An official caught him and asked: *"Everyone was present—
Why did you snatch another’s gold?"*
He replied: *"When I took the gold,
I saw only the gold, not the man—
Only the gold, not the man."*
This illustrates how intense desire can cloud the mind.
Thus the ancients said:
*"He who scrutinizes the tip of an autumn hair
Does not perceive the shape of Tàishān.
He who fine-tunes the harmony of the five tones
Does not hear the thunderous roar of the heavens.*
Indeed, when the mind is fixed on what it craves,
The body forgets all else—
How can one not be cautious?"*

**Extended Meaning states:** *From the "Rú Huángōng Shìyīn" (Spring and Autumn Annals—Duchy of Lu, Year Ten) chapter of the *Chūnqiū*.*
From the *Chūnqiū*—*its meaning has already been detailed in the text, so it is not repeated here.*

**Extended Meaning states:** *Like Zhōumò’s Zhīxiāngzǐ seeking land from Hán Kāngzǐ.*
From the *Chūnqiū*—*its meaning is already fully recorded in the text, so it is not written again.*
Thus, the sufficiency of *knowing contentment* is everlasting.

**Extended Meaning states:** *Once, Hàn Yán Jūn Píng was friendly with Luó Chōng, a wealthy man from Shù Jùn.*
From the text—*its meaning is already fully recorded, so it is not repeated here.*

**Chapter on "Not Leaving the Door"** (Forty-Seventh):
*"No guilt is greater than covetousness,
No calamity is greater than insatiability,
No blame is greater than the desire to seize."*

**Chapter on "Not Leaving the Door" (Forty-Seventh):**
*"To not leave the door, yet know the world.
To not peer through the window, yet see the Dao of Heaven.*
**Extended Meaning states:** *All things are complete within me; reflect inwardly to find them.*
From the text—*its meaning is already fully recorded, so it is not repeated here.*

**Extended Meaning states:** *The Master Mencius said: All things are complete within me.*
*This speaks of the inherent nature of principles.*
From great to small—rulers and subjects, fathers and sons—
to trivial matters of daily life,
the natural order of things is none other than
what is already contained within the inherent nature of each.
To reflect inwardly and act sincerely—
no greater joy exists.*

**Huì’ān’s Annotation:** *This chapter discusses how the principles of all things*
*are fully embodied in one’s own person—*
*thus, the Dao resides within me,*
*and joy is abundant.*
If practiced with *rú* (benevolence/forbearance),
then selfishness has no place,
and *rén* (humanity) can be attained.

**Extended Meaning states:** *Yángzǐ said: Is not the human heart divine?*
From the text—*its meaning is already fully recorded in the *Yángzǐ Fǎyán* [□ *Wènshén* ("Discussing the Divine")] chapter.*
*Divinity lies in its hidden potential.*
To cultivate the heart is to manifest Heaven’s divine illumination—
the interplay of yin and yang, the divine essence of Heaven.
The movement of the sun and moon—Heaven’s clarity.
Illuminating all four directions—
Heaven’s divine and luminous radiance
can clarify the murky and penetrate the dark,
shedding light upon all.
The essence and purity of Heaven—
the *Qián* (Heaven) embodies vigor and rectitude,
its utmost refinement.
All things follow their kind,
nurturing and covering them,
each fulfilling its own nature.
Is not the human heart divine?
In Heaven and Earth, it is divine;
in humans, it is the heart.
The heart is the conduit to divinity.
Hold it, and it remains;
abandon it, and it perishes.
Only the Sage can constantly hold and preserve it.*

**Extended Meaning states:** *The timing of rain, wind, and sun aligns with virtue.*
From the text—*its meaning is already fully recorded in the *Shàngshū* [□ *Hóngfàn* ("The Grand Tract")] chapter.*
*It says: "When virtue is at rest, the rain is timely;
when virtue is grave, the wind is timely;
when virtue is sagacious, the heat is timely;
when virtue is prudent, the cold is timely;
when virtue is holy, the breezes are timely."*
This refers to reckless arrogance, usurpation, complacency, haste, and ignorance.
In Heaven, it manifests as the Five Phases (*wǔxíng*);
in humans, as the Five Affairs (*wǔshì*).
When the Five Affairs are cultivated, the auspicious signs appear,
each corresponding to its kind.
When the Five Affairs are neglected, calamitous signs arise,
each corresponding to its kind.
This is the natural order of things.

**Extended Meaning states:** *The stars and constellations shift their degrees.*
From the text—*its meaning is already fully recorded in the *Guǎngshèngyì* ("Comprehensive Sage Teachings").*
*It says: "This is the principle of the Emperor.
He follows Heaven’s cover, follows Earth’s bearing,
observes the symbols of the sun and moon,
and reverently instructs the people in the proper times.
With the *xuánpú* (suspended pivot) and *yùhéng* (jade axis),
he harmonizes the Seven Luminaries (*qīzhèng*),
thus aligning with Heaven’s Dao.
When the stars and constellations shift their degrees,
and the sun and moon lose their proper times,
one must rectify conduct, offer incense,
and recite the scriptures."*
From the *Língbǎo Dùrén Jīng* ("Scripture of the Soul’s Treasure for Saving People"):
*The stars and constellations shift their degrees,*
*the sun and moon lose their proper times,*
*and one must rectify conduct, offer incense,*
*and recite the scriptures.*

**Extended Meaning states:** *The Sage aligns in virtue with Heaven and Earth,*
*in luminosity with the sun and moon,*
*acting before Heaven and Heaven does not oppose,*
*acting after Heaven and following Heaven’s timing.*
From the *Zhōuyǐ* (I Ching)—*the "Nine-Five" commentary of the *Qián* (Heaven) Hexagram:*
*"The Great Person aligns in virtue with Heaven and Earth,
in luminosity with the sun and moon,
in sequence with the Four Seasons,
in fortune with the spirits and gods.
Acting before Heaven, Heaven does not oppose;
acting after Heaven, one follows Heaven’s timing.
*This means: when one acts before Heaven’s timing,*
*Heaven’s Dao responds in harmony and does not resist.*
*When one acts after Heaven’s timing,*
*one submits in reverence to Heaven’s Dao and dares not defy it.*
Thus, if a ruler can be so,
he need not peer through windows or gaze afar—
Heaven’s Dao becomes clear of itself.
Therefore, the Sage acts without exertion yet knows,
sees without looking yet understands,
accomplishes without doing yet succeeds.

**Extended Meaning states:** *Yáo’s virtue was as vast as Heaven,*
*his wisdom as divine as the gods.*
*Shùn’s understanding was clear in human relationships,*
*his insight profound in all things.*
*This can be called knowing the world.*
*Only Heaven is great; only Yáo followed it.*
*When violent winds and thunderous rains came,*
*Shùn did not falter.*
*This can be called seeing Heaven’s Dao.*
*What then did he do?*
*Only to be reverent and upright,*
*and to govern from the southern face.*
From the *Shǐjì*—*the "Běnjì" (Basic Records) chapter:*
*Yáo, by the posthumous name law, is called:*
*"Yáo—his virtue spread goodness and transmitted the sacred;*
*his virtue was as vast as Heaven,*
*his wisdom as divine as the gods.*
*Approaching him was like the warmth of the sun,*
*gazing upon him like the light of clouds.*
*Though wealthy, he was not proud,*
*though noble, he did not relax.*
*He ruled for seventy years,*
*and found Shùn.*
*After twenty years,*
*he grew old,*
*and commanded Shùn to assume the role of Son of Heaven,*
*recommending his governance to Heaven.*
*Yáo withdrew from the throne,*
*and after twenty-eight years,*
*he passed away.*
*After the three-year mourning,*
*Shùn ascended to the throne of Son of Heaven.*
*It also cites Shùn’s clarity in human relationships,*
*his insight in all things—*
*this is from the *Mèngzǐ*—*the "Líliú" (Leaving and Staying) chapter, upper section.*
*Shùn’s understanding was clear in all things,*
*his insight profound in human relationships.*
*He acted through virtue and righteousness,*
*not by forcing virtue and righteousness upon others.*
*Only Heaven is great—*
*this is from the *Lǔlùn* (Lu’s Discourses)—*the "Tàibó" (Tai Bo) chapter.*
*The Master said:*
*'Great indeed was Yáo as a ruler!*
*Majestic and awe-inspiring!*
*Only Heaven is great,*
*and only Yáo followed it.*
*Gentle and broad in his governance,*
*the people could not name his virtues.*
*Majestic and awe-inspiring!*
*His achievements were grand.*
*His conduct was refined.*
*When violent winds and thunderous rains came,*
*Shùn did not falter—*
*this is from the *Shùn Diǎn* (Shun’s Approbation).*
*It says:*
*'When reflecting upon antiquity,*
*the Emperor Shùn, also called Zhònghuá,*
*was in harmony with the Emperor.*
*He was profound in wisdom and clear in virtue,*
*warm in reverence and broad in benevolence,*
*his sincerity and trust were complete.*
*His mysterious virtue ascended and was heard by Heaven,*
*and thus he was entrusted with the throne.*
*He reverently observed the Five Precepts,*
*and the Five Precepts were fulfilled.*
*He received the hundred measures of governance,*
*and the hundred measures were in harmony.*
*He welcomed guests at the Four Gates,*
*and the Four Gates were orderly.*
*He ascended the great mound,*
*and even violent winds and thunderous rains did not confuse him.*
*Yáo commanded Shùn to enter the mountains, forests, rivers, and marshes,*
*where violent winds and thunderous rains arose,*
*yet Shùn walked without faltering.*
*What then did he do?*
*Only to be reverent and upright,*
*and to govern from the southern face.*
*This is from the *Lúnyǔ*—*the "Wèilínggōng" (Wei Ling Gong) chapter, fifteenth section.*
*The Master said:*
*'The one who governs without action is Shùn.*
*And what did he do?*
*Only to be reverent and upright,*
*and to govern from the southern face.*
*When eating, he thought of the hungry in the world;*
*when clothing himself, he thought of the cold in the world."*

This is from the *Mencius*—the *"Líliú Zhāngjū Shàng"* (上), where Mencius says:

*"Yǔ, Jì, and Yán Huí shared the same Dao.
Yǔ thought: 'If there are those drowning in the world, it is as if I myself were drowning.'
Jì thought: 'If there are those starving in the world, it is as if I myself were starving.'
Thus was their urgency so great.
They loved their parents and extended that love to the aged of the world,
and loved their children and extended that love to the young of the world."*

This is from the *Mencius*—the *"Liáng Huìwáng Zhāngjū Shàng"* (上), where Mencius, in reply to King Huì of Liang, said:

*"Revere my elders, and extend that reverence to the elders of others;
love my young, and extend that love to the young of others.
Thus, the world may be governed as if in the palm of one’s hand.
This is the Dao of observing oneself to understand one’s family,
and observing one’s family to understand the world,
until one observes the world to understand the world.
If such is the case, then why must one step outside the door
before knowing the world?"*

This speaks of the sage-rulers Yáo and Shùn,
who followed the effortless, natural Dao of Heaven and Earth,
transforming the world as grass bends with the wind—
lying down naturally and being transformed thereby.

Thus it is said:
*"Therefore, the sage acts without action, knows without seeing,
discerns without looking, achieves without doing—
this is how."*

The extended meaning states:
*"The *Spring and Autumn Annals* records the Duke Zhuāng of Zhèng’s attack on his younger brother, the Great Minister Duàn.
Quoted from the *Spring and Autumn Annals*.
Its meaning has already been detailed—no need for repetition."*

For Learning, Daily Gain—Chapter Forty-Eight
To learn is to daily gain; to follow the Dao is to daily lose.

Extended meaning states:
Of the kind where one hangs one’s head, pricks one’s thigh, studies by candlelight, or gathers fireflies.
Quoted:
The one who hangs his head: *The Chronicles of the Worthy Sage of Chu* says,
Na Sun Jing, styled Wén Bǎo,
always shut his door and read. When he slept, he tied a string to his head
and hung it from the rafters. Later, he became a great scholar.
The Emperor summoned him, but he did not respond.

The one who pricks his thigh: *The Records of the Grand Historian* says,
Sū Qín was a man of Luòyáng in the Eastern Zhōu.
He studied under Master Guǐgu, wandering for several years,
then returned in great distress. His brothers, sisters-in-law,
wives, and concubines all mocked him.
Qín heard this and felt ashamed, so he closed himself in his room.
He obtained *The Stratagems of Tàigōng*, lay down and read it,
and when he wanted to sleep, he pulled a thorn to prick his thigh,
so that blood flowed down to his heel. Later, he counselled Zhào,
and Zhào heeded him. Thus, he wore the seals of the Six Kingdoms’ ministers,
and roamed the world with his schemes.

The one who studies by candlelight: In the past, Sun Kāng,
being poor, struggled to learn without money to buy oil.
He often studied by the light of snow. Later, he became Grand Scribe.

The one who gathers fireflies: Sòng Chē Yìn, styled Wǔzǐ,
also suffered poverty and devoted himself to learning.
At night, he used a crimson silk bag to gather dozens of fireflies,
and read by their light. His relentless pursuit of learning
thus led him to become a great scholar as well.

Now, those who seek learning,
all begin by daily studying to grasp the Dao,
cultivating skill to mend their faults,
and end by awakening to truth and forgetting words,
discarding skill and letting go of attachment—
using perception and hearing as the gradual stages of cultivation,
and diminishing skillful actions as the gateway to enlightenment in the Dao.
These few men here,
have lifelong worries but no single day’s joy,
laboring their minds and exhausting their spirits,
drowning themselves in vast learning—
then they must inevitably reach their end.

Extended meaning states:
Only by humbling one’s ambition and striving for timely diligence
does true cultivation arise.
Quoted from *The Book of Documents* [□] *Saying of Mìng* [below]:
*"Only by humbling one’s ambition and striving for timely diligence
does true cultivation arise.
With sincere devotion to this, the Dao accumulates within oneself."*

This means:
To humble one’s ambition is as if unable to do so,
To diligently study is as if unable to keep up—
Receiving others with emptiness,
Exerting oneself with earnestness—
Then one’s cultivation will flow like a spring,
uninterrupted and abundant in its coming.

Extended meaning states:
To drown oneself in vast learning,
To use culture to extinguish one’s innate nature.
Quoted from *Zhuāngzǐ* [□] *The Chapter on Refining One’s Nature*:
*"Knowledge without depth,
Seeking to establish the world,
Then adorn it with culture,
And enrich it with vast learning.
Culture extinguishes the innate nature,
Vast learning drowns the mind.
One does not know that the innate nature is the foundation of culture.
Culture’s brilliance hides within simplicity,
The innate nature’s depth endures in the mind.
Vast learning obscures the mind’s essence—
Only by abandoning learning and letting go of culture
Can one attain the beauty of effortless action."*

Extended meaning states:
Thus, the *Yì* [□] *Benefit* [hexagram] is great.
The *Xìang* [□] says: *"Wind and thunder, Benefit.*
The gentleman, seeing good, moves toward it,
Having faults, corrects them."

Quoted:
The words of the *Benefit* [hexagram] say,
*"Wind must precede thunder,
Thunder must follow wind,
Then all things will be benefited.*
Thus it is said: *Wind and thunder, Benefit.*
To move means to migrate and emulate,
To correct means to amend and restrain.
Moving toward good and correcting faults—
No greater benefit exists."*

Extended meaning states:
The greatness of *Diminishing* [□].
The *Xìang* [□] says: *"Mountain and marsh, Diminishing.*
The gentleman uses this to restrain anger and suppress desire."

Quoted:
The words of the *Diminishing* [hexagram] say,
*"Mountain beneath, marsh above—Diminishing.*
The gentleman uses this to restrain anger and suppress desire."

Annotation:
*"The desires of man are stirred by things,
And the world’s circumstances bring joy or sorrow—
Thus, emotions have anger and craving.
To restrain means to still what has already arisen,
To suppress means to block what is yet to come.
Anger and desire both have past and future—
Restraining and suppressing are profound principles that complement each other."*

Taking the world requires the Way of non-action.
When one has affairs, it is not enough to take the world.

Extended meaning states:
Qín conquered the Six Kingdoms.
Quoted:
Qín Shǐhuáng’s name was Zhèng,
The son of Zhuāngxiāng Wáng.
In the twenty-sixth year of his reign,
He extinguished the Six Kingdoms,
Conquered the world,
And proclaimed himself the First Emperor.
The Six Kingdoms—Yān, Qí, Chǔ, Wèi, Hàn, and Zhào—
Were all annihilated in his time.
The *Records of the Grand Historian* says:
*"Qín Shǐhuáng rose to greatness,
And with one stroke of his policy, vanished without a trace."*

Extended meaning states:
He abolished the *well-field system*,
Opened the *intermediate fields*,
Removed the *straight roads*,
Built the *A Fáng*,
Constructed the *Great Wall*,
And developed the *Five Ridges*.

Quoted:
The *well-field system*: According to *The Records of the Grand Historian* and the *Gǔliáng Transmission*,
In ancient times, three hundred paces made a *lǐ*,
Called *well-field*. The *well-field system*
Allocated nine hundred *mǔ* of land, with one hundredth as public fields.
Abolishing the *well-field system* and opening the *intermediate fields*—
In the nineteenth year, when Qín Xiàogōng appointed Shāng Yáng,
The *well-field* was replaced by *intermediate fields*,
And from then, the troubles of兼并 (*combination/annexation*) began.
This is the same as the Qín laws.
In the twelfth year, he opened the *intermediate fields* for farming.
In the fourteenth year, he abolished the *well-field system*,
Opened the *intermediate fields*,
And collected taxes.

Removing the *straight roads*—
In the thirty-fifth year of Qín Shǐhuáng’s reign,
He ordered Mèng Tiān to remove the *straight roads*,
From Jiǔyuán to Yún Yáng,
Carving through mountains and filling valleys for a thousand eight hundred *lǐ*.
It took several years but was not completed.
Qín Shǐhuáng thought the people of Xiànyáng were too numerous,
And the palaces of former kings too small,
So he began constructing the imperial palace in Wèinán’s *Shànglín Garden*.
First, he built the *forecourt*, named it *Āfáng*—
Annotation: *"The four eaves of the hall were all made into chambers."*
Five hundred paces east to west,
Fifty *zhàng* north to south,
Its height could seat ten thousand men,
Its depth could raise a five-*zhàng* banner.
From its base straight to the southern mountains,
He marked the peak of the southern mountains as the *gateway*,
But the work was unfinished when it was burned by Xiàng Yǔ.

Constructing the *Great Wall*—
In the thirty-second year,
Qín Shǐhuáng inspected the northern border,
Where Lúshēng advised him to enter the sea and return,
And thus he ordered the recording of books, saying:
*"The one who destroys Qín will be the Hu."*

Qín Shǐhuáng then dispatched General **Mèng Tián** to lead **three hundred thousand men** in a northern expedition against the **Xióngnú**,
Seizing the lands south of the **Hé River** and establishing **forty-four prefectures**,
Constructing the **Great Wall** and utilizing the terrain to create formidable passes—
From **Lín Táozhōu** to **Liáodōng**, stretching over **ten thousand li**,
Its might shook the **Xióngnú**.

The **Five Ranges** are:
The **Dàyǔ Range**, the **Shǐ’ān Range**, the **Lín Hé Range**, the **Guìyáng Range**, and the **Jiēyáng Range**.

In the **thirty-third year**,
He made a preliminary conquest of the **Nán Yuè** lands,
Establishing the **commanderies of Guìlín**, **Nánhǎi**, and **Xiàng**,
And thus banished and relocated **five hundred thousand people** to garrison the **Five Ranges**.

**Extended annotation** states:
*"The **Xiàng** clan broke through the **Hángǔ Pass**, burned the Qín palaces, seized treasures, slew **Zǐ Yīng**, and deposed the **Yì Emperor**."*
**Excerpt:** *"The **Xiàng** clan refers to **Chǔ Xiàng Jié**, courtesy name **Yǔ**, a native of **Bǐn County**."*

In the **third year of Qín Èrshì’s reign**,
As the **ruler of all under Heaven**,
He declared himself **West Chǔ’s Conqueror-King** and made **Péngchéng** his capital.

That year,
He broke through the **Hángǔ Pass**,
Massacred **Xiàngyáng**,
Slew **Zǐ Yīng**,
And burned the Qín palaces—
The flames raged for **three months** before extinguishing.
He took their treasures, womenfolk, and departed eastward.
The Qín people were utterly disheartened.

**Translation:**

For eight years, **Yǔ** raised his forces and contended with **Pèi Gōng** for all under Heaven, ultimately defeated at **Gāixià**, and surrendered by presenting his head.

He ruled as **Conqueror-King** over all under Heaven, his reign established in **Hàn**. In the second year of **Èrshì’s reign**, the tenth month, **Xiàng Wáng** secretly dispatched **Jiǔjiāng Wáng Bù** and **Yīng Bù** to strike down **Yì Dì**, killing him in the river.

When **Hàn Wáng** arrived at **Luòyáng’s New City**, **Sān Lǎo Dǒng Gōng** intercepted and addressed the king, saying:
*"I have heard: Those who align with virtue prosper; those who defy it perish. When armies march, none stand to their right. Thus, undertakings fail. Therefore, it is said: Make clear who the bandit is, and the enemy may be subdued. **Xiàng Yǔ** acts without the **Dao**; thus, he slays his sovereign—he is the bandit of all under Heaven."*

---
**Moral Teachings from the *Dàodé Jīng* – Extended Commentary, Volume 13 Complete**
**Moral Teachings from the *Dàodé Jīng* – Handwritten Notes, Volume 14**

**From *Five-Peak Hermit Róng Shǒuzhèng’s Collection* – Chapter 49: The Sage Has No Fixed Mind**
The Sage has no fixed mind; he takes the minds of the people as his own.

**Extended Commentary:** *Zhuāngzǐ* says: *"The Perfect Man’s use of mind is like a mirror—neither grasping nor repelling."*

**Handwritten Notes** (*Zhuāngzǐ – "Yìng Dì Wáng" Section*): *"Do not be a hollow name, do not be a scheming treasury, do not be a burden of affairs, do not be a master of knowledge. Exhausting the boundless, yet wandering without trace, exhausting what Heaven has bestowed, yet seeing no gain—this is emptiness indeed. The Perfect Man’s use of mind is like a mirror: neither grasping nor repelling, responding yet not hoarding. Thus, he overcomes all things without harm."*

**Extended Commentary:** In ancient times, **Qín Mù Gōng** once lost his thousand-*lǐ* steed.

**Handwritten Notes:** This is the text from *The Spring and Autumn Annals*; it is already recorded in the *Extended Commentary* and need not be repeated.

*"I believe those who trust me, and I also believe those who do not."*

**Extended Commentary:** In ancient times, a man presented a live fish to **Zǐ Chǎn**.

**Handwritten Notes** (*Mèngzǐ – "Wàn Zhāng Shàng" Section*): The text is already detailed in the *Extended Commentary* and need not be repeated again.

「Yet further, he spoke of the tranquil and contented manner of aligning with one’s **Dao/Way** and **qi/vital energy**, this being the text from *The Analects – "Xiāng Páng"* Section. Its general meaning is that the tranquil and sincere demeanor of one who embodies **virtue/power** appears as though he cannot speak, yet it is the humble and yielding posture of one who does not presume his wisdom over others.

**Chapter Fifty: "Life and Death"**
Life and death, entering and exiting.

**Extended Commentary:** Observing the origin and returning to the end, one thus understands the doctrine of life and death. The handwritten notes from *The Zhouyi* (I Ching) state: "Hence, he knows the **Dao/Way** of encompassing all things." Gazing upward to study the celestial patterns, looking downward to examine the earthly structures, thus one knows the reasons behind the hidden and the manifest. Observing the origin and returning to the end, one thus understands the doctrine of life and death. This speaks of using the **Dao/Way** of *The Zhouyi* in upward contemplation and downward observation, thereby knowing the unseen yet profound (幽) and the seen yet apparent (明), the principles and their applications.

Observing the origin and returning to the end, one thus understands the doctrine of life and death. It means that by applying the principles of *The Zhouyi* to trace the initial stages and the final outcomes of all things, one comprehends the auspicious and inauspicious fate of their beginnings and endings, thereby knowing the doctrine of life and death.

Of those who live, ten in every thirteen are reckless; of those who die, ten in every thirteen are reckless. In the course of human life, ten in every thirteen meet their death through reckless actions.

**Extended Commentary:** This refers to the use of the nine orifices and four limbs, or the three activities and ten evils.
The handwritten notes from **Hán Fēi** state: "Ten in every thirteen refers to the nine orifices and four limbs." That is, the two ears, two eyes, two nostrils (considered as two orifices), along with the mouth and the two lower orifices, totaling nine orifices. The two hands and two feet are termed the four limbs. **Hé Shàng Gōng** also agrees with this interpretation.

Another interpretation cites the three activities and ten evils. The three activities refer to **body, speech, and mind**. The ten evils are:
1. Killing living beings,
2. Theft and robbery,
3. Lewdness and licentiousness,
4. Treason and rebellion against superiors,
5. Defaming and slandering sacred scriptures and laws,
6. Cursing and blaspheming in darkness,
7. Secret plotting, resentment, and jealousy,
8. Slanderous speech and causing discord,
9. Breaking vows and violating precepts,
10. Touching or defiling altars and sacred grounds.

**Sūn Dēng’s** explanation states: Among the things of Heaven and Earth, those of the living kind, few abide by the **Dao/Way**, while many defy it. Hence, of those who live recklessly, three in every ten conform to the **Dao/Way**. Those who live not in accordance with the **Dao/Way** and die not in accordance with reason are few, while those who defy them are many. Thus, of those who die, three in every ten submit to their fate.

The *Broad Explication of the Sacred* states: One who does not take joy in life yet remains content with it is among those who live recklessly. If one does not let desires and greed disrupt his mind while alive, nor let excessive nourishment harm his nature, but remains at ease with simplicity and humility, aligns with the natural flow of **qi/vital energy**, maintains the tranquility of his spirit, and steadies his mind—then, without seeking to prolong life, life itself will be extended. Without striving to advance in the **Dao/Way**, the **Dao/Way** will progress naturally. This is the teaching of **Dù Tiānshī**.

In my humble view, those who preserve life in such a manner are not merely three in every ten. Even among a thousand or ten thousand, there may be none or only one or two. Thus, by this reasoning, are they not few indeed?

The *Yanyì* states: **"To dwell in harmony with the times and align with the natural order."** It cites the text from the *Zhuangzi*’s **"Great Master"** chapter:

*"To gain is to follow the times; to lose is to conform. He who dwells in harmony with the times and aligns with the natural order is impervious to sorrow and joy. This is what the ancients called **‘suspended detachment’**—detachment without bonds or attachments. Why so? Because his vital essence is profound and unexhausted."*

The *Yanyì* elaborates: *"Excessive pursuit of life-nourishing substances, when taken to excess, leads to death."* It cites the *Zhuangzi*’s **"Mastering Life"** chapter:

*"The first requirement for nurturing the form is moderation. There are cases where nourishment abounds, yet the body remains unnourished. Truly, those skilled in preserving life traverse land without fear of rhinoceros or tiger, enter battlefields unarmored by weapons. The rhinoceros finds no place to strike with its horn, the tiger no place to extend its claws, and weapons no opening to pierce—why? Because they dwell in **‘non-death’**."*

The *Yanyì* further explains: *"Zhuangzi said: ‘If one empties oneself to wander through the world, who could harm them?’"* It cites the *Zhuangzi*’s **"Mountain Trees"** chapter:

*"Two square boats drifted side by side upon the river. A hollow vessel came to collide with them—though the passage was narrow and constricted, no anger arose, for the vessel was empty. A man of such a mind, if another stood upon him, would call out, ‘Expand and contract!’ If the first call went unheard, the second unheard, then a third—he would not respond with harshness. Earlier, he was not angry yet now he is; earlier, he was empty yet now he is full. If one empties themselves to wander through the world, who could harm them?"*

The *Yanyì* concludes: *"As in the Daoist transmission of Liu Gang."* It notes: *"This is recorded in the *List of Immortals*, with its meaning already fully elaborated. This is Chapter Fifty-One of *Dao Sheng*."*

The Dao/Way gives birth to it, virtue/power nurtures it, things shape it, and circumstances complete it.

*Yanyì* notes: *"Thus, the naked cannot but be naked, and those with scales, shells, fur, or feathers cannot but have scales, shells, fur, or feathers."*

Recorded in *The Analects of Confucius*, Chapter Twenty-Four of *The Five Emperors*: These are the words of Confucius in response to Zigong.

Confucius said: *"Indeed, I once heard from Laozi that it was as you say. Thus, those born of firm earth are sturdy, those of weak earth are yielding, those of crumbling earth are vast, those of sandy earth are refined, those of damp earth are beautiful, and those of exhausted earth are ugly. Those who eat water excel in swimming and endure cold, those who eat earth are without desire and do not tire—*they do not breathe*—those who eat wood are strong but unrefined—*their vital essence is ungoverned*—those who eat grass are swift but foolish, those who eat mulberry leaves have silken threads like silkworms, those who eat meat are courageous and defensive, those who eat qi are spiritually enlightened and long-lived, those who eat grains are wise and clear-sighted, and those who eat nothing do not die but become divine."*

*The Huainanzi* states: *"Though strong yet not violent, this too is the appearance of the unrefined."*

Thus it is said: *"Among feathered creatures, there are three hundred and sixty, and the phoenix is their sovereign. Among furred creatures, there are three hundred and sixty, and the qilin is their sovereign. Among shelled creatures, there are three hundred and sixty, and the tortoise is their sovereign. Among creatures with hidden forms, there are three hundred and sixty, and humanity is their sovereign."* This is the beauty of Heaven and Earth.

Though their forms and kinds differ, the sovereign must act in accordance with the Dao/Way when moving and must rest in accordance with the Dao/Way, ever following nature without harming what is born. This is called *ren* and longevity.

*Yanyì* notes: *"As for the young, the strong, and the old—death is inevitable."*

Recorded in *Liezi* (missing character in *"Heavenly Blessing"* chapter): The Yellow Emperor said: *"When the spirit enters its gate and the bones return to their roots, what remains of me?"*

From birth to death, the Great Transformation has four stages: infancy, youth and vigor, old age, and death. In infancy, the qi is concentrated and the will is singular—harmony at its peak. Nothing harms it, and no virtue surpasses it. In youth and vigor, blood and qi overflow, desires and thoughts surge—things assail it, and virtue declines. In old age, desires and thoughts soften, the body nears rest—nothing precedes it. Though it does not reach the fullness of infancy, it is already distant from youth and vigor. In death, it returns to stillness, reaching its extreme.

Thus, all things revere the Dao/Way and honor virtue/power.

*Yanyì* notes: *"This is what Zhuangzi called the True Sovereign."* It also cites Zhuangzi’s *"True Steward"*:

*"One does not know what it is that commands, yet if there is a True Sovereign, it is only that its traces and succession are not grasped—how could it be otherwise than sovereign and minister?"* A True Sovereign indeed exists.

The Dao/Way is exalted, virtue/power is noble—none confer titles upon it, yet it is ever naturally so.

*Yanyì* notes: *"As in the case of the five ranks of duke, marquis, earl, viscount, and baron."*

Recorded: The Zhou established five ranks of nobility—duke, marquis, earl, viscount, and baron. The ancient kings’ laws also had five grades: sovereign, nobles, ministers and great officers, scholars, and commoners—including the sovereign himself.

In the world, the ruler governs not because he lacks talent or tools. When he selects and employs them, he then confers titles and honors, bestowing nobility and prestige. Yet this nobility and prestige have their limits. The Dao/Way gives birth to things and is revered by them. Virtue/power nurtures all things and is honored by them. Endlessly generating without cessation, the function of giving birth is eternally revered. Endlessly completing without exhaustion, the function of perfecting is eternally honored. How could there be any limit?

*Yanyì* notes: *"Mencius said: There is the noble title of Heaven, and there is the noble title of humanity."*

Recorded in *Mencius*, *"Telling Zigong of Proper Conduct"* (Upper): *"There is the noble title of Heaven, and there is the noble title of humanity. Ren, yi, zhong, and xin—joy in goodness without weariness—this is the noble title of Heaven. Dukes, ministers, and great officers—this is the noble title of humanity. The noble title of Heaven is earned through virtue/power, and the noble title of humanity is earned through emolument. The ancients cultivated their noble title of Heaven, and the noble title of humanity followed. Today’s people cultivate their noble title of Heaven to seek the noble title of humanity. Once they obtain the noble title of humanity, they abandon their noble title of Heaven—this is the greatest confusion, and it will surely lead to ruin."*

Now Mencius takes *ren*, *yi*, *zhong*, and *xin* as the noble title of Heaven. How much more so the Dao/Way and virtue/power, which surpass *ren* and *yi*! Thus it is said: *"The Dao/Way is exalted, virtue/power is noble—none confer titles upon it, yet it is ever naturally so."*

*Yanyì* notes: *"Zhuangzi fishing in the Pu River."*

Recorded in *Zhuangzi*, *"Autumn Water"* chapter: Pu is the name of a river, belonging to Dong Commandery—today’s Pu County in Puyang. The King of Chu (Chu King Wei) knew Zhuangzi was a sage of great insight, so he sent envoys to offer him the position of minister, first testing his intentions. He wished to entrust the governance of his realm to a worthy, for state affairs were burdensome and he bore many worries. The meaning is already fully elaborated; there is no need to repeat it.

*"To act without possession, to labor without reliance, to grow without domination—this is called the Mysterious Virtue."*

*Yanyì* notes: *"Retreating and hiding in secrecy—this is profound and distant."*

Recorded in *The System of the Classic of Changes*: The Master said: *"What is the *Yijing* for? It opens what is hidden and completes undertakings, encompassing the Dao/Way of all under Heaven. Such is its function. Thus, the sage uses it to harmonize the wills of all under Heaven, to establish the undertakings of all under Heaven, and to resolve the doubts of all under Heaven. Therefore, the virtue/power of the stalks is round and divine, the virtue/power of the trigrams is square and knowing, and the meanings of the six lines are easy yet profound. The sage uses this to purify the heart, retreating and hiding in secrecy, sharing in the joys and sorrows of the people. Divine insight knows the future, and knowledge preserves the past—who could rival this?"*

The ancient were the wise and enlightened, the sagely and martial yet without killing.

Originally, when Confucius spoke of *retreat and concealment in secrecy* (退藏於密), he referred to the ancient wise and enlightened sages, who without exception followed the Dao/Way of the *Yijing*, harmonizing their virtue/power with the divine knowledge of the trigrams and lines, purifying their hearts, nurturing their virtue/power, and retiring in secrecy—yet never revealing their traces.

Is this not the *profound virtue/power* (玄德)?

Under Heaven, there exists the *Beginning Chapter* (有始), the fifty-second chapter:

*"Block its *Yui* (兑), close its gates—then one’s entire life will not labor."*

Elaborated meaning: The six senses each have their own delight.

Recorded: The six senses (六根)—as the Left Venerable Master of the *Taiji* (太极) Confession states—are the cause of the entanglement of life, birth, and suffering. They arise from the three actions (三业): the six senses are the eye-sense (眼根), ear-sense (耳根), nose-sense (鼻根), tongue-sense (舌根), body-sense (身根), and mind-sense (意根). All senses give rise to all actions (业), hence it is said: the six senses are like plants and trees, producing all flowers and fruits, perpetually generating one another.

Elaborated meaning: The Yellow Emperor inquired about *governing the body* (治身) of the Dao/Way from Guangchengzi.

Recorded in *The Zhuangzi* (《庄子》□*On Nurturing the Perfect*—在宥篇): The meaning is already detailed in the *Recorded Sayings* (义中载之), and thus it is not repeated here.

Elaborated meaning: This is called *having the Joyous* (兑). When the Joyous is present and the heart (心) emerges, it engages with things (物). If one blocks the Joyous so that it does not flow, then the heart will not emerge.

Recorded in *The Sayings on the Hexagrams* (《易说卦》): "Joy is spoken of as *Joyous* (兑), which is the marsh. Nothing delights all things as much as the marsh does." Again, *Joyous* (兑) is likened to the eye, the gate, and the mouth, and thus it warns against their closure and the disasters that follow. If this gate of disaster is applied to a state, governance will falter; if practiced in the body, the Dao/Way will decline. If properly closed and sealed, then the body will be at ease and the state will be orderly. To open the Joyous and advance its affairs will never bring salvation in the end.

Elaborated meaning: Once the five desires’ gate is opened, the six dusts’ affairs are accomplished, and the mind and spirit grow weary within.

Recorded in *The Zhuangzi*: The words of Zhiqi (盗跖) admonishing Confucius: "Now I shall tell you of human nature: the eyes desire to see colors, the ears desire to hear sounds, the nose desires to smell fragrances, the tongue desires to taste flavors, and the will desires fulfillment. These are the six dusts’ affairs."

This is the original language from the *Scripture of the Gathering of the Jade Emperor* (玉皇本《行集经》), Chapter One: At that time, the Jade Emperor issued a grand and wondrous sound, addressing all the celestial beings and holy assemblies of the Ten Directions: "You must listen carefully. These guilty beings, across countless kalpas, though lacking in wisdom, have committed the ten evil deeds. The six dusts pervade their defilement, the three actions bind them tightly, and they indulge their hearts without restraint, never awakening. Their hidden sins and manifest transgressions accumulate day by day, month by month.

What are the six dusts? They are the dusts of the six senses—eye, ear, nose, tongue, body, and mind.

These guilty beings, having long fallen into the sufferings of kalpas, indulged their dust-obscured nature, thus becoming thoroughly defiled by the six dusts and entangled by the three actions. These are all the types of transgressions.

Elaborated meaning: There was a man named Zhang Bao in the Song Dynasty.

Recorded: Zhang Bao was a man from Chengdu. He admired the wife of the magistrate of Huayang County. He made me, the fifth chapter of the *Jie* (介) preface, stand out.

If I were to have knowledge, I would walk the Great Dao,
Yet only fear to act with benevolence.

**Elaborated meaning:** This is how *Niè Quē* questioned *Wáng Ní*:
*"Do you know what all things hold in common?"*
Recorded from the text of *Zhuāngzǐ’s* ["齐物论"] *On Equalizing Things*:
Niè Quē was a disciple of Wáng Ní.
Wáng Ní replied: *"How should I know?"*
*"Do you know what you do not know?"*
*"How should I know?"*
*"Then do things have no knowing?"*
*"How should I know?"*
*"Well, let me try to speak of it.*
*How do I know that what I call knowing is not ignorance?"*

The Great Dao is most level, yet people love shortcuts.

**Elaborated meaning:** As with *Tán Tái Miè Míng*, who never took shortcuts.
Recorded from the words of the *Lúnyǔ’s* ["雍也"] *Yōng Yě* (Sixth Chapter):
Zǐyóu, named Yǎn, styled Zǐyǔ, was a disciple of Confucius.
He served as magistrate of Wǔchéng, a lower city of Lǔ.
The Master said: *"Have you found a worthy man there?"*
He replied: *"There is Tán Tái Miè Míng—he never takes shortcuts.
Unless it is official business, he never enters my home.
His courtyard is always swept clean, his fields well tilled,
And his granaries are always empty."*

**Elaborated meaning:** Such as the excesses of *Zhòu*—his palaces and pavilions,
The *Chu*’s *Chānghuá*, and *Qín*’s *Āfáng*—all are of this kind.
Recorded:
*"Palaces and pavilions"*—from the *Shūjīng’s* ["泰誓上"] *Tài Shì (Upper)*:
King Wǔ said: *"Now King Zhòu of the Shāng,
Does not revere the Highest Heaven,
Brings calamity upon the people,
Drowns himself in wine and debauchery.
He indulges in licentiousness, corrupts women,
Commits atrocities—punishing whole clans,
Condemning officials by generations—
His palaces, pavilions, ponds, and extravagant attire
Exhaust the common folk.
He burns and roasts loyal ministers,
Dismembers pregnant women.
The August Heaven, enraged, commands my father, the Duke,
To solemnly enforce Heaven’s might—
The great vengeance has yet to be fulfilled."*

And the *Chu*’s *Chānghuá*—from the *Chūnqiū*, *Zhāo Gōng* Year 7:
The Lord of Chu built the *Chānghuá* terrace,
Wishing to sacrifice the feudal lords upon it.
*"Fall"* here means "sacrifice."
As in *Hú Zēng’s* poetic history:
*"Endless withered grass buries Chānghuá,*
*Laughing at King Líng’s past extravagance—
*Before the platform’s earth dried, the music ceased,
*Alas, his body lies in a commoner’s grave."*

And *Qín*’s *Āfáng*—from poetic history:
*"Newly built Āfáng’s walls were yet un-dried,
When Pèngyōng’s army already entered Cháng’ān.
The emperors drained the people’s strength for grand schemes—
Great undertakings crumble as sand, not hard to foresee."*

This speaks of rulers who neglect the roots,
Pursuing only splendor,
Nearly destroying the foundation—
How can this be a lasting Dao?

**Elaborated meaning:** Towering halls and carved walls.
Recorded from the *Shàngshū’s* ["五子之歌"] *Wǔzǐ’s Song*.

「The Five Sons were the younger brothers of Tàikāng.  When Tàikāng sat upon the throne in name only, indulging in ease and pleasure to extinguish his own virtue, they composed this song to admonish him.  It says:

*"The Way teaches thus:*
*Within, he indulged in wanton excess,*
*Without, he hunted beasts without restraint.*
*Drunk on wine, lost in music,*
*Towering halls and carved walls—*
*One such act, and none shall escape ruin."*

**Elaborated meaning:** Fields and rice paddies left barren.
This is how the poets censured King Yōu.
Recorded from the *Máo Shī*’s ["小雅·楚茨"] *Xiǎoyǎ·Chǔcú*, censuring King Yōu.

*"Excessive governance and heavy taxation,*
*Fields and rice paddies left barren,*
*Famine and pestilence descend,*
*The people perish and flee.*
*Sacrifices fail to nourish the spirits,*
*Thus the virtuous ones ponder the ancient ways.*

*Clad in fine embroidery,*
*Wearing sharp blades,*
*Disdaining ordinary food,*
*Yet wealth overflows.*
*This is called ‘robber’s ostentation’—*
*How can it be the true Dao?"*

**Elaborated meaning:** Clad in fine embroidery, like Emperor Yángdì of Sui, who clipped silk to make flower-like adornments for his dragon boat.

Recorded: In the first year of Sui’s *Dàyì* era (605 CE), the fifth month, Emperor Yángdì constructed the Western Gardens, two hundred *lǐ* in circumference. Within it lay a man-made sea, ten *lǐ* or more in breadth. He built the Fāngzhàng, Pénglái, and Yíngzhōu mountains, each a hundred *zǐ* in height, with pavilions, towers, and palaces dotting their peaks. To the north of the sea, he dug a canal winding into its heart. Sixteen gateways of the Green Canal were all built along its banks, each presided over by a consort of the fourth rank. The gardens were lavish beyond measure. When the trees within the palace were trimmed, he would clip silk to fashion flower and leaf decorations, replacing them whenever the colors faded. Within the ponds, he similarly clipped silk to create lotus and water chestnut plants, exchanging them for new ones when their hues grew dull. The sixteen gateways vied with each other in offering exquisite delicacies, seeking favor through extravagant displays. The Emperor delighted in nightly excursions through the gardens with thousands of attendants, composing the *Qīngyè Yóu* ("Clear Night Journey") song to be performed on horseback. In the eighth month, the Emperor traveled to Jiāngdū. When he boarded the dragon boat there, it was four tiers high, forty-five *zǐ* in height, and two hundred *zǐ* long, with a main hall, inner hall, and court chamber on the topmost level. The middle two tiers housed a hundred and twenty rooms, all adorned with gold and jade. The lowest tier served as quarters for the eunuch attendants. The Empress rode in a floating pavilion, its design slightly smaller than the Emperor’s. There were also nine *Fújǐng* ("Floating Scenes") vessels, three tiers high, each a water palace. Thousands more boats carried the consorts, royal princes, officials, monks, nuns, Daoists, and foreign guests. Altogether, they employed over eighty thousand oarsmen, all dressed in brocade robes. A thousand more vessels bore the escorting guards, their prows and sterns linked in an unbroken line for two hundred *lǐ*. Cavalry patrolled the shores on either side. Every prefecture and county within five hundred *lǐ* was commanded to offer tribute—some sending as much as a hundred cartloads of the rarest aquatic and terrestrial delicacies. The palace women, sated with drink, would often discard the remainder of their meals in the dirt before departing.

**Elaborated meaning:** The Jin lord Hécāi made a purple silk windbreak forty *lǐ* long, while Shí Chóng made a brocade windbreak fifty *lǐ* long. Recorded from the *Jìn Shū*: Shí Chóng, styled Jìlún, was by nature extravagant. At that time, the Jin lord Hécāi had made a forty-*lǐ* purple silk windbreak, and Chóng, to surpass him in wealth, made a fifty-*lǐ* brocade windbreak. Later, when his concubine Lǜzhū was executed, he went to the Eastern Market and sighed, *"These slaves profit from my wealth!"* The executioners replied, *"You knew wealth could bring harm—why not disperse it earlier?"* Chóng had no answer.

**Elaborated meaning:** The Later Han official Mǎ Liáo submitted a memorial stating that in the capital, men favored wide sleeves. Recorded: In the first year of the *Jiànchū* era (76 CE) under Emperor Zhāng of Later Han, he issued an edict to correct customs and promote virtue, which must have its origin. The *Shū* says: *"King Wú of the State of Wú loved swordsmen, and the people were many with wounds."* *"King Fēn of the State of Zhāo delighted in slender waists, and many starved to death in the palace."* The people of Cháng’ān would say: *"In the capital, men favor tall hairdos—so the four quarters raise their hair by a foot."* *"In the capital, men favor broad eyebrows—so the four quarters pluck theirs to half their foreheads."* *"In the capital, men favor wide sleeves—so the four quarters wear entire bolts of silk."* Though these words seem like jest, they reflect reality.

**Elaborated meaning:** Wearing sharp blades, like King Wú of Wú, who commissioned the smith Gān Jiāng to forge swords. The swords came in two forms: one called *Gān Jiāng*, the other *Mòyē*. The earlier records mention the same.

Recorded from the *Zhuāngzǐ* ["说剑篇"] *Shuō Jiàn* chapter: In ancient times, King Wén of Zhāo—also known as Zhāo Hùiwén Wáng—was the son of Zhāo Líng Wáng. He loved swords, and three thousand or more swordsmen gathered at his gates, day and night clashing before him, with hundreds dying or wounded. Yet he never tired of it. Thus for three years, until his realm declined and the feudal lords conspired against him.

**Elaborated meaning:** If Hé Zéng of the Jin dynasty ate ten thousand coins’ worth of food daily, he would still claim there was no place to set his chopsticks. Recorded: During the reign of Jin Wǔdì, the Chancellor Hé Zéng, styled Yǐngkǎo, consumed ten thousand coins’ worth of food daily, yet still complained there was no place to set his chopsticks. He would not eat steamed buns unless they were cut into ten pieces. At that time, the general Qín Xiù remarked, *"Zéng’s arrogance and excess are beyond measure—he should be posthumously named with a shameful title."* A title that does not match the person’s deeds is called *miù* (谬), and one who flouts order and behaves wantonly is called *chǒu* (丑).

**Elaborated meaning:** King Zhòu of the Shang dynasty hoarded the wealth of Lùtái and amassed the grain of Jiǔqiáo. Recorded: King Zhòu of the Shang accumulated wealth without dispersing it, filled his granaries without knowing contentment. The *Shàngshū* states: When King Wǔ of the Zhōu defeated the Shang, he declared, *"Scatter the wealth of Lùtái and distribute the grain of Jiǔqiáo, bestow bounty upon all under heaven, and the myriad peoples will rejoice and submit."*»

The *Elaborated Meanings of the Daoist Classic* concludes here.
The *Daoist Classic’s Elaborated Meanings* continue in the fifteenth scroll.

**Chapter Fifty-Four: "The Well-Established Shall Not Be Uprooted"**
From *The Collected Works of the Reclusive Gentleman Wáng Shǒuzhèng of Wǔfēng*

The well-established shall not be uprooted,
The well-embraced shall not be lost—
Then descendants shall never cease their sacrifices.

**Elaborated Meaning:** First, establish the roots firmly, then cultivate the branches.

Quoted from *The Zhuāngzi* [□ *Heaven and Earth*]:
In the age of supreme virtue,
There is no veneration of the worthy,
No employment of the capable.
The ruler is like a branch marking the way,
The people like wild deer.
This speaks of the ruler’s non-action above,
And the people’s natural simplicity below—
Like wild deer, they are free from royal rites.

**Elaborated Meaning:** Those who embrace the people with the Dao
Shall have the hundred folk return and never depart.

Quoted: The *Elaborated Meaning* takes those who embrace the people with the Dao—
Like the Great King (Tàiwáng)—
As the very model of a Dao-bearing ruler.
When he left Yín and they followed him as to market,
This is called the hundred folk returning and never departing.
Thus it is said: *The well-embraced shall not be lost.*

**Elaborated Meaning:** Those who with the Dao embrace the Primordial and hold to Oneness
Shall have their spirit complete and never lost.

Quoted: The *Zhuāngzi* [□ *On Nurturing Life*] says:
Guāngchéngzǐ spoke: *"Come, I shall tell you of the supreme Dao."*
And again: *"I hold to its Oneness and dwell in its harmony."*
Thus I have cultivated my body for twelve hundred years,
Yet my form has never decayed.
The Yellow Emperor bowed twice and knelt in homage, saying:
*"This is what we call Heaven!"*
Thus the *Elaborated Meaning* cites those who with the Dao embrace the Primordial and hold to Oneness—
Their spirit is complete and never lost.
Guāngchéngzǐ is the very model of one who with the Dao embraces the Primordial and holds to Oneness.
To cultivate the body for twelve hundred years without decay—
This is called the spirit complete and never lost.
Thus it is said: *The well-embraced shall not be lost.*

**Elaborated Meaning:** When the Great King dwelt in Yín,
The Rú people invaded him.
He served them with hides, jade, and pearls,
Yet could not escape.

Quoted from *The Mèngzǐ* [□ *Sentences on King Huì of Lǐ, Lower*]:
Téng was a small state.
If it exhausted its strength to serve a great power,
It could not escape—how then could it?
Mèngzǐ replied:
*"In ancient times, the Great King dwelt in Yín.
The Rú people invaded him.
He served them with hides and jade—
Yet could not escape.
He served them with dogs and horses—
Yet could not escape.
He served them with pearls and jade—
Yet could not escape.
Then he gathered his elders and told them:
'What the Rú people desire is our land.
I have heard: A gentleman does not use what nourishes others to harm them.
My lords, what fear is there of lacking a ruler?
I shall depart.'
He left Yín, crossed the Liáng Mountains,
And settled beneath Qí Mountain.
The people of Yín said:
'A benevolent man—
We must not lose him!'
Those who followed him came as to market."*

The span of the Zhou Dynasty’s reign extended through the ages of King Wén, King Wǔ, King Chéng, and King Kāng—all sovereigns who embraced the **Dao** and cultivated **virtue**. Thus, their descendants succeeded one another for thirty-seven generations, spanning eight hundred and sixty-seven years. Their enduring prosperity and unbroken sacrifices were such that even when later rulers desecrated ancestral temples, the **sacrificial rites** to **Hòujì**, King Wén, and the **sacrifices at the altar of heaven** (*jiāosì*) never ceased.

The *Yǎn Yì* states: *"Even after the destruction of ancestral temples, the sacrifices to Hòujì, King Wén, and the **sacrifices at the altar of heaven** were never interrupted."*

From the *Shǐjì* records: When Confucius was in Qí, he lodged in the outer guesthouse. Qí Jinggōng visited him.
