After the formal exchanges between host and guest concluded, an attendant reported: *"A Zhou envoy has just arrived, saying that a calamity has befallen one of the ancestral temples of the former kings."* Jinggōng inquired: *"Which king’s temple has been stricken?"*

Confucius replied: *"This must be the temple of King Lǐ."*

The duke asked: *"How do you know?"*

Confucius said: *"The *Shī* says: ‘Majestic, O Heaven above, your commands are without error. Heaven rewards virtue with virtue.’ This is a lost poem. ‘Majestic’ (*huánghuáng*) means splendid. ‘Error’ (*tè*) means deviation. Calamity follows the same principle. King Lǐ abandoned the institutions of Kings Wén and Wǔ, introducing dark yellow and extravagant adornments, towering palaces, and lavish carriages and horses—none of which could be rectified. Thus, Heaven’s punishment rightly fell upon his temple. This divination aligns with the signs."*

The duke asked: *"Why did Heaven punish his temple instead of his person?"*

Confucius replied: *"It was for the sake of Kings Wén and Wǔ. If Heaven had punished his person, would not the descendants of Wén and Wǔ have been extinguished? Therefore, the calamity struck his temple to expose his transgressions."*

Soon, an attendant reported: *"The temple stricken was indeed that of King Lǐ."* Jinggōng rose in astonishment, bowed twice, and said: *"Truly, the wisdom of the Sage surpasses others far beyond measure. If his teachings were followed throughout the world, his **virtue** would be universal."*

「Elucidating its meaning, it states: *"When kin and relatives are harmonious, and undertakings are well-ordered, **virtue** is cultivated."*

Quoting *The Book of Documents [□Yáo Diǎn]*:
*"He [Yáo] selected and appointed men of outstanding **virtue**, thus bringing harmony to the nine clans."* The annotation reads: *"This means Yáo could recognize and employ men of exceptional **virtue**, thereby fostering harmony among his high ancestors, descendants, and all collateral relations—together comprising the nine clans."*

With the nine clans in harmony, he governed the people with clarity, making them bright and well-ordered. He united all under Heaven in concord, and the common folk thrived in their changing times.

Zhang’s interpretation states: *"Yáo cultivated **virtue** through selecting and appointing men of outstanding **virtue**—this was his way of perfecting himself. To bring harmony to the nine clans was his way of perfecting his family. To govern the people with clarity was his way of perfecting his state. To unite all under Heaven in concord was his way of perfecting the world. This is the proper sequence: first perfect oneself, then harmonize the family, then govern the state, and finally bring peace to the world."*

Elucidating its meaning further, it states: *"The blessings of accumulated goodness extend to future generations."* Quoting *The Commentary on the Hexagrams of the Yijing [Kūn Guà Wén Yán]*:
*"A family that accumulates goodness will surely have abundant blessings.
A family that accumulates evil will surely have lingering calamities."*

When a subject slays his lord, or a son slays his father, such deeds are not the result of a single day or night. Their origins lie in gradual decline. From failing to discern **virtue** early, one may say: *"Those who govern states and families can they not take warning?"*

The extended meaning states: *"All treasures flourish abundantly."* Quoting *Zhuangzi* [□ *Gēngsāng Chú*]:

The Master said: *"How do my disciples differ from me? When spring qi arises, all grasses sprout; when autumn arrives, all treasures mature."*

The extended meaning states: *"This is what is meant by using its remnants to govern people."* *"This is what is meant by using its dregs to govern the state."* Quoting *Zhuangzi* [□ *Ràng Wáng Piān*]:

*"The true Dao is used to cultivate the self. Its remnants are used to govern the state; its dregs are used to govern the world. Therefore, one observes the self to understand the self, the family to understand the family, the village to understand the village, the state to understand the state, and the world to understand the world. How do I know the world is thus? By this."*

The extended meaning states: *"The Yijing says: 'Observe my life, and the gentleman will have no blame.'" It is a matter of reflecting inward and seeking within oneself."* Quoting the *Guàn* hexagram’s ninth-five line:

*"Nine-five: Observe my life, and the gentleman will have no blame.*
*The Commentary says: 'Observe my life'—this means observing the people.*
*Master Bào’s interpretation says: This nine-five line, with its gentle yet upright position, embodies the just and central. It embodies yielding compliance to cultivate virtue, and uses rectitude to govern the people, becoming the sovereign of great observation. Yet those below follow those above, like shadows responding to form; the people follow the ruler, like clay shaped by the potter’s wheel. If you show them evil, evil will take root; if you lead them with goodness, goodness will flourish. Transformation begins from the self, and its influence extends to the four seas. To examine one’s virtue, first observe the people. When the gentleman’s conduct is exemplary, there will be no blame.*

*Thus, this line from the *Guàn* hexagram’s ninth-five is cited to verify the meaning of 'How do I know the world is thus?'—speaking of the five methods of observation, and knowing the world through observing its people."*

**Chapter Fifty-Five: The Depth of Contained Virtue**
*"The depth of contained virtue is like that of an infant. Poisonous insects do not sting, fierce beasts do not harm, and predatory birds do not snatch."*

The extended meaning states: *"If life’s blessings increase daily, the depth of virtue will diminish."* Quoting *Zhuangzi* [□ *Dé Chóng Fú Piān*]:

Huizi said to Zhuangzi: *"Is it true that humans have no emotions?"*
Zhuangzi replied: *"Yes."*
Huizi said: *"If humans have no emotions, why call them human?"*
Zhuangzi said: *"The Dao gives them form, Heaven gives them shape—how could they not be called human?"*
Huizi said: *"If they are called human, how can they lack emotions?"*
Zhuangzi said: *"This is not what I call emotion. What I call 'no emotion' is that humans do not let their likes and dislikes internally harm themselves; they follow nature without excess."*

The extended meaning states: *"Silent, as if having lost their mate."* Quoting *Zhuangzi* [□ *Qí Wù Lùn*]:

Nánguò Zǐqí sat leaning against his elbow, gazing upward and sighing deeply, silent as if having lost his mate. This describes the perfected sage, silent as if having lost their mate—like an infant’s innocence, so nothing can harm them.

The extended meaning states: *"Their harmony is in perfect accord with all things; who could harm them?"* Quoting *Liezi* [□ *Huángdì Piān*:

Zhào Xiāngzǐ led ten thousand soldiers on a hunt in the mountains, setting fires to drive game. The flames scorched a hundred *li*. A man emerged from a rocky crevice, moving with the smoke’s rise and fall. The soldiers took him for a ghost. After the fire passed, he emerged slowly, as if untouched. Xiāngzǐ was astonished and detained him. Upon close inspection, his form, color, and orifices were human; his breath, voice, and manner were human. Xiāngzǐ asked: *"What way did you dwell in stone? What way did you pass through fire?"*
The man replied: *"What do you call 'stone'? What do you call 'fire'?"*
Xiāngzǐ said: *"What you emerged from was stone; what you passed through was fire."*
The man said: *"I do not know."*
When Wèi Wénhòu heard of this, he asked Zǐxià: *"Who is this man?"*
Zǐxià replied: *"Based on what I’ve heard from the Master, those in perfect harmony are in perfect accord with all things. Nothing can harm them, so they can wander among metals and stones, tread upon water and fire—all is possible."*
Wénhòu said: *"Why doesn’t my teacher do this?"*
Zǐxià replied: *"He has hollowed his heart and discarded wisdom—I cannot do it either. Still, I will speak of it when I have leisure."*
Wénhòu said: *"Why doesn’t my teacher do this?"*
Zǐxià replied: *"The Master can do it but chooses not to."*
Wénhòu was greatly pleased.

The extended meaning states: *"Like in the Spring and Autumn Annals, where Dù Bǐ compared with the daughter of the Duke of Chu, and bore a son, Wén."* Quoting *Spring and Autumn* (Lí Gōng 21st year): The meaning is already recorded; no need for repetition.

The extended meaning states: *"Like in the *Shijing*, which says: 'When Houji was born to Jiāngléi, he was abandoned in a narrow lane, where cows and sheep trampled him. He was abandoned in a dense forest, where loggers felled trees. He was abandoned on the ice, where a bird sheltered him with its wings; the bird flew away, and Houji cried.'"* Quoting *Máo Shī* [*Dà Yá* • *Shēng Mín*]:

*"Revering the ancestral Houji, born to Jiāngléi, the achievements of Wén and Wǔ began with him. Thus, he was matched with Heaven. Abandoned in a narrow lane, cows and sheep trampled him. Abandoned in a dense forest, loggers felled trees. Abandoned on the ice, a bird sheltered him with its wings; the bird flew away, and Houji cried. Though his bones were frail and his sinews soft, his grip was firm. Before knowing the union of male and female, his vigor was already strong—the peak of vital essence. He cried all day, yet his throat did not grow hoarse—the peak of harmony."*

The extended meaning states: *"Zhuangzi said: 'The true person’s breath originates from the heels; the common person’s breath originates from the throat.'"*
Quoting *Zhuangzi* [*Dà Zōngshī Piān*]:

*"The ancient true persons slept without dreaming, awoke without worry, ate without relishing flavor, and breathed deeply. This describes the inner breath. The true person’s breath originates from the heels—the root of the feet. The common person’s breath is shallow and labored, originating from the throat."*

「When the throat is constricted, the voice falters.
　　As if taking a single step.
　　The throat, being the throat, when *qi* and breath are unregulated,
　　Within the throat, it is always as if obstructed by a frog.
　　Those whose desires are deep, their *heavenly mechanism* is shallow.
　　Knowing harmony as constancy, knowing constancy as clarity,
　　Nourishing life as auspiciousness, the heart directing *qi* as strength.
　　When things are at their peak, they begin to decay—
　　This is called *not-Dao*, and *not-Dao* perishes early.
　　The extended meaning states: *"It is not merely without benefit but also harmful."*
　　Quoting *Mencius* [*Gōngsūnchǒu Shàng*]:

*"There must be some action, yet do not force it—
　　Do not forget the heart, yet do not aid its growth.
　　Do not be like the people of Song:
　　The people of Song had one who pitied his sprouts for not growing,
　　So he bent them over—
　　Weary, he returned home and told his family,
　　'Sick today—I helped the sprouts grow!'
　　His son hurried to see, and the sprouts had withered.
　　Few in the world do not aid the sprouts' growth.
　　Those who think it useless and abandon them are like those who do not weed the fields.
　　Those who aid their growth are like those who bend them over—
　　Not merely useless, but also harmful.
　　Yet if one nourishes them directly, they will also thrive.
　　This is also from *Gōngsūnchǒu*’s words.
　　Mencius answered *Gōngsūnchǒu*’s question:
　　'Dare I ask what *hàorán zhī qì* is?'
　　He said: 'It is difficult to speak of.
　　As for its nature, it is supremely vast and supremely firm—
　　Nourished directly without harm, it fills the space between heaven and earth.'
　　*Programzi* said: 'Heaven and man are one.
　　No further distinction.
　　This *hàorán zhī qì* is my own *qi*—
　　Nourished without harm, it fills the space between heaven and earth.
　　Yet when obscured by private intentions, it contracts and suddenly knows its smallness.'
　　The extended meaning states: *"The *Shū* says: 'Doing good brings a hundred blessings.'*
　　Quoting *The Book of Documents* [*Yī Xuàn Piān*]:

*'Alas! The succeeding king must guard his person,
　　And reflect upon this.
　　The sacred admonitions are vast and resplendent,
　　The praiseworthy words are clearly manifest.
　　Only the Supreme Lord is not constant—
　　Doing good brings a hundred blessings,
　　Doing evil brings a hundred calamities.
　　If your *virtue* is not small, all the states rejoice.
　　If your *virtue* is not great, your lineage will collapse.'
　　The extended meaning states: *"The Dao exceeds the age of the ancient past yet does not grow old."*
　　Quoting *Zhuangzi* [*Yìng Dì Wáng Piān*]:

*"From its own origin and root,
　　Before heaven and earth existed, it has always been.
　　The divine spirits and divine lords,
　　Generating heaven and earth,
　　Are within the *Great Ultimate*—the *Great Ultimate*, five *qi* energies.
　　Above them yet not exalted, within the *Six Extremes*—the *Six Extremes*, the *Six Harmonies*.
　　Below them yet not abyssal, born before heaven and earth yet not ancient,
　　Exceeding the age of the ancient past yet not growing old.
　　This has already been recorded in the *Dao Jing* [*Wù Hùnchéng Zhāng*], so no need to repeat it.
　　The chapter on *Knowers Do Not Speak* (*Zhī Zhě Bù Yán*, Chapter 56):

*'Knowers do not speak; speakers do not know.'*
　　The extended meaning states: *"Thus, the Sage of Confucius wished for no speech."*
　　Quoting *The Analects* [*Yáng Huò Shíqī Piān*]:

*'The Master said: 'I wish to speak not at all.'
　　Zigong said: 'If you do not speak, how shall we, your junior disciples, transmit your teachings?'
　　The Master said: 'How does heaven speak? The four seasons follow their course,
　　And all things are born. How does heaven speak?'
　　The extended meaning states: *"Mencius did not enjoy debate."*
　　Quoting *Mencius* [*Téng Wén Gōng Zhāngjù Xià*]:

*'I also wish to rectify the hearts of men,
　　To dispel perverse doctrines,
　　To repel false practices,
　　To banish licentious speech,
　　So as to uphold the teachings of the Three Sages—
　　Do I enjoy debate? I do not!
　　I am compelled by necessity.'
　　The extended meaning states: *"Rectify the form to comprehend things."*
　　Quoting *Zhuangzi* [*Tián Zǐ Fāng Piān*]:

*'Clear and receptive, allowing things to be contained.
　　Things have no inherent Dao—
　　Rectify the form to awaken them,
　　And people’s intentions will dissolve—
　　No choice remains, how could it be named?
　　This has already been detailed in the *Dao Jing*’s eighth chapter, so no need to repeat it.'
　　The extended meaning states: *"Seeing the Dao with one’s eyes."*
　　Quoting *Zhuangzi* [*Tián Zǐ Fāng Piān*]:

*'Zhongni saw it yet did not speak.
　　Zilu said: 'My teacher has long wished to see Wen Bo Xuezi.
　　Yet seeing him, he says nothing—why is this?'
　　Zhongni said: 'If one like that person exists,
　　The Dao is preserved by a single glance—
　　It cannot even be contained by sound.'
　　The extended meaning states: *"The Sage of *Wuwei* was asked three questions yet gave no answer."*
　　Quoting *Zhuangzi* [*Zhī Běi Yóu Piān*]:

*'Zhīběi wandered above the *Mysterious Waters*,
　　Ascended the mound of the *Hidden Well*,
　　And just happened upon the Sage of *Wuwei*.
　　Zhīběi asked the Sage of *Wuwei*: 'I wish to ask you,
　　What thoughts and deliberations lead to knowing the Dao?
　　What attire and conduct ensure the Dao’s ease?
　　What path and method attain the Dao?'
　　The Sage of *Wuwei* answered none of the three questions,
　　Not that he did not answer, but that he did not know how.
　　Zhīběi could not ask further, so he returned to the south of the *White Waters*,
　　Ascended the mound of *Húxiá*,
　　And beheld *Kuáng Qu* there.
　　Zhīběi asked him with these same words.
　　*Kuáng Qu* said: 'Ah! I know it.
　　I would tell you, but in the midst of speaking, I forget what I wished to say.'
　　Zhīběi could not ask further, so he returned to the *Imperial Palace*,
　　And saw the Yellow Emperor, asking him.
　　The Yellow Emperor said: 'Without thought or deliberation, one begins to know the Dao.
　　Without attire or conduct, one begins to ensure the Dao’s ease.
　　Without path or method, one begins to attain the Dao.'
　　Zhīběi asked the Yellow Emperor: 'You and I know it,
　　Yet he and he do not—who is correct?'
　　The Yellow Emperor said: 'That Sage of *Wuwei* is truly the Dao.
　　*Kuáng Qu* resembles it.
　　You and I are far from it.
　　The *Way* of the Sage is the teaching of silence—
　　Thus knowers do not speak, and speakers do not know,
　　So the Sage acts through the *Dao* of unspeaking.'
　　The extended meaning states: *"Wang Ni was asked four questions yet gave no answer."*
　　Quoting *Zhuangzi* [*Yìng Dì Wáng Piān*]:

*'Kē Què asked Wang Ni four questions yet received no answer.
　　Kē Què then leapt with great joy and went to tell Pú Yī Zi.
　　Pú Yī Zi said: 'Now you know it?'
　　The extended meaning states: *"Even those who recognize sound cannot transmit it with their mouths."*

「Quoting *Mencius* [*Lí Lóu Zhāngjù Shàng*]:

*"Shī Kuàng’s* [1] *acuteness of hearing could not harmonize the five tones without the six pitch-pipes. Shī Kuàng was the music master of Jìn, a connoisseur of sound. Yet even he, with his mastery of the six pitch-pipes, could not rectify the five tones without first attaining them in his mind and responding with his hands. Who then could truly transmit those subtle, refined, and resonant tones within the five tones?*

*The extended meaning states: *"The wheelwright’s craft cannot be spoken of."*

Quoting from *Zhuangzi* [*Tiāndào Piān*]:

*"Huan Gōng of Qí, whose personal name was Xiao. He was reading in the hall above, while the wheelwright Lún Biān was carving wheels below. Lún Biān put down his mallet and chisel, ascended, and asked Huan Gōng: 'May I boldly inquire—what is the speech you are reading?' Huan Gōng replied: 'It is the speech of the Sage.' Lún Biān asked: 'Is the Sage still alive?' Huan Gōng said: 'He has already died.' Lún Biān said: 'Then what you read is merely the leavings and dregs of the ancients.' Huan Gōng said: 'My lord reads the books, how dare the wheelwright comment? If he has understanding, let him speak; if not, let him die.' Lún Biān said: 'I observe it through my own craft. When carving wheels, if I proceed too slowly, the wood becomes soft yet unyielding; if too swiftly, it becomes bitter yet does not penetrate. Neither too slow nor too swift, I attain it with my hands and resonate it in my mind—yet my mouth cannot speak it. There is a measure inherent within this process. I cannot teach my craft to my son, nor can my son receive it from me, hence at seventy years old I am still a wheelwright. The ancients’ true teachings could not be transmitted, so they died with them. Thus what you read is merely the leavings and dregs of the ancients.'*

*Therefore, it cannot be grasped as near or distant, beneficial or harmful, honored or degraded—yet for this very reason, it is held in highest esteem by all under Heaven.*

*The extended meaning states: *"If* [Zǐ] *Lièzi lived in the Jìng gardens for forty years, yet no one recognized him."*

Quoting from *Lièzi* [*Tiānrú Piān*]:

*"Zǐ Lièzi dwelled in the Jìng gardens for forty years, and no one knew him. The rulers and ministers of the state regarded him no differently than the common folk."*

*The extended meaning states: *"If* [Zǐ] *Xuānwáng of Qí wished to govern all under Heaven and bestowed upon Mèngzǐ a residence, maintaining his disciples with ten thousand* [qìng] *of grain—six* [hú] *four* [dòu] *for one* [qìng]—*and ordered all ministers and the people to emulate their conduct."*

Quoting from *Mencius* [*Gōngsūn Chǒu Zhāngjù Xià*]:

*"Xuānwáng of Qí later told Shǐ Zǐ: 'I wish to govern all under Heaven and bestow upon Mèngzǐ a residence, maintaining his disciples with ten thousand* [qìng] *of grain—six* [hú] *four* [dòu] *for one* [qìng]—*and order all ministers and the people to emulate their conduct.' Shǐ Zǐ, why do you not tell me this?" Shǐ Zǐ relayed the words through Chén Zǐ, and Chén Zǐ told Mèngzǐ. Mèngzǐ said: 'Indeed, Shǐ Zǐ knows not why it is impossible. If I were to desire wealth, I would decline ten thousand* [qìng] *and accept a thousand—would that not be to desire wealth?'*

*The extended meaning states: *"Mèngzǐ spoke of what people truly honor—it is not the kind of honor they seek."*

Quoting from *Mencius* [*Gàozǐ Zhāngjù Shàng*]:

*"Mèngzǐ said: 'To desire honor is the common aspiration of all people. Everyone has something they honor in themselves—it is just that they do not reflect upon it. What people truly honor is not the kind of honor they seek. Zhào Mèng’s* [2] *honor can be taken away by Zhào Mèng himself. The note states: Zhào Mèng’s honor refers to the honor bestowed upon him by his rank and title, while* [liáng] *honor is the inherent goodness of virtue. Zhào Mèng was a minister of Jìn. If he could confer rank and wealth upon others to make them honored, he could also strip them of it to make them degraded. If it were* [liáng] *honor, how could others degrade him?'*

*The extended meaning states: *"The* [Hàn] *Filempéror heard of* [Héshàng Gōng] *tying reeds into a hut by the riverbank."*

*Quoted from* [Zhuǎn] *Chronicles* [Běnjì] *as recorded. The meaning has already been elaborated upon in earlier passages and will not be repeated here.*

**Dao/Way and Virtue/Power True Classics Extended Meaning: Fifteenth Volume of the *Miaoquan* [妙卷] completed; Sixteenth Volume of the *Yanquan* [衍卷]**

**Five-Peak Qing’an Hermit Wang Shouzheng’s Collection: Chapter Fifty-Seven, *With Rectitude Govern the Realm* [以正治国]**

With rectitude govern the realm,
With stratagem employ the military,
With non-action seize the world.

*Extended Meaning states:* Mencius said: *"Rectify the ruler, and the realm is settled."*

Quoted from the *Mencius*—[□离娄上] [上] *Chapter*—text.

Mencius said:
*"One cannot discuss music with those unworthy of harmony,
Nor debate governance with those unfit to rule.
Only the great sage can correct the ruler’s unrighteous heart.
If the ruler is virtuous, none will fail to be virtuous;
If the ruler is righteous, none will fail to be righteous;
If the ruler is rectified, none will fail to be rectified.
Rectify the ruler, and the realm is settled."*

*Extended Meaning states:* The *Three Stratagems* [三略] and *Nine Variations* [九奇]—
Calming strife to pacify rebels—
are the achievements of martial prowess.

Quoted:
*The Three Stratagems* [三略]—
Han Lord Hou [留侯] Zhang Zifang [张子房], at the Weiyang Bridge [圯桥] in Xiaopi [下邳], encountered Huangshigong [黄石公],
who taught him the *Three Stratagems*, saying:
*"You have obtained them,
and you shall surely become a teacher of emperors and kings—
also a master of tactical maneuvers [机铃] in warfare.*
Zhang Yi [子牙] applied them,
assisting King Wu to conquer Shang and subdue Jie [纣] and thus complete the royal enterprise.
Zhang Zifang applied them,
aiding the Han Ancestor to annihilate Xiang Jie [项籍] and secure imperial dominion.
Thus, a sovereign who deeply comprehends the *Upper Stratagem* [上略] can appoint worthy ministers and capture enemies;
who deeply comprehends the *Middle Stratagem* [中略] can command generals and organize troops;
who deeply comprehends the *Lower Stratagem* [下略] can discern the sources of prosperity and decline,
and carefully examine the principles of governing the realm.
A subject who deeply comprehends the *Three Stratagems* can preserve merit and safeguard his life.

*The Nine Variations* [九奇]—
The Yellow Emperor established the *Nine Variations* based on the *well-field system* [井田] to regulate the military,
dividing the land into nine squares.
Five were used for battle formations,
four for concealed positions,
leaving the center empty,
where the supreme commander resided.
Zhuge Liang [诸葛孔明] expanded upon this method:
formations within formations,
units within units—
advancing, the rear becomes the front,
retreating, the front becomes the rear—
no hasty flight when advancing,
no sudden retreat when withdrawing,
strike the head and the tail responds,
strike the tail and the head responds,
strike the center and both ends respond—
this is the *Changshan Snake Array* [常山蛇势] of Huan Wen [桓温].

Generally, military arts favor ingenious changes.
From the first to the ninth,
like an endless cycle,
unable to exhaust.

How do I know the world is thus?
By this."

The world abounds with taboos and prohibitions,
yet the people grow ever more impoverished.
*Extended Meaning*: As with the Qin laws—if one spoke in couplets, he was executed in the marketplace.
*Quoted from* *The Mirror of History* [通鉴]: In the thirty-fourth year of the First Emperor’s reign [始皇三十四年], Chancellor Li Si [丞相李斯] submitted a memorial stating:
*"I request that all historical records not of the Qin be burned.
Those not pertaining to the Imperial Scholars [博士]’s duties,
any who possess hidden poems, books, or the sayings of a hundred schools [藏诗、书、百家语],
must all deliver them to the local magistrates [守尉] for destruction.
Those who dare to recite poems or books in couplets [偶语者] shall be executed in the marketplace.
Those who criticize the present by citing the past [以古非今者] shall be exterminated."*

*Extended Meaning*: As with the Tang Empress Wu’s [武后] opening of the door to denunciations,
people walked with heavy steps and held their breath,
daring not to speak with one another,
using only glances along the roads.
*Quoted*: In the first month of the first year of the Light Residence era [光宅元年], the Empress set up bronze boxes [铜匦] to receive secret denunciations.
She cast a bronze box, its shape like a chamber with four corners,
able to admit but not expel.
Since the rebellion of Xu Jingye [徐敬业], she feared the world plotted against her.
Moreover, having long monopolized state affairs [久专国事],
with internal conduct unrighteous [内行不正],
she knew the princes and ministers harbored resentment and disobedience,
and sought to intimidate them with mass slaughter.
Thus, she widely encouraged denunciations.
Those who denounced received provisions for horses and food,
and were sent to the imperial residence [行在所].
Even farmers and woodcutters could be summoned,
some promoted without due rank [不次除官],
and none were punished if their accusations proved false.
Thereupon, denunciations swarmed from all quarters.
The people multiplied their ingenious tools [利器], yet the state grew ever more corrupt.
The people multiplied their cunning arts [技巧], yet strange and licentious things arose.
Laws and decrees multiplied, yet thieves and bandits grew numerous.
*Extended Meaning*: Then there must be scheming minds.
*Quoted from* *Zhuangzi* [庄子]—*The Earth and Heaven* [天地篇] passage:
Child Gong [子贡] journeyed south to Chu [於楚],
passing through Han Yin [过汉阴],
where he saw a man of a hundred cubits [一丈人]
busily preparing his garden plots [为圃畦],
dug channels [凿隧] to lead water into the well [入井],
carried a jar [抱瓮] to draw water out [出灌],
laboring strenuously yet seeing little effect.
Child Gong said:
*"There is a device here—
in one day, it can irrigate a hundred plots [浸百畦],
requiring little effort yet achieving much.
Would you not desire it, Master?"*
The gardener looked up and replied:
*"How so?"*
*"Carve wood into a lever [凿木为机],
heavy at the rear, light at the front,
drawing water like pulling a cord.
Repeated as needed, its name is* *the well sweep* [槔].
The gardener angrily flushed and laughed:
*"I heard from my teacher—
he who uses devices must engage in scheming deeds,
and he who engages in scheming deeds must harbor scheming minds.
Scheming minds dwell in the chest,
leaving no room for pure whiteness.
When pure whiteness is incomplete,
the spirit’s birth is unstable.
An unstable spirit—
the Dao does not bear it.
I am not ignorant, but I am ashamed and do not act."*

*Extended Meaning*: Refers to carving and ornamenting.
*Quoted from* *Zhuangzi* [庄子]—*The Way of Heaven* [天道篇] passage:
*"It covers and sustains Heaven and Earth,
carves and molds all forms,
yet does not contrive cleverness—
this is called* *Heaven’s Joy* [天乐]."

*Extended Meaning*: As with King Zhou’s [纣王] creation of strange and licentious arts to please women.
*Quoted from* *The Book of Documents* [尚书]—*The Great Declaration, Lower Part* [泰誓下]:
*"King Wu [武王] said:
Now, the Shang king Jie [商王受],
wields tyranny and inflicts slaughter,
poisoning and tormenting all under Heaven.
He exalts and trusts deceitful flatterers,
casts aside loyal ministers and advisors,
abandons the established codes and punishments,
imprisons slaves and upright scholars,
neglects the ancestral altars and sacrificial rites,
fails to maintain the rituals of the land and grain.
Instead, he crafts strange and licentious arts to please women.
The Celestial Lord does not comply with him,
and the diviner declares his fate shall perish with the times.
You shall diligently serve me,
executing the Heavenly punishment upon him."*

The **Dao/Way** section’s text states:

*"The scope of its meaning is: Even the petty theft of hooks may escalate to the grand scale of usurping a state—such as Tian Chengzi [田成子], who in a single day assassinated the Qi king [齐君] and stole his realm. What he stole was not merely the kingdom—he also took its sacred laws and virtuous governance. Thus, Tian Chengzi bore the name of a thief, yet enjoyed the security of Yao [尧] and Shun [舜]. Small states dared not oppose him, and great states dared not punish him; twelve generations held Qi’s sovereignty. The scope of its meaning is: Li Si [李斯] advised Er Shi [二世] to employ the **supervision and punishment** technique to suppress theft. Quoted from *The Comprehensive Mirror* [通鉴]: In the second winter of Er Shi’s reign in the Qin [秦], the Second Emperor frequently rebuked Li Si, who held the Three Ducal positions [三公]. How could it be that theft persisted under such authority? Li Si, fearful and trembling, enriched his titles and ranks, unsure of his fate, and thus curried Er Shi’s favor, replying in writing: The sage ruler must be capable of employing the **supervision and punishment** technique. Hence, Shen Zi [申子] said: ‘He who holds the world yet does not indulge in wantonness, but instead treats the world as a shackle upon himself—there is no other reason than his inability to supervise and punish, while instead burdening himself with the labor of the people, like Yao and Yu [尧、禹]. Thus, he is called a shackle.’ If one cannot practice the clear techniques of Shen and Han [申、韩], nor follow the **Dao/Way of supervision and punishment**, but instead merely seeks to gratify himself with the world’s ease, while exhausting his own form and spirit to serve the people, then he is but a drudge for the common folk—not one who nurtures the world. What is there to honor in this? Therefore, the enlightened ruler employs the **supervision and punishment** technique to make absolute judgments from above, so that authority rests not in his ministers. Then he can extinguish the paths of benevolence and righteousness, sever the eloquence of persuasive speech, and halt the clamor of carts and horses. The Leftist School [左氏] was particularly adept at this. Yet, though his heart yearned for wantonness, none dared resist him. Thus, the ministers and people could not even offer timely warnings of errors—who would dare to plot change? Er Shi was pleased, and so the **supervision and punishment** grew ever stricter: those who taxed the people heavily were hailed as sagacious historians; those who killed many were called loyal ministers. Half of the travelers on the roads were under sentence, and corpses piled daily in the markets; the Qin people grew ever more terrified and prone to rebellion. The scope of its meaning is: Han Wudi [汉武帝] entrusted Zhang Tang [张汤] and Zhao Yu [赵禹] to codify the laws. Quoted from *The Comprehensive Mirror* [通鉴]: In the fifth year of the *Jian Yuan* era [建元], the Emperor commissioned the Grand Master of Justice Zhang Tang and the Middle Master of Justice Zhao Yu to jointly revise the legal codes. The decree emphasized harsh interpretations and binding officials to their duties. Zhang Tang’s biography was recorded, and his method of ‘**foresightful legal scholars**’ [见知法史] was adopted, with officials mutually overseeing each other’s application of the law, growing ever stricter from this point onward. Zhao Yu’s biography was also recorded. Hence, the sages proclaimed: ‘I take no action, yet the people transform themselves. I involve myself in no affairs, yet the people grow rich. I love tranquility, yet the people align themselves. I have no desires, yet the people return to simplicity.’ The scope of its meaning is: Like Yao and Shun, who were silent and still in their governance, reclining in their halls while the people flourished. Quoted from the words of *Yangzi* [杨子] in *The Inquiry into the Dao/Way* [问道篇]: ‘In ancient times, Yu and Xia [虞、夏] inherited Yao’s title and followed Yao’s Dao/Way. The laws were clear, and the rites and music were established. They ruled with ease, their hands folded in their sleeves, and the people flourished. *Fu* [阜] means *rich* [富]. They reclined with folded sleeves upon the rocky veranda, observing the people’s natural prosperity.’ The scope of its meaning is: ‘**Wen** [文] and **Wu** [武] neither knew nor recognized [不识不知], yet they conformed to the Heavenly Emperor’s will. With folded sleeves and hands at rest, the world was governed.’ Quoted from the words in *The Mao Commentary* [毛诗] on *The Great Odes* [大雅], *The Sovereign’s Majesty* [皇矣篇]: ‘The Emperor spoke to King Wen: “I cherish the virtue/power of the people, neither raising my voice nor showing favor, neither prolonging summers nor shortening winters, not knowing nor recognizing—yet I follow the Heavenly Emperor’s will.”’ The scope of its meaning is: ‘Like Cao Can [曹参], who served as Han’s chancellor, where Hui Di [惠帝] criticized him for not attending to affairs. The account in the *Book of Han* [汉书] has already been cited—no need to repeat it here.’ His governance is described in *The Fifth and Eighty Chapter of the Bright and Virtuous* [闷闷章第五十八]."

Misfortune, oh misfortune, is the reverse of fortune,
Fortune, oh fortune, harbors misfortune within.
*Extended meaning:* Fortune concealed within misfortune—
as in *Liezi*’s account of a Song man who practiced benevolence and righteousness.
*Quoted from* *Liezi* [列子] *□* *Sayings of the Symbol* [说符篇], *already cited in meaning—no need to repeat.*

*Extended meaning:* Misfortune hidden within fortune—
as in *Liezi*’s account of the wealthy man of Yu’s ancestral hall.
*Quoted from* *Liezi* [列子] *□* *Sayings of the Symbol* [说符篇], *already cited in meaning—no need to repeat.*

Thus, the Sage is square yet does not cut,
Sharp yet does not pierce,
Upright yet does not overreach,
Luminous yet does not dazzle.
*Extended meaning:* The *Classic* says: "Upright and gentle."
*Quoted from* *The Book of Documents* [尚书] *□* *The Admonitions of Gao Yao* [皋陶谟].
Gao Yao spoke: "Du, also, in conduct has nine virtues.
It is said that if a man has virtue, then his words are adopted.
Yu asked: 'How?'
Gao Yao replied: 'Wide yet cautious, wide and gentle yet dignified and cautious.
Gentle yet steadfast, gentle and obedient yet firmly rooted.
Reverent yet respectful, earnestly reverent and meticulous.
In chaos yet respectful—chaos is order.
Agitated yet resolute, trained in agitation yet firmly determined.
Upright yet gentle, direct yet warm and temperate.
Simple yet honorable, simple yet principled.
Strong yet solid, strong and robust yet sincere.
Powerful yet righteous—powerful in courage yet virtuous.
Manifest their constant virtue—manifest means evident.
Their virtue is evident in their person, and yet they remain constant from beginning to end—truly auspicious scholars.'
*Chapter on Governing Men and Serving Heaven* [治人事天] *Fifty-Ninth.*

Now, only frugality is called early preparation.
Early preparation is the accumulation of virtue.
Accumulate virtue greatly, and nothing shall you fail to overcome.
Fail to overcome nothing, and none shall know your limit.
Know no limit, and you may have a realm.
*Extended meaning:* Mencius said: "Cultivate the mind, nurture the nature—this is how one serves Heaven."
*Quoted from* *Mencius* [孟子] *□* *Exhausting the Heart* [尽心章句上].
*Note:* "Cultivate" means to hold fast without abandoning; "nurture" means to follow without harm; "serve" means to attend without transgression.

*Extended meaning:* The utmost virtue—fire cannot burn it, water cannot drown it.
*Quoted from* *Liezi* [列子] *□* *The Yellow Emperor* [黄帝篇].
All things are without love or fear—
entering water, not drowned;
entering fire, not burned;
hewn or beaten, no pain;
struck or battered, no injury;
walking on air as on solid ground,
sleeping on void as on a couch—
clouds and mists do not obstruct his sight,
thunder and lightning do not disturb his hearing,
praise and blame do not sway his heart,
mountains and valleys do not impede his step—
his movement is divine.

*Extended meaning:* Once, Geng Sangzi dwelt on the slopes of Yu Mountain.
*Quoted from* *Zhuangzi* [庄子] *□* *Geng Sang Chu* [庚桑楚].
Once, Geng Sangzi bore the surname Geng, given name Chu, a disciple of the Old Master.
He dwelt on the slopes of Yu Mountain.
The mountain is named in the land of Lu.

The meaning already cited is complete, and no repetition is made.

**Chapter Sixty: Governing the Great Realm**
To rule the world with the Dao,
its spirits shall lack divinity.

*Extended meaning:* When birds, beasts, fish, and turtles all thrive,
then the spirits and gods also find peace.

*Quoted from* *The Book of Documents* [《尚书》] *□* *The Instructions of Yi* [《伊训》], admonishing King Tai Jia, the heir to the throne:

*"Alas! In ancient Xia, the kings and ministers diligently cultivated their virtue,
and there were no celestial calamities.
Mountains, rivers, ghosts, and spirits—
none failed to find peace.
Even birds, beasts, fish, and turtles all flourished.
But their descendants did not follow,
nor did they adhere to the Way of the former kings.
The August Heaven sent disaster,
using me as its instrument.
It attacked from Mingtiao—
Mingtiao, where Xia dwelled.
Bo, where Tang resided.
I came from Bo.
This speaks of how Jie accumulated evil at Mingtiao,
while Tang’s virtue flourished only after he settled at Bo."*

*Extended meaning:* In the *Spring and Autumn Annals*, during the 32nd year of Duke Zhuang of Lu,
a spirit descended at Cuì.

*Quoted from* *Spring and Autumn Annals* [《春秋》] *□* *Duke Zhuang of Lu, Year 32*—
already cited in full, no repetition made.

*Extended meaning:* In the 30th year of Duke Xiang,
the people of Zheng killed Boyou.

*Quoted from* *Spring and Autumn Annals* [《春秋》] *□* *Duke Xiang of Lu, Year 30*—
already cited in full, no repetition made.

*Extended meaning:* By the 7th year of Duke Zhao,
Zichan appointed Gongsun Xie and Liangzhi to pacify them.
Xie was the son of Zikong.
Liangzhi was the son of Boyou.
Two men were appointed as ministers
to ease the spirits of Zikong and Boyou.
Thus, it was settled.

Zidazu, named Youji, asked its meaning.
Zichan replied:
*"When ghosts have a place to rest,
they no longer become malevolent."*

When Zichan traveled to Jin,
Zhao Jingzi asked him:
*"Can Boyou still act as a ghost?"*
Zichan replied:
*"He can.
When life begins, it is called po [魄].
At first, it is transformation.
Once born, po becomes hun [魂] in the yang.
If one receives much vital essence from things,
then hun and po grow strong.
If one dwells in high office and enjoys abundant offerings,
then the body and po grow strong.
If one takes much vital essence from things,
then the hun-qi grows strong.
Thus, with vitality reaching divine clarity,
this is how one nourishes vitality to divinity,
and nourishes the spirit to illumination.
Vitality is the spirit before it manifests;
illumination is the spirit before it shines.
Even commoners, when they die,
their hun and po can still cling to people,
becoming licentious and malevolent.
How much more so for one of Liangzhi’s noble name—
our former lord, Duke Mu of Jin,
the grandson of Ziliang,
the son of Zier,
who held office for three generations!
If, across three generations,
they wielded political power,
and could act as ghosts—
is this not fitting?"*

*Extended meaning:* During the reign of Empress Wu of Tang,
Wu Sansi purchased two concubines.

*Quoted from* *The Zuo Tradition*—
already cited in full, no repetition made.

**Chapter Sixty-One: The Great Realm as the Lowlands**

**Chapter Sixty-One: The Great Realm as the Lowlands**

The great realm is the confluence of the world,
the meeting of all under heaven,
the receptive of all under heaven.
The receptive ever triumphs through stillness over the active,
and stillness is its natural state.

*Extended meaning:* As when the people carry their infants to come of their own accord.

Quoted from *The Analects*—**Zilu, Thirteenth Chapter**—
where Zilu asked to learn farming.
The Master said, *"I am not as skilled as the old farmers."*
He asked to learn gardening.
The Master said, *"I am not as skilled as the old gardeners."*
Zilu departed.
The Master said, *"A petty man, this Zilu!"*

When the ruler cherishes propriety, the people dare not fail to show respect.
When the ruler cherishes righteousness, the people dare not fail to submit.
When the ruler cherishes trustworthiness, the people dare not fail to act sincerely.
Thus, the people of all directions will come bearing their children upon their backs.
What need is there for farming?

Some take from below to gain,
others take by being below.

*Extended meaning:* Duke Xuan of Qi asked, *"Is there a Way to treat neighboring states?"*
Mencius replied, *"There is."*

Quoted from *Mencius*—**Discourses on King Hui of Liang, Lower**—
where Mencius said, *"Only the virtuous can serve the great with the small,
thus Tang served Ge.
From *Mencius*—**Discourses on Lord Wen of Teng, Lower**—
Mencius said: *"Tang resided in Bo, neighboring Ge.
The Lord of Ge was dissolute and did not perform sacrifices.
Tang sent someone to ask, 'Why do you not sacrifice?'
He replied, 'I have no offerings for the sacrificial beasts.'
Tang sent him cattle and sheep.
The Lord of Ge ate them but still did not sacrifice.
Tang sent someone again to ask, 'Why do you not sacrifice?'
He replied, 'I have no grain for the ritual vessels.'
Tang sent the people of Bo to till his fields, while the elderly and weak brought food.
The Lord of Ge led his people to seize those who had wine, food, or grain—
those who refused, he killed.
A boy offered him millet and meat, and he killed him and took it.
The *Book of Documents* says: 'The Lord of Ge was hostile to the offering.'
This is what is meant.
Hostile to the offering—he was an enemy to those who gave it.
King Wen served the Kunyi.
From *The Mao Commentary*—**Great Odes, Glosses**—
The rise of King Wen originated with the Great King.
It says:
*'He did not spurn the humble, nor did he reject the lowly.
The gnarled trees were uprooted, the path was made straight,
the Kunyi were subdued, their mouths were sealed.
Only the wise can serve the small with the great,
thus the Great King and King Zhou’s ancestor left Yin.
They served the Xirong.
The northern barbarians were strong—like the Xiongnu.
From *Mencius*—**Discourses on King Hui of Liang, Lower**—
Lord Wen of Teng asked: *"Teng is a small state.
If we exhaust our strength to serve a great state, we cannot escape harm.
What should we do?"*
Mencius replied: *"In ancient times, the Great King resided in Na.
The Di barbarians invaded.
He served them with furs and silk, but could not escape.
He served them with dogs and horses, but could not escape.
He served them with jade and pearls, but could not escape.
Then he gathered his elders and said: 'What the Di desire is our land.
I have heard: The virtuous do not harm others with what they nurture.
My lords, what fear is there of losing a ruler?
I will depart.'
He left Yin, crossed the Liang Mountains, and settled beneath the Qi Mountains.
The people of Yin said: 'A virtuous man—we must not lose him!'
They followed him like returning from market.
He also cited Goujian’s service to Wu.
Goujian was the name of the King of Yue.
The account appears in *The Spring and Autumn Annals* and *Records of the Grand Historian*.
Zigong advised King Gou of Yue, saying: 'If Your Majesty truly sends troops to aid him,
and uses precious treasures to please his heart,
and employs humble words to honor his rites,
this is what the sages call bending to achieve reach.'
The King of Yue sent his minister Wen Zhen to kneel before the King of Wu, saying:
'Yue offers all its warriors—three thousand men—to serve Wu.'
The King of Wu told Zigong: 'Does the King of Yue wish to come himself?'
Zigong said: 'To offer all our people, and then have their ruler come as well—
this is not proper.'
The King of Wu then accepted the King of Yue’s troops,
and thanked him for leaving Goujian behind.

The great realm seeks only to encompass others,
the small realm seeks only to serve others.
Both obtain what they desire,
thus the great realm should act as the lowly.

*Extended meaning:* The small realm presents tribute and taxes.

Quoted: *"To hold the tribute."* From *The Rites of Zhou*:
*"With birds, six offerings are made to rank the ministers.
The duke offers hides and silk, the minister offers a lamb,
the great officer offers a goose, the scholar offers a partridge,
the commoner offers a duck or its kind, and the artisans and merchants offer a chicken."*

*Extended meaning:* In the third year of Duke Wen of Lu,
the Duke went to Jin.
Quoted from *The Spring and Autumn Annals*:
*"In the third year of Duke Wen of Lu, the Duke went to Jin and made an alliance with the Duke of Jin.
The Duke of Jin feasted him and recited *The Thick and Thriving*—
'The thick and thriving is the *gai* plant,
as seen in the *Odes*—
Take it when it is visible,
the gentleman rejoices with propriety.'
Zhuangzi, descending with the Duke, bowed in thanks,
saying: 'The small state receives commands from the great state.
How dare we not be cautious in our rites?
The great state honors us with grand ceremony—
what greater joy is there?'
The Duke of Jin descended the steps to decline,
then ascended and completed the bowing.
Together, they returned to the upper steps for the completed bowing.
The Duke of Lu recited the *Joyous Odes*.
The *Joyous Odes*—from *The Great Odes*—
says: 'The bright and manifest virtue,
suitable for the people and the gentleman,
receives blessings from Heaven.'*

*Extended meaning:* In the first year of Duke Xiang,
Wei Zichu and Jin Zhizhi came as envoys—
according to ritual.
Quoted from *The Spring and Autumn Annals*:
*"In the first year of Duke Xiang, winter,
Wei Zichu and Jin Zhizhi came as envoys—ritual protocol.
Whenever a ruler ascended the throne,
small states paid homage to him—
this is serving the great with the small.
The great state sent envoys—
this is the great serving the small,
to renew friendship, establish trust,
plan affairs, and mend deficiencies.
These are the greatest of rites."*

The *Obstruction*—that is, the *Transgression*—
For the rites, what is greatest is to establish the state in peace and benefit the people.

**Extended Meaning:** *The Daoist Classic of Virtue and Power—Extended Meanings*—
*Handwritten Notes, Volume Sixteen complete.*
*Handwritten Notes, Volume Seventeen.*

**Five-Peak Qing’an Hermit Wang Shouzheng’s Collected Works**
*Chapter Sixty-Two: The Dao as the Essence of All Things*

The Dao is the essence of all things,
the treasure of the virtuous,
the safeguard of the unvirtuous.

**Extended Meaning:** All things derive their origin.

Quoted from *The Book of Changes—Symbolic Text of the Qian Hexagram*:
The *Symbol* says:
*"Great indeed is the Primordial Power of Qian!
All things derive their origin,
And it unifies Heaven.
Clouds move, rain descends—
All things take form in their kinds.
The Great Clarity completes the cycle,
Six positions established in due time—
At the appointed hour, it rides the Six Dragons to govern Heaven.
The Dao of Qian transforms and changes,
Each rectifying its nature and vital essence."*

The *Simian*—that is, the elucidation defining the meaning of a hexagram—
is so named because it *interprets* it.

It summarizes the *Qian* and the *Yuan*.
*Qian* is the name of the hexagram,
*Yuan* is the first virtue of *Qian*’s power,
hence it is paired with *Qian* to elucidate it.

*"Great indeed is the Primordial Power of Qian!"*
The *qi* of *Qian* is vast and expansive,
its essence broad and far-reaching—
and since it is the great origin from which all things arise,
it is said: *"Great indeed is the Primordial Power of Qian,
all things derive their origin."*

Thus, though one may establish a Son of Heaven
and appoint the Three Ministers,
even with a jade tablet and four horses as tribute,
it is not as valuable as advancing this Dao.

**Extended Meaning:** *Establishing the Son of Heaven, appointing the Three Ministers.*

Quoted:
*"Within the Four Seas’ vastness,
amidst the myriad things’ abundance,
when first mankind was born,
there could be no ruler—
thus the sovereign was established to shepherd them.
The Son of Heaven—
revering the Supreme Deity,
treating Heaven as father,
Earth as mother—
is called the Son of Heaven.
One person cannot govern broadly—
thus officials are appointed to oversee them,
and so the Son of Heaven has the Three Ministers."*

From *The Book of Documents [□ Zhou Officials]*:
*"It is the Three Ministers who
discuss the Dao and govern the state.
The Grand Teacher—
whose wisdom is a boundless wellspring,
whose conduct is a model for emulation—
when consulted, responds;
when sought, is found.
This is called the Grand Teacher.
Also called Shangfu.
*Tai* means great.
As the king’s teacher, he rides in a ceremonial carriage
with green canopy,
wearing a gold seal and purple sash."*

The Grand Tutor—*xun*, to instruct; *bao*, to safeguard.
From *The Great Dai Li*:
*"He transmits the Son of Heaven’s virtue and righteousness.
If the Son of Heaven shows no filial piety to his parents,
no kindness to the common people,
lacks propriety toward great ministers,
or fails in judicial impartiality—
these are the Grand Tutor’s derelictions of duty."*

The Grand Guardian—*bao*, to lean upon; *ren*, to bear responsibility.
From *The Great Dai Li*:
*"If the Son of Heaven sits improperly in his position,
receives scriptures without reverence,
speaks without proper tone,
sings without harmonic accord,
advances or retreats without rising or lowering his seat,
or neglects the rites of bowing and greeting—
these are the Grand Guardian’s derelictions of duty."*

**Extended Meaning:** When two rulers meet,
there are always the rites of tribute offerings.

Quoted from *The Rites of Zhou*.

The **Grand Tutor**—*fù*, to instruct; *rén*, to bear responsibility.
Using the six beasts as tribute offerings,
to establish the rites for two rulers meeting,
already detailed in the **Sixty-First Chapter**,
now not repeated.

**Extended Meaning:** As in the *Spring and Autumn Annals*, **Duke Xi of Zhou**, Year 33,
when the Qin army reached **Hua**,
**Zheng’s merchant Xian Gao** encountered them.
With a carriage covered in **raw oxhide** (韦, *wéi*—leather, *shú gē*—unprocessed hide),
he offered twelve **first-calved oxen** (先牛, *xiān niú*—prime oxen),
four **prime oxen** (先, *xiān*—prime; 韦牛, *wéi niú*—leather-oxen),
for the ancient custom of presenting gifts to others
always required something to precede the offering.
He said to the Qin army: *"My humble lord hears that Your Excellency is about to lead your troops against my city,
so I dare to offer provisions to your followers.
I do not presume to be lavish (不腆, *bù tiǎn*—not excessive),
but merely to provide what is sufficient.
My city’s contributions are but a day’s accumulation for those who stay,
and a night’s provisions for those who march.
Moreover, I have sent urgent messengers to Zheng,
so that they may prepare accordingly."*

**Extended Meaning:** Mencius said: *"No one from Qi dares to speak to the king with words of virtue and righteousness."*
Quoted from *Mencius*, **Chapter on Gong Sun Chou** (下, *xià*—lower part):
*"Mencius replied to Gong Sun Chou’s words: 'Your Reverence has shown me respect as a child,
but I have yet to see how Your Reverence respects the king.'
Chou said: 'Alas, what strange words are these?
Do the people of Qi not speak of virtue and righteousness to the king?
Is it that they regard virtue and righteousness as unattractive?
Their true thought is: *Why would such a one be worthy of speaking of virtue and righteousness?*
Then there is nothing greater than this in failing to show respect.
I do not dare to present the Dao of Yao and Shun before the king,
for it is not mine to offer.
Thus, the people of Qi do not respect me as I respect the king.
Why was this Dao held in such esteem in antiquity?
Was it not because it could be sought and obtained,
and with it, crimes could be pardoned?
Hence, it is most precious for all under Heaven.'*

**Extended Meaning:** As in *The Spring and Autumn Annals*, **Duke Xi of Zhou**, Year 33,
*"Summer, the 4th month, on the day *xīnwù* (辛巳),
the Qin army was defeated at **Jiao**,
capturing **Bai Li Mengming**, **Xi Qi Shu**, and **Bai Yi Bing**,
and returning them to their lord.
The Qin lord, in **plain mourning attire** (素服, *sù fú*—undyed garments),
stood outside the capital, facing his army and weeping:
*'Alone, I have disobeyed the admonitions of **Jian Shou**,
bringing shame upon my ministers—this is my fault.
I do not blame **Mengming**,
for this was my error.
What crime did the great officers commit?
Moreover, I did not cover their great virtue
with a single minor offense.
This is the meaning of:
*"Prevent difficulties before they arise,
and magnify greatness before it grows small."*
Quoted from *The Book of Changes*, **Chapter on Non-Action** (为无为, *wéi wúwéi*—"As Non-Action"),
the **Sixty-Third Paragraph**:
*'Difficulties should be addressed when they are still easy,
and greatness should be cultivated when it is still small.'*

**Extended Meaning:** *The Book of Documents* says:
*"Resentment lies not in what is clear,
but in what is unseen—this is the danger."*
Quoted from *The Five Children’s Song* (五子之歌, *Wǔ Zǐ zhī Gē*) in *The Book of Documents*:
*"Even the most foolish among the people—
one who can defeat me—
if they lose three times,
resentment does not lie in what is clear,
but in what is unseen—this is the danger.
I stand before the myriad people,
as solemn as driving six horses with a rotting whip.
How can those above not show reverence?
*'I'* refers to the five children’s self-address.
A ruler who loses the people’s hearts
becomes a solitary tyrant,
and even the most foolish among them
can defeat me.
Three losses—
this means losing many.
Resentment and estrangement of the people—
does it only become known
when it is already manifest?
One must address it
before it even arises.
Light promises bring little trust,
many minor changes lead to great difficulties.
Thus, the sage finds difficulty in this,
and so ultimately faces none."*

**Extended Meaning:** **Jin Jie Yang** had no **double commands**,
and **Lu Zhong You** had no **premature pledges**—
these are praised in both ancient and modern times.
Quoted:
*"**Jin Jie Yang** had no double commands"—
in *The Spring and Autumn Annals*, **Duke Xuān of Lu**, Year 15,
spring, *"Gong Sun Guifu met the Chu lord at Song.
The people of Song sent **Le Ying** to plead for help in Jin.
The Jin lord wished to assist them."*

Lord Bo Zong said: *"No, it cannot be done."* And so the matter was halted.

He was sent to Song by Jin, with the instruction not to surrender to Chu.
When he arrived, the people of Song said: *"The Jin army is fully mobilized and will soon arrive."*
The people of Zheng took this and presented it to Chu.
The Chu lord lavished him with heavy bribes,
urging him to reverse his words—
but he refused.
After three such entreaties,
the Chu lord had him placed upon a battle chariot
and commanded to call out to the people of Song,
declaring his intent to carry out the order of his lord.
Thus, he fulfilled his sovereign’s command.

The Chu lord prepared to execute him,
but allowed him to speak first:
*"You have already granted me leave not to submit,
yet now you demand I do so—why?"*
*"It is not that I lack trust,
but you have abandoned me—
hasten now to your punishment!"*

He replied:
*"I have heard this saying:
When a sovereign can restrain his commands, that is **virtue**;
when a subject can uphold his sovereign’s commands, that is **trust**.
To act with **trust** while guided by **virtue** is **benefit**.
If my counsel does not fail to bring **benefit**,
and I thereby safeguard the state,
I am the people’s guardian.
**Virtue** cannot tolerate **double trust**,
**trust** cannot tolerate **double commands**.
Your sovereign has entrusted me with a command,
yet you do not know it—
how can I betray it?
I received the command and set out,
knowing that death would be my fate if I failed.
If I betray it now,
how can I still be trusted?
My pledge to your sovereign
was to fulfill his command.
To fulfill his command,
even unto death,
is my reward.
My lord has a subject of **trust**,
and I have not failed in my command.
The Chu lord has already been fully persuaded—
my mission is complete.
Death?
What more could I seek?"*

The Chu lord then released him and let him return.

**Lu Zhong You** had no **premature pledges**—
this is found in *The Analects* (Book of Yan Yuan, Twelfth):
*"Host"* (here meaning *to leave a lingering matter*),
just as in *host resentment* (lingering grudge).
He was swift to act on his words,
leaving no room for doubt in his pledge.
This shows why **Zi You** (Lu Zhong You’s courtesy name)
earned such **trust** from the people.
It was due to his long-standing cultivation of **virtue**,
and so both ancient and modern times praise him.

**Extended Meaning** states:
*"In the past, when Qi attacked Lu,
they demanded the **Zhen Ding** (岑鼎).
The Lu sovereign falsely offered another vessel
and then requested a covenant.
The Qi sovereign did not trust him.
Within the **virtue** of the covenant,
the pledge was already complete—
there was no need for further confirmation."*

Quoted from *Liu Zi* (Book of **Stepping on Trust**, □):
*"In the past, when Qi attacked Lu,
they demanded the **Zhen Ding** (岑鼎).
The Lu sovereign falsely offered another vessel
and then requested a covenant.
The Qi sovereign did not trust him.
Within the **virtue** of the covenant,
the pledge was already complete—
there was no need for further confirmation."*

**Extended Meaning** states:
From the *Spring and Autumn Annals*, Duke Lǐ of the 22nd year—when the people of Zhū [a minor state] marched out due to the *Mò Qǔ* affair—**the meaning was already complete**; no further recording was needed.
**Annotation:** Though Zhū was a separate state, weakened and unable to communicate on its own, it was a private vassal of Lu, like Zhēn yú under Lu—called *"ministers of the ancestral temple and grainary."* [i.e., loyal retainers.]

**On Ease and Difficulty:**
*"The easy to hold is easy to lose,
The unseen is easy to plot against,
The brittle is easy to shatter,
The faint is easy to disperse.
Act before it exists,
Order before it is disordered."*

**Extended Meaning** states:
In the past, Duke Jǐng of Qi fell ill and sought a physician from Qin.
**Quoted from** *The Zuo Tradition*—the meaning is already complete; no further recording was needed.

*"A tree thick enough to embrace grows from a tiny sprout.
A nine-tiered terrace rises from a single clod of earth.
A thousand-li journey begins beneath one’s feet."*

**Extended Meaning** states:
*"A nine-tiered high terrace begins with a single basket of soil."*
**Quoted from** *The Analects* [□, Book of *Zǐ Hǎn* 9]:
The Master said:
*"To build a mountain, if it lacks but one basket of earth when nearly complete—stopping is my own doing.
To level ground, though it requires but one basket’s removal—advancing is my own doing."*
**Annotation:** *"Basket"* refers to a soil basket.
*The Book of Documents* says:
*"To build a mountain nine *rèn* high, its labor fails at the last basket."*
The Master’s words derive from this:
*"If the mountain is nearly complete but lacks one basket, its stopping is my own doing.
If the ground is leveled but requires one basket’s removal, its completion is my own doing."*
Truly, the scholar who does not persist in self-strengthening accumulates little from much,
But if he halts midway, all prior effort is wasted.
Whether he stops or proceeds—it lies within himself, not others.

*"The people’s undertakings often fail at the final stage.
If one treats the end as carefully as the beginning, there will be no failed matters."*

Thus, the Sage desires not what he desires not,
Does not prize difficult-to-obtain treasures.
He learns what others have already failed to learn,
Aiding the natural course of all things without forcing it.

**Extended Meaning** states:
*The Book of Documents* says:
*"To build a mountain nine *rèn* high, its labor fails at the last basket."*
**Quoted from** *The Shangshu* [□, *Lǚ Dà*]:
The Western Travelers presented a *dà*—Chu Gōng deemed it improper and composed a document to admonish King Wǔ:
*"Alas! Night and day, I never cease striving—
Not boasting of minor deeds, but cherishing the completion of great virtue.
To build a mountain nine *rèn* high, its labor fails at the last basket."*

**Extended Meaning** states:
*"None fail to begin, few complete the end."*
This is how the *Poet* criticized King Lǐ of Zhōu.
**Quoted from** *The Mao Poems* [*Dà Yá* □, *Dàng*]:
*"Dàng summoned Duke Mù of Zhōu—
Lamenting the great ruin of the Zhōu court.
King Lǐ was unprincipled; the realm was in chaos,
Lacking laws and propriety, hence this poem was made."*

*"Chaotic, the Heavenly Mandate,
The people’s sovereign is perverse."*
*"Chaotic"*—the appearance of ruined laws.
*"Heavenly Mandate"*—entrusted to the sovereign.
*"Perverse"*—the sovereign himself.
This speaks of King Lǐ ruling above the people as their sovereign,
Yet his governance was so extreme that he fell ill from heavy taxation and harsh punishments,
His decrees and commands grew increasingly twisted, deviating from ancient norms.

*"Heaven nurtures the common folk,
Yet his commands were not sincere."*
*"Common folk"*—the masses.
*"Sincere"*—truthful.
This means Heaven nurtures the people, and their commands should be taught with sincerity and loyalty.
But now it is not so—initially, the people nearly followed the good path,
Yet later they were corrupted by evil customs.
Thus, *"none fail to begin, few complete the end."*

Now, **Extended Meaning** takes this to illustrate:
*"If one treats the end as carefully as the beginning, there will be no failed matters."*

**Extended Meaning** states:
Wei Zēng of the Tang dynasty submitted a memorial to Emperor Taizong, stating:
*"Your Majesty’s ambitions, compared to the early Ac Zhenguan era, have gradually failed to persist in ten such respects."*

**Quoted:**
In the 13th year of Zhenguan (Tang), Wei Yīzhēng feared Emperor Taizong could not persist in frugality, as of late he had grown fond of extravagance, and thus memorialized:
*"In recent years, Your Majesty has gradually strayed from your former resolve.
The principles of simplicity and modesty have not been fully sustained.
I humbly list what I have heard, as follows:

In the early Zhenguan era, Your Majesty practiced non-action and non-desire,
His pure and simple governance extended far and wide.
Examining the present, this virtue has gradually declined.
How do I know this?
Han Wén and Jìn Wǔ—though not supreme sages—
Han Wén sought a thousand-*lǐ* horse, Jìn Wǔ burned the *zhùtóu* [exotic] furs.
Now, Your Majesty seeks swift horses from afar,
Buys rare treasures from foreign lands,
Accepts abandoned gifts from travelers,
And is held in contempt by barbarians.
This is the first failure to persist.

In the past, Zǐ Gōng asked Confucius how to govern people.
Confucius said:
*'Awe-inspiring, as if driving six horses with a rotting whip.'*
Zǐ Gōng said:
*'How fearful!'*
Confucius replied:
*'If one does not govern with the Dao, then I am the one who fails.'*
Thus, *The Book of Documents* says:
*'People are the foundation of the state; if the foundation is firm, the state is secure.'*
As one who rules above others, how can one not be reverent?

In the early Zhenguan era, Your Majesty treated the people as if they were wounded,
Loving them as his own children.
But in recent years, Your Majesty’s heart has turned to extravagance,
Forgotten simplicity and humility.
You say:
*'If the people are at ease, they become arrogant and idle;
If they are labored, they are easier to command.'*
From ancient times, there have been cases where the people’s ease led to the state’s ruin.
This is not the highest saying for the state’s prosperity,
Nor is it a long-term strategy for the people’s peace.
This is the second failure to persist.

In the early Zhenguan era, Your Majesty reduced his own burdens to benefit the people.
Now, Your Majesty indulges his desires while exhausting the people.
The traces of humility and frugality change yearly,
The emotions of arrogance and excess grow daily.
You say:
*'If I do not do this, it will not benefit my own body.'*
The sentiments of a subject are beyond reproach—
This is merely a way to silence advisors,
How can it be said that one chooses the good and acts upon it?"*

This is the third failure to persist.

In the early Zhenguan era, Your Majesty honed your reputation and integrity, took nothing for yourself, and associated only with the virtuous. You cherished the upright and distanced the petty.

Now it is not so. You treat the petty lightly, familiarizing yourself with them and drawing near. To be intimate with the petty is not the Dao of governance. To distance the upright is not the virtue of a flourishing state. This is the fourth failure to persist.

In the early Zhenguan era, Your Majesty modeled yourself after the sage-kings of Tang and Yu, reducing gold and returning to simplicity. In recent years, however, you have taken delight in rare and difficult-to-obtain treasures, sparing no effort to acquire them—no matter how distant. Your fondness for luxuries has grown, yet the pursuit of substance remains unattainable. This is the fifth failure to persist.

In the early Zhenguan era, Your Majesty sought the worthy as if parched. Those recommended by the virtuous were trusted and entrusted. In recent years, however, you favor and detest those whom the virtuous condemn, and those you recommend are not necessarily trustworthy. A lifetime of conduct does not vanish in an instant. Moreover, the upright cultivate benevolence and magnanimity, while the petty thrive on slander and calumny for their own gain. If Your Majesty does not discern their roots but lightly judges them, you drive the steadfast from the path while advancing the scheming, causing men to seek only to evade blame and exert no true effort. This is the sixth failure to persist.

When Your Majesty first ascended the throne, you abolished the hunting grounds within and cut off the sources of the chase without. Yet after several years, you could not sustain your resolve. Though you did not indulge in prolonged hunts, you sometimes exceeded the proper rites of the three drives, making the pleasures of the chase visible to the people and the tribute of falcons and hounds reaching even to the distant barbarians. You depart at dawn and return only at night, finding joy in galloping, heedless of unforeseen calamities. Once such recklessness takes hold, how can it be rectified? This is the seventh failure to persist.

Confucius said: *"A ruler governs his ministers with propriety, and a minister serves his ruler with loyalty."* When Your Majesty first took the throne, you received your subjects with reverence, and the benevolence of the ruler flowed downward while the loyalty of the ministers ascended. In recent years, however, you have neglected much. When they wish to speak, your demeanor is not welcoming; when they wish to petition, your grace is not extended. You often seize upon their faults and interrogate them over trivial errors. Even with the most eloquent arguments, none can fully express their loyalty. How then can you expect harmony between ruler and subjects, or mutual trust between sovereign and minister? This is the eighth failure to persist.

Arrogance must not be indulged, desires must not be gratified, pleasures must not be carried to excess, and ambition must not be sated. In the early Zhenguan era, Your Majesty diligently governed, bending yourself to serve others. Yet in recent years, you have subtly grown proud, relying on the grandeur of your achievements, and scorned the wisdom of past sages. You look down upon the present age—this is the growth of arrogance. Though you may suppress your inclinations and follow counsel, you cannot forget it—this is the gratification of desire. Your mind is set on amusement, your heart knows no satiety. Though you have not entirely abandoned governance, you no longer devote yourself wholly to the Dao—this is pleasure nearing its peak. The land is at peace, the barbarians submit, yet you still exhaust the soldiers and horses, punishing distant tribes—this is ambition nearing fulfillment. This is the ninth failure to persist.

In the time of Yao and Tang, or Shun and Wu, there were no absent disasters. Yet they were called sages because they had both beginning and end, doing nothing and desiring nothing. In recent years, however, the people have been weary from labor, and those of the Central Plains are especially exhausted. The artisans of the capital are all detained daily for forced labor. The soldiers of the regular armies are frequently sent on separate expeditions. The markets of the villages are never empty of conscripts, and the roads are filled with relays of forced laborers.

The harm once taken root, it is easy to be startled and disturbed. Should drought or flood strike, and the grain and wheat fail to harvest, the people’s hearts may not remain as settled as before. This is the tenth failure to persist.

**On External Treasures:**
The common world values gold, jade, pearls, and jewels. The *Qianziwen* commentary states: *"Gold is born in Lishui."* Lishui is in Yongchang Commandery of Yizhou, where gold often floats to the surface—all of it superior quality. Jade comes from the southern Kun Mountain. In the valleys of Kun Mountain in Chu and Jing, fine jade is found. Pearls, sometimes called *nightlight*, may reach an inch in diameter, their glow illuminating twelve chariots. *Bao* refers to rare and precious plants, minerals, feathers, and furs—whatever the world holds dear. Yet these external treasures, sought beyond measure, exhaust life and harm nature. Unlike the Daoist ruler, who treasures virtue, or the cultivator, who values the Way alone, they do not covet what lies beyond. Thus, they preserve their natural state, unburdened by rare and costly goods.

**Chapter 65: The Difficulty of Governing the People**
The people are hard to govern because their cleverness is excessive. Therefore, ruling by cleverness is ruinous to the state, while not ruling by cleverness brings fortune.

**Elaboration:**
Zhuangzi said: *"If the ruler sincerely loves cleverness but lacks the Way, the world will fall into great disorder."* The *Zhuangzi* ["Bùkē Qìe" (器蠹)] states:
*"If the ruler sincerely loves cleverness but lacks the Way, the world will fall into great disorder. How do we know this? When the cleverness of crossbows, snares, and mechanical traps increases, birds are thrown into confusion above. The net is small and fine, resembling the star *Bi*, hence its name. Shooting with a bowstring is called *yi*. The crossbow’s trigger is called *ji*. When the cleverness of hooks, bait, and nets increases, fish are thrown into confusion in the water. *Gǔ* and *zhuì* are both nets; *sǔn* is also a trap. Hooks are fishing hooks; bait is fish bait. When the cleverness of *xuēgé*—the frames for spreading nets—and *luòluò*, *jiéfú* increases, beasts are thrown into confusion in the marshes. The *Erya* says: Nets for birds are called *luò*; nets for hares are called *jié* or *fú*—all are hare nets. Cleverness in deceit, gradual poisoning, and sophistry—such as *jiéhuá* (pretense of knowledge), *jiānbái* (rigid argument), and convoluted speech—when excessive, confuses the people with debate. The *Xihua* Master’s commentary states: *"Cleverness in calculation, deceit, and gradual corruption harms things. Jiéhuá is probing; jiānbái is rigid argument; sophistry and pretense are deceitful."* The cleverness of rigid argument and the sophistry of *jiān* (contradiction) and *bái* (dogmatic assertion), along with the deceit of *jié* (pretense) and *tóngyì* (false agreement), all deviate from true principle. Thus, the world often falls into great disorder, the fault lying in the love of cleverness. For true sages, wisdom is a sharp tool for the state.

**Elaboration:**
Take Emperor Ming of Han, whose narrow and scrutinizing nature—
*The Han Shu* records this in detail; no need for repetition.

**Chapter 66: The River and Sea as Kings of All Valleys**
The river and sea reign over all valleys because they excel in humility, thus becoming kings of all valleys.

**Elaboration:**
The *Yijing* says: *"The Earth’s Way transforms fullness into flowing humility."* The *Yi* ["Qian" (谦) Hexagram] states:
*"Qian (Humility) is auspicious. The Heavenly Way descends to aid and shines brightly. The Earthly Way is low yet ascends. The Heavenly Way diminishes fullness and increases humility. The Earthly Way transforms fullness into flowing humility. Ghosts and spirits harm fullness but bless humility. Human nature abhors fullness and loves humility. Humility is exalted yet uncrossable; the virtuous person’s end is thus. Therefore, the sage, wishing to surpass others, speaks humbly. Wishing to lead others, places himself last. Thus, though in high position, he is not burdensome; though in front, he does no harm. Therefore, the world gladly follows without tiring."*

**Elaboration:**
Yet the sage knows that fullness invites loss.
*The Shangshu* ["Dà Yǔ Mó" (大禹谟)] states:
*"Thirty days, the people rebelled against the command. Yi Zhan advised Yu: 'Only virtue moves Heaven, reaching even the distant. Fullness invites loss; humility invites gain. This is the Way of Heaven.'"*
It further cites *"restraining oneself in humility and retreat"*—the fourth line of *Qian* is without misfortune, for those who do not violate principle move in accord with reason, thus facing no adversity.

**Elaboration:**
As in *Zhuangzi*, where Duke Ai of Lu asked Zhongni:
*The Zhuangzi* ["Dé Chōng Fú" (德充符)] states:

The meaning is already complete, so it shall not be repeated again.
Because he does not contend, therefore none under Heaven can contend with him.

**Elaboration:**
*The Classic of Documents* states:
*"You alone do not boast, and none under Heaven will contend with you in ability.
You alone do not vaunt, and none under Heaven will contend with you in merit."*

Quoting from *The Classic of Documents* ["Dà Yǔ Mó" (大禹谟)]:
The Emperor said, *"O, Shun, the Emperor!
Come, O Yu.
The floodwaters are vast and turbulent.
Heed my warning, and fulfill your success—only you are virtuous.
You have been diligent in the state, frugal in your household, and have not been arrogant or presumptuous—only you are virtuous.
You alone do not boast, and none under Heaven will contend with you in ability.
You alone do not vaunt, and none under Heaven will contend with you in merit.
I extol your virtue and praise your great achievements.
The celestial mandate rests upon you—you shall ascend to become the sovereign of the realm."*

*End of the hand-copied annotations for Volume Seventeen of The Daoist Classic of Virtue and Power*
*Beginning of Volume Eighteen*
*Collected Works of the Recluse Wang Shouzheng of Five-Peak Serenity*
*Chapter Sixty-Seven: "All Under Heaven"*

I possess three treasures, which I cherish and uphold:
One is called **benevolence (慈)**, two is called **frugality (俭)**, three is called **"not daring to act before all under Heaven (不敢为天下先)**."

*Annotations expand upon:*
The Great Yu cultivated his conduct, and **restraint (克检)** was his virtue.
King Wen of Zhou governed with **frugality as his Dao (化)**.

*Hand-copied excerpt from The Classic of Documents (尚书)□Yu’s Discourse (大禹谟):*
"Frugal in your household, not arrogant or presumptuous—only you are virtuous.
Again, it is said that **frugality was King Wen’s Dao**.
This is the **achievement (功致)** of the ode *"The Ram and the Magpie’s Nest"* from *The Mao Poems (毛诗)□Country Songs (国风)□羔羊□鹊巢*.
The customs of the Southern Chu state reflect King Wen’s governance, where all in office practiced **frugality and rectitude (节俭正直)**, their virtue as gentle as a ram.

*The Classic of Documents (书) states:*
‘**Reverence and frugality are the essence of virtue (恭俭惟德)**.
This is the language of *The Classic of Documents□The Officers of Zhou (周官篇)*:
**"One’s station does not invite arrogance, one’s emoluments do not invite excess—reverence and frugality are the essence of virtue, lest you bear falsehood (无载尔伪).**
To act with **virtue brings ease to the heart and rest on the day (作德心逸日休)**,
To act with **falsehood brings labor to the heart and clumsiness on the day (作伪心劳日拙).**

**Benevolence (慈)** thus enables **courage (勇)**.
**Frugality (俭)** thus enables **breadth (广)**.
**"Not daring to act before all under Heaven (不敢为天下先)"** thus enables **completion of great works and enduring leadership (成器长)**.

*Annotations expand upon:*
Mencius said: **"The virtuous have no rival (仁者无敌)."**
*Hand-copied excerpt from Mencius□The Sentences of King Hui of Liang (梁惠王章句上):*
Mencius’ reply to King Hui of Liang.
If he drowned his people, and you went to punish him—who would dare rival the king?
Thus it is said: **"The virtuous have no rival, O king—do not doubt."**

*Annotations expand upon:*
Emperor Shun was **by nature virtuous (性仁)**, and his punishments were limited to four crimes.
*Hand-copied excerpt from Mencius□The Exhaustion of the Heart (尽心上):*
Mencius said: **"Yao and Shun were virtuous by nature, Tang and Wu by personal cultivation, the Five Hegemons by borrowed virtue. If borrowed virtue does not return, how can one know it is not their own?"**

The four crimes:
**Banish Gonggong to Yzhou (幽州)**, sending him far away, like water flowing.
**Exile Huantuo to the lofty mountains (崇山)**, placing him there and not allowing him to go elsewhere.
**Drive the Three Miao to the Three Perils (三危)**, banishing them into turmoil and confinement.
**Imprison Gun at Yushan (羽山)**.
**Imprisonment (殛)** means to **detain, confine, and subject to hardship**.
With these four crimes, all under Heaven submitted.

*Annotations expand upon:*
King Tang of Shang **cultivated virtue (克仁)**, and could overcome the tyranny of Xia Jie.
*Hand-copied excerpt from The Classic of Documents□The Admonitions of Zhonghu (仲虺之诰):*
"Only the king does not indulge in sounds and colors, does not amass wealth and profit.
**He magnifies virtue in his appointments, and magnifies virtue in his rewards (德懋懋官，功懋懋赏).**
He uses people only for their own worth, and does not spare correction.
**He is broad in virtue and broad in benevolence (克宽克仁), making his trust manifest to the myriad people (彰信兆民).**

*The Admonitions of Tang (汤诰) say:*
‘The king of Xia extinguished virtue and relied on coercion, spreading his cruelty to all your myriad regions and people.
Your myriad regions and people suffered his calamities, unable to endure his torments, and together they reported their innocence to the gods above and below.
**The Dao of Heaven bestows blessings upon the virtuous and misfortune upon the licentious (福善祸淫)**,
