Bringing disaster upon Xia to **make their crimes evident (彰厥罪)**.’

*Annotations expand upon:*
King Wen **regarded the people as wounded (视民如伤)**.
*Hand-copied excerpt from Mencius□The Sentences of Li Lou (离娄章句下):*
Mencius said:
Yu **hated fine food and drink but loved virtuous words (恶旨酒而好善言)**.
Tang **held the middle course (执中)**, establishing the virtuous without regard to rank.
King Wen **regarded the people as wounded**, seeking the Dao but not yet seeing it.
King Wu **did not neglect the near (不泄迩)**, nor **forgot the far (不忘远)**.
Duke Zhou of Zhou **pondered the Three Kings (思兼三王)**, applying the **Four Affairs (四事)**.
If there were any discrepancies, he **looked upward and reflected (仰而思之)**, continuing through the night.
Fortunate if he found them, he **sat in contemplation until dawn (坐以待旦)**.’

「Extended commentary states: *"To pacify the people of the world with a single act of righteous anger—is this not courage?"*
From the hand-copied *Mencius□Liang Hui Wang Chapter (梁惠王章句下)*, Mencius replied to King Xiang of Qi:
*"This is the courage of a commoner—able to confront one man. Your Majesty seeks greater virtue.*
*The Odes say:*
*'The King’s wrath blazed forth—
He marshaled his host,
To check the march of the Zou and Ju,
To strengthen Zhou’s blessings,
And answer the world’s longing gaze.'*
*This is the courage of King Wen.
King Wen pacified the world with a single act of righteous anger.*
*Annotation (from *Odes□Great Odes□Huang Yi*):*
*‘Heru’—form of anger.
‘Yuan’—‘at’ (indicating purpose).
‘Lǚ’—the multitude.
‘È’—to restrain.
‘Cu’—‘to go’ (referring to the Min people invading the Ruan and Cu clans).
‘Dù’—‘thick’ (i.e., abundant).
‘Hù’—‘blessing’ (favor).
‘Duì’—‘to respond’ (to the people’s hopes).
*This is the great courage of King Wen.*

Extended commentary states: *The *Book of Documents* says:*
*"Be cautious with your frugal virtue,
For it holds eternal designs."*
From the hand-copied *Book of Documents□Tai Jia Shang (太甲上)*, Yi Yin spoke:
*"The ancient kings were enlightened yet humble,
Sitting in contemplation till dawn.
They sought worthy talent,
Enlightening later generations,
Lest they exceed their mandate and bring ruin upon themselves.
Be cautious with your frugal virtue,
For it holds eternal designs."*

Extended commentary states: *As when Emperor Wen abandoned the Luotai Pavilion,
Wearing coarse hemp garments himself,
Setting frugality as an example for the world.*
Hand-copied excerpt:
*Emperor Wen intended to build the Luotai Pavilion and consulted artisans, who estimated the cost at a hundred *jin* (gold coins).
The Emperor said: *"A hundred *jin*—enough to support ten average families! I maintain the palaces of my ancestors, often fearing they shame me. Why build a pavilion?"*
He wore hemp garments himself,
And though his concubines’ robes were thick silk,
They did not trail the ground,
Nor were curtains embroidered—
Thus demonstrating frugality as an example for the world.*

Extended commentary states: *As when Emperor Taizong halted palace construction,
Taking heed of Qin’s downfall,
Wearing no brocade,
Yet ensuring prosperity for both court and commoners.*
Hand-copied excerpt from *Tang Taizong’s Zhenguan Era (贞观四年诏)*:
*He ordered soldiers to repair the Buyan Palace’s Qianyuan Hall for imperial inspections.
Chancellor Zhang Xuan-su submitted a memorial urging cessation:
*"Qin Shihuang, seizing Zhou’s remnants and the strength of the Six Kingdoms,
Was told: ‘When Alang Palace was finished, Qin’s people scattered;
When Zhanghua was built, Chu’s forces fractured.
May Your Majesty take warning.
If labor is to be done, let it be halted now.
Abandon mercy for boldness,
Frugality for extravagance,
Posterity for immediate gain—
Then you are doomed."*

Extended commentary states: *Thus leading to the ultimate calamity of drowning in excess.*
From the *I Ching□Great Excess (大过)*:
*Wind (巽) below, Lake (兑) above.
Wind’s joyful movement—
With this, one crosses difficulties, and difficulties are overcome.*
*Hexagram line six: "Excessive transgression—drowning peril, yet no blame."*
*Annotation: At the extreme of *Great Excess*, the transgression is severe.
Crossing difficulties to the point of drowning.
Though perilous, the intent to save the age brings no fault.*
*Mercy—
In war, it brings victory;
In defense, it secures strength.
When Heaven intends to save, it shields with mercy.*

Extended commentary states: *As in the *Spring and Autumn Annals*, Duke Xuan of Lu, Year 15,
When Duke Huan of Qin attacked Jin at Fu Shi.*
*The event is detailed in the *Commentary*, so it is not repeated here.*

Extended commentary states: *Five chapters warn against militarism.
Chapter 30: "Do not use force to dominate the world."
Chapter 31: "Fine weapons are instruments of ill omen."
Chapter 57: "Use unconventional tactics in war."
Chapter 69: "War has its principles."
Chapter 76: "Military strength leads to defeat."

One chapter warns against killing.
Chapter 72: "Courage without restraint leads to slaughter."

Three chapters advocate frugality over extravagance.
Chapter 29: "The Sage avoids excess, extravagance, and extremism."
Chapter 67: "First, mercy; second, frugality; third, not daring to lead the world."
Chapter 59: "In governing people and serving Heaven, nothing surpasses frugality."

Fifteen chapters caution against weakness and passivity.
Chapter 3: "Weaken the will, strengthen the bones."
Chapter 40: "The weak are the Way’s application."
Chapter 55: "Though bones are weak and sinews soft, the grip is firm."
Chapter 36: "The soft overcomes the hard."
Chapter 76: "Human life begins soft and weak."
Chapter 78: "Nothing in the world is softer than water."
Chapter 39: "I call myself ‘lonely and barren.’"
Chapter 52: "Seeing what is small is clarity; holding to softness is strength."
Chapter 66: "Because it does not contend, none can contend with it."
Chapter 73: "Heaven’s Way—victory without contention."
Chapter 43: "The world’s ultimate softness conquers its ultimate hardness."
Chapter 68: "The skilled warrior does not rage."
Chapter 8: "Water benefits all things without contending."
Chapter 22: "Only the non-contending can ensure none contend with it."
Chapter 81: "The *Dao* does not contend, yet it is good at victory."
*Chapter on the Virtuous Warrior (善为士):*
Chapter 68: "The skilled warrior does not rage."

Extended commentary states: *As with Shì Nán Yí Liáo,
Who resolved the dispute between the Nine and Two families.*
From the hand-copied *Zhuangzi□Xu Wu Gui (徐无鬼)*:
*When Confucius traveled to Chu, the Chu King offered him wine.
Sun Chu’ao held the cup, while Shì Nán Yí Liáo received the wine and poured a libation, saying:
*"The ancients spoke thus—
I have heard the unsaid words,
But never spoken them myself."*
Shì Nán Yí Liáo played with a ball—
Yí Liáo, Chu’s mighty warrior.
During the White Banner Rebellion,
He killed the Prime Minister Zi Xi and Zi Qi.
Stone Qi said:
*"Shì Nán Yí Liáo—
If secured, he could rival five hundred men—
Yet when sent for, he refused."*

*Chapter on the Virtuous Warrior (善为士):*
Chapter 69: "The skilled warrior does not contend with arms, yet he is good at victory."

Sun Chu’ao lay in comfort, fanning himself with an ostrich-feather fan, while the people of Ying cast their weapons aside. Sun Chu’ao was the Prime Minister (令尹) of Chu—a man of great virtue and power (賢德). Ying was the capital of Chu. With his ease and high pillow, Chu’ao wielded his fan in leisure, yet his influence ensured that no enemy state dared invade. This demonstrates the two sons' ability to achieve such harmony.

Extended commentary states: *As with Tang’s release of Jie,
And King Wu’s conquest of Zhou—
Both men’s single burst of anger brought peace to all under Heaven.*

From the hand-copied *Shàngshū□Zhōnghuì Zhī Guì (仲虺之诰)*:
*On the first month, the 10th day (壬辰), Tang released Jie to the southern nest (南巢)—
A place name, referring to the city of Hao (巢城) in Lujiang (庐江).*
*The* *Wǔchéng (武成)* *chapter states:
*"On the 10th day of the first month, the sun’s shadow was cast to the side (旁死魄).
The next day, the 11th (癸巳), the king of Zhou marched forth from the capital,
To subdue the Shang in battle."*
*The* *Mèngzǐ□Liáng Huì Wáng Xià (梁惠王下)* *states:
*"One man alone could have balanced the world—
Yet King Wu was ashamed of such a thought."*
*This was King Wu’s martial virtue (勇).*
*King Wu too, in his single burst of anger, brought peace to all under Heaven.*

The skilled warrior does not contend (善勝敵者不爭), yet he is good at victory.
The skilled ruler does not vie for position (善用人者為之下).

Extended commentary states: *To contend over land with war,
And fill the fields with corpses—
To contend over cities with war,
And fill the cities with corpses—
This is called leading the land to devour human flesh,
A crime too heinous even for death.*
Extended commentary cites *Mèngzǐ□Lí Lóu Shàng (離娄上)*:
*"How much more so when one makes others fight fiercely,
Contending over land with war,
And filling the fields with corpses—
Contending over cities with war,
And filling the cities with corpses!
This is called leading the land to devour human flesh,
A crime too heinous even for death."*

Extended commentary states: *The* *Shǐjì (史记)* *records how the Wei envoy, General Xin (新), and General Huan (桓衍), entered Han Dan (邯郸).*
*The meaning has already been detailed in the commentary above; it will not be repeated here.*

It is further stated: *As with King Mu of Zhou’s commissioning of Jun Ya (君牙) as his stock and sinew (股肱), heart and strength (心膂).*
From the hand-copied *Shàngshū□Jūn Yá (君牙)*:
*"It is I, the lesser heir, who now upholds the legacy of Wen, Wu, Cheng, and Kang.
I am also the servant of the former kings,
Who could pacify chaos both left and right, in all directions.
My heart’s concerns are grave—
Like stepping on a tiger’s tail,
Or wading through spring ice.
Now I entrust you with my wings,
To serve as my stock and sinew (股肱),
Heart and strength (心膂),
And pierce through your old armor (舊服).
Do not shame your ancestors."*

Extended commentary states: *The* *Hànshū (汉书)* *records how the High Ancestor (高祖) ruled Han Zhong (汉中).*
*The meaning has already been detailed in the commentary above; it will not be repeated here.*

This is called the virtue of non-contention (不爭之德),
This is called the power of using others (用人之力),
This is called aligning with Heaven (配天),
The ultimate ideal of the ancients.

Extended commentary states: *As with Wei’s Wu Qi (吳起) as a general.*
*The meaning has already been detailed in the* *Shǐjì (史记)* *above; it will not be repeated here.*

*Chapter on the Use of Arms (用兵有言)* concludes.

This is called walking without walking,
Pushing without arms,
Returning without enemies,
Seizing without weapons.

Extended commentary states: *The disciplined troops of the Banded Teacher.*
Quoted from *The Book of Documents (尚书)*—*Great Yu’s Discourse (大禹谟)*—it says:
*"With utmost sincerity, one moves the spirits; how much more so with the Miao rebels!*
Yu bowed and said to Chang: *‘Indeed, let the Banded Teacher rally the troops.’*
The Emperor then displayed his refined virtue,
Dancing with the dry-grass feathers on the two tiers,
And in seventy days, the Miao rebels were subdued.
*Banded (斑), to return (还);*
*Rally (振), to discipline (整).*
This means disciplining the troops to return them.
The Banded Teacher went to the Miao state,
And rallied the troops in the capital.

Extended commentary states: *Weapons are the five arms—bows, spears, maces, halberds, and axes.*
Quoted: *The five arms are:*
*One, the bow;*
*Two, the spear—*as in the *Book of Songs (诗)*: *"The elder holds the spear."*
*Three, the mace;*
*Four, the halberd;*
*Five, the axe—*with branches, it is called an axe.
*No greater calamity than underestimating the enemy,*
For underestimating the enemy risks losing our treasure.

Extended commentary states: *Underestimating the enemy—*
As in the *Spring and Autumn Annals (春秋)*, Duke Ling of Lu, 33rd year.
Quoted from the *Spring and Autumn Annals (春秋)*, Duke Ling of Lu, 33rd year:
*The Qin troops passed through the northern gate.*
The meaning has already been detailed; it will not be repeated.

**Chapter: My Teachings Are Very Easy to Know, Very Easy to Practice (吾言甚易知章第七十)**

My teachings are very easy to know,
Very easy to practice.
Yet none under Heaven can know them,
None can practice them.

Extended commentary states: *Mencius said:*
*"The Dao is like a great road;*
Yet who would say it is difficult to know?
People fail because they do not seek it earnestly.
If you return and seek it,
There will be many teachers.
This means the Dao is not difficult to know.
If you return and seek it,
Regarding filial piety and respecting elders,
Then all ten thousand principles within human nature are complete.
Teachers can be found everywhere without fail,
There is no need to stay here and receive instruction.
Thus, the Sage wears coarse cloth while holding precious virtue."*

Extended commentary states: *Zhuangzi saw Duke Ai of Lu.*
Duke Ai said: *"Lu has many Confucians (儒)."*
Quoted from *Zhuangzi*—*The Master’s Son, Tian Zi Fang (田子方篇)*—
The meaning has already been fully detailed; it will not be repeated.

**Chapter: Know Not Knowing (知不知上章第七十)**

Know not knowing—*this is superior.*
Not knowing knowing—*this is a sickness.*

Extended commentary states: *The Dao cannot be reached by mere thought and deliberation; thus, it cannot be known.*
Quoted from *Zhuangzi*—*The Wanderings of the Knowing (知北游篇)*—it says:
*Zhi Bei wandered above the Dark Waters,*
Ascended the Hidden Peak’s hill,
And met the Master of Non-Action.
Zhi Bei said to the Master of Non-Action:
*"May I ask you, Master:
What thoughts and deliberations lead one to know the Dao?
What place and manner bring one to dwell in the Dao?
What path and method allow one to attain the Dao?"*
The Master of Non-Action gave no answer.
Not that he refused to answer,
But that he did not know how to answer.

Extended commentary states: *The Master said to Zigong:*
*"Zigong, do you think I am one who learns much and knows it all?
No.
I merely use one principle to penetrate all things."*
Quoted from *The Analects*—*Chapter on Duke Ling of Wei (卫灵公十五)*—it says:
The Master said: *"It is not that I learn much and know it all,
But that I use one principle to penetrate all things."*
This means that all virtues under Heaven are unified by one principle.

Extended commentary states: *Zhuangzi said:*
*"The depth of not knowing is profound,
The shallowness of knowing is slight.
Not knowing is inward,
Knowing is outward.
Not knowing aligns with reason, thus profound and mysterious within,
Knowing defies the Dao, thus coarse and superficial without.
Only the sick worry about sickness,
And thus, they do not fall ill."*

Extended commentary states: *The Master said:*
*"The noble person worries about his own lack of ability,
Not about others’ failure to recognize him."*
Quoted from *The Analects*—*Chapter on Duke Ling of Wei (卫灵公十五)*—it says:
The noble person studies for himself,
Not seeking others’ recognition.
The Sage does not fall ill,
For he worries about his own lack of ability,
And thus, does not fall ill.

Extended commentary states: *As in* *Zhuangzi* *—*
*The recluse Nan Guo Zi Qi sat hidden in his chamber.*
Quoted from *Zhuangzi*—*The Spiritless Xu (徐无鬼篇)*—
The meaning has already been detailed; it will not be repeated.

**Chapter: The People Do Not Fear the Threat (民不畏威章第七十二)**

The people do not fear the threat,
Then the greatest threat arrives.

Extended commentary states: *That which is feared as a threat is called a threat.*
Quoted: In the *Spring and Autumn Annals (春秋)*, Duke Xiang of Lu, 31st year,
The Duke of Wei was in Chu.
North Palace Wenzi saw the Minister of War’s imposing manner and said to the Duke of Wei:
*"The Minister of War resembles a ruler with ambitions beyond his station.
His speech and bearing are not constant."*
The Duke said: *"How do you know this?"*

The Minister replied: *"The* **Odes** *say:*
*'With reverence and caution toward dignity and bearing,
This is the standard for the people.'*
If the Minister of War lacks dignity and bearing,
The people have no standard to follow.
That which the people do not emulate,
If it exists among those above them,
They cannot endure it long.
The Duke said: 'Well spoken!'
What do you mean by *dignity and bearing*?'

The Minister replied: *"There is the awe-inspiring aspect that commands fear,
And the conduct that serves as a model—
This is called *dignity and bearing*.
The ruler possesses the ruler’s *dignity and bearing*;
His ministers, in awe yet loving him,
Take him as their standard and emulate him.
Thus he can hold his state,
And his fame endure through generations.
The minister possesses the minister’s *dignity and bearing*;
His subordinates, in awe yet loving him,
Thus can guard their offices,
And preserve their clans, making their households prosperous."*

Extended commentary states: *The virtuous person has three things to fear:*
*Fear of Heaven’s command,*
*Fear of the great ones,*
*Fear of the words of the sages.*
Quoted from *The Analects*—*Chapter on the Ji Family (季氏第十六)*—
Confucius said:
*"The virtuous person has three things to fear:*
*Fear of Heaven’s command,*
*Fear of the great ones,*
*Fear of the words of the sages.*
The petty person does not know Heaven’s command and does not fear it,
He treats the great ones with familiarity and scorns the words of the sages.
He does not narrow his dwelling,
Nor exhaust his life’s sustenance.
Only because he does not exhaust it,
Is he not exhausted."*

Extended commentary states: *The heart is the dwelling of the spirit.*
Quoted from *Zhuangzi*—*Knowing the North (知北游)*—
**Bìxiè** questioned **Pīyī** while he sat with his robe unfastened.
**Bìxiè** was a disciple of **Wáng Ní**,
And **Pīyī** was **Wáng Ní**’s teacher.
**Pīyī** said:
*"If you straighten your form,
And unify your gaze,
The harmony of Heaven will arrive.
Gather your knowledge,
And unify your measure,
The spirit will come to dwell within you.
Your *virtue* will become your adornment,
And the *Dao* will become your abode."*

The extended commentary states:
*"Only by purging defilement and stilling thought,
And calming the heart to hold fast to Oneness,
Does one attain the Way’s essence.
This is quoted from the *Sutra of Ascension to the West* (《西升经》), in the chapter on Precepts and Admonitions.
The Most High [Laozi] transmitted the scripture to Master Yin [Emperor of Truth], and when the transmission was complete, he prepared to ascend to Taixi [the Celestial Pole].
Thus, he spoke again to Master Yin:
*'Purge defilement, still thought,
Calm the heart, hold fast to Oneness.
When all defilements are purged, all matters are complete—
This is the essence of my Dao.
Therefore, the Sage knows himself without self-regard,
Loves himself without self-exaltation,
And thus discards that to take this.'*

The extended commentary states:
*'Nanguo Ziqiu sat reclined at his ease.'
This is quoted from *Zhuangzi*—*Equating Things* (《齐物篇》).
The meaning is already complete; no need to repeat it.

The extended commentary states:
*'If one were to govern as Lie Yukou did,
He would turn back mid-course.'
This is quoted from *Zhuangzi*—*Lie Yukou*.
The meaning is already detailed; no need to repeat it.*

The *Extended Commentary on the Dao/Way and Virtue Classic* (《道德真经衍义》) concludes its eighteenth handwritten scroll.
The *Extended Commentary on the Dao/Way and Virtue Classic* begins its nineteenth handwritten scroll.
Collected by the reclusive scholar of Five Peaks, Wang Shouzheng (王守正).
Chapter Seventy-Three: *Courage in Daring* (《勇於敢》)."

Knowing these two [paths], one may bring benefit or harm.
What Heaven abhors, who can fathom its reason?
Thus, even the Sage finds this difficult.

The *Extended Commentary* states:
Mencius said: *"Is the archer less benevolent than the coffin-maker?
The archer fears not striking a man, while the coffin-maker fears striking one.
So too with sorcerers and artisans—thus, their methods must be approached with caution."*
**Note:** *Han* (函) means *armor* (甲).

The heart of compassion is innate in all.
The archer’s intent is not inherently less benevolent than the coffin-maker’s.
The sorcerer invokes blessings for life’s continuation,
The artisan crafts coffins—serving death’s necessity.
It is further said: *"Those who align with Heaven endure; those who defy it perish."*
Quoting from *Mencius—*[*Li Lou (离娄) Upper*]:
Mencius said: *"In an age of Dao, lesser virtue serves greater virtue, lesser wisdom serves greater wisdom.
In an age without Dao, the weak serve the strong, the small serve the great.
These two outcomes are Heaven’s decree.
Those who align with Heaven endure; those who defy it perish.
In an age of Dao, all cultivate virtue, and rank accords with its magnitude.
In an age without Dao, virtue is neglected, and only brute force dictates servitude.
Heaven’s Way is the natural order of principle and efficacy.
Heaven’s Dao conquers without strife, responds without speech, and arrives without summons.
Its designs unfold effortlessly and wisely."*

The *Extended Commentary* states:
*"What does Heaven speak?*
The four seasons proceed, myriad things flourish—what does Heaven speak?"
Quoting from *Analects—*[*Yang Huo (阳货) Seventeenth*]:
The Master said: *"I wish to say nothing."*
Zigong replied: *"If you speak not, how shall we be enlightened?"*
The Master said: *"What does Heaven speak?
The four seasons proceed, myriad things flourish—what does Heaven speak?"*

The *Extended Commentary* states:
*"Yan Hui, the foremost disciple of Confucius’ school, the greatest of benevolent sages, yet Heaven cut short his life."*
Quoting from *Analects—*[*Yong Ye (雍也) Sixth*]:
Duke Ai asked which disciple was most studious.
Confucius replied: *"There was Yan Hui, who loved learning.
Alas, his life was brief—he has passed.
Now, he is gone."*

Some say he died at thirty-two.
Zhuangzi records: *"He died at eighteen."*

The *Extended Commentary* states:
*"Dizhi the Bandit was the robber of Dongling—
though the origin of violence and oppression,
he yet preserved his natural lifespan."*

Quoting from *Zhuangzi—[The Bandit Dizhi]*:
*"Dongling"*—a place name.
*"Dizhi"*—the Bandit, younger brother of Liu Xiaohui.
The *Book of Han* states:
*"Dizhi was a great thief of Qin,
bold and resolute, gathering thousands of armed men
to roam the world.
He plundered the feudal lords, drove off their cattle and horses,
seized their wives and daughters—
the common people suffered greatly."*

The *Extended Commentary* states:
*"Deng You perished at the end of Yongjia under Shi Le.
It also cites Li Maozhen, the Jiedu of Fengxiang in the late Tang.
These two events are fully recorded in the *Yi Zhong*—
no need to repeat them here."*

**Chapter Seventy-Four: The People Do Not Fear Death**
*"The people do not fear death—
why then threaten them with death?"*

The *Extended Commentary* states:
*"Man is born in stillness—
this is the nature of Heaven."*

Quoting from *Records of Rites—[The Record of Music]*:
*"Man is born in stillness—
this is the nature of Heaven.
Stirred by things, he moves—
this is the desire of nature."*

This speaks of man’s original state,
when he received Heaven’s pure, natural essence—
untainted and clear.
But once stirred by external influences,
desires arise, passions clash,
and the heart’s cravings defile the nature—
then Heaven’s pure essence is obscured and cannot manifest.

The *Extended Commentary* states:
*"When governance is burdensome and punishments severe,
the people have no place to turn."*

Quoting from *Analects—[Zilu Thirteenth]*:
*"..."*

「Zilu said: "The ruler of Wei awaits you to govern, what would you prioritize?"
The Master replied: "You must first rectify the names."
Zilu said: "Indeed, Master, your ways are so roundabout. Why rectify names?"
The Master said: "How uncouth, Ru! A gentleman, when faced with what he does not know, simply remains silent.
When names are not rectified, words lack harmony.
When words lack harmony, deeds cannot be accomplished.
When deeds cannot be accomplished, rituals and music do not flourish.
When rituals and music do not flourish, punishments are not just.
When punishments are not just, the people have no place to turn.
There will always be those who execute the kill—
this is what the *Extended Commentary* calls *‘substituting the executioner to hew.’*

The *Extended Commentary* states:
*"Like Shun’s punishment of the Four Evils,
or the Duke of Zhou’s handling of the Guans and Caix,
this is the case."*

Quoting from *The Book of Documents—[Shun’s Canon]*:
*"Exile Gonggong to Yōuzhōu,
banish Huàntuó to Chóngshān,
banish the Sānmáo to Sānwēi,
slay Gǔn at Yǔshān.
With these four punishments, all under Heaven submitted."*

And citing the Duke of Zhou’s actions against the Guans and Caix from *The Book of Documents—[The Golden Seal]*:
*"After King Wu’s demise,
Guan Shu and his band of brothers spread false rumors in the state:
‘The Duke will harm the young king.’
Guan Shu, whose name was Xiān,
was King Wu’s younger brother,
and the Duke of Zhou’s elder brother.
His fellow brothers included Cai Shu, whose name was Dù,
and Huo Shu, whose name was Chǔ.
False rumors—words without foundation,
like flowing water, spreading from there to here."*

The *Extended Commentary* states:
*"The Southern Palace holds the Vermilion Register,
awarding merit and overseeing life;
the Northern Palace holds the Black Ledger,
recording offenses and presiding over death."*

Quoting from *The Three Elements Scripture*:
*"The Southern Palace holds the Vermilion Register,
awarding merit and overseeing life;
the Northern Palace holds the Black Ledger,
recording offenses and presiding over death.*
These four stars examine humanity’s good and evil,
judging the weight of their deeds,
to admonish and encourage all,
so that the world may cultivate virtue and avoid vice.
Those who do not cultivate virtue,
the Heavenly Executioners will slay them themselves.
Heaven’s gaze is clear—
none escape its scrutiny.
Heaven’s net is vast,
though wide, it does not fail to catch.
To defy Heaven and invert the Dao—
how could one evade retribution?

To substitute the Executioner’s role in killing—
this is called *‘substituting the master carpenter to hew.’*
To substitute the master carpenter’s hewing—
rarely does one not harm their own hands."*

The *Extended Commentary* states:
*"Heaven punishes the guilty with the Five Punishments."*

Quoting from *The Book of Documents—[Gāoyáo’s Discourse]* (Fourth Annotation):
*"Heaven punishes those who commit crimes,
applying the Five Graded Punishments to admonish them.
To bestow noble titles and administer rewards and punishments—
this is the duty of the sovereign.
When the ruler’s ministers employ them,
they must do so with diligence and not slackness."*

The *Extended Commentary* states:
*"The people of Qi attacked Yan;
some asked Mencius: ‘Is there justification for urging Qi to attack Yan?’"*

Quoting from *Mencius—[Gōngsūn Chǒu, Lower]*:
*"Shen Tong asked whether Yan could be attacked.
This has already been recorded in the *‘Righteousness’* section—
no need to repeat it."*

The *Extended Commentary* states:
*"King Ling of Chu said:
‘I have slain many a man’s son.
Could it be that Heaven is punishing me?’"*

Quoting from *The Spring and Autumn Annals*, Year 13 of Duke Zhao:
*"The Chu prince Bǐ, along with his four clansmen,
invaded Chu and killed Prince Lù and Prince Bàdí.
When the king heard of the deaths of the princes,
he threw himself beneath the carriage and cried:
‘How could people love their own sons
as I love mine?’
The king said:
‘I have slain many a man’s son—
could it be that I have escaped this fate?
Is this not Heaven’s retribution upon me?’"*

The *Extended Commentary* states:
*"The Qin’s cruelty was widespread—
this is not worth mentioning."*

Quoting from *The Comprehensive Mirror to Aid in Government*, Sima Guang said:
*"When Qin Shǐhuáng was poisoning the world,
Meng Tian acted as his enforcer—
his lack of humanity is clear.
Yet, though he had no crime to merit execution,
he remained steadfast to his death—
this is also worthy of commendation."*

The *Extended Commentary* states:
*"Li Junxian was killed due to suspicion and hearsay."*

Quoting from *The Comprehensive Mirror*, Year 22 of Zhenguan:
*"Initially, the General of the Martial Guard,
Li Junxian, the Marquis of Wǔlián County,
stood watch at the Xuanwu Gate.
At the time, the Great White Star appeared in daylight,
and the astrologers divined:
‘A woman will prosper.’
Commoners also spread the *Secret Record*:
‘After the Tang’s third generation,
a female sovereign, the Martial King,
will inherit the realm.’
The Emperor loathed this.
At a banquet with the martial officers in the palace,
he commanded them to recite their childhood names.
Li Junxian spoke his own name as Wǔniáng.
The Emperor was startled,
then laughed and said:
‘What kind of woman is this?
Yet how bold and vigorous she is!’
Moreover, Li Junxian’s official title and封邑 all bore the character *‘martial’* (武),
deepening the Emperor’s disdain.
Later, he was sent to govern Huá Zhōu,
where he was executed.
He was killed due to suspicion and hearsay."*

The *Extended Commentary* states:
*"Wang Jingwen was killed due to jealousy and distrust."*

Quoting:
*"During the first year of Emperor Ming of Song’s reign,
when the Emperor’s illness grew severe,
he feared that after his death,
the Empress Dowager would rule as regent.
Wang Jingwen, as the uncle of the Emperor’s generation,
would surely become the Chancellor.
His clan was powerful,
and some might harbor treacherous intentions,
so the Emperor sent envoys to grant Jingwen’s death.
He wrote in his own hand:
‘I have dealt with you as such,
to preserve your family’s safety,
thus this arrangement.’
Jingwen accepted the imperial decree and drank poison,
dying immediately.
He was killed due to jealousy and distrust."*

The *Extended Commentary* states:
*"The scholars of Han and Tang,
plotted to exterminate the eunuchs—
the eunuchs’ crimes were indeed grave."*

Quoting from *The Detailed Annals of the Eastern Han*:
*"During Emperor Ling of Han’s reign,
the General of the Army, Hé Jìn,
and the warlord, Dǒng Zhuó,
conspired to eliminate the eunuchs.
The eunuchs’ crimes were indeed grave."*

The Clerk Chen Lin remonstrated, saying:
*"The* Classic of Changes *declares, ‘Even a fawn cannot be captured without alarm.’*
To hunt a deer is still to follow its tracks—
*Without alarm* means it cannot be done.
The proverb says, ‘Plucking sparrows with covered eyes.’
Even small things cannot be deceived—
how much less can one deceive when in power?
Much less can one establish deceit in great national affairs!"

Hé Jìn would not listen.
He then summoned the regional garrisons, speaking of exterminating the eunuchs.
Hé Jìn’s resolve wavered, and later, when he entered the palace, he was beheaded by Zhang Let, Qu Mu, and others.

From *The Detailed Annals of the Tang*:
The eunuch Cheng Yuanzhen, a native of San Yuan in Jingzhao,
served Emperor Daizong of Tang and was promoted to General of the Cavalry.
At the beginning of Guangde,
the Taichang Scholar Gǒu Liú remonstrated in a memorial:
*"The people’s resentment stems from Your Majesty’s distance from the worthy and virtuous,
your reliance on eunuchs,
your estrangement of generals and ministers,
and the near-destruction of the dynasty.
If you wish to preserve the imperial lineage,
you must behead Yuanzhen and proclaim it to the realm."*

The memorial was received, and an edict was issued to strip Yuanzhen of all titles and ranks,
exiling him to Chenzhou, where he died.

Though five centuries separated Han and Tang,
both fell into chaos and ruin,
yet they followed the same path.
The calamity of eunuchs—
whether they wielded military power
or relied on favor—
is a root of disaster that cannot be fully eradicated.
Yet it is not Heaven’s will to abandon them;
it is the work of human plotting.

The *Yì* states: *"The Chu-Han War."* It quotes *Tongjian*: *"In the fourth year of Emperor Gaozū’s reign, Qiāo Chè said: Now that Chu and Han are locked in contention, the land is drenched with the blood of its people, their bones exposed in the wild—numberless father and sons. The *Yì* states: *"The chaos of the Five Dynasties saw ten families reduced to one, slaughter and annihilation rampant."* It quotes *Tongjian’s Detailed Notes*, where Hú says: *"The Five Dynasties’ turmoil—this ‘flooding of rivers and seas’—where subjects slew their sovereigns, sons slew their fathers, the innocent were butchered, blood ran like streams, corpses blanketed the fields—all these were the work of human desire to kill, and none escaped the fate of harming their own hands like the carpenter’s axe. Such was the inevitable injury."*

**The People’s Hunger (Chapter 75):**
*"The people’s hunger arises from the heavy burdens of their rulers’ taxes. Thus, hunger is born."*

The *Yì* states: *"Duke Ai asked Yǒu Ruò: ‘In a year of famine, when resources are scarce, what should we do?’"* It quotes *The Analects (Lún Yǔ, □Yán Wén, 12th Chapter)*: *"Yǒu Ruò replied to Duke Ai’s words."*

You Ruò replied: *"Why not abolish the tithes?"*
The Duke said: *"If I reduce them to one in ten, I still lack sufficient revenue—how could I abolish them entirely?"*
**Simplified:** *Simplified* means *universal*—the practice of the *shí-yī* (tenth) tax being uniformly applied across the realm, hence *simplified*.

---
**Extended Commentary:**
The *Yòu* (幽王) of Zhou grew oppressive in taxation, and the poem *"Chǔ Cí"* (《楚茨》) censures him.
Quoted from *The Mao Commentary on the Odes* (《毛诗》□《小雅》□《楚茨》).
It censures King Yòu for his heavy burdens.
Excessive governance and taxation left fields and orchards barren, famine and plague descended, and the people perished in droves, fleeing en masse. Sacrifices went unfulfilled, hence the virtuous reflect upon antiquity.

**Extended Commentary:**
When the Duke of Wèi imposed heavy exactions, the poem *"Shuò Shǔ"* (《硕鼠》) arose.
Quoted from *The Mao Commentary on the Odes* (《毛诗》□《国风》□《硕鼠》).
The commoners censured their ruler for his greed, devouring the people like a great rat, neglecting governance, and hoarding wealth in fear like a monstrous rodent.

**Extended Commentary:**
As with Emperor Dàzōng of Táng hunting at Xīndiàn—already fully addressed, no repetition needed.

---
The people are difficult to govern because their rulers act excessively—thus, they are difficult to govern.

**Extended Commentary:**
The ruler’s gluttony for *jīxiān yùshí* (击鲜玉食)—*freshly slaughtered meat and jade food*—is a sign of decadence.
Quoted from *The Liezi* (《列子》□《周穆王》).
The *Huàrén* (化人) presented jade garments monthly and jade delicacies daily, yet still the ruler was not satisfied.
The mention of *jade garments and jade food* merely emphasizes their extravagance.

**Extended Commentary:**
Emperor Wǔ of Hàn ruled through strict legal codes—already fully recorded in *The Annals of Hàn*, no need to repeat.

---
The people grow reckless with death because their desire for life is insatiable—thus, they grow reckless with death.

**Extended Commentary:**
To the point where they would tread upon fire and water without fear.
Quoted from *The Analects* (《论语》□《卫灵公第十五》):
The Master said: *"The people’s attachment to *rén* (virtue) is greater than to water and fire. I have seen those who tread upon fire and die, but never those who tread upon virtue and die."*

Again, it cites those who face blades without flinching—this is from *The Zhōng Yōng* (《中庸》):
The Master said: *"The affairs of the world and the state may be equalized, honors and emoluments may be declined, blades may be trodden upon—but the *Zhōng Yōng* (Middle Way) cannot be attained."*

**Extended Commentary:**
As in *Zhuāngzi’s* account of the Great King (Dàwáng) dwelling at Qín:
Quoted from *The Zhuāngzi* (《庄子》□《让王篇_):
The Great King Zhòufù resided at Qín, but the *Dì* (狄人) attacked. Zhòufù—father of King Jì and ancestor of King Wén—refused their tribute in silk and horses, for they sought only land. He said: *"To dwell with a man’s brother while slaying his sibling, to dwell with a man’s father while slaying his child—I cannot bear it. Let them all reside here. What difference is there between serving me and serving the Dì?"*
Moreover, he had heard: *"Do not harm the nourisher with what is used to nourish."* Thus, he took his staff and departed.
The people followed in chains, and he established his state beneath the Qí Mountains.
The Great King Zhòufù may be said to have truly honored life.
Those who honor life neither, in wealth and nobility, harm their nourishment, nor, in poverty and lowliness, burden their form with gain.

**Extended Commentary:**
The mouth craves rich flavors, the body fine garments, the eyes beautiful colors, the ears pleasing sounds—
Quoted from *Zhuāngzi’s* words to Confucius in *The Thief Zhí* (《盗跖》).
Already detailed within, no need to repeat.

The span of human life is seventy-six years.
Thus, when strength is excessive in arms, it cannot prevail;
When strength is excessive in wood, it snaps.

**Extended Commentary:**
As when Wang Mang dispatched Wang Yi and Wang Lin to pacify the Eastern Plains.
Quoted from *The Han Biographies*, Section 69.
Wang Mang, styled Jùjūn, was the son of Emperor Xuan’s empress’s younger brother.
Ban Gu’s eulogy states:
*"Wang Mang first arose from the clan of the imperial consort,
Curbed his nature and exerted himself in virtue,
Seeking fame and reputation through sincerity.
His kin praised his filial piety, his teachers and friends revered his benevolence.
When he held office and assisted in governance,
During the reigns of Emperor Cheng and Emperor Ai,
He labored diligently for the state,
Upright in his conduct, his deeds always commended—
How could he be one who, though praised at home and abroad,
Feigned virtue while acting contrary to it?"*

Yet Mang, though lacking in virtue, possessed cunning and deceitful talent,
And leveraged the four generations of his family’s power,
Encountering a weakened Han dynasty with three severed imperial lines,
While the dowager empress’s longevity made him the clan’s patron—
Thus he indulged his treachery, consummating the crime of usurpation.

Speaking of this, it was also the work of fate,
Not something human strength could govern.

When he usurped the throne and ruled illegitimately,
His tyranny over the people reached the heavens,
His cruelty and wickedness knew no bounds,
His poison spread across the Central States,
His chaos extended to the barbarian lands—
Yet he still had not sated his desires.

Thus, within the Four Seas, the people lost their joy in life,
Their voices rose in tumult, their words clamored like dust.
Both court and commoners seethed with anger,
From near and far, they rose in revolt.
Cities fell unguarded, the state fractured,
Until the land’s towns and cities became ruins,
Mounds and graves were dug up,
Harm extended to all living beings,
And guilt fell upon the bones of the dead.

Among the treacherous ministers and wicked tyrants recorded in history,
None matched Wang Mang in the scale of his ruin.

Some say: *"Later Han’s Emperor Guangwu, with eight thousand men,
Defeated Wang Mang’s army of a million and executed him,
Restoring the Han dynasty to its former glory."*

**Extended Commentary:**
The Qin King Fú Jiān mobilized ninety-seven hundred thousand soldiers.
Quoted from: According to *The Book of Jin*,
Fú Jiān, styled Yǒnggù, was Jiān’s grandson and Xióng’s son.
Having usurped the imperial title, he appointed Wáng Měng as chancellor,
And pacified all the states—only the southeastern corner remained for Jin.
When Měng died, Jiān frequently convened his ministers,
Desiring to attack Jin.
The ministers all cited the Year Star and the Constellation Star in the Dipper and Ox,
Saying Jin’s division was not yet ripe for conquest.
There were omens of long stars in wells and water shadows,
And other portents too numerous to recount.
The monk Dào Ān joined in pleading against the war, but Jiān would not listen.

Thus he conscripted ninety-seven hundred thousand soldiers,
A thousand li long, their banners stretching into the distance—
Yet Mùróng Wěi, Mùróng Chuí, and others all secretly held back their troops,
And the army reached the city of Yī.
Jin’s Xiè Xuán, styled Yòuliú, led his forces to meet the enemy.
Jiān advanced to Leisurely Hill, arraying his troops along the Fēishuǐ River.
Xuán’s army could not cross.
Xuán sent word to Fú Róng:
*"You have crossed far into our lands,
Yet camp beside the river in formation—
Do you not wish for swift battle?"*
The troops gradually withdrew,
Allowing their soldiers room to maneuver.
Xuán said to his generals:
*"Let us rein in our horses and observe—
Is this not delightful?"*
Jiān’s men all said:
*"We should block the Fēishuǐ River,
Preventing them from crossing.
Our numbers are vast, theirs few—
We must ensure complete victory."*
Jiān said:
*"Retreat the army,
Let them pass,
And I will press my iron cavalry—
Hundreds of thousands strong—
To the riverbank and slaughter them."*
Róng also agreed.
Thus Jiān ordered the retreat,
And the troops, in confusion, could not halt.

「Then Xuán, along with Xié Yàn and Huán Yī, led eight thousand elite troops across the Fēishuǐ River. Xié Ān’s stone army withdrew slightly from Zhāng Wēi’s forces, and Xuán and Yàn pressed forward, engaging in a decisive battle south of the Fēishuǐ River.

Firm struck by an arrow in mid-stream, he beheaded Fú Róng on the battlefield. The enemy forces fled in disarray, trampling one another and drowning in the river, their numbers beyond reckoning. The Fēishuǐ ran red with blood. The remaining troops abandoned their armor and fled in the night; upon hearing the sound of wind and cranes, they mistook it for the approach of the imperial forces.

Exposed to the elements and suffering from hunger and cold, seventeen or eighteen out of every ten perished. Firm was thus utterly defeated and retreated to Cháng’ān.

Later, Mùróng Chōng raised his army, causing great turmoil within the Passes. Mùróng Chuí also rebelled against Firm and returned to Yè, proclaiming himself the Later Yān. Yáo Xīn likewise rebelled against Firm, proclaiming himself the Later Qín. Additionally, Lǚ Guāng declared himself the Later Liáng in Liáng State. Rebels and deserters proliferated like bristles on a hedgehog, with uprisings erupting everywhere. Cháng’ān was besieged and pressed by Mùróng Zhòng, its inhabitants facing dire peril.

Firm then left his eldest son, Tàizǐ Hóng, to defend the city, and fled with five hundred horsemen to Wǔjiāng Shān. There, he was captured by Yáo Xīn’s general Yǐn Wéi and delivered to Xīn, who ordered his abdication. Firm cursed him, and was slain by Xīn.

The realm’s Tàizǐ Hóng, along with Zhāng Dàfū and others, surrendered the city to Mùróng Chōng, and the state thereby perished.

Yǎn Yì further notes: If it were Emperor Yáng of the Sui Dynasty, commanding four hundred thousand troops to assemble at Píngyáng.

Quoted from *The Book of Sui*: Emperor Yáng’s given name was Guǎng. Upon Emperor Wén’s ascension, he was封 as Jìn Róng. His nature was one of artificial display; though he was praised for his仁孝 virtue, he deposed the heir-apparent and installed himself instead.

After taking the throne, he visited Luòyáng, established the Eastern Capital, and conscripted over a hundred thousand able-bodied men from Hé Nán to dredge the Píng Canal, reaching Huái and Sì. He commissioned a hundred thousand dragon and phoenix vessels, establishing their collective name. All were adorned with gold and jade, and he then proceeded to Jiāngdū.

His fleet stretched unbroken for a thousand miles, the prow and stern of his ships linked in endless succession.»

Eight years into his reign, he personally led troops to campaign against Liáo, claiming an army of two hundred thousand. The battle against Gāolí failed, and he recalled his forces.

In the thirteenth year, he visited Jiāngdū—now Yángzhōu—where the ancient Chǔ royal palace stood. There, Lǐ Mì, Dòu Jiàndé, and others raised their banners, and bandits proliferated, seizing and plundering counties across all the **Dào**. Rebel leaders vied for prominence, and no state or prefecture in the empire remained free of them. To compound the misery, famine and drought ravaged the land, with men and women dying in such numbers they could not be counted. Thus, Emperor Gāozǔ of Táng raised the banner of righteousness in Tàiyuán, entered Cháng'ān, and rescued the empire from its calamities, replacing Emperor Yáng (also called Yì or Shì).

Yáng was the son of the reigning sovereign. Initially, he was honored as the **Shànghuáng**, but later he was killed by Yǔhuà Guāngjí.

The following year, the Sui dynasty abdicated in favor of the Táng.

Emperor Gāozǔ and Emperor Tàizōng seized the opportunity to restore order, conquering the empire with merely three hundred thousand troops—like pulling down rotting wood or crushing withered branches—until the realm was finally pacified. Such were the fates of **Fú Jiān** and Emperor Yáng: true **"fēnbēng"** in the making.

As the *Lǔyǔ* states: *"A state divided by strife and disintegration is thus."* All the above examples share one fate: they relied on their strength and waged war to seek victory, yet met crushing defeat from which they could not recover.

As for the **Yìzhōng** of the *Chūnqiū* (Lǐ Gōng), when **Qín Mù Gōng** sought to attack Jìn, the matter has already been detailed in the **Yìzhōng**—no need to repeat it here.

Thus concludes the **Dàodé ZhēnJīng Yìzhōng Miàojiàn** scroll, the nineteenth of the **Miàojiàn**.

**Dao/Way and Virtue True Classic Elucidations Manuscript Roll Twenty**
*Collected by the Recluse of Five Peaks, Qing’an Rongsheng, Wang Shouzheng*

**Chapter Seventy-Seventh: The Dao/Way of Heaven**

The Dao/Way of Heaven is like drawing a bow.
The high it depresses, the low it raises,
The excessive it diminishes, the deficient it supplements.

**Elucidations:**
The bow’s use requires harmonizing materials and form,
So that slack and tension are balanced and effective.
*Quoted from the Zhou Li:*
The bowmaker crafts bows, gathering six materials—dry wood, sinew, horn, silk, glue, and lacquer—at their proper times.
When the six materials are gathered, the skilled artisan blends them.
The wood must be red-black with a resonant tone.
For long-range shooting, power is used; for deep penetration, straightness.
The horn should be green-white with a full tip.
Generally, autumn-killed horn is thick, spring-killed horn is thin.
The horn of young oxen is straight and glossy; that of old oxen is curved and rough.
When the three colors are complete, it is excellent—
Then in winter, dry the wood;
In summer, treat the sinew;
In spring, soften the horn;
In autumn, combine silk, glue, and lacquer.
When the form is set in cold, the bow neither sags nor strains.
With fine materials and skilled craftsmanship, made at the right time,
This is called *Sanjun* [Three Harmonies].

**Elucidations:**
*The Classic of Changes* says:
*"Establishing the Dao/Way of Heaven: yin and yang."*
*Quoted from the Zhouyi’s* Sayings on the Trigrams:
*"In ancient times, the Sage composed the Changes*
*To align with the principles of nature and life.*
Thus, the Dao/Way of Heaven is established as yin and yang,
The Dao/Way of Earth as softness and hardness,
The Dao/Way of Humanity as virtue and righteousness.
Combining the Three Powers and pairing them,
Thus the Changes form six lines into a hexagram.
Dividing yin and yang, alternating softness and hardness,
Thus the Changes form six positions into a chapter."

**Elucidations:**
The sun and moon, cold and heat,
Come and go in turn—
Thus, the excessive is diminished.
What departs is given to the deficient,
And the year’s work is fulfilled.
*Quoted from the Yi System:*
*"The sun departs, the moon arrives; the moon departs, the sun arrives.
The sun and moon push against each other, and light is born.
Cold departs, heat arrives; heat departs, cold arrives.
Cold and heat push against each other, and the year’s work is fulfilled.
Is this not so?"*

**Elucidations:**
Thus, raising the deficient and diminishing the excessive through humility,
Seeking balance and equitable distribution.
*Quoted:*
From the above text, the *Yi’s* Humility Hexagram’s symbol says:
*"The Dao/Way of Heaven diminishes the excessive and supplements humility."*
Its image:
*"Earth over mountain: Humility.
The superior person, with compassion, aids the few,
Balancing things and distributing evenly."*
Now, taking these passages to illustrate
The warning of Heaven’s Dao/Way—depressing the high, raising the low.

Heaven’s Dao/Way diminishes the excessive and supplements the deficient,
But humanity’s Dao/Way is the opposite—
Diminishing the deficient to serve the excessive.

**Elucidations:**
The *Yi’s* Diminishing Hexagram says:
*"Diminish the lower, benefit the upper; its Dao/Way ascends."*
The Benefiting Hexagram says:
*"Diminish the upper, benefit the lower; the people rejoice without bound."*
*Quoted from the Yi:*
The Diminishing Hexagram’s *Simian* says:
*"Diminish the lower, benefit the upper; its Dao/Way ascends."*
The Benefiting Hexagram’s *Simian* says:
*"Diminish the upper, benefit the lower; the people rejoice without bound."*
From above to below, its Dao/Way shines greatly.
Moreover, according to the original hexagram’s interpretation,
In the T’ai Hexagram, diminishing the Nine at the third position
And benefiting the Nine at the top forms Diminishing.
In the Pi Hexagram, diminishing the Nine at the fourth position
And benefiting the Nine at the first forms Benefiting.
Thus, taking from the upper’s excess
To benefit the lower’s deficiency—
This is called Benefiting.
Its meaning:
Heaven’s Dao/Way is balanced—
The excessive must be diminished, the deficient must be supplemented,
Opposite to humanity’s Dao/Way.
This is diminishing the deficient to serve the excessive,
Taking from the many to benefit the few.
Now, combining the meanings of
Depressing the high, raising the low,
Diminishing the excessive, supplementing the deficient,
The essentials for governing the state and harmonizing the people,
The rules for cultivating oneself and aligning with the Dao/Way—
This is its unique virtue.
Who can serve the world with their excess?
Only those in accord with the Dao/Way.

**Elucidations:**
In the past, the Celestial Master Zhi Qianyou,
When alive, was renowned for his *Kaochao* method.
*Quoted from the Celestial Master’s Family Transmission:*
The full account is already in the *Elucidations*—
No need to repeat it here.

**Chapter Seventy-Eight: The Soft and Weak of the World**

The soft and weak of the world have none more so than water,
Yet in assaulting the firm and strong, none can surpass it—
Its power cannot be exchanged.

**Elucidations:**
Water’s nature is gentle, yielding without contention,
Its motion and stillness adapt to the times,
Its form and shape conform to the vessel.

Quoted from

**Thus the Sage speaks:**

To bear the filth of the state—this is called the steward of the ancestral altars.
To bear the misfortunes of the state—this is called the sovereign of all under Heaven.
Upright speech as if inverted.

**Elucidations:**
If the sovereign can contain impurity and endure defilement,
Drawing all the faults of the myriad regions unto himself.

Quoted from *The Book of Documents* [□ *Tang’s Admonition*]:
*"If you have virtue, I dare not conceal it.
If the guilt lies with me, I dare not pardon myself—
Only let it be weighed in the heart of the Most High.
As for the myriad regions, if they have guilt, it lies with me alone.
If I alone bear guilt, I shall not harm the myriad regions."*

**Elucidations:**
In the Spring and Autumn period, Jin attacked Chu, advancing three *shè* without halt.
The ministers requested permission to strike.
Quoted from *The Honglei Explanations*:
*"Jin attacked Chu—three *shè* (each *shè* being thirty *li*)—and did not stop.
The ministers said: ‘May we strike?’
Zhuang Wang replied: ‘In the days of my predecessors, Jin did not attack Chu.’*
(The meaning is complete; no need to repeat the full text.)

**Elucidations:**
Song Jing Gong was afflicted by Upholder of the Stars [Mars] in the Heart constellation.
Quoted from *The Honglei Explanations* (the full text is preserved there; no need to repeat.)

「Chapter Seventy-Nine: Resolving Great Grievances
Resolving great grievances will inevitably leave lingering grievances—
how can this be considered a good deed?

**Elucidations:**
Zhuangzi said: *"Those who seek vengeance do not break the *nuè* handle,*
*even if they harbor resentment, they do not blame the stray tiles.*
The *nuè* handle is a component of the *nuè* sword (a legendary weapon).
Though one might resent the stray tiles,
people do not blame them—
for they lack sentience.
Thus, the sage holds only the left contract,
and does not demand repayment from others.*

**Elucidations:**
From *The Book of Rites (Liji)*:
*"The one presenting millet holds the contract’s terms.*
(The meaning is complete; no need to repeat the full text.)

Therefore, those with **virtue** oversee contracts (*qì*), those without oversee the *chè* (lit. ‘piercing’).
The Dao of Heaven shows no favoritism—
it always aligns with those of **virtue**.

**Elucidations:**
(Like Guo Ju’s filial piety toward his mother—
the meaning is already complete; no need to repeat.)

**Chapter Eighty: The Small State with Few People**
The small state with few people...»

Let the state have tenfold and hundredfold implements yet not use them.
Let the people hold death in reverence, yet not flee their homes.

**Elucidations:**
As with the Three Migrations of Pan Geng—
Pan Geng, younger brother of Yang Jia,
from his ancestor Yi’s capital at Geng Yi by the River,
moved to Yin.
**Annotation:** *The Three Migrations of Pan Geng* (the *Rites of Zhou* states):
The upper section addressed the nobles,
the middle the common folk,
the lower the officials, clans, and surnames—
illustrating the benefits of relocation
and the perils of refusal.
After Pan Geng’s migration,
he settled his people in their new homes,
then ordered their ranks,
and soothed the myriad multitudes.

The Great King’s relocation beneath Ji Mountain
is already detailed in the *Sixteen Chapters of the Great State* (under the *Downflow* section),
so it need not be repeated.

**Elucidations:**
When the people scatter and dwell not in peace,
yet still return to labor and restore their homes—
even the aged and widowed
find their proper place.
Though there are boats and carriages,
none are mounted upon them.
Though there are armor and weapons,
none are arrayed in battle.
Let the people return to knotted cords for their use.

**Elucidations:**
*Carving wood into boats*
to cross the waters.
**Annotation:** *The Zhou易 (Easy)* (in its *Lower Appendices*) states:
*"Carving wood into boats, hewing wood into oars—*
the advantage of boats and oars
lies in ferrying what cannot pass
and reaching distant lands,
thus benefiting all under Heaven."
This is drawn from the *Yan* (泱) hexagram,
whose *Simile* (彖辞) reads:
*"Advantageous to cross great rivers—*
to ride upon wood is to achieve merit."*

**Elucidations:**
*Zhuangzi* says:
In the age of **ultimate virtue**,
mountains lack paths or tunnels,
marshes lack boats or bridges—
all things thrive in clusters,
bound to their native lands.
**Annotation:** *From Zhuangzi’s* **《Ma Ti (马蹄篇)》** *it reads:*
*"In the age of **ultimate virtue**,
their movement is **filling**—*
**filling** (填填) denotes contentment.
Their gaze is **precious**—*
**precious** (颠颠) means upright vision."*

At this time,
mountains had no paths or tunnels,
marshes had no boats or bridges—
all things flourished in clusters,
bound to their native lands,
birds and beasts gathered in herds,
grasses and trees grew in abundance.

Therefore, beasts could be tethered and led to roam,
and the nests of birds could be climbed to observe.

In the age of **ultimate virtue**,
people dwelled together with beasts,
and their clans mingled with all things—
how could they distinguish between the **noble** and the **petty**?

At this time,
the people were self-sufficient,
with no need for trade, commerce, or migration.
Thus, it is said:
*"Though there were boats and carriages,
there was nothing to ride upon."*

**Elucidations:**
*"Though there were strong armor and sharp weapons,
there was no occasion to display them."*

Quoted from *Mencius* **《Liang Hui Wang Zhang Ju Shang (梁惠王章句上)》**,
Mencius spoke to **King Hui of Liang** (梁惠王):
*"If you, O King, were to practice **benevolent governance** (仁政) among the people,
reduce punishments,
lighten taxes and levies,
and encourage deep plowing with frequent crop rotation—
then the strong would use their leisure days
to cultivate filial piety, fraternal respect, loyalty, and trustworthiness,
serving their fathers and elder brothers at home,
and their superiors abroad.
Thus, they could be made to wield their spears
and repel the **fierce armor and sharp weapons** of Qin and Chu."*

**Elucidations:**
*The Book of Changes* (《易》) system states:
*"In the upper antiquity, they governed by knotting cords.
Later sages replaced this with written contracts,
and the myriad officials governed with them,
while the common people discerned right from wrong.
This is drawn from the **Jue** (夬) hexagram,
which signifies **resolution** (决).
Five yang lines jointly resolve one yin line—
taking the meaning of decisive judgment."*

*"They found their food sweet,
their garments fine,
their dwellings secure,
and their customs joyful.
Neighboring countries were visible to one another,
and the sounds of roosters and dogs could be heard—
yet the people lived and died
without ever visiting one another."*

The **Elucidations** state:

*"Though their fare be coarse—vegetable gruel and hemp porridge—
what they eat is always sweet.
**Annotations:**
*Vegetable fare* is taken from *The Analects* (《论语》·述而篇), where Confucius says:
*'To eat coarse rice, drink water, bend one’s arm as a pillow—
joy lies therein.'*
*Unrighteous wealth is like floating clouds to me.*

*[Further], hemp porridge* is cited from *Zhuangzi* (《庄子》·让王篇):
*When Confucius was besieged between Chen and Cai,
for seven days he ate no cooked food,
his hemp porridge unseasoned,
his complexion wan—
yet he played his lute and sang within his chamber.*
Here, the intent transcends mere sweetness of food;
it lies in the joy of the Dao itself.*

---

*"Though their garments be rough—hemp cloth and deer-fur pelts—
what they wear is always fine.
**Annotations:**
From *Liezi* (《列子》·天瑞篇):
*Confucius wandered on Mount Tai,
and saw Rong Qichang walking in the wilds of Yong,
clad in deer-fur, girded with a rope,
plucking his lute and singing.
Confucius asked: ‘Master, whence comes your joy?’
He replied: ‘My joys are manifold.
That I am born human—this is my first joy.
That I am male, honored above female—this is my second.
That I have lived to ninety—this is my third.
Poverty is the common lot; death, the end of all.
To dwell in the common and reach the end—what is there to fear?’
Confucius said: ‘Truly, one who knows how to ease himself!’
Thus, this is cited to prove the joy of deer-fur attire.*

---

*"Though their dwellings be humble—reed-thatched huts and wattle walls—
their homes are always secure.
**Annotations:**
*Reed-thatched huts and wattle walls* are taken from *Zhuangzi* (《庄子》·让王篇):
*Yuan Xian dwelt in Lu,
in a walled chamber of four paces,
thatched with living grass.
His wicker door was incomplete,
a willow branch its hinge.
His jar-windowed rooms,
patched with coarse cloth—
leaking above, damp below—
he sat cross-legged, plucking his strings.
Thus is proven the security of such humble dwellings.*

---

*"Though they endure the torments of southern heat and northern cold,
their abode is ever joyous.
**Annotations:**
*Southern heat and northern cold*—
The southernmost lands are lands of pure yang, scorched by fire-mountains,
where rats spin their fur into cloth, called *fire-washed silk*.
The northernmost lands are lands of pure yin, frozen by ice-mountains,
where silkworms dwell, known as *ice-silkworms*.
Though neither heat nor cold can harm them,
yet each abides in its place,
each fulfills its nature—
thus is proven the harmony of *dwelling in one’s place*.

---

*"By nature, their customs know no untimely death,
their land no pestilence.
**Annotations:**
From *Liezi* (《列子》·黄帝篇):
*The Listening-to-the-Maiden Mountain lies in the River-Sea Isles,
as recorded in *The Classic of Mountains and Seas*.
Upon it dwell divine beings.
They breathe the wind, drink the dew,
consume no five grains,
their hearts as deep as springs,
their forms as pure as maidens.
They neither cling nor love;
the sage and the immortal serve them.
They neither fear nor rage;
the spirits and demons obey them.
They neither bestow nor repay,
yet all is sufficient.
They neither hoard nor gather,
yet lack nothing.
*Shen* (蹇) means deficiency.
The harmony of yin and yang is constant,
the sun and moon ever bright,
the seasons ever true,
the winds and rains ever balanced,
the harvests ever bountiful.
Their land knows no injury,
their people no calamity,
their creatures no blemish,
their spirits no malevolence.*

---

*"Like fish that swim in rivers and lakes,
or men who forget each other in the Way.
**Annotations:**
From *Zhuangzi* (《庄子》·大宗师篇):
*When the spring dries up,
the fish gather on land,
blowing on one another to keep moist,
splashing each other with spittle—
yet this is not as good as forgetting one another in the rivers and lakes.*

---

*"In one village, only the Zhu and Chen clans,
intermarried through generations.
**Annotations:**
Bai Juyi composed a poem in praise of this.
*[Quoted from] ‘Trustworthy Words Are Not Beautiful’ (信言不美), Chapter Eighty.*

True words are not beautiful,
beautiful words are not true.
The virtuous do not argue,
those who argue are not virtuous.
The wise are not all-knowing,
the all-knowing are not wise.

**Extended Meaning:**
As the *Classic of Poetry* says:
*"Thieving words are most sweet,
the finest of tastes."*
*"Crafty words flow like a reed-pipe,
the finest of sounds."*

Quoted from *Mao’s Commentary on the Odes* (《毛诗》·《小雅》·《巧言》):
*"The noble man makes many oaths,
yet disorder grows long.*
*The noble man trusts thieves,
yet disorder grows violent.*
*Thieving words are most sweet,
yet disorder grows gluttonous.*
*Not their steadfastness—
it is the king’s fault.*
*Tenderly nurtured, the willow grows,*
*the noble man plants it.*
*Coming and going, words are spoken,*
*yet the heart counts them.*
*Snake-like, grand words,*
*they issue from the mouth.*
*Crafty words flow like a reed-pipe,*
*their falsehood is thick."*

**Extended Meaning:**
Confucius said: *"Words, merely to convey meaning—
thus, no need for eloquence."*
Quoted from *The Analects* (《论语》·《卫灵公》 15):
*"The Master said: ‘Words, merely to convey meaning—
nothing more.’*
*Language seeks only to express intent,*
*not to adorn with richness."*

**Extended Meaning:**
*"One thread runs through it all."*
Quoted from *The Analects* (《论语》·《里仁》 4):
*"The Master said: ‘Zeng, my Way is one thread running through it all.’"*
*Zeng* was the name of Zengzi.

**Extended Meaning:**
Zhuangzi said: *"When ornamentation extinguishes substance,
and erudition drowns the heart,
thus it is said: ‘The all-knowing are not wise.’"*
Quoted from *Zhuangzi* (《庄子》·《缮性篇》):
*"Then they adorn it with ornament,
enrich it with erudition.
Ornamentation extinguishes substance,
erudition drowns the heart—
then the people begin to be confused and disordered,
unable to return to their true nature
and restore their original state.*
*The great principle speaks of how the world indulges in artificial pursuits,
using superficial elegance to obscure their innate simplicity.
With vast learning and broad reading,
they drown their pure and sincere hearts.
Thus they lose the Dao of effortless spontaneity.*
*Hence the Classic says: ‘The wise are not all-knowing,
the all-knowing are not wise.’*
*The Sage does not hoard.*
*Having given to others,
he grows ever more abundant.*
*Having given to others,
he grows ever more plentiful."*

**Extended Meaning:**
Fan Zhongyan instructed his son, Yaofu.
*[Quoted in detail in *Yizhong*, no need to repeat.]*

**End of the Twenty-First Volume of *Extended Meanings of the Daoist Classic of Virtue and the Way*.**
